    44	TWENTY THOUSAND LEAGUES UNDER THE SEA
    45	
    46	
    47	by
    48	
    49	JULES VERNE
    50	
    51	
    52	
    53	
    54	
    55	PART ONE
    56	
    57	
    58	
    59	CHAPTER I
    60	
    61	A SHIFTING REEF
    62	
    63	The year 1866 was signalised by a remarkable incident, a mysterious and
    64	puzzling phenomenon, which doubtless no one has yet forgotten.  Not to
    65	mention rumours which agitated the maritime population and excited the
    66	public mind, even in the interior of continents, seafaring men were
    67	particularly excited.  Merchants, common sailors, captains of vessels,
    68	skippers, both of Europe and America, naval officers of all countries,
    69	and the Governments of several States on the two continents, were
    70	deeply interested in the matter.
    71	
    72	For some time past vessels had been met by "an enormous thing," a long
    73	object, spindle-shaped, occasionally phosphorescent, and infinitely
    74	larger and more rapid in its movements than a whale.
    75	
    76	The facts relating to this apparition (entered in various log-books)
    77	agreed in most respects as to the shape of the object or creature in
    78	question, the untiring rapidity of its movements, its surprising power
    79	of locomotion, and the peculiar life with which it seemed endowed.  If
    80	it was a whale, it surpassed in size all those hitherto classified in
    81	science.  Taking into consideration the mean of observations made at
    82	divers times--rejecting the timid estimate of those who assigned to
    83	this object a length of two hundred feet, equally with the exaggerated
    84	opinions which set it down as a mile in width and three in length--we
    85	might fairly conclude that this mysterious being surpassed greatly all
    86	dimensions admitted by the learned ones of the day, if it existed at
    87	all.  And that it DID exist was an undeniable fact; and, with that
    88	tendency which disposes the human mind in favour of the marvellous, we
    89	can understand the excitement produced in the entire world by this
    90	supernatural apparition.  As to classing it in the list of fables, the
    91	idea was out of the question.
    92	
    93	On the 20th of July, 1866, the steamer Governor Higginson, of the
    94	Calcutta and Burnach Steam Navigation Company, had met this moving mass
    95	five miles off the east coast of Australia.  Captain Baker thought at
    96	first that he was in the presence of an unknown sandbank; he even
    97	prepared to determine its exact position when two columns of water,
    98	projected by the mysterious object, shot with a hissing noise a hundred
    99	and fifty feet up into the air.  Now, unless the sandbank had been
   100	submitted to the intermittent eruption of a geyser, the Governor
   101	Higginson had to do neither more nor less than with an aquatic mammal,
   102	unknown till then, which threw up from its blow-holes columns of water
   103	mixed with air and vapour.
   104	
   105	Similar facts were observed on the 23rd of July in the same year, in
   106	the Pacific Ocean, by the Columbus, of the West India and Pacific Steam
   107	Navigation Company.  But this extraordinary creature could transport
   108	itself from one place to another with surprising velocity; as, in an
   109	interval of three days, the Governor Higginson and the Columbus had
   110	observed it at two different points of the chart, separated by a
   111	distance of more than seven hundred nautical leagues.
   112	
   113	Fifteen days later, two thousand miles farther off, the Helvetia, of
   114	the Compagnie-Nationale, and the Shannon, of the Royal Mail Steamship
   115	Company, sailing to windward in that portion of the Atlantic lying
   116	between the United States and Europe, respectively signalled the
   117	monster to each other in 42° 15' N. lat. and 60° 35' W. long.
   118	In these simultaneous observations they thought themselves justified in
   119	estimating the minimum length of the mammal at more than three hundred
   120	and fifty feet, as the Shannon and Helvetia were of smaller dimensions
   121	than it, though they measured three hundred feet over all.
   122	
   123	Now the largest whales, those which frequent those parts of the sea
   124	round the Aleutian, Kulammak, and Umgullich islands, have never
   125	exceeded the length of sixty yards, if they attain that.
   126	
   127	In every place of great resort the monster was the fashion.  They sang
   128	of it in the cafes, ridiculed it in the papers, and represented it on
   129	the stage.  All kinds of stories were circulated regarding it.  There
   130	appeared in the papers caricatures of every gigantic and imaginary
   131	creature, from the white whale, the terrible "Moby Dick" of sub-arctic
   132	regions, to the immense kraken, whose tentacles could entangle a ship
   133	of five hundred tons and hurry it into the abyss of the ocean.  The
   134	legends of ancient times were even revived.
   135	
   136	Then burst forth the unending argument between the believers and the
   137	unbelievers in the societies of the wise and the scientific journals.
   138	"The question of the monster" inflamed all minds.  Editors of
   139	scientific journals, quarrelling with believers in the supernatural,
   140	spilled seas of ink during this memorable campaign, some even drawing
   141	blood; for from the sea-serpent they came to direct personalities.
   142	
   143	During the first months of the year 1867 the question seemed buried,
   144	never to revive, when new facts were brought before the public.  It was
   145	then no longer a scientific problem to be solved, but a real danger
   146	seriously to be avoided.  The question took quite another shape.  The
   147	monster became a small island, a rock, a reef, but a reef of indefinite
   148	and shifting proportions.
   149	
   150	On the 5th of March, 1867, the Moravian, of the Montreal Ocean Company,
   151	finding herself during the night in 27° 30' lat. and 72° 15'
   152	long., struck on her starboard quarter a rock, marked in no chart for
   153	that part of the sea.  Under the combined efforts of the wind and its
   154	four hundred horse power, it was going at the rate of thirteen knots.
   155	Had it not been for the superior strength of the hull of the Moravian,
   156	she would have been broken by the shock and gone down with the 237
   157	passengers she was bringing home from Canada.
   158	
   159	The accident happened about five o'clock in the morning, as the day was
   160	breaking.  The officers of the quarter-deck hurried to the after-part
   161	of the vessel.  They examined the sea with the most careful attention.
   162	They saw nothing but a strong eddy about three cables' length distant,
   163	as if the surface had been violently agitated.  The bearings of the
   164	place were taken exactly, and the Moravian continued its route without
   165	apparent damage.  Had it struck on a submerged rock, or on an enormous
   166	wreck?  They could not tell; but, on examination of the ship's bottom
   167	when undergoing repairs, it was found that part of her keel was broken.
   168	
   169	This fact, so grave in itself, might perhaps have been forgotten like
   170	many others if, three weeks after, it had not been re-enacted under
   171	similar circumstances.  But, thanks to the nationality of the victim of
   172	the shock, thanks to the reputation of the company to which the vessel
   173	belonged, the circumstance became extensively circulated.
   174	
   175	The 13th of April, 1867, the sea being beautiful, the breeze
   176	favourable, the Scotia, of the Cunard Company's line, found herself in
   177	15° 12' long. and 45° 37' lat.  She was going at the speed of
   178	thirteen knots and a half.
   179	
   180	At seventeen minutes past four in the afternoon, whilst the passengers
   181	were assembled at lunch in the great saloon, a slight shock was felt on
   182	the hull of the Scotia, on her quarter, a little aft of the port-paddle.
   183	
   184	The Scotia had not struck, but she had been struck, and seemingly by
   185	something rather sharp and penetrating than blunt.  The shock had been
   186	so slight that no one had been alarmed, had it not been for the shouts
   187	of the carpenter's watch, who rushed on to the bridge, exclaiming, "We
   188	are sinking! we are sinking!"  At first the passengers were much
   189	frightened, but Captain Anderson hastened to reassure them.  The danger
   190	could not be imminent.  The Scotia, divided into seven compartments by
   191	strong partitions, could brave with impunity any leak.  Captain
   192	Anderson went down immediately into the hold.  He found that the sea
   193	was pouring into the fifth compartment; and the rapidity of the influx
   194	proved that the force of the water was considerable.  Fortunately this
   195	compartment did not hold the boilers, or the fires would have been
   196	immediately extinguished.  Captain Anderson ordered the engines to be
   197	stopped at once, and one of the men went down to ascertain the extent
   198	of the injury.  Some minutes afterwards they discovered the existence
   199	of a large hole, two yards in diameter, in the ship's bottom.  Such a
   200	leak could not be stopped; and the Scotia, her paddles half submerged,
   201	was obliged to continue her course.  She was then three hundred miles
   202	from Cape Clear, and, after three days' delay, which caused great
   203	uneasiness in Liverpool, she entered the basin of the company.
   204	
   205	The engineers visited the Scotia, which was put in dry dock.  They
   206	could scarcely believe it possible; at two yards and a half below
   207	water-mark was a regular rent, in the form of an isosceles triangle.
   208	The broken place in the iron plates was so perfectly defined that it
   209	could not have been more neatly done by a punch.  It was clear, then,
   210	that the instrument producing the perforation was not of a common stamp
   211	and, after having been driven with prodigious strength, and piercing an
   212	iron plate 1 3/8 inches thick, had withdrawn itself by a backward
   213	motion.
   214	
   215	Such was the last fact, which resulted in exciting once more the
   216	torrent of public opinion.  From this moment all unlucky casualties
   217	which could not be otherwise accounted for were put down to the monster.
   218	
   219	Upon this imaginary creature rested the responsibility of all these
   220	shipwrecks, which unfortunately were considerable; for of three
   221	thousand ships whose loss was annually recorded at Lloyd's, the number
   222	of sailing and steam-ships supposed to be totally lost, from the
   223	absence of all news, amounted to not less than two hundred!
   224	
   225	Now, it was the "monster" who, justly or unjustly, was accused of their
   226	disappearance, and, thanks to it, communication between the different
   227	continents became more and more dangerous.  The public demanded sharply
   228	that the seas should at any price be relieved from this formidable
   229	cetacean.[1]
   230	
   231	
   232	[1] Member of the whale family.
   233	
   234	
   235	
   236	CHAPTER II
   237	
   238	PRO AND CON
   239	
   240	At the period when these events took place, I had just returned from a
   241	scientific research in the disagreeable territory of Nebraska, in the
   242	United States.  In virtue of my office as Assistant Professor in the
   243	Museum of Natural History in Paris, the French Government had attached
   244	me to that expedition.  After six months in Nebraska, I arrived in New
   245	York towards the end of March, laden with a precious collection.  My
   246	departure for France was fixed for the first days in May.  Meanwhile I
   247	was occupying myself in classifying my mineralogical, botanical, and
   248	zoological riches, when the accident happened to the Scotia.
   249	
   250	I was perfectly up in the subject which was the question of the day.
   251	How could I be otherwise?  I had read and reread all the American and
   252	European papers without being any nearer a conclusion.  This mystery
   253	puzzled me.  Under the impossibility of forming an opinion, I jumped
   254	from one extreme to the other.  That there really was something could
   255	not be doubted, and the incredulous were invited to put their finger on
   256	the wound of the Scotia.
   257	
   258	On my arrival at New York the question was at its height.  The theory
   259	of the floating island, and the unapproachable sandbank, supported by
   260	minds little competent to form a judgment, was abandoned.  And, indeed,
   261	unless this shoal had a machine in its stomach, how could it change its
   262	position with such astonishing rapidity?
   263	
   264	From the same cause, the idea of a floating hull of an enormous wreck
   265	was given up.
   266	
   267	There remained, then, only two possible solutions of the question,
   268	which created two distinct parties:  on one side, those who were for a
   269	monster of colossal strength; on the other, those who were for a
   270	submarine vessel of enormous motive power.
   271	
   272	But this last theory, plausible as it was, could not stand against
   273	inquiries made in both worlds.  That a private gentleman should have
   274	such a machine at his command was not likely.  Where, when, and how was
   275	it built? and how could its construction have been kept secret?
   276	Certainly a Government might possess such a destructive machine.  And
   277	in these disastrous times, when the ingenuity of man has multiplied the
   278	power of weapons of war, it was possible that, without the knowledge of
   279	others, a State might try to work such a formidable engine.
   280	
   281	But the idea of a war machine fell before the declaration of
   282	Governments.  As public interest was in question, and transatlantic
   283	communications suffered, their veracity could not be doubted.  But how
   284	admit that the construction of this submarine boat had escaped the
   285	public eye?  For a private gentleman to keep the secret under such
   286	circumstances would be very difficult, and for a State whose every act
   287	is persistently watched by powerful rivals, certainly impossible.
   288	
   289	Upon my arrival in New York several persons did me the honour of
   290	consulting me on the phenomenon in question.  I had published in France
   291	a work in quarto, in two volumes, entitled Mysteries of the Great
   292	Submarine Grounds.  This book, highly approved of in the learned world,
   293	gained for me a special reputation in this rather obscure branch of
   294	Natural History.  My advice was asked.  As long as I could deny the
   295	reality of the fact, I confined myself to a decided negative.  But
   296	soon, finding myself driven into a corner, I was obliged to explain
   297	myself point by point.  I discussed the question in all its forms,
   298	politically and scientifically; and I give here an extract from a
   299	carefully-studied article which I published in the number of the 30th
   300	of April.  It ran as follows:
   301	
   302	"After examining one by one the different theories, rejecting all other
   303	suggestions, it becomes necessary to admit the existence of a marine
   304	animal of enormous power.
   305	
   306	"The great depths of the ocean are entirely unknown to us.  Soundings
   307	cannot reach them.  What passes in those remote depths--what beings
   308	live, or can live, twelve or fifteen miles beneath the surface of the
   309	waters--what is the organisation of these animals, we can scarcely
   310	conjecture.  However, the solution of the problem submitted to me may
   311	modify the form of the dilemma.  Either we do know all the varieties of
   312	beings which people our planet, or we do not.  If we do NOT know them
   313	all--if Nature has still secrets in the deeps for us, nothing is more
   314	conformable to reason than to admit the existence of fishes, or
   315	cetaceans of other kinds, or even of new species, of an organisation
   316	formed to inhabit the strata inaccessible to soundings, and which an
   317	accident of some sort has brought at long intervals to the upper level
   318	of the ocean.
   319	
   320	"If, on the contrary, we DO know all living kinds, we must necessarily
   321	seek for the animal in question amongst those marine beings already
   322	classed; and, in that case, I should be disposed to admit the existence
   323	of a gigantic narwhal.
   324	
   325	"The common narwhal, or unicorn of the sea, often attains a length of
   326	sixty feet.  Increase its size fivefold or tenfold, give it strength
   327	proportionate to its size, lengthen its destructive weapons, and you
   328	obtain the animal required.  It will have the proportions determined by
   329	the officers of the Shannon, the instrument required by the perforation
   330	of the Scotia, and the power necessary to pierce the hull of the
   331	steamer.
   332	
   333	"Indeed, the narwhal is armed with a sort of ivory sword, a halberd,
   334	according to the expression of certain naturalists.  The principal tusk
   335	has the hardness of steel.  Some of these tusks have been found buried
   336	in the bodies of whales, which the unicorn always attacks with success.
   337	Others have been drawn out, not without trouble, from the bottoms of
   338	ships, which they had pierced through and through, as a gimlet pierces
   339	a barrel.  The Museum of the Faculty of Medicine of Paris possesses one
   340	of these defensive weapons, two yards and a quarter in length, and
   341	fifteen inches in diameter at the base.
   342	
   343	"Very well! suppose this weapon to be six times stronger and the animal
   344	ten times more powerful; launch it at the rate of twenty miles an hour,
   345	and you obtain a shock capable of producing the catastrophe required.
   346	Until further information, therefore, I shall maintain it to be a
   347	sea-unicorn of colossal dimensions, armed not with a halberd, but with
   348	a real spur, as the armoured frigates, or the `rams' of war, whose
   349	massiveness and motive power it would possess at the same time.  Thus
   350	may this puzzling phenomenon be explained, unless there be something
   351	over and above all that one has ever conjectured, seen, perceived, or
   352	experienced; which is just within the bounds of possibility."
   353	
   354	These last words were cowardly on my part; but, up to a certain point,
   355	I wished to shelter my dignity as professor, and not give too much
   356	cause for laughter to the Americans, who laugh well when they do laugh.
   357	I reserved for myself a way of escape.  In effect, however, I admitted
   358	the existence of the "monster." My article was warmly discussed, which
   359	procured it a high reputation.  It rallied round it a certain number of
   360	partisans.  The solution it proposed gave, at least, full liberty to
   361	the imagination.  The human mind delights in grand conceptions of
   362	supernatural beings.  And the sea is precisely their best vehicle, the
   363	only medium through which these giants (against which terrestrial
   364	animals, such as elephants or rhinoceroses, are as nothing) can be
   365	produced or developed.
   366	
   367	The industrial and commercial papers treated the question chiefly from
   368	this point of view.  The Shipping and Mercantile Gazette, the Lloyd's
   369	List, the Packet-Boat, and the Maritime and Colonial Review, all papers
   370	devoted to insurance companies which threatened to raise their rates of
   371	premium, were unanimous on this point.  Public opinion had been
   372	pronounced.  The United States were the first in the field; and in New
   373	York they made preparations for an expedition destined to pursue this
   374	narwhal.  A frigate of great speed, the Abraham Lincoln, was put in
   375	commission as soon as possible.  The arsenals were opened to Commander
   376	Farragut, who hastened the arming of his frigate; but, as it always
   377	happens, the moment it was decided to pursue the monster, the monster
   378	did not appear.  For two months no one heard it spoken of.  No ship met
   379	with it.  It seemed as if this unicorn knew of the plots weaving around
   380	it.  It had been so much talked of, even through the Atlantic cable,
   381	that jesters pretended that this slender fly had stopped a telegram on
   382	its passage and was making the most of it.
   383	
   384	So when the frigate had been armed for a long campaign, and provided
   385	with formidable fishing apparatus, no one could tell what course to
   386	pursue.  Impatience grew apace, when, on the 2nd of July, they learned
   387	that a steamer of the line of San Francisco, from California to
   388	Shanghai, had seen the animal three weeks before in the North Pacific
   389	Ocean.  The excitement caused by this news was extreme.  The ship was
   390	revictualled and well stocked with coal.
   391	
   392	Three hours before the Abraham Lincoln left Brooklyn pier, I received a
   393	letter worded as follows:
   394	
   395	To M. ARONNAX, Professor in the Museum of Paris, Fifth Avenue Hotel,
   396	New York.
   397	
   398	SIR,--If you will consent to join the Abraham Lincoln in this
   399	expedition, the Government of the United States will with pleasure see
   400	France represented in the enterprise.  Commander Farragut has a cabin
   401	at your disposal.
   402	
   403	Very cordially yours, J.B. HOBSON, Secretary of Marine.
   404	
   405	
   406	
   407	CHAPTER III
   408	
   409	I FORM MY RESOLUTION
   410	
   411	Three seconds before the arrival of J. B. Hobson's letter I no more
   412	thought of pursuing the unicorn than of attempting the passage of the
   413	North Sea.  Three seconds after reading the letter of the honourable
   414	Secretary of Marine, I felt that my true vocation, the sole end of my
   415	life, was to chase this disturbing monster and purge it from the world.
   416	
   417	But I had just returned from a fatiguing journey, weary and longing for
   418	repose.  I aspired to nothing more than again seeing my country, my
   419	friends, my little lodging by the Jardin des Plantes, my dear and
   420	precious collections--but nothing could keep me back!  I forgot
   421	all--fatigue, friends and collections--and accepted without hesitation
   422	the offer of the American Government.
   423	
   424	"Besides," thought I, "all roads lead back to Europe; and the unicorn
   425	may be amiable enough to hurry me towards the coast of France.  This
   426	worthy animal may allow itself to be caught in the seas of Europe (for
   427	my particular benefit), and I will not bring back less than half a yard
   428	of his ivory halberd to the Museum of Natural History." But in the
   429	meanwhile I must seek this narwhal in the North Pacific Ocean, which,
   430	to return to France, was taking the road to the antipodes.
   431	
   432	"Conseil," I called in an impatient voice.
   433	
   434	Conseil was my servant, a true, devoted Flemish boy, who had
   435	accompanied me in all my travels.  I liked him, and he returned the
   436	liking well.  He was quiet by nature, regular from principle, zealous
   437	from habit, evincing little disturbance at the different surprises of
   438	life, very quick with his hands, and apt at any service required of
   439	him; and, despite his name, never giving advice--even when asked for it.
   440	
   441	Conseil had followed me for the last ten years wherever science led.
   442	Never once did he complain of the length or fatigue of a journey, never
   443	make an objection to pack his portmanteau for whatever country it might
   444	be, or however far away, whether China or Congo.  Besides all this, he
   445	had good health, which defied all sickness, and solid muscles, but no
   446	nerves; good morals are understood.  This boy was thirty years old, and
   447	his age to that of his master as fifteen to twenty.  May I be excused
   448	for saying that I was forty years old?
   449	
   450	But Conseil had one fault:  he was ceremonious to a degree, and would
   451	never speak to me but in the third person, which was sometimes
   452	provoking.
   453	
   454	"Conseil," said I again, beginning with feverish hands to make
   455	preparations for my departure.
   456	
   457	Certainly I was sure of this devoted boy.  As a rule, I never asked him
   458	if it were convenient for him or not to follow me in my travels; but
   459	this time the expedition in question might be prolonged, and the
   460	enterprise might be hazardous in pursuit of an animal capable of
   461	sinking a frigate as easily as a nutshell.  Here there was matter for
   462	reflection even to the most impassive man in the world.  What would
   463	Conseil say?
   464	
   465	"Conseil," I called a third time.
   466	
   467	Conseil appeared.
   468	
   469	"Did you call, sir?" said he, entering.
   470	
   471	"Yes, my boy; make preparations for me and yourself too.  We leave in
   472	two hours."
   473	
   474	"As you please, sir," replied Conseil, quietly.
   475	
   476	"Not an instant to lose; lock in my trunk all travelling utensils,
   477	coats, shirts, and stockings--without counting, as many as you can, and
   478	make haste."
   479	
   480	"And your collections, sir?" observed Conseil.
   481	
   482	"They will keep them at the hotel."
   483	
   484	"We are not returning to Paris, then?" said Conseil.
   485	
   486	"Oh! certainly," I answered, evasively, "by making a curve."
   487	
   488	"Will the curve please you, sir?"
   489	
   490	"Oh! it will be nothing; not quite so direct a road, that is all.  We
   491	take our passage in the Abraham, Lincoln."
   492	
   493	"As you think proper, sir," coolly replied Conseil.
   494	
   495	"You see, my friend, it has to do with the monster--the famous narwhal.
   496	We are going to purge it from the seas.  A glorious mission, but a
   497	dangerous one!  We cannot tell where we may go; these animals can be
   498	very capricious.  But we will go whether or no; we have got a captain
   499	who is pretty wide-awake."
   500	
   501	Our luggage was transported to the deck of the frigate immediately.  I
   502	hastened on board and asked for Commander Farragut.  One of the sailors
   503	conducted me to the poop, where I found myself in the presence of a
   504	good-looking officer, who held out his hand to me.
   505	
   506	"Monsieur Pierre Aronnax?" said he.
   507	
   508	"Himself," replied I. "Commander Farragut?"
   509	
   510	"You are welcome, Professor; your cabin is ready for you."
   511	
   512	I bowed, and desired to be conducted to the cabin destined for me.
   513	
   514	The Abraham Lincoln had been well chosen and equipped for her new
   515	destination.  She was a frigate of great speed, fitted with
   516	high-pressure engines which admitted a pressure of seven atmospheres.
   517	Under this the Abraham Lincoln attained the mean speed of nearly
   518	eighteen knots and a third an hour--a considerable speed, but,
   519	nevertheless, insufficient to grapple with this gigantic cetacean.
   520	
   521	The interior arrangements of the frigate corresponded to its nautical
   522	qualities.  I was well satisfied with my cabin, which was in the after
   523	part, opening upon the gunroom.
   524	
   525	"We shall be well off here," said I to Conseil.
   526	
   527	"As well, by your honour's leave, as a hermit-crab in the shell of a
   528	whelk," said Conseil.
   529	
   530	I left Conseil to stow our trunks conveniently away, and remounted the
   531	poop in order to survey the preparations for departure.
   532	
   533	At that moment Commander Farragut was ordering the last moorings to be
   534	cast loose which held the Abraham Lincoln to the pier of Brooklyn.  So
   535	in a quarter of an hour, perhaps less, the frigate would have sailed
   536	without me.  I should have missed this extraordinary, supernatural, and
   537	incredible expedition, the recital of which may well meet with some
   538	suspicion.
   539	
   540	But Commander Farragut would not lose a day nor an hour in scouring the
   541	seas in which the animal had been sighted.  He sent for the engineer.
   542	
   543	"Is the steam full on?" asked he.
   544	
   545	"Yes, sir," replied the engineer.
   546	
   547	"Go ahead," cried Commander Farragut.
   548	
   549	
   550	
   551	CHAPTER IV
   552	
   553	NED LAND
   554	
   555	Captain Farragut was a good seaman, worthy of the frigate he commanded.
   556	His vessel and he were one.  He was the soul of it.  On the question of
   557	the monster there was no doubt in his mind, and he would not allow the
   558	existence of the animal to be disputed on board.  He believed in it, as
   559	certain good women believe in the leviathan--by faith, not by reason.
   560	The monster did exist, and he had sworn to rid the seas of it.  Either
   561	Captain Farragut would kill the narwhal, or the narwhal would kill the
   562	captain.  There was no third course.
   563	
   564	The officers on board shared the opinion of their chief.  They were
   565	ever chatting, discussing, and calculating the various chances of a
   566	meeting, watching narrowly the vast surface of the ocean.  More than
   567	one took up his quarters voluntarily in the cross-trees, who would have
   568	cursed such a berth under any other circumstances.  As long as the sun
   569	described its daily course, the rigging was crowded with sailors, whose
   570	feet were burnt to such an extent by the heat of the deck as to render
   571	it unbearable; still the Abraham Lincoln had not yet breasted the
   572	suspected waters of the Pacific.  As to the ship's company, they
   573	desired nothing better than to meet the unicorn, to harpoon it, hoist
   574	it on board, and despatch it.  They watched the sea with eager
   575	attention.
   576	
   577	Besides, Captain Farragut had spoken of a certain sum of two thousand
   578	dollars, set apart for whoever should first sight the monster, were he
   579	cabin-boy, common seaman, or officer.
   580	
   581	I leave you to judge how eyes were used on board the Abraham Lincoln.
   582	
   583	For my own part I was not behind the others, and, left to no one my
   584	share of daily observations.  The frigate might have been called the
   585	Argus, for a hundred reasons.  Only one amongst us, Conseil, seemed to
   586	protest by his indifference against the question which so interested us
   587	all, and seemed to be out of keeping with the general enthusiasm on
   588	board.
   589	
   590	I have said that Captain Farragut had carefully provided his ship with
   591	every apparatus for catching the gigantic cetacean.  No whaler had ever
   592	been better armed.  We possessed every known engine, from the harpoon
   593	thrown by the hand to the barbed arrows of the blunderbuss, and the
   594	explosive balls of the duck-gun.  On the forecastle lay the perfection
   595	of a breech-loading gun, very thick at the breech, and very narrow in
   596	the bore, the model of which had been in the Exhibition of 1867.  This
   597	precious weapon of American origin could throw with ease a conical
   598	projectile of nine pounds to a mean distance of ten miles.
   599	
   600	Thus the Abraham Lincoln wanted for no means of destruction; and, what
   601	was better still she had on board Ned Land, the prince of harpooners.
   602	
   603	Ned Land was a Canadian, with an uncommon quickness of hand, and who
   604	knew no equal in his dangerous occupation.  Skill, coolness, audacity,
   605	and cunning he possessed in a superior degree, and it must be a cunning
   606	whale to escape the stroke of his harpoon.
   607	
   608	Ned Land was about forty years of age; he was a tall man (more than six
   609	feet high), strongly built, grave and taciturn, occasionally violent,
   610	and very passionate when contradicted.  His person attracted attention,
   611	but above all the boldness of his look, which gave a singular
   612	expression to his face.
   613	
   614	Who calls himself Canadian calls himself French; and, little
   615	communicative as Ned Land was, I must admit that he took a certain
   616	liking for me.  My nationality drew him to me, no doubt.  It was an
   617	opportunity for him to talk, and for me to hear, that old language of
   618	Rabelais, which is still in use in some Canadian provinces.  The
   619	harpooner's family was originally from Quebec, and was already a tribe
   620	of hardy fishermen when this town belonged to France.
   621	
   622	Little by little, Ned Land acquired a taste for chatting, and I loved
   623	to hear the recital of his adventures in the polar seas.  He related
   624	his fishing, and his combats, with natural poetry of expression; his
   625	recital took the form of an epic poem, and I seemed to be listening to
   626	a Canadian Homer singing the Iliad of the regions of the North.
   627	
   628	I am portraying this hardy companion as I really knew him.  We are old
   629	friends now, united in that unchangeable friendship which is born and
   630	cemented amidst extreme dangers.  Ah, brave Ned!  I ask no more than to
   631	live a hundred years longer, that I may have more time to dwell the
   632	longer on your memory.
   633	
   634	Now, what was Ned Land's opinion upon the question of the marine
   635	monster?  I must admit that he did not believe in the unicorn, and was
   636	the only one on board who did not share that universal conviction.  He
   637	even avoided the subject, which I one day thought it my duty to press
   638	upon him.  One magnificent evening, the 30th July (that is to say,
   639	three weeks after our departure), the frigate was abreast of Cape
   640	Blanc, thirty miles to leeward of the coast of Patagonia.  We had
   641	crossed the tropic of Capricorn, and the Straits of Magellan opened
   642	less than seven hundred miles to the south.  Before eight days were
   643	over the Abraham Lincoln would be ploughing the waters of the Pacific.
   644	
   645	Seated on the poop, Ned Land and I were chatting of one thing and
   646	another as we looked at this mysterious sea, whose great depths had up
   647	to this time been inaccessible to the eye of man.  I naturally led up
   648	the conversation to the giant unicorn, and examined the various chances
   649	of success or failure of the expedition.  But, seeing that Ned Land let
   650	me speak without saying too much himself, I pressed him more closely.
   651	
   652	"Well, Ned," said I, "is it possible that you are not convinced of the
   653	existence of this cetacean that we are following?  Have you any
   654	particular reason for being so incredulous?"
   655	
   656	The harpooner looked at me fixedly for some moments before answering,
   657	struck his broad forehead with his hand (a habit of his), as if to
   658	collect himself, and said at last, "Perhaps I have, Mr. Aronnax."
   659	
   660	"But, Ned, you, a whaler by profession, familiarised with all the great
   661	marine mammalia--YOU ought to be the last to doubt under such
   662	circumstances!"
   663	
   664	"That is just what deceives you, Professor," replied Ned.  "As a whaler
   665	I have followed many a cetacean, harpooned a great number, and killed
   666	several; but, however strong or well-armed they may have been, neither
   667	their tails nor their weapons would have been able even to scratch the
   668	iron plates of a steamer."
   669	
   670	"But, Ned, they tell of ships which the teeth of the narwhal have
   671	pierced through and through."
   672	
   673	"Wooden ships--that is possible," replied the Canadian, "but I have
   674	never seen it done; and, until further proof, I deny that whales,
   675	cetaceans, or sea-unicorns could ever produce the effect you describe."
   676	
   677	"Well, Ned, I repeat it with a conviction resting on the logic of
   678	facts.  I believe in the existence of a mammal power fully organised,
   679	belonging to the branch of vertebrata, like the whales, the cachalots,
   680	or the dolphins, and furnished with a horn of defence of great
   681	penetrating power."
   682	
   683	"Hum!" said the harpooner, shaking his head with the air of a man who
   684	would not be convinced.
   685	
   686	"Notice one thing, my worthy Canadian," I resumed.  "If such an animal
   687	is in existence, if it inhabits the depths of the ocean, if it
   688	frequents the strata lying miles below the surface of the water, it
   689	must necessarily possess an organisation the strength of which would
   690	defy all comparison."
   691	
   692	"And why this powerful organisation?" demanded Ned.
   693	
   694	"Because it requires incalculable strength to keep one's self in these
   695	strata and resist their pressure.  Listen to me.  Let us admit that the
   696	pressure of the atmosphere is represented by the weight of a column of
   697	water thirty-two feet high.  In reality the column of water would be
   698	shorter, as we are speaking of sea water, the density of which is
   699	greater than that of fresh water.  Very well, when you dive, Ned, as
   700	many times 32 feet of water as there are above you, so many times does
   701	your body bear a pressure equal to that of the atmosphere, that is to
   702	say, 15 lb. for each square inch of its surface.  It follows, then,
   703	that at 320 feet this pressure equals that of 10 atmospheres, of 100
   704	atmospheres at 3,200 feet, and of 1,000 atmospheres at 32,000 feet,
   705	that is, about 6 miles; which is equivalent to saying that if you could
   706	attain this depth in the ocean, each square three-eighths of an inch of
   707	the surface of your body would bear a pressure of 5,600 lb.  Ah! my
   708	brave Ned, do you know how many square inches you carry on the surface
   709	of your body?"
   710	
   711	"I have no idea, Mr. Aronnax."
   712	
   713	"About 6,500; and as in reality the atmospheric pressure is about 15
   714	lb.  to the square inch, your 6,500 square inches bear at this moment a
   715	pressure of 97,500 lb."
   716	
   717	"Without my perceiving it?"
   718	
   719	"Without your perceiving it.  And if you are not crushed by such a
   720	pressure, it is because the air penetrates the interior of your body
   721	with equal pressure.  Hence perfect equilibrium between the interior
   722	and exterior pressure, which thus neutralise each other, and which
   723	allows you to bear it without inconvenience.  But in the water it is
   724	another thing."
   725	
   726	"Yes, I understand," replied Ned, becoming more attentive; "because the
   727	water surrounds me, but does not penetrate."
   728	
   729	"Precisely, Ned:  so that at 32 feet beneath the surface of the sea you
   730	would undergo a pressure of 97,500 lb.; at 320 feet, ten times that
   731	pressure; at 3,200 feet, a hundred times that pressure; lastly, at
   732	32,000 feet, a thousand times that pressure would be 97,500,000
   733	lb.--that is to say, that you would be flattened as if you had been
   734	drawn from the plates of a hydraulic machine!"
   735	
   736	"The devil!" exclaimed Ned.
   737	
   738	"Very well, my worthy harpooner, if some vertebrate, several hundred
   739	yards long, and large in proportion, can maintain itself in such
   740	depths--of those whose surface is represented by millions of square
   741	inches, that is by tens of millions of pounds, we must estimate the
   742	pressure they undergo.  Consider, then, what must be the resistance of
   743	their bony structure, and the strength of their organisation to
   744	withstand such pressure!"
   745	
   746	"Why!" exclaimed Ned Land, "they must be made of iron plates eight
   747	inches thick, like the armoured frigates."
   748	
   749	"As you say, Ned.  And think what destruction such a mass would cause,
   750	if hurled with the speed of an express train against the hull of a
   751	vessel."
   752	
   753	"Yes--certainly--perhaps," replied the Canadian, shaken by these
   754	figures, but not yet willing to give in.
   755	
   756	"Well, have I convinced you?"
   757	
   758	"You have convinced me of one thing, sir, which is that, if such
   759	animals do exist at the bottom of the seas, they must necessarily be as
   760	strong as you say."
   761	
   762	"But if they do not exist, mine obstinate harpooner, how explain the
   763	accident to the Scotia?"
   764	
   765	
   766	
   767	CHAPTER V
   768	
   769	AT A VENTURE
   770	
   771	The voyage of the Abraham Lincoln was for a long time marked by no
   772	special incident.  But one circumstance happened which showed the
   773	wonderful dexterity of Ned Land, and proved what confidence we might
   774	place in him.
   775	
   776	The 30th of June, the frigate spoke some American whalers, from whom we
   777	learned that they knew nothing about the narwhal.  But one of them, the
   778	captain of the Monroe, knowing that Ned Land had shipped on board the
   779	Abraham Lincoln, begged for his help in chasing a whale they had in
   780	sight.  Commander Farragut, desirous of seeing Ned Land at work, gave
   781	him permission to go on board the Monroe.  And fate served our Canadian
   782	so well that, instead of one whale, he harpooned two with a double
   783	blow, striking one straight to the heart, and catching the other after
   784	some minutes' pursuit.
   785	
   786	Decidedly, if the monster ever had to do with Ned Land's harpoon, I
   787	would not bet in its favour.
   788	
   789	The frigate skirted the south-east coast of America with great
   790	rapidity.  The 3rd of July we were at the opening of the Straits of
   791	Magellan, level with Cape Vierges.  But Commander Farragut would not
   792	take a tortuous passage, but doubled Cape Horn.
   793	
   794	The ship's crew agreed with him.  And certainly it was possible that
   795	they might meet the narwhal in this narrow pass.  Many of the sailors
   796	affirmed that the monster could not pass there, "that he was too big
   797	for that!"
   798	
   799	The 6th of July, about three o'clock in the afternoon, the Abraham
   800	Lincoln, at fifteen miles to the south, doubled the solitary island,
   801	this lost rock at the extremity of the American continent, to which
   802	some Dutch sailors gave the name of their native town, Cape Horn.  The
   803	course was taken towards the north-west, and the next day the screw of
   804	the frigate was at last beating the waters of the Pacific.
   805	
   806	"Keep your eyes open!" called out the sailors.
   807	
   808	And they were opened widely.  Both eyes and glasses, a little dazzled,
   809	it is true, by the prospect of two thousand dollars, had not an
   810	instant's repose.
   811	
   812	I myself, for whom money had no charms, was not the least attentive on
   813	board.  Giving but few minutes to my meals, but a few hours to sleep,
   814	indifferent to either rain or sunshine, I did not leave the poop of the
   815	vessel.  Now leaning on the netting of the forecastle, now on the
   816	taffrail, I devoured with eagerness the soft foam which whitened the
   817	sea as far as the eye could reach; and how often have I shared the
   818	emotion of the majority of the crew, when some capricious whale raised
   819	its black back above the waves!  The poop of the vessel was crowded on
   820	a moment.  The cabins poured forth a torrent of sailors and officers,
   821	each with heaving breast and troubled eye watching the course of the
   822	cetacean.  I looked and looked till I was nearly blind, whilst Conseil
   823	kept repeating in a calm voice:
   824	
   825	"If, sir, you would not squint so much, you would see better!"
   826	
   827	But vain excitement!  The Abraham Lincoln checked its speed and made
   828	for the animal signalled, a simple whale, or common cachalot, which
   829	soon disappeared amidst a storm of abuse.
   830	
   831	But the weather was good.  The voyage was being accomplished under the
   832	most favourable auspices.  It was then the bad season in Australia, the
   833	July of that zone corresponding to our January in Europe, but the sea
   834	was beautiful and easily scanned round a vast circumference.
   835	
   836	The 20th of July, the tropic of Capricorn was cut by 105d of longitude,
   837	and the 27th of the same month we crossed the Equator on the 110th
   838	meridian.  This passed, the frigate took a more decided westerly
   839	direction, and scoured the central waters of the Pacific.  Commander
   840	Farragut thought, and with reason, that it was better to remain in deep
   841	water, and keep clear of continents or islands, which the beast itself
   842	seemed to shun (perhaps because there was not enough water for him!
   843	suggested the greater part of the crew). The frigate passed at some
   844	distance from the Marquesas and the Sandwich Islands, crossed the
   845	tropic of Cancer, and made for the China Seas.  We were on the theatre
   846	of the last diversions of the monster:  and, to say truth, we no longer
   847	LIVED on board.  The entire ship's crew were undergoing a nervous
   848	excitement, of which I can give no idea:  they could not eat, they
   849	could not sleep--twenty times a day, a misconception or an optical
   850	illusion of some sailor seated on the taffrail, would cause dreadful
   851	perspirations, and these emotions, twenty times repeated, kept us in a
   852	state of excitement so violent that a reaction was unavoidable.
   853	
   854	And truly, reaction soon showed itself.  For three months, during which
   855	a day seemed an age, the Abraham Lincoln furrowed all the waters of the
   856	Northern Pacific, running at whales, making sharp deviations from her
   857	course, veering suddenly from one tack to another, stopping suddenly,
   858	putting on steam, and backing ever and anon at the risk of deranging
   859	her machinery, and not one point of the Japanese or American coast was
   860	left unexplored.
   861	
   862	The warmest partisans of the enterprise now became its most ardent
   863	detractors.  Reaction mounted from the crew to the captain himself, and
   864	certainly, had it not been for the resolute determination on the part
   865	of Captain Farragut, the frigate would have headed due southward.  This
   866	useless search could not last much longer.  The Abraham Lincoln had
   867	nothing to reproach herself with, she had done her best to succeed.
   868	Never had an American ship's crew shown more zeal or patience; its
   869	failure could not be placed to their charge--there remained nothing but
   870	to return.
   871	
   872	This was represented to the commander.  The sailors could not hide
   873	their discontent, and the service suffered.  I will not say there was a
   874	mutiny on board, but after a reasonable period of obstinacy, Captain
   875	Farragut (as Columbus did) asked for three days' patience.  If in three
   876	days the monster did not appear, the man at the helm should give three
   877	turns of the wheel, and the Abraham Lincoln would make for the European
   878	seas.
   879	
   880	This promise was made on the 2nd of November.  It had the effect of
   881	rallying the ship's crew.  The ocean was watched with renewed
   882	attention.  Each one wished for a last glance in which to sum up his
   883	remembrance.  Glasses were used with feverish activity.  It was a grand
   884	defiance given to the giant narwhal, and he could scarcely fail to
   885	answer the summons and "appear."
   886	
   887	Two days passed, the steam was at half pressure; a thousand schemes
   888	were tried to attract the attention and stimulate the apathy of the
   889	animal in case it should be met in those parts.  Large quantities of
   890	bacon were trailed in the wake of the ship, to the great satisfaction
   891	(I must say) of the sharks.  Small craft radiated in all directions
   892	round the Abraham Lincoln as she lay to, and did not leave a spot of
   893	the sea unexplored.  But the night of the 4th of November arrived
   894	without the unveiling of this submarine mystery.
   895	
   896	The next day, the 5th of November, at twelve, the delay would (morally
   897	speaking) expire; after that time, Commander Farragut, faithful to his
   898	promise, was to turn the course to the south-east and abandon for ever
   899	the northern regions of the Pacific.
   900	
   901	The frigate was then in 31° 15' N. lat. and 136° 42' E. long.
   902	The coast of Japan still remained less than two hundred miles to
   903	leeward.  Night was approaching.  They had just struck eight bells;
   904	large clouds veiled the face of the moon, then in its first quarter.
   905	The sea undulated peaceably under the stern of the vessel.
   906	
   907	At that moment I was leaning forward on the starboard netting.
   908	Conseil, standing near me, was looking straight before him.  The crew,
   909	perched in the ratlines, examined the horizon which contracted and
   910	darkened by degrees.  Officers with their night glasses scoured the
   911	growing darkness:  sometimes the ocean sparkled under the rays of the
   912	moon, which darted between two clouds, then all trace of light was lost
   913	in the darkness.
   914	
   915	In looking at Conseil, I could see he was undergoing a little of the
   916	general influence.  At least I thought so.  Perhaps for the first time
   917	his nerves vibrated to a sentiment of curiosity.
   918	
   919	"Come, Conseil," said I, "this is the last chance of pocketing the two
   920	thousand dollars."
   921	
   922	"May I be permitted to say, sir," replied Conseil, "that I never
   923	reckoned on getting the prize; and, had the government of the Union
   924	offered a hundred thousand dollars, it would have been none the poorer."
   925	
   926	"You are right, Conseil.  It is a foolish affair after all, and one
   927	upon which we entered too lightly.  What time lost, what useless
   928	emotions!  We should have been back in France six months ago."
   929	
   930	"In your little room, sir," replied Conseil, "and in your museum, sir;
   931	and I should have already classed all your fossils, sir.  And the
   932	Babiroussa would have been installed in its cage in the Jardin des
   933	Plantes, and have drawn all the curious people of the capital!"
   934	
   935	"As you say, Conseil.  I fancy we shall run a fair chance of being
   936	laughed at for our pains."
   937	
   938	"That's tolerably certain," replied Conseil, quietly; "I think they
   939	will make fun of you, sir.  And, must I say it----?"
   940	
   941	"Go on, my good friend."
   942	
   943	"Well, sir, you will only get your deserts."
   944	
   945	"Indeed!"
   946	
   947	"When one has the honour of being a _savant_ as you are, sir, one should
   948	not expose one's self to----"
   949	
   950	Conseil had not time to finish his compliment.  In the midst of general
   951	silence a voice had just been heard.  It was the voice of Ned Land
   952	shouting:
   953	
   954	"Look out there!  The very thing we are looking for--on our weather
   955	beam!"
   956	
   957	
   958	
   959	CHAPTER VI
   960	
   961	AT FULL STEAM
   962	
   963	At this cry the whole ship's crew hurried towards the
   964	harpooner--commander, officers, masters, sailors, cabin boys; even the
   965	engineers left their engines, and the stokers their furnaces.
   966	
   967	The order to stop her had been given, and the frigate now simply went
   968	on by her own momentum.  The darkness was then profound, and, however
   969	good the Canadian's eyes were, I asked myself how he had managed to
   970	see, and what he had been able to see.  My heart beat as if it would
   971	break.  But Ned Land was not mistaken, and we all perceived the object
   972	he pointed to.  At two cables' length from the Abraham Lincoln, on the
   973	starboard quarter, the sea seemed to be illuminated all over.  It was
   974	not a mere phosphoric phenomenon.  The monster emerged some fathoms
   975	from the water, and then threw out that very intense but mysterious
   976	light mentioned in the report of several captains.  This magnificent
   977	irradiation must have been produced by an agent of great SHINING power.
   978	The luminous part traced on the sea an immense oval, much elongated,
   979	the centre of which condensed a burning heat, whose overpowering
   980	brilliancy died out by successive gradations.
   981	
   982	"It is only a massing of phosphoric particles," cried one of the
   983	officers.
   984	
   985	"No, sir, certainly not," I replied.  "That brightness is of an
   986	essentially electrical nature.  Besides, see, see! it moves; it is
   987	moving forwards, backwards; it is darting towards us!"
   988	
   989	A general cry arose from the frigate.
   990	
   991	"Silence!" said the captain.  "Up with the helm, reverse the engines."
   992	
   993	The steam was shut off, and the Abraham Lincoln, beating to port,
   994	described a semicircle.
   995	
   996	"Right the helm, go ahead," cried the captain.
   997	
   998	These orders were executed, and the frigate moved rapidly from the
   999	burning light.
  1000	
  1001	I was mistaken.  She tried to sheer off, but the supernatural animal
  1002	approached with a velocity double her own.
  1003	
  1004	We gasped for breath.  Stupefaction more than fear made us dumb and
  1005	motionless.  The animal gained on us, sporting with the waves.  It made
  1006	the round of the frigate, which was then making fourteen knots, and
  1007	enveloped it with its electric rings like luminous dust.
  1008	
  1009	Then it moved away two or three miles, leaving a phosphorescent track,
  1010	like those volumes of steam that the express trains leave behind.  All
  1011	at once from the dark line of the horizon whither it retired to gain
  1012	its momentum, the monster rushed suddenly towards the Abraham Lincoln
  1013	with alarming rapidity, stopped suddenly about twenty feet from the
  1014	hull, and died out--not diving under the water, for its brilliancy did
  1015	not abate--but suddenly, and as if the source of this brilliant
  1016	emanation was exhausted.  Then it reappeared on the other side of the
  1017	vessel, as if it had turned and slid under the hull.  Any moment a
  1018	collision might have occurred which would have been fatal to us.
  1019	However, I was astonished at the manoeuvres of the frigate.  She fled
  1020	and did not attack.
  1021	
  1022	On the captain's face, generally so impassive, was an expression of
  1023	unaccountable astonishment.
  1024	
  1025	"Mr. Aronnax," he said, "I do not know with what formidable being I
  1026	have to deal, and I will not imprudently risk my frigate in the midst
  1027	of this darkness.  Besides, how attack this unknown thing, how defend
  1028	one's self from it?  Wait for daylight, and the scene will change."
  1029	
  1030	"You have no further doubt, captain, of the nature of the animal?"
  1031	
  1032	"No, sir; it is evidently a gigantic narwhal, and an electric one."
  1033	
  1034	"Perhaps," added I, "one can only approach it with a torpedo."
  1035	
  1036	"Undoubtedly," replied the captain, "if it possesses such dreadful
  1037	power, it is the most terrible animal that ever was created.  That is
  1038	why, sir, I must be on my guard."
  1039	
  1040	The crew were on their feet all night.  No one thought of sleep.  The
  1041	Abraham Lincoln, not being able to struggle with such velocity, had
  1042	moderated its pace, and sailed at half speed.  For its part, the
  1043	narwhal, imitating the frigate, let the waves rock it at will, and
  1044	seemed decided not to leave the scene of the struggle.  Towards
  1045	midnight, however, it disappeared, or, to use a more appropriate term,
  1046	it "died out" like a large glow-worm. Had it fled?  One could only
  1047	fear, not hope it.  But at seven minutes to one o'clock in the morning
  1048	a deafening whistling was heard, like that produced by a body of water
  1049	rushing with great violence.
  1050	
  1051	The captain, Ned Land, and I were then on the poop, eagerly peering
  1052	through the profound darkness.
  1053	
  1054	"Ned Land," asked the commander, "you have often heard the roaring of
  1055	whales?"
  1056	
  1057	"Often, sir; but never such whales the sight of which brought me in two
  1058	thousand dollars.  If I can only approach within four harpoons' length
  1059	of it!"
  1060	
  1061	"But to approach it," said the commander, "I ought to put a whaler at
  1062	your disposal?"
  1063	
  1064	"Certainly, sir."
  1065	
  1066	"That will be trifling with the lives of my men."
  1067	
  1068	"And mine too," simply said the harpooner.
  1069	
  1070	Towards two o'clock in the morning, the burning light reappeared, not
  1071	less intense, about five miles to windward of the Abraham Lincoln.
  1072	Notwithstanding the distance, and the noise of the wind and sea, one
  1073	heard distinctly the loud strokes of the animal's tail, and even its
  1074	panting breath.  It seemed that, at the moment that the enormous
  1075	narwhal had come to take breath at the surface of the water, the air
  1076	was engulfed in its lungs, like the steam in the vast cylinders of a
  1077	machine of two thousand horse-power.
  1078	
  1079	"Hum!" thought I, "a whale with the strength of a cavalry regiment
  1080	would be a pretty whale!"
  1081	
  1082	We were on the qui vive till daylight, and prepared for the combat.
  1083	The fishing implements were laid along the hammock nettings.  The
  1084	second lieutenant loaded the blunder busses, which could throw harpoons
  1085	to the distance of a mile, and long duck-guns, with explosive bullets,
  1086	which inflicted mortal wounds even to the most terrible animals.  Ned
  1087	Land contented himself with sharpening his harpoon--a terrible weapon
  1088	in his hands.
  1089	
  1090	At six o'clock day began to break; and, with the first glimmer of
  1091	light, the electric light of the narwhal disappeared.  At seven o'clock
  1092	the day was sufficiently advanced, but a very thick sea fog obscured
  1093	our view, and the best spy glasses could not pierce it.  That caused
  1094	disappointment and anger.
  1095	
  1096	I climbed the mizzen-mast. Some officers were already perched on the
  1097	mast-heads. At eight o'clock the fog lay heavily on the waves, and its
  1098	thick scrolls rose little by little.  The horizon grew wider and
  1099	clearer at the same time.  Suddenly, just as on the day before, Ned
  1100	Land's voice was heard:
  1101	
  1102	"The thing itself on the port quarter!" cried the harpooner.
  1103	
  1104	Every eye was turned towards the point indicated.  There, a mile and a
  1105	half from the frigate, a long blackish body emerged a yard above the
  1106	waves.  Its tail, violently agitated, produced a considerable eddy.
  1107	Never did a tail beat the sea with such violence.  An immense track, of
  1108	dazzling whiteness, marked the passage of the animal, and described a
  1109	long curve.
  1110	
  1111	The frigate approached the cetacean.  I examined it thoroughly.
  1112	
  1113	The reports of the Shannon and of the Helvetia had rather exaggerated
  1114	its size, and I estimated its length at only two hundred and fifty
  1115	feet.  As to its dimensions, I could only conjecture them to be
  1116	admirably proportioned.  While I watched this phenomenon, two jets of
  1117	steam and water were ejected from its vents, and rose to the height of
  1118	120 feet; thus I ascertained its way of breathing.  I concluded
  1119	definitely that it belonged to the vertebrate branch, class mammalia.
  1120	
  1121	The crew waited impatiently for their chief's orders.  The latter,
  1122	after having observed the animal attentively, called the engineer.  The
  1123	engineer ran to him.
  1124	
  1125	"Sir," said the commander, "you have steam up?"
  1126	
  1127	"Yes, sir," answered the engineer.
  1128	
  1129	"Well, make up your fires and put on all steam."
  1130	
  1131	Three hurrahs greeted this order.  The time for the struggle had
  1132	arrived.  Some moments after, the two funnels of the frigate vomited
  1133	torrents of black smoke, and the bridge quaked under the trembling of
  1134	the boilers.
  1135	
  1136	The Abraham Lincoln, propelled by her wonderful screw, went straight at
  1137	the animal.  The latter allowed it to come within half a cable's
  1138	length; then, as if disdaining to dive, it took a little turn, and
  1139	stopped a short distance off.
  1140	
  1141	This pursuit lasted nearly three-quarters of an hour, without the
  1142	frigate gaining two yards on the cetacean.  It was quite evident that
  1143	at that rate we should never come up with it.
  1144	
  1145	"Well, Mr. Land," asked the captain, "do you advise me to put the boats
  1146	out to sea?"
  1147	
  1148	"No, sir," replied Ned Land; "because we shall not take that beast
  1149	easily."
  1150	
  1151	"What shall we do then?"
  1152	
  1153	"Put on more steam if you can, sir.  With your leave, I mean to post
  1154	myself under the bowsprit, and, if we get within harpooning distance, I
  1155	shall throw my harpoon."
  1156	
  1157	"Go, Ned," said the captain.  "Engineer, put on more pressure."
  1158	
  1159	Ned Land went to his post.  The fires were increased, the screw
  1160	revolved forty-three times a minute, and the steam poured out of the
  1161	valves.  We heaved the log, and calculated that the Abraham Lincoln was
  1162	going at the rate of 18 1/2 miles an hour.
  1163	
  1164	But the accursed animal swam at the same speed.
  1165	
  1166	For a whole hour the frigate kept up this pace, without gaining six
  1167	feet.  It was humiliating for one of the swiftest sailers in the
  1168	American navy.  A stubborn anger seized the crew; the sailors abused
  1169	the monster, who, as before, disdained to answer them; the captain no
  1170	longer contented himself with twisting his beard--he gnawed it.
  1171	
  1172	The engineer was called again.
  1173	
  1174	"You have turned full steam on?"
  1175	
  1176	"Yes, sir," replied the engineer.
  1177	
  1178	The speed of the Abraham Lincoln increased.  Its masts trembled down to
  1179	their stepping holes, and the clouds of smoke could hardly find way out
  1180	of the narrow funnels.
  1181	
  1182	They heaved the log a second time.
  1183	
  1184	"Well?" asked the captain of the man at the wheel.
  1185	
  1186	"Nineteen miles and three-tenths, sir."
  1187	
  1188	"Clap on more steam."
  1189	
  1190	The engineer obeyed.  The manometer showed ten degrees.  But the
  1191	cetacean grew warm itself, no doubt; for without straining itself, it
  1192	made 19 3/10 miles.
  1193	
  1194	What a pursuit!  No, I cannot describe the emotion that vibrated
  1195	through me.  Ned Land kept his post, harpoon in hand.  Several times
  1196	the animal let us gain upon it.--"We shall catch it! we shall catch
  1197	it!" cried the Canadian.  But just as he was going to strike, the
  1198	cetacean stole away with a rapidity that could not be estimated at less
  1199	than thirty miles an hour, and even during our maximum of speed, it
  1200	bullied the frigate, going round and round it.  A cry of fury broke
  1201	from everyone!
  1202	
  1203	At noon we were no further advanced than at eight o'clock in the
  1204	morning.
  1205	
  1206	The captain then decided to take more direct means.
  1207	
  1208	"Ah!" said he, "that animal goes quicker than the Abraham Lincoln.
  1209	Very well! we will see whether it will escape these conical bullets.
  1210	Send your men to the forecastle, sir."
  1211	
  1212	The forecastle gun was immediately loaded and slewed round.  But the
  1213	shot passed some feet above the cetacean, which was half a mile off.
  1214	
  1215	"Another, more to the right," cried the commander, "and five dollars to
  1216	whoever will hit that infernal beast."
  1217	
  1218	An old gunner with a grey beard--that I can see now--with steady eye
  1219	and grave face, went up to the gun and took a long aim.  A loud report
  1220	was heard, with which were mingled the cheers of the crew.
  1221	
  1222	The bullet did its work; it hit the animal, and, sliding off the
  1223	rounded surface, was lost in two miles depth of sea.
  1224	
  1225	The chase began again, and the captain, leaning towards me, said:
  1226	
  1227	"I will pursue that beast till my frigate bursts up."
  1228	
  1229	"Yes," answered I; "and you will be quite right to do it."
  1230	
  1231	I wished the beast would exhaust itself, and not be insensible to
  1232	fatigue like a steam engine.  But it was of no use.  Hours passed,
  1233	without its showing any signs of exhaustion.
  1234	
  1235	However, it must be said in praise of the Abraham Lincoln that she
  1236	struggled on indefatigably.  I cannot reckon the distance she made
  1237	under three hundred miles during this unlucky day, November the 6th.
  1238	But night came on, and overshadowed the rough ocean.
  1239	
  1240	Now I thought our expedition was at an end, and that we should never
  1241	again see the extraordinary animal.  I was mistaken.  At ten minutes to
  1242	eleven in the evening, the electric light reappeared three miles to
  1243	windward of the frigate, as pure, as intense as during the preceding
  1244	night.
  1245	
  1246	The narwhal seemed motionless; perhaps, tired with its day's work, it
  1247	slept, letting itself float with the undulation of the waves.  Now was
  1248	a chance of which the captain resolved to take advantage.
  1249	
  1250	He gave his orders.  The Abraham Lincoln kept up half steam, and
  1251	advanced cautiously so as not to awake its adversary.  It is no rare
  1252	thing to meet in the middle of the ocean whales so sound asleep that
  1253	they can be successfully attacked, and Ned Land had harpooned more than
  1254	one during its sleep.  The Canadian went to take his place again under
  1255	the bowsprit.
  1256	
  1257	The frigate approached noiselessly, stopped at two cables' lengths from
  1258	the animal, and following its track.  No one breathed; a deep silence
  1259	reigned on the bridge.  We were not a hundred feet from the burning
  1260	focus, the light of which increased and dazzled our eyes.
  1261	
  1262	At this moment, leaning on the forecastle bulwark, I saw below me Ned
  1263	Land grappling the martingale in one hand, brandishing his terrible
  1264	harpoon in the other, scarcely twenty feet from the motionless animal.
  1265	Suddenly his arm straightened, and the harpoon was thrown; I heard the
  1266	sonorous stroke of the weapon, which seemed to have struck a hard body.
  1267	The electric light went out suddenly, and two enormous waterspouts
  1268	broke over the bridge of the frigate, rushing like a torrent from stem
  1269	to stern, overthrowing men, and breaking the lashings of the spars.  A
  1270	fearful shock followed, and, thrown over the rail without having time
  1271	to stop myself, I fell into the sea.
  1272	
  1273	
  1274	
  1275	CHAPTER VII
  1276	
  1277	AN UNKNOWN SPECIES OF WHALE
  1278	
  1279	This unexpected fall so stunned me that I have no clear recollection of
  1280	my sensations at the time.  I was at first drawn down to a depth of
  1281	about twenty feet.  I am a good swimmer (though without pretending to
  1282	rival Byron or Edgar Poe, who were masters of the art), and in that
  1283	plunge I did not lose my presence of mind.  Two vigorous strokes
  1284	brought me to the surface of the water.  My first care was to look for
  1285	the frigate.  Had the crew seen me disappear?  Had the Abraham Lincoln
  1286	veered round?  Would the captain put out a boat?  Might I hope to be
  1287	saved?
  1288	
  1289	The darkness was intense.  I caught a glimpse of a black mass
  1290	disappearing in the east, its beacon lights dying out in the distance.
  1291	It was the frigate!  I was lost.
  1292	
  1293	"Help, help!"  I shouted, swimming towards the Abraham Lincoln in
  1294	desperation.
  1295	
  1296	My clothes encumbered me; they seemed glued to my body, and paralysed
  1297	my movements.
  1298	
  1299	I was sinking!  I was suffocating!
  1300	
  1301	"Help!"
  1302	
  1303	This was my last cry.  My mouth filled with water; I struggled against
  1304	being drawn down the abyss.  Suddenly my clothes were seized by a
  1305	strong hand, and I felt myself quickly drawn up to the surface of the
  1306	sea; and I heard, yes, I heard these words pronounced in my ear:
  1307	
  1308	"If master would be so good as to lean on my shoulder, master would
  1309	swim with much greater ease."
  1310	
  1311	I seized with one hand my faithful Conseil's arm.
  1312	
  1313	"Is it you?" said I, "you?"
  1314	
  1315	"Myself," answered Conseil; "and waiting master's orders."
  1316	
  1317	"That shock threw you as well as me into the sea?"
  1318	
  1319	"No; but, being in my master's service, I followed him."
  1320	
  1321	The worthy fellow thought that was but natural.
  1322	
  1323	"And the frigate?"  I asked.
  1324	
  1325	"The frigate?" replied Conseil, turning on his back; "I think that
  1326	master had better not count too much on her."
  1327	
  1328	"You think so?"
  1329	
  1330	"I say that, at the time I threw myself into the sea, I heard the men
  1331	at the wheel say, `The screw and the rudder are broken.'
  1332	
  1333	"Broken?"
  1334	
  1335	"Yes, broken by the monster's teeth.  It is the only injury the Abraham
  1336	Lincoln has sustained.  But it is a bad look-out for us--she no longer
  1337	answers her helm."
  1338	
  1339	"Then we are lost!"
  1340	
  1341	"Perhaps so," calmly answered Conseil.  "However, we have still several
  1342	hours before us, and one can do a good deal in some hours."
  1343	
  1344	Conseil's imperturbable coolness set me up again.  I swam more
  1345	vigorously; but, cramped by my clothes, which stuck to me like a leaden
  1346	weight, I felt great difficulty in bearing up.  Conseil saw this.
  1347	
  1348	"Will master let me make a slit?" said he; and, slipping an open knife
  1349	under my clothes, he ripped them up from top to bottom very rapidly.
  1350	Then he cleverly slipped them off me, while I swam for both of us.
  1351	
  1352	Then I did the same for Conseil, and we continued to swim near to each
  1353	other.
  1354	
  1355	Nevertheless, our situation was no less terrible.  Perhaps our
  1356	disappearance had not been noticed; and, if it had been, the frigate
  1357	could not tack, being without its helm.  Conseil argued on this
  1358	supposition, and laid his plans accordingly.  This quiet boy was
  1359	perfectly self-possessed. We then decided that, as our only chance of
  1360	safety was being picked up by the Abraham Lincoln's boats, we ought to
  1361	manage so as to wait for them as long as possible.  I resolved then to
  1362	husband our strength, so that both should not be exhausted at the same
  1363	time; and this is how we managed:  while one of us lay on our back,
  1364	quite still, with arms crossed, and legs stretched out, the other would
  1365	swim and push the other on in front.  This towing business did not last
  1366	more than ten minutes each; and relieving each other thus, we could
  1367	swim on for some hours, perhaps till day-break. Poor chance! but hope
  1368	is so firmly rooted in the heart of man!  Moreover, there were two of
  1369	us.  Indeed I declare (though it may seem improbable) if I sought to
  1370	destroy all hope--if I wished to despair, I could not.
  1371	
  1372	The collision of the frigate with the cetacean had occurred about
  1373	eleven o'clock in the evening before.  I reckoned then we should have
  1374	eight hours to swim before sunrise, an operation quite practicable if
  1375	we relieved each other.  The sea, very calm, was in our favour.
  1376	Sometimes I tried to pierce the intense darkness that was only
  1377	dispelled by the phosphorescence caused by our movements.  I watched
  1378	the luminous waves that broke over my hand, whose mirror-like surface
  1379	was spotted with silvery rings.  One might have said that we were in a
  1380	bath of quicksilver.
  1381	
  1382	Near one o'clock in the morning, I was seized with dreadful fatigue.
  1383	My limbs stiffened under the strain of violent cramp.  Conseil was
  1384	obliged to keep me up, and our preservation devolved on him alone.  I
  1385	heard the poor boy pant; his breathing became short and hurried.  I
  1386	found that he could not keep up much longer.
  1387	
  1388	"Leave me! leave me!"  I said to him.
  1389	
  1390	"Leave my master?  Never!" replied he.  "I would drown first."
  1391	
  1392	Just then the moon appeared through the fringes of a thick cloud that
  1393	the wind was driving to the east.  The surface of the sea glittered
  1394	with its rays.  This kindly light reanimated us.  My head got better
  1395	again.  I looked at all points of the horizon.  I saw the frigate!  She
  1396	was five miles from us, and looked like a dark mass, hardly
  1397	discernible.  But no boats!
  1398	
  1399	I would have cried out.  But what good would it have been at such a
  1400	distance!  My swollen lips could utter no sounds.  Conseil could
  1401	articulate some words, and I heard him repeat at intervals, "Help!
  1402	help!"
  1403	
  1404	Our movements were suspended for an instant; we listened.  It might be
  1405	only a singing in the ear, but it seemed to me as if a cry answered the
  1406	cry from Conseil.
  1407	
  1408	"Did you hear?"  I murmured.
  1409	
  1410	"Yes!  Yes!"
  1411	
  1412	And Conseil gave one more despairing cry.
  1413	
  1414	This time there was no mistake!  A human voice responded to ours!  Was
  1415	it the voice of another unfortunate creature, abandoned in the middle
  1416	of the ocean, some other victim of the shock sustained by the vessel?
  1417	Or rather was it a boat from the frigate, that was hailing us in the
  1418	darkness?
  1419	
  1420	Conseil made a last effort, and, leaning on my shoulder, while I struck
  1421	out in a desperate effort, he raised himself half out of the water,
  1422	then fell back exhausted.
  1423	
  1424	"What did you see?"
  1425	
  1426	"I saw----" murmured he; "I saw--but do not talk--reserve all your
  1427	strength!"
  1428	
  1429	What had he seen?  Then, I know not why, the thought of the monster
  1430	came into my head for the first time!  But that voice!  The time is
  1431	past for Jonahs to take refuge in whales' bellies!  However, Conseil
  1432	was towing me again.  He raised his head sometimes, looked before us,
  1433	and uttered a cry of recognition, which was responded to by a voice
  1434	that came nearer and nearer.  I scarcely heard it.  My strength was
  1435	exhausted; my fingers stiffened; my hand afforded me support no longer;
  1436	my mouth, convulsively opening, filled with salt water.  Cold crept
  1437	over me.  I raised my head for the last time, then I sank.
  1438	
  1439	At this moment a hard body struck me.  I clung to it: then I felt that
  1440	I was being drawn up, that I was brought to the surface of the water,
  1441	that my chest collapsed--I fainted.
  1442	
  1443	It is certain that I soon came to, thanks to the vigorous rubbings that
  1444	I received.  I half opened my eyes.
  1445	
  1446	"Conseil!"  I murmured.
  1447	
  1448	"Does master call me?" asked Conseil.
  1449	
  1450	Just then, by the waning light of the moon which was sinking down to
  1451	the horizon, I saw a face which was not Conseil's and which I
  1452	immediately recognised.
  1453	
  1454	"Ned!"  I cried.
  1455	
  1456	"The same, sir, who is seeking his prize!" replied the Canadian.
  1457	
  1458	"Were you thrown into the sea by the shock to the frigate?"
  1459	
  1460	"Yes, Professor; but more fortunate than you, I was able to find a
  1461	footing almost directly upon a floating island."
  1462	
  1463	"An island?"
  1464	
  1465	"Or, more correctly speaking, on our gigantic narwhal."
  1466	
  1467	"Explain yourself, Ned!"
  1468	
  1469	"Only I soon found out why my harpoon had not entered its skin and was
  1470	blunted."
  1471	
  1472	"Why, Ned, why?"
  1473	
  1474	"Because, Professor, that beast is made of sheet iron."
  1475	
  1476	The Canadian's last words produced a sudden revolution in my brain.  I
  1477	wriggled myself quickly to the top of the being, or object, half out of
  1478	the water, which served us for a refuge.  I kicked it.  It was
  1479	evidently a hard, impenetrable body, and not the soft substance that
  1480	forms the bodies of the great marine mammalia.  But this hard body
  1481	might be a bony covering, like that of the antediluvian animals; and I
  1482	should be free to class this monster among amphibious reptiles, such as
  1483	tortoises or alligators.
  1484	
  1485	Well, no! the blackish back that supported me was smooth, polished,
  1486	without scales.  The blow produced a metallic sound; and, incredible
  1487	though it may be, it seemed, I might say, as if it was made of riveted
  1488	plates.
  1489	
  1490	There was no doubt about it!  This monster, this natural phenomenon
  1491	that had puzzled the learned world, and over thrown and misled the
  1492	imagination of seamen of both hemispheres, it must be owned was a still
  1493	more astonishing phenomenon, inasmuch as it was a simply human
  1494	construction.
  1495	
  1496	We had no time to lose, however.  We were lying upon the back of a sort
  1497	of submarine boat, which appeared (as far as I could judge) like a huge
  1498	fish of steel.  Ned Land's mind was made up on this point.  Conseil and
  1499	I could only agree with him.
  1500	
  1501	Just then a bubbling began at the back of this strange thing (which was
  1502	evidently propelled by a screw), and it began to move.  We had only
  1503	just time to seize hold of the upper part, which rose about seven feet
