  1504	out of the water, and happily its speed was not great.
  1505	
  1506	"As long as it sails horizontally," muttered Ned Land, "I do not mind;
  1507	but, if it takes a fancy to dive, I would not give two straws for my
  1508	life."
  1509	
  1510	The Canadian might have said still less.  It became really necessary to
  1511	communicate with the beings, whatever they were, shut up inside the
  1512	machine.  I searched all over the outside for an aperture, a panel, or
  1513	a manhole, to use a technical expression; but the lines of the iron
  1514	rivets, solidly driven into the joints of the iron plates, were clear
  1515	and uniform.  Besides, the moon disappeared then, and left us in total
  1516	darkness.
  1517	
  1518	At last this long night passed.  My indistinct remembrance prevents my
  1519	describing all the impressions it made.  I can only recall one
  1520	circumstance.  During some lulls of the wind and sea, I fancied I heard
  1521	several times vague sounds, a sort of fugitive harmony produced by
  1522	words of command.  What was, then, the mystery of this submarine craft,
  1523	of which the whole world vainly sought an explanation?  What kind of
  1524	beings existed in this strange boat?  What mechanical agent caused its
  1525	prodigious speed?
  1526	
  1527	Daybreak appeared.  The morning mists surrounded us, but they soon
  1528	cleared off.  I was about to examine the hull, which formed on deck a
  1529	kind of horizontal platform, when I felt it gradually sinking.
  1530	
  1531	"Oh! confound it!" cried Ned Land, kicking the resounding plate.
  1532	"Open, you inhospitable rascals!"
  1533	
  1534	Happily the sinking movement ceased.  Suddenly a noise, like iron works
  1535	violently pushed aside, came from the interior of the boat.  One iron
  1536	plate was moved, a man appeared, uttered an odd cry, and disappeared
  1537	immediately.
  1538	
  1539	Some moments after, eight strong men, with masked faces, appeared
  1540	noiselessly, and drew us down into their formidable machine.
  1541	
  1542	
  1543	
  1544	CHAPTER VIII
  1545	
  1546	MOBILIS IN MOBILI
  1547	
  1548	This forcible abduction, so roughly carried out, was accomplished with
  1549	the rapidity of lightning.  I shivered all over.  Whom had we to deal
  1550	with?  No doubt some new sort of pirates, who explored the sea in their
  1551	own way.  Hardly had the narrow panel closed upon me, when I was
  1552	enveloped in darkness.  My eyes, dazzled with the outer light, could
  1553	distinguish nothing.  I felt my naked feet cling to the rungs of an
  1554	iron ladder.  Ned Land and Conseil, firmly seized, followed me.  At the
  1555	bottom of the ladder, a door opened, and shut after us immediately with
  1556	a bang.
  1557	
  1558	We were alone.  Where, I could not say, hardly imagine.  All was black,
  1559	and such a dense black that, after some minutes, my eyes had not been
  1560	able to discern even the faintest glimmer.
  1561	
  1562	Meanwhile, Ned Land, furious at these proceedings, gave free vent to
  1563	his indignation.
  1564	
  1565	"Confound it!" cried he, "here are people who come up to the Scotch for
  1566	hospitality.  They only just miss being cannibals.  I should not be
  1567	surprised at it, but I declare that they shall not eat me without my
  1568	protesting."
  1569	
  1570	"Calm yourself, friend Ned, calm yourself," replied Conseil, quietly.
  1571	"Do not cry out before you are hurt.  We are not quite done for yet."
  1572	
  1573	"Not quite," sharply replied the Canadian, "but pretty near, at all
  1574	events.  Things look black.  Happily, my bowie knife I have still, and
  1575	I can always see well enough to use it.  The first of these pirates who
  1576	lays a hand on me----"
  1577	
  1578	"Do not excite yourself, Ned," I said to the harpooner, "and do not
  1579	compromise us by useless violence.  Who knows that they will not listen
  1580	to us?  Let us rather try to find out where we are."
  1581	
  1582	I groped about.  In five steps I came to an iron wall, made of plates
  1583	bolted together.  Then turning back I struck against a wooden table,
  1584	near which were ranged several stools.  The boards of this prison were
  1585	concealed under a thick mat, which deadened the noise of the feet.  The
  1586	bare walls revealed no trace of window or door.  Conseil, going round
  1587	the reverse way, met me, and we went back to the middle of the cabin,
  1588	which measured about twenty feet by ten.  As to its height, Ned Land,
  1589	in spite of his own great height, could not measure it.
  1590	
  1591	Half an hour had already passed without our situation being bettered,
  1592	when the dense darkness suddenly gave way to extreme light.  Our prison
  1593	was suddenly lighted, that is to say, it became filled with a luminous
  1594	matter, so strong that I could not bear it at first.  In its whiteness
  1595	and intensity I recognised that electric light which played round the
  1596	submarine boat like a magnificent phenomenon of phosphorescence.  After
  1597	shutting my eyes involuntarily, I opened them, and saw that this
  1598	luminous agent came from a half globe, unpolished, placed in the roof
  1599	of the cabin.
  1600	
  1601	"At last one can see," cried Ned Land, who, knife in hand, stood on the
  1602	defensive.
  1603	
  1604	"Yes," said I; "but we are still in the dark about ourselves."
  1605	
  1606	"Let master have patience," said the imperturbable Conseil.
  1607	
  1608	The sudden lighting of the cabin enabled me to examine it minutely.  It
  1609	only contained a table and five stools.  The invisible door might be
  1610	hermetically sealed.  No noise was heard.  All seemed dead in the
  1611	interior of this boat.  Did it move, did it float on the surface of the
  1612	ocean, or did it dive into its depths?  I could not guess.
  1613	
  1614	A noise of bolts was now heard, the door opened, and two men appeared.
  1615	
  1616	One was short, very muscular, broad-shouldered, with robust limbs,
  1617	strong head, an abundance of black hair, thick moustache, a quick
  1618	penetrating look, and the vivacity which characterises the population
  1619	of Southern France.
  1620	
  1621	The second stranger merits a more detailed description.  I made out his
  1622	prevailing qualities directly:  self-confidence--because his head was
  1623	well set on his shoulders, and his black eyes looked around with cold
  1624	assurance; calmness--for his skin, rather pale, showed his coolness of
  1625	blood; energy--evinced by the rapid contraction of his lofty brows; and
  1626	courage--because his deep breathing denoted great power of lungs.
  1627	
  1628	Whether this person was thirty-five or fifty years of age, I could not
  1629	say.  He was tall, had a large forehead, straight nose, a clearly cut
  1630	mouth, beautiful teeth, with fine taper hands, indicative of a highly
  1631	nervous temperament.  This man was certainly the most admirable
  1632	specimen I had ever met.  One particular feature was his eyes, rather
  1633	far from each other, and which could take in nearly a quarter of the
  1634	horizon at once.
  1635	
  1636	This faculty--(I verified it later)--gave him a range of vision far
  1637	superior to Ned Land's. When this stranger fixed upon an object, his
  1638	eyebrows met, his large eyelids closed around so as to contract the
  1639	range of his vision, and he looked as if he magnified the objects
  1640	lessened by distance, as if he pierced those sheets of water so opaque
  1641	to our eyes, and as if he read the very depths of the seas.
  1642	
  1643	The two strangers, with caps made from the fur of the sea otter, and
  1644	shod with sea boots of seal's skin, were dressed in clothes of a
  1645	particular texture, which allowed free movement of the limbs.  The
  1646	taller of the two, evidently the chief on board, examined us with great
  1647	attention, without saying a word; then, turning to his companion,
  1648	talked with him in an unknown tongue.  It was a sonorous, harmonious,
  1649	and flexible dialect, the vowels seeming to admit of very varied
  1650	accentuation.
  1651	
  1652	The other replied by a shake of the head, and added two or three
  1653	perfectly incomprehensible words.  Then he seemed to question me by a
  1654	look.
  1655	
  1656	I replied in good French that I did not know his language; but he
  1657	seemed not to understand me, and my situation became more embarrassing.
  1658	
  1659	"If master were to tell our story," said Conseil, "perhaps these
  1660	gentlemen may understand some words."
  1661	
  1662	I began to tell our adventures, articulating each syllable clearly, and
  1663	without omitting one single detail.  I announced our names and rank,
  1664	introducing in person Professor Aronnax, his servant Conseil, and
  1665	master Ned Land, the harpooner.
  1666	
  1667	The man with the soft calm eyes listened to me quietly, even politely,
  1668	and with extreme attention; but nothing in his countenance indicated
  1669	that he had understood my story.  When I finished, he said not a word.
  1670	
  1671	There remained one resource, to speak English.  Perhaps they would know
  1672	this almost universal language.  I knew it--as well as the German
  1673	language--well enough to read it fluently, but not to speak it
  1674	correctly.  But, anyhow, we must make ourselves understood.
  1675	
  1676	"Go on in your turn," I said to the harpooner; "speak your best
  1677	Anglo-Saxon, and try to do better than I."
  1678	
  1679	Ned did not beg off, and recommenced our story.
  1680	
  1681	To his great disgust, the harpooner did not seem to have made himself
  1682	more intelligible than I had.  Our visitors did not stir.  They
  1683	evidently understood neither the language of England nor of France.
  1684	
  1685	Very much embarrassed, after having vainly exhausted our speaking
  1686	resources, I knew not what part to take, when Conseil said:
  1687	
  1688	"If master will permit me, I will relate it in German."
  1689	
  1690	But in spite of the elegant terms and good accent of the narrator, the
  1691	German language had no success.  At last, nonplussed, I tried to
  1692	remember my first lessons, and to narrate our adventures in Latin, but
  1693	with no better success.  This last attempt being of no avail, the two
  1694	strangers exchanged some words in their unknown language, and retired.
  1695	
  1696	The door shut.
  1697	
  1698	"It is an infamous shame," cried Ned Land, who broke out for the
  1699	twentieth time.  "We speak to those rogues in French, English, German,
  1700	and Latin, and not one of them has the politeness to answer!"
  1701	
  1702	"Calm yourself," I said to the impetuous Ned; "anger will do no good."
  1703	
  1704	"But do you see, Professor," replied our irascible companion, "that we
  1705	shall absolutely die of hunger in this iron cage?"
  1706	
  1707	"Bah!" said Conseil, philosophically; "we can hold out some time yet."
  1708	
  1709	"My friends," I said, "we must not despair.  We have been worse off
  1710	than this.  Do me the favour to wait a little before forming an opinion
  1711	upon the commander and crew of this boat."
  1712	
  1713	"My opinion is formed," replied Ned Land, sharply.  "They are rascals."
  1714	
  1715	"Good! and from what country?"
  1716	
  1717	"From the land of rogues!"
  1718	
  1719	"My brave Ned, that country is not clearly indicated on the map of the
  1720	world; but I admit that the nationality of the two strangers is hard to
  1721	determine.  Neither English, French, nor German, that is quite certain.
  1722	However, I am inclined to think that the commander and his companion
  1723	were born in low latitudes.  There is southern blood in them.  But I
  1724	cannot decide by their appearance whether they are Spaniards, Turks,
  1725	Arabians, or Indians.  As to their language, it is quite
  1726	incomprehensible."
  1727	
  1728	"There is the disadvantage of not knowing all languages," said Conseil,
  1729	"or the disadvantage of not having one universal language."
  1730	
  1731	As he said these words, the door opened.  A steward entered.  He
  1732	brought us clothes, coats and trousers, made of a stuff I did not know.
  1733	I hastened to dress myself, and my companions followed my example.
  1734	During that time, the steward--dumb, perhaps deaf--had arranged the
  1735	table, and laid three plates.
  1736	
  1737	"This is something like!" said Conseil.
  1738	
  1739	"Bah!" said the angry harpooner, "what do you suppose they eat here?
  1740	Tortoise liver, filleted shark, and beef steaks from seadogs."
  1741	
  1742	"We shall see," said Conseil.
  1743	
  1744	The dishes, of bell metal, were placed on the table, and we took our
  1745	places.  Undoubtedly we had to do with civilised people, and, had it
  1746	not been for the electric light which flooded us, I could have fancied
  1747	I was in the dining-room of the Adelphi Hotel at Liverpool, or at the
  1748	Grand Hotel in Paris.  I must say, however, that there was neither
  1749	bread nor wine.  The water was fresh and clear, but it was water and
  1750	did not suit Ned Land's taste.  Amongst the dishes which were brought
  1751	to us, I recognised several fish delicately dressed; but of some,
  1752	although excellent, I could give no opinion, neither could I tell to
  1753	what kingdom they belonged, whether animal or vegetable.  As to the
  1754	dinner-service, it was elegant, and in perfect taste.  Each
  1755	utensil--spoon, fork, knife, plate--had a letter engraved on it, with a
  1756	motto above it, of which this is an exact facsimile:
  1757	
  1758	
  1759	MOBILIS IN MOBILI N
  1760	
  1761	The letter N was no doubt the initial of the name of the enigmatical
  1762	person who commanded at the bottom of the seas.
  1763	
  1764	Ned and Conseil did not reflect much.  They devoured the food, and I
  1765	did likewise.  I was, besides, reassured as to our fate; and it seemed
  1766	evident that our hosts would not let us die of want.
  1767	
  1768	However, everything has an end, everything passes away, even the hunger
  1769	of people who have not eaten for fifteen hours.  Our appetites
  1770	satisfied, we felt overcome with sleep.
  1771	
  1772	"Faith!  I shall sleep well," said Conseil.
  1773	
  1774	"So shall I," replied Ned Land.
  1775	
  1776	My two companions stretched themselves on the cabin carpet, and were
  1777	soon sound asleep.  For my own part, too many thoughts crowded my
  1778	brain, too many insoluble questions pressed upon me, too many fancies
  1779	kept my eyes half open.  Where were we?  What strange power carried us
  1780	on?  I felt--or rather fancied I felt--the machine sinking down to the
  1781	lowest beds of the sea.  Dreadful nightmares beset me; I saw in these
  1782	mysterious asylums a world of unknown animals, amongst which this
  1783	submarine boat seemed to be of the same kind, living, moving, and
  1784	formidable as they.  Then my brain grew calmer, my imagination wandered
  1785	into vague unconsciousness, and I soon fell into a deep sleep.
  1786	
  1787	
  1788	
  1789	CHAPTER IX
  1790	
  1791	NED LAND'S TEMPERS
  1792	
  1793	How long we slept I do not know; but our sleep must have lasted long,
  1794	for it rested us completely from our fatigues.  I woke first.  My
  1795	companions had not moved, and were still stretched in their corner.
  1796	
  1797	Hardly roused from my somewhat hard couch, I felt my brain freed, my
  1798	mind clear.  I then began an attentive examination of our cell.
  1799	Nothing was changed inside.  The prison was still a prison--the
  1800	prisoners, prisoners.  However, the steward, during our sleep, had
  1801	cleared the table.  I breathed with difficulty.  The heavy air seemed
  1802	to oppress my lungs.  Although the cell was large, we had evidently
  1803	consumed a great part of the oxygen that it contained.  Indeed, each
  1804	man consumes, in one hour, the oxygen contained in more than 176 pints
  1805	of air, and this air, charged (as then) with a nearly equal quantity of
  1806	carbonic acid, becomes unbreathable.
  1807	
  1808	It became necessary to renew the atmosphere of our prison, and no doubt
  1809	the whole in the submarine boat.  That gave rise to a question in my
  1810	mind.  How would the commander of this floating dwelling-place proceed?
  1811	Would he obtain air by chemical means, in getting by heat the oxygen
  1812	contained in chlorate of potash, and in absorbing carbonic acid by
  1813	caustic potash?  Or--a more convenient, economical, and consequently
  1814	more probable alternative--would he be satisfied to rise and take
  1815	breath at the surface of the water, like a whale, and so renew for
  1816	twenty-four hours the atmospheric provision?
  1817	
  1818	In fact, I was already obliged to increase my respirations to eke out
  1819	of this cell the little oxygen it contained, when suddenly I was
  1820	refreshed by a current of pure air, and perfumed with saline
  1821	emanations.  It was an invigorating sea breeze, charged with iodine.  I
  1822	opened my mouth wide, and my lungs saturated themselves with fresh
  1823	particles.
  1824	
  1825	At the same time I felt the boat rolling.  The iron-plated monster had
  1826	evidently just risen to the surface of the ocean to breathe, after the
  1827	fashion of whales.  I found out from that the mode of ventilating the
  1828	boat.
  1829	
  1830	When I had inhaled this air freely, I sought the conduit pipe, which
  1831	conveyed to us the beneficial whiff, and I was not long in finding it.
  1832	Above the door was a ventilator, through which volumes of fresh air
  1833	renewed the impoverished atmosphere of the cell.
  1834	
  1835	I was making my observations, when Ned and Conseil awoke almost at the
  1836	same time, under the influence of this reviving air.  They rubbed their
  1837	eyes, stretched themselves, and were on their feet in an instant.
  1838	
  1839	"Did master sleep well?" asked Conseil, with his usual politeness.
  1840	
  1841	"Very well, my brave boy.  And you, Mr. Land?"
  1842	
  1843	"Soundly, Professor.  But, I don't know if I am right or not, there
  1844	seems to be a sea breeze!"
  1845	
  1846	A seaman could not be mistaken, and I told the Canadian all that had
  1847	passed during his sleep.
  1848	
  1849	"Good!" said he.  "That accounts for those roarings we heard, when the
  1850	supposed narwhal sighted the Abraham Lincoln."
  1851	
  1852	"Quite so, Master Land; it was taking breath."
  1853	
  1854	"Only, Mr. Aronnax, I have no idea what o'clock it is, unless it is
  1855	dinner-time."
  1856	
  1857	"Dinner-time! my good fellow?  Say rather breakfast-time, for we
  1858	certainly have begun another day."
  1859	
  1860	"So," said Conseil, "we have slept twenty-four hours?"
  1861	
  1862	"That is my opinion."
  1863	
  1864	"I will not contradict you," replied Ned Land.  "But, dinner or
  1865	breakfast, the steward will be welcome, whichever he brings."
  1866	
  1867	"Master Land, we must conform to the rules on board, and I suppose our
  1868	appetites are in advance of the dinner hour."
  1869	
  1870	"That is just like you, friend Conseil," said Ned, impatiently.  "You
  1871	are never out of temper, always calm; you would return thanks before
  1872	grace, and die of hunger rather than complain!"
  1873	
  1874	Time was getting on, and we were fearfully hungry; and this time the
  1875	steward did not appear.  It was rather too long to leave us, if they
  1876	really had good intentions towards us.  Ned Land, tormented by the
  1877	cravings of hunger, got still more angry; and, notwithstanding his
  1878	promise, I dreaded an explosion when he found himself with one of the
  1879	crew.
  1880	
  1881	For two hours more Ned Land's temper increased; he cried, he shouted,
  1882	but in vain.  The walls were deaf.  There was no sound to be heard in
  1883	the boat; all was still as death.  It did not move, for I should have
  1884	felt the trembling motion of the hull under the influence of the screw.
  1885	Plunged in the depths of the waters, it belonged no longer to earth:
  1886	this silence was dreadful.
  1887	
  1888	I felt terrified, Conseil was calm, Ned Land roared.
  1889	
  1890	Just then a noise was heard outside.  Steps sounded on the metal flags.
  1891	The locks were turned, the door opened, and the steward appeared.
  1892	
  1893	Before I could rush forward to stop him, the Canadian had thrown him
  1894	down, and held him by the throat.  The steward was choking under the
  1895	grip of his powerful hand.
  1896	
  1897	Conseil was already trying to unclasp the harpooner's hand from his
  1898	half-suffocated victim, and I was going to fly to the rescue, when
  1899	suddenly I was nailed to the spot by hearing these words in French:
  1900	
  1901	"Be quiet, Master Land; and you, Professor, will you be so good as to
  1902	listen to me?"
  1903	
  1904	
  1905	
  1906	CHAPTER X
  1907	
  1908	THE MAN OF THE SEAS
  1909	
  1910	It was the commander of the vessel who thus spoke.
  1911	
  1912	At these words, Ned Land rose suddenly.  The steward, nearly strangled,
  1913	tottered out on a sign from his master.  But such was the power of the
  1914	commander on board, that not a gesture betrayed the resentment which
  1915	this man must have felt towards the Canadian.  Conseil interested in
  1916	spite of himself, I stupefied, awaited in silence the result of this
  1917	scene.
  1918	
  1919	The commander, leaning against the corner of a table with his arms
  1920	folded, scanned us with profound attention.  Did he hesitate to speak?
  1921	Did he regret the words which he had just spoken in French?  One might
  1922	almost think so.
  1923	
  1924	After some moments of silence, which not one of us dreamed of breaking,
  1925	"Gentlemen," said he, in a calm and penetrating voice, "I speak French,
  1926	English, German, and Latin equally well.  I could, therefore, have
  1927	answered you at our first interview, but I wished to know you first,
  1928	then to reflect.  The story told by each one, entirely agreeing in the
  1929	main points, convinced me of your identity.  I know now that chance has
  1930	brought before me M. Pierre Aronnax, Professor of Natural History at
  1931	the Museum of Paris, entrusted with a scientific mission abroad,
  1932	Conseil, his servant, and Ned Land, of Canadian origin, harpooner on
  1933	board the frigate Abraham Lincoln of the navy of the United States of
  1934	America."
  1935	
  1936	I bowed assent.  It was not a question that the commander put to me.
  1937	Therefore there was no answer to be made.  This man expressed himself
  1938	with perfect ease, without any accent.  His sentences were well turned,
  1939	his words clear, and his fluency of speech remarkable.  Yet, I did not
  1940	recognise in him a fellow-countryman.
  1941	
  1942	He continued the conversation in these terms:
  1943	
  1944	"You have doubtless thought, sir, that I have delayed long in paying
  1945	you this second visit.  The reason is that, your identity recognised, I
  1946	wished to weigh maturely what part to act towards you.  I have
  1947	hesitated much.  Most annoying circumstances have brought you into the
  1948	presence of a man who has broken all the ties of humanity.  You have
  1949	come to trouble my existence."
  1950	
  1951	"Unintentionally!" said I.
  1952	
  1953	"Unintentionally?" replied the stranger, raising his voice a little.
  1954	"Was it unintentionally that the Abraham Lincoln pursued me all over
  1955	the seas?  Was it unintentionally that you took passage in this
  1956	frigate?  Was it unintentionally that your cannon-balls rebounded off
  1957	the plating of my vessel?  Was it unintentionally that Mr. Ned Land
  1958	struck me with his harpoon?"
  1959	
  1960	I detected a restrained irritation in these words.  But to these
  1961	recriminations I had a very natural answer to make, and I made it.
  1962	
  1963	"Sir," said I, "no doubt you are ignorant of the discussions which have
  1964	taken place concerning you in America and Europe.  You do not know that
  1965	divers accidents, caused by collisions with your submarine machine,
  1966	have excited public feeling in the two continents.  I omit the theories
  1967	without number by which it was sought to explain that of which you
  1968	alone possess the secret.  But you must understand that, in pursuing
  1969	you over the high seas of the Pacific, the Abraham Lincoln believed
  1970	itself to be chasing some powerful sea-monster, of which it was
  1971	necessary to rid the ocean at any price."
  1972	
  1973	A half-smile curled the lips of the commander:  then, in a calmer tone:
  1974	
  1975	"M. Aronnax," he replied, "dare you affirm that your frigate would not
  1976	as soon have pursued and cannonaded a submarine boat as a monster?"
  1977	
  1978	This question embarrassed me, for certainly Captain Farragut might not
  1979	have hesitated.  He might have thought it his duty to destroy a
  1980	contrivance of this kind, as he would a gigantic narwhal.
  1981	
  1982	"You understand then, sir," continued the stranger, "that I have the
  1983	right to treat you as enemies?"
  1984	
  1985	I answered nothing, purposely.  For what good would it be to discuss
  1986	such a proposition, when force could destroy the best arguments?
  1987	
  1988	"I have hesitated some time," continued the commander; "nothing obliged
  1989	me to show you hospitality.  If I chose to separate myself from you, I
  1990	should have no interest in seeing you again; I could place you upon the
  1991	deck of this vessel which has served you as a refuge, I could sink
  1992	beneath the waters, and forget that you had ever existed.  Would not
  1993	that be my right?"
  1994	
  1995	"It might be the right of a savage," I answered, "but not that of a
  1996	civilised man."
  1997	
  1998	"Professor," replied the commander, quickly, "I am not what you call a
  1999	civilised man!  I have done with society entirely, for reasons which I
  2000	alone have the right of appreciating.  I do not, therefore, obey its
  2001	laws, and I desire you never to allude to them before me again!"
  2002	
  2003	This was said plainly.  A flash of anger and disdain kindled in the
  2004	eyes of the Unknown, and I had a glimpse of a terrible past in the life
  2005	of this man.  Not only had he put himself beyond the pale of human
  2006	laws, but he had made himself independent of them, free in the
  2007	strictest acceptation of the word, quite beyond their reach!  Who then
  2008	would dare to pursue him at the bottom of the sea, when, on its
  2009	surface, he defied all attempts made against him?
  2010	
  2011	What vessel could resist the shock of his submarine monitor?  What
  2012	cuirass, however thick, could withstand the blows of his spur?  No man
  2013	could demand from him an account of his actions; God, if he believed in
  2014	one--his conscience, if he had one--were the sole judges to whom he was
  2015	answerable.
  2016	
  2017	These reflections crossed my mind rapidly, whilst the stranger
  2018	personage was silent, absorbed, and as if wrapped up in himself.  I
  2019	regarded him with fear mingled with interest, as, doubtless, OEdiphus
  2020	regarded the Sphinx.
  2021	
  2022	After rather a long silence, the commander resumed the conversation.
  2023	
  2024	"I have hesitated," said he, "but I have thought that my interest might
  2025	be reconciled with that pity to which every human being has a right.
  2026	You will remain on board my vessel, since fate has cast you there.  You
  2027	will be free; and, in exchange for this liberty, I shall only impose
  2028	one single condition.  Your word of honour to submit to it will
  2029	suffice."
  2030	
  2031	"Speak, sir," I answered.  "I suppose this condition is one which a man
  2032	of honour may accept?"
  2033	
  2034	"Yes, sir; it is this:  It is possible that certain events, unforeseen,
  2035	may oblige me to consign you to your cabins for some hours or some
  2036	days, as the case may be.  As I desire never to use violence, I expect
  2037	from you, more than all the others, a passive obedience.  In thus
  2038	acting, I take all the responsibility:  I acquit you entirely, for I
  2039	make it an impossibility for you to see what ought not to be seen.  Do
  2040	you accept this condition?"
  2041	
  2042	Then things took place on board which, to say the least, were singular,
  2043	and which ought not to be seen by people who were not placed beyond the
  2044	pale of social laws.  Amongst the surprises which the future was
  2045	preparing for me, this might not be the least.
  2046	
  2047	"We accept," I answered; "only I will ask your permission, sir, to
  2048	address one question to you--one only."
  2049	
  2050	"Speak, sir."
  2051	
  2052	"You said that we should be free on board."
  2053	
  2054	"Entirely."
  2055	
  2056	"I ask you, then, what you mean by this liberty?"
  2057	
  2058	"Just the liberty to go, to come, to see, to observe even all that
  2059	passes here save under rare circumstances--the liberty, in short, which
  2060	we enjoy ourselves, my companions and I."
  2061	
  2062	It was evident that we did not understand one another.
  2063	
  2064	"Pardon me, sir," I resumed, "but this liberty is only what every
  2065	prisoner has of pacing his prison.  It cannot suffice us."
  2066	
  2067	"It must suffice you, however."
  2068	
  2069	"What! we must renounce for ever seeing our country, our friends, our
  2070	relations again?"
  2071	
  2072	"Yes, sir.  But to renounce that unendurable worldly yoke which men
  2073	believe to be liberty is not perhaps so painful as you think."
  2074	
  2075	"Well," exclaimed Ned Land, "never will I give my word of honour not to
  2076	try to escape."
  2077	
  2078	"I did not ask you for your word of honour, Master Land," answered the
  2079	commander, coldly.
  2080	
  2081	"Sir," I replied, beginning to get angry in spite of my self, "you
  2082	abuse your situation towards us; it is cruelty."
  2083	
  2084	"No, sir, it is clemency.  You are my prisoners of war.  I keep you,
  2085	when I could, by a word, plunge you into the depths of the ocean.  You
  2086	attacked me.  You came to surprise a secret which no man in the world
  2087	must penetrate--the secret of my whole existence.  And you think that I
  2088	am going to send you back to that world which must know me no more?
  2089	Never!  In retaining you, it is not you whom I guard--it is myself."
  2090	
  2091	These words indicated a resolution taken on the part of the commander,
  2092	against which no arguments would prevail.
  2093	
  2094	"So, sir," I rejoined, "you give us simply the choice between life and
  2095	death?"
  2096	
  2097	"Simply."
  2098	
  2099	"My friends," said I, "to a question thus put, there is nothing to
  2100	answer.  But no word of honour binds us to the master of this vessel."
  2101	
  2102	"None, sir," answered the Unknown.
  2103	
  2104	Then, in a gentler tone, he continued:
  2105	
  2106	"Now, permit me to finish what I have to say to you.  I know you, M.
  2107	Aronnax.  You and your companions will not, perhaps, have so much to
  2108	complain of in the chance which has bound you to my fate.  You will
  2109	find amongst the books which are my favourite study the work which you
  2110	have published on `the depths of the sea.'  I have often read it.  You
  2111	have carried out your work as far as terrestrial science permitted you.
  2112	But you do not know all--you have not seen all.  Let me tell you then,
  2113	Professor, that you will not regret the time passed on board my vessel.
  2114	You are going to visit the land of marvels."
  2115	
  2116	These words of the commander had a great effect upon me.  I cannot deny
  2117	it.  My weak point was touched; and I forgot, for a moment, that the
  2118	contemplation of these sublime subjects was not worth the loss of
  2119	liberty.  Besides, I trusted to the future to decide this grave
  2120	question.  So I contented myself with saying:
  2121	
  2122	"By what name ought I to address you?"
  2123	
  2124	"Sir," replied the commander, "I am nothing to you but Captain Nemo;
  2125	and you and your companions are nothing to me but the passengers of the
  2126	Nautilus."
  2127	
  2128	Captain Nemo called.  A steward appeared.  The captain gave him his
  2129	orders in that strange language which I did not understand.  Then,
  2130	turning towards the Canadian and Conseil:
  2131	
  2132	"A repast awaits you in your cabin," said he.  "Be so good as to follow
  2133	this man.
  2134	
  2135	"And now, M. Aronnax, our breakfast is ready.  Permit me to lead the
  2136	way."
  2137	
  2138	"I am at your service, Captain."
  2139	
  2140	I followed Captain Nemo; and as soon as I had passed through the door,
  2141	I found myself in a kind of passage lighted by electricity, similar to
  2142	the waist of a ship.  After we had proceeded a dozen yards, a second
  2143	door opened before me.
  2144	
  2145	I then entered a dining-room, decorated and furnished in severe taste.
  2146	High oaken sideboards, inlaid with ebony, stood at the two extremities
  2147	of the room, and upon their shelves glittered china, porcelain, and
  2148	glass of inestimable value.  The plate on the table sparkled in the
  2149	rays which the luminous ceiling shed around, while the light was
  2150	tempered and softened by exquisite paintings.
  2151	
  2152	In the centre of the room was a table richly laid out.  Captain Nemo
  2153	indicated the place I was to occupy.
  2154	
  2155	The breakfast consisted of a certain number of dishes, the contents of
  2156	which were furnished by the sea alone; and I was ignorant of the nature
  2157	and mode of preparation of some of them.  I acknowledged that they were
  2158	good, but they had a peculiar flavour, which I easily became accustomed
  2159	to.  These different aliments appeared to me to be rich in phosphorus,
  2160	and I thought they must have a marine origin.
  2161	
  2162	Captain Nemo looked at me.  I asked him no questions, but he guessed my
  2163	thoughts, and answered of his own accord the questions which I was
  2164	burning to address to him.
  2165	
  2166	"The greater part of these dishes are unknown to you," he said to me.
  2167	"However, you may partake of them without fear.  They are wholesome and
  2168	nourishing.  For a long time I have renounced the food of the earth,
  2169	and I am never ill now.  My crew, who are healthy, are fed on the same
  2170	food."
  2171	
  2172	"So," said I, "all these eatables are the produce of the sea?"
  2173	
  2174	"Yes, Professor, the sea supplies all my wants.  Sometimes I cast my
  2175	nets in tow, and I draw them in ready to break.  Sometimes I hunt in
  2176	the midst of this element, which appears to be inaccessible to man, and
  2177	quarry the game which dwells in my submarine forests.  My flocks, like
  2178	those of Neptune's old shepherds, graze fearlessly in the immense
  2179	prairies of the ocean.  I have a vast property there, which I cultivate
  2180	myself, and which is always sown by the hand of the Creator of all
  2181	things."
  2182	
  2183	"I can understand perfectly, sir, that your nets furnish excellent fish
  2184	for your table; I can understand also that you hunt aquatic game in
  2185	your submarine forests; but I cannot understand at all how a particle
  2186	of meat, no matter how small, can figure in your bill of fare."
  2187	
  2188	"This, which you believe to be meat, Professor, is nothing else than
  2189	fillet of turtle.  Here are also some dolphins' livers, which you take
  2190	to be ragout of pork.  My cook is a clever fellow, who excels in
  2191	dressing these various products of the ocean.  Taste all these dishes.
  2192	Here is a preserve of sea-cucumber, which a Malay would declare to be
  2193	unrivalled in the world; here is a cream, of which the milk has been
  2194	furnished by the cetacea, and the sugar by the great fucus of the North
  2195	Sea; and, lastly, permit me to offer you some preserve of anemones,
  2196	which is equal to that of the most delicious fruits."
  2197	
  2198	I tasted, more from curiosity than as a connoisseur, whilst Captain
  2199	Nemo enchanted me with his extraordinary stories.
  2200	
  2201	"You like the sea, Captain?"
  2202	
  2203	"Yes; I love it!  The sea is everything.  It covers seven tenths of the
  2204	terrestrial globe.  Its breath is pure and healthy.  It is an immense
  2205	desert, where man is never lonely, for he feels life stirring on all
  2206	sides.  The sea is only the embodiment of a supernatural and wonderful
  2207	existence.  It is nothing but love and emotion; it is the `Living
  2208	Infinite,' as one of your poets has said.  In fact, Professor, Nature
  2209	manifests herself in it by her three kingdoms--mineral, vegetable, and
  2210	animal.  The sea is the vast reservoir of Nature.  The globe began with
  2211	sea, so to speak; and who knows if it will not end with it?  In it is
  2212	supreme tranquillity.  The sea does not belong to despots.  Upon its
  2213	surface men can still exercise unjust laws, fight, tear one another to
  2214	pieces, and be carried away with terrestrial horrors.  But at thirty
  2215	feet below its level, their reign ceases, their influence is quenched,
  2216	and their power disappears.  Ah! sir, live--live in the bosom of the
  2217	waters!  There only is independence!  There I recognise no masters!
  2218	There I am free!"
  2219	
  2220	Captain Nemo suddenly became silent in the midst of this enthusiasm, by
  2221	which he was quite carried away.  For a few moments he paced up and
  2222	down, much agitated.  Then he became more calm, regained his accustomed
  2223	coldness of expression, and turning towards me:
  2224	
  2225	"Now, Professor," said he, "if you wish to go over the Nautilus, I am
  2226	at your service."
  2227	
  2228	Captain Nemo rose.  I followed him.  A double door, contrived at the
  2229	back of the dining-room, opened, and I entered a room equal in
  2230	dimensions to that which I had just quitted.
  2231	
  2232	It was a library.  High pieces of furniture, of black violet ebony
  2233	inlaid with brass, supported upon their wide shelves a great number of
  2234	books uniformly bound.  They followed the shape of the room,
  2235	terminating at the lower part in huge divans, covered with brown
  2236	leather, which were curved, to afford the greatest comfort.  Light
  2237	movable desks, made to slide in and out at will, allowed one to rest
  2238	one's book while reading.  In the centre stood an immense table,
  2239	covered with pamphlets, amongst which were some newspapers, already of
  2240	old date.  The electric light flooded everything; it was shed from four
  2241	unpolished globes half sunk in the volutes of the ceiling.  I looked
  2242	with real admiration at this room, so ingeniously fitted up, and I
  2243	could scarcely believe my eyes.
  2244	
  2245	"Captain Nemo," said I to my host, who had just thrown himself on one
  2246	of the divans, "this is a library which would do honour to more than
  2247	one of the continental palaces, and I am absolutely astounded when I
  2248	consider that it can follow you to the bottom of the seas."
  2249	
  2250	"Where could one find greater solitude or silence, Professor?" replied
  2251	Captain Nemo.  "Did your study in the Museum afford you such perfect
  2252	quiet?"
  2253	
  2254	"No, sir; and I must confess that it is a very poor one after yours.
  2255	You must have six or seven thousand volumes here."
  2256	
  2257	"Twelve thousand, M. Aronnax.  These are the only ties which bind me to
  2258	the earth.  But I had done with the world on the day when my Nautilus
  2259	plunged for the first time beneath the waters.  That day I bought my
  2260	last volumes, my last pamphlets, my last papers, and from that time I
  2261	wish to think that men no longer think or write.  These books,
  2262	Professor, are at your service besides, and you can make use of them
  2263	freely."
  2264	
  2265	I thanked Captain Nemo, and went up to the shelves of the library.
  2266	Works on science, morals, and literature abounded in every language;
  2267	but I did not see one single work on political economy; that subject
  2268	appeared to be strictly proscribed.  Strange to say, all these books
  2269	were irregularly arranged, in whatever language they were written; and
  2270	this medley proved that the Captain of the Nautilus must have read
  2271	indiscriminately the books which he took up by chance.
  2272	
  2273	"Sir," said I to the Captain, "I thank you for having placed this
  2274	library at my disposal.  It contains treasures of science, and I shall
  2275	profit by them."
  2276	
  2277	"This room is not only a library," said Captain Nemo, "it is also a
  2278	smoking-room."
  2279	
  2280	"A smoking-room!" I cried.  "Then one may smoke on board?"
  2281	
  2282	"Certainly."
  2283	
  2284	"Then, sir, I am forced to believe that you have kept up a
  2285	communication with Havannah."
  2286	
  2287	"Not any," answered the Captain.  "Accept this cigar, M. Aronnax; and,
  2288	though it does not come from Havannah, you will be pleased with it, if
  2289	you are a connoisseur."
  2290	
  2291	I took the cigar which was offered me; its shape recalled the London
  2292	ones, but it seemed to be made of leaves of gold.  I lighted it at a
  2293	little brazier, which was supported upon an elegant bronze stem, and
  2294	drew the first whiffs with the delight of a lover of smoking who has
  2295	not smoked for two days.
  2296	
  2297	"It is excellent, but it is not tobacco."
  2298	
  2299	"No!" answered the Captain, "this tobacco comes neither from Havannah
  2300	nor from the East.  It is a kind of sea-weed, rich in nicotine, with
  2301	which the sea provides me, but somewhat sparingly."
  2302	
  2303	At that moment Captain Nemo opened a door which stood opposite to that
  2304	by which I had entered the library, and I passed into an immense
  2305	drawing-room splendidly lighted.
  2306	
  2307	It was a vast, four-sided room, thirty feet long, eighteen wide, and
  2308	fifteen high.  A luminous ceiling, decorated with light arabesques,
  2309	shed a soft clear light over all the marvels accumulated in this
  2310	museum.  For it was in fact a museum, in which an intelligent and
  2311	prodigal hand had gathered all the treasures of nature and art, with
  2312	the artistic confusion which distinguishes a painter's studio.
  2313	
  2314	Thirty first-rate pictures, uniformly framed, separated by bright
  2315	drapery, ornamented the walls, which were hung with tapestry of severe
  2316	design. I saw works of great value, the greater part of which I had
  2317	admired in the special collections of Europe, and in the exhibitions of
  2318	paintings. The several schools of the old masters were represented by a
  2319	Madonna of Raphael, a Virgin of Leonardo da Vinci, a nymph of Corregio,
  2320	a woman of Titan, an Adoration of Veronese, an Assumption of Murillo, a
  2321	portrait of Holbein, a monk of Velasquez, a martyr of Ribera, a fair of
  2322	Rubens, two Flemish landscapes of Teniers, three little "genre"
  2323	pictures of Gerard Dow, Metsu, and Paul Potter, two specimens of
  2324	Gericault and Prudhon, and some sea-pieces of Backhuysen and Vernet.
  2325	Amongst the works of modern painters were pictures with the signatures
  2326	of Delacroix, Ingres, Decamps, Troyon, Meissonier, Daubigny, etc.; and
  2327	some admirable statues in marble and bronze, after the finest antique
  2328	models, stood upon pedestals in the corners of this magnificent museum.
  2329	Amazement, as the Captain of the Nautilus had predicted, had already
  2330	begun to take possession of me.
  2331	
  2332	"Professor," said this strange man, "you must excuse the unceremonious
  2333	way in which I receive you, and the disorder of this room."
  2334	
  2335	"Sir," I answered, "without seeking to know who you are, I recognise in
  2336	you an artist."
  2337	
  2338	"An amateur, nothing more, sir.  Formerly I loved to collect these
  2339	beautiful works created by the hand of man.  I sought them greedily,
  2340	and ferreted them out indefatigably, and I have been able to bring
  2341	together some objects of great value.  These are my last souvenirs of
  2342	that world which is dead to me.  In my eyes, your modern artists are
  2343	already old; they have two or three thousand years of existence; I
  2344	confound them in my own mind.  Masters have no age."
  2345	
  2346	"And these musicians?" said I, pointing out some works of Weber,
  2347	Rossini, Mozart, Beethoven, Haydn, Meyerbeer, Herold, Wagner, Auber,
  2348	Gounod, and a number of others, scattered over a large model
  2349	piano-organ which occupied one of the panels of the drawing-room.
  2350	
  2351	"These musicians," replied Captain Nemo, "are the contemporaries of
  2352	Orpheus; for in the memory of the dead all chronological differences
  2353	are effaced; and I am dead, Professor; as much dead as those of your
  2354	friends who are sleeping six feet under the earth!"
  2355	
  2356	Captain Nemo was silent, and seemed lost in a profound reverie. I
  2357	contemplated him with deep interest, analysing in silence the strange
  2358	expression of his countenance. Leaning on his elbow against an angle of
  2359	a costly mosaic table, he no longer saw me,--he had forgotten my
  2360	presence.
  2361	
  2362	I did not disturb this reverie, and continued my observation of the
  2363	curiosities which enriched this drawing-room.
  2364	
  2365	Under elegant glass cases, fixed by copper rivets, were classed and
  2366	labelled the most precious productions of the sea which had ever been
  2367	presented to the eye of a naturalist.  My delight as a professor may be
  2368	conceived.
  2369	
  2370	The division containing the zoophytes presented the most curious
  2371	specimens of the two groups of polypi and echinodermes. In the first
  2372	group, the tubipores, were gorgones arranged like a fan, soft sponges
  2373	of Syria, ises of the Moluccas, pennatules, an admirable virgularia of
  2374	the Norwegian seas, variegated unbellulairae, alcyonariae, a whole
  2375	series of madrepores, which my master Milne Edwards has so cleverly
  2376	classified, amongst which I remarked some wonderful flabellinae
  2377	oculinae of the Island of Bourbon, the "Neptune's car" of the Antilles,
  2378	superb varieties of corals--in short, every species of those curious
  2379	polypi of which entire islands are formed, which will one day become
  2380	continents. Of the echinodermes, remarkable for their coating of
  2381	spines, asteri, sea-stars, pantacrinae, comatules, asterophons, echini,
  2382	holothuri, etc., represented individually a complete collection of this
  2383	group.
  2384	
  2385	A somewhat nervous conchyliologist would certainly have fainted before
  2386	other more numerous cases, in which were classified the specimens of
  2387	molluscs. It was a collection of inestimable value, which time fails me
  2388	to describe minutely. Amongst these specimens I will quote from memory
  2389	only the elegant royal hammer-fish of the Indian Ocean, whose regular
  2390	white spots stood out brightly on a red and brown ground, an imperial
  2391	spondyle, bright-coloured, bristling with spines, a rare specimen in
  2392	the European museums--(I estimated its value at not less than L1000); a
  2393	common hammer-fish of the seas of New Holland, which is only procured
  2394	with difficulty; exotic buccardia of Senegal; fragile white bivalve
  2395	shells, which a breath might shatter like a soap-bubble; several
  2396	varieties of the aspirgillum of Java, a kind of calcareous tube, edged
  2397	with leafy folds, and much debated by amateurs; a whole series of
  2398	trochi, some a greenish-yellow, found in the American seas, others a
  2399	reddish-brown, natives of Australian waters; others from the Gulf of
  2400	Mexico, remarkable for their imbricated shell; stellari found in the
  2401	Southern Seas; and last, the rarest of all, the magnificent spur of New
  2402	Zealand; and every description of delicate and fragile shells to which
  2403	science has given appropriate names.
  2404	
  2405	Apart, in separate compartments, were spread out chaplets of pearls of
  2406	the greatest beauty, which reflected the electric light in little
  2407	sparks of fire; pink pearls, torn from the pinna-marina of the Red Sea;
  2408	green pearls of the haliotyde iris; yellow, blue and black pearls, the
  2409	curious productions of the divers molluscs of every ocean, and certain
  2410	mussels of the water-courses of the North; lastly, several specimens of
  2411	inestimable value which had been gathered from the rarest pintadines.
  2412	Some of these pearls were larger than a pigeon's egg, and were worth as
  2413	much, and more than that which the traveller Tavernier sold to the Shah
  2414	of Persia for three millions, and surpassed the one in the possession
  2415	of the Imaum of Muscat, which I had believed to be unrivalled in the
  2416	world.
  2417	
  2418	Therefore, to estimate the value of this collection was simply
  2419	impossible.  Captain Nemo must have expended millions in the
  2420	acquirement of these various specimens, and I was thinking what source
  2421	he could have drawn from, to have been able thus to gratify his fancy
  2422	for collecting, when I was interrupted by these words:
  2423	
  2424	"You are examining my shells, Professor?  Unquestionably they must be
  2425	interesting to a naturalist; but for me they have a far greater charm,
  2426	for I have collected them all with my own hand, and there is not a sea
  2427	on the face of the globe which has escaped my researches."
  2428	
  2429	"I can understand, Captain, the delight of wandering about in the midst
  2430	of such riches.  You are one of those who have collected their
  2431	treasures themselves.  No museum in Europe possesses such a collection
  2432	of the produce of the ocean.  But if I exhaust all my admiration upon
  2433	it, I shall have none left for the vessel which carries it.  I do not
  2434	wish to pry into your secrets:  but I must confess that this Nautilus,
  2435	with the motive power which is confined in it, the contrivances which
  2436	enable it to be worked, the powerful agent which propels it, all excite
  2437	my curiosity to the highest pitch.  I see suspended on the walls of
  2438	this room instruments of whose use I am ignorant."
  2439	
  2440	"You will find these same instruments in my own room, Professor, where
  2441	I shall have much pleasure in explaining their use to you.  But first
  2442	come and inspect the cabin which is set apart for your own use.  You
  2443	must see how you will be accommodated on board the Nautilus."
  2444	
  2445	I followed Captain Nemo who, by one of the doors opening from each
  2446	panel of the drawing-room, regained the waist.  He conducted me towards
  2447	the bow, and there I found, not a cabin, but an elegant room, with a
  2448	bed, dressing-table, and several other pieces of excellent furniture.
  2449	
  2450	I could only thank my host.
  2451	
  2452	"Your room adjoins mine," said he, opening a door, "and mine opens into
  2453	the drawing-room that we have just quitted."
  2454	
  2455	I entered the Captain's room:  it had a severe, almost a monkish
  2456	aspect.  A small iron bedstead, a table, some articles for the toilet;
  2457	the whole lighted by a skylight.  No comforts, the strictest
  2458	necessaries only.
  2459	
  2460	Captain Nemo pointed to a seat.
  2461	
  2462	"Be so good as to sit down," he said.  I seated myself, and he began
  2463	thus:
  2464	
  2465	
  2466	
  2467	CHAPTER XI
  2468	
  2469	ALL BY ELECTRICITY
  2470	
  2471	"Sir," said Captain Nemo, showing me the instruments hanging on the
  2472	walls of his room, "here are the contrivances required for the
  2473	navigation of the Nautilus.  Here, as in the drawing-room, I have them
  2474	always under my eyes, and they indicate my position and exact direction
  2475	in the middle of the ocean.  Some are known to you, such as the
  2476	thermometer, which gives the internal temperature of the Nautilus; the
  2477	barometer, which indicates the weight of the air and foretells the
  2478	changes of the weather; the hygrometer, which marks the dryness of the
  2479	atmosphere; the storm-glass, the contents of which, by decomposing,
  2480	announce the approach of tempests; the compass, which guides my course;
  2481	the sextant, which shows the latitude by the altitude of the sun;
  2482	chronometers, by which I calculate the longitude; and glasses for day
  2483	and night, which I use to examine the points of the horizon, when the
  2484	Nautilus rises to the surface of the waves."
  2485	
  2486	"These are the usual nautical instruments," I replied, "and I know the
  2487	use of them.  But these others, no doubt, answer to the particular
  2488	requirements of the Nautilus.  This dial with movable needle is a
  2489	manometer, is it not?"
  2490	
  2491	"It is actually a manometer.  But by communication with the water,
  2492	whose external pressure it indicates, it gives our depth at the same
  2493	time."
  2494	
  2495	"And these other instruments, the use of which I cannot guess?"
  2496	
  2497	"Here, Professor, I ought to give you some explanations.  Will you be
  2498	kind enough to listen to me?"
  2499	
  2500	He was silent for a few moments, then he said:
  2501	
  2502	"There is a powerful agent, obedient, rapid, easy, which conforms to
  2503	every use, and reigns supreme on board my vessel.  Everything is done
  2504	by means of it.  It lights, warms it, and is the soul of my mechanical
  2505	apparatus.  This agent is electricity."
  2506	
  2507	"Electricity?"  I cried in surprise.
  2508	
  2509	"Yes, sir."
  2510	
  2511	"Nevertheless, Captain, you possess an extreme rapidity of movement,
  2512	which does not agree well with the power of electricity.  Until now,
  2513	its dynamic force has remained under restraint, and has only been able
  2514	to produce a small amount of power."
  2515	
  2516	"Professor," said Captain Nemo, "my electricity is not everybody's.
  2517	You know what sea-water is composed of.  In a thousand grammes are
  2518	found 96 1/2 per cent. of water, and about 2 2/3 per cent.  of chloride
  2519	of sodium; then, in a smaller quantity, chlorides of magnesium and of
  2520	potassium, bromide of magnesium, sulphate of magnesia, sulphate and
  2521	carbonate of lime.  You see, then, that chloride of sodium forms a
  2522	large part of it.  So it is this sodium that I extract from the
  2523	sea-water, and of which I compose my ingredients.  I owe all to the
  2524	ocean; it produces electricity, and electricity gives heat, light,
  2525	motion, and, in a word, life to the Nautilus."
  2526	
  2527	"But not the air you breathe?"
  2528	
  2529	"Oh!  I could manufacture the air necessary for my consumption, but it
  2530	is useless, because I go up to the surface of the water when I please.
  2531	However, if electricity does not furnish me with air to breathe, it
  2532	works at least the powerful pumps that are stored in spacious
  2533	reservoirs, and which enable me to prolong at need, and as long as I
  2534	will, my stay in the depths of the sea.  It gives a uniform and
  2535	unintermittent light, which the sun does not.  Now look at this clock;
  2536	it is electrical, and goes with a regularity that defies the best
  2537	chronometers.  I have divided it into twenty-four hours, like the
  2538	Italian clocks, because for me there is neither night nor day, sun nor
  2539	moon, but only that factitious light that I take with me to the bottom
  2540	of the sea.  Look! just now, it is ten o'clock in the morning."
  2541	
  2542	"Exactly."
  2543	
  2544	"Another application of electricity.  This dial hanging in front of us
  2545	indicates the speed of the Nautilus.  An electric thread puts it in
  2546	communication with the screw, and the needle indicates the real speed.
  2547	Look! now we are spinning along with a uniform speed of fifteen miles
  2548	an hour."
  2549	
  2550	"It is marvelous!  And I see, Captain, you were right to make use of
  2551	this agent that takes the place of wind, water, and steam."
  2552	
  2553	"We have not finished, M. Aronnax," said Captain Nemo, rising.  "If you
  2554	will allow me, we will examine the stern of the Nautilus."
  2555	
  2556	Really, I knew already the anterior part of this submarine boat, of
  2557	which this is the exact division, starting from the ship's head: the
  2558	dining-room, five yards long, separated from the library by a
  2559	water-tight partition; the library, five yards long; the large
  2560	drawing-room, ten yards long, separated from the Captain's room by a
  2561	second water-tight partition; the said room, five yards in length;
  2562	mine, two and a half yards; and, lastly a reservoir of air, seven and a
  2563	half yards, that extended to the bows.  Total length thirty five yards,
  2564	or one hundred and five feet.  The partitions had doors that were shut
  2565	hermetically by means of india-rubber instruments, and they ensured the
  2566	safety of the Nautilus in case of a leak.
  2567	
  2568	I followed Captain Nemo through the waist, and arrived at the centre of
  2569	the boat.  There was a sort of well that opened between two partitions.
  2570	An iron ladder, fastened with an iron hook to the partition, led to the
  2571	upper end.  I asked the Captain what the ladder was used for.
  2572	
  2573	"It leads to the small boat," he said.
  2574	
  2575	"What! have you a boat?"  I exclaimed, in surprise.
  2576	
  2577	"Of course; an excellent vessel, light and insubmersible, that serves
  2578	either as a fishing or as a pleasure boat."
  2579	
  2580	"But then, when you wish to embark, you are obliged to come to the
  2581	surface of the water?"
  2582	
  2583	"Not at all.  This boat is attached to the upper part of the hull of
  2584	the Nautilus, and occupies a cavity made for it.  It is decked, quite
  2585	water-tight, and held together by solid bolts.  This ladder leads to a
  2586	man-hole made in the hull of the Nautilus, that corresponds with a
  2587	similar hole made in the side of the boat.  By this double opening I
  2588	get into the small vessel.  They shut the one belonging to the
  2589	Nautilus; I shut the other by means of screw pressure.  I undo the
  2590	bolts, and the little boat goes up to the surface of the sea with
  2591	prodigious rapidity.  I then open the panel of the bridge, carefully
  2592	shut till then; I mast it, hoist my sail, take my oars, and I'm off."
  2593	
  2594	"But how do you get back on board?"
  2595	
  2596	"I do not come back, M. Aronnax; the Nautilus comes to me."
  2597	
  2598	"By your orders?"
  2599	
  2600	"By my orders.  An electric thread connects us.  I telegraph to it, and
  2601	that is enough."
  2602	
  2603	"Really," I said, astonished at these marvels, "nothing can be more
  2604	simple."
  2605	
  2606	After having passed by the cage of the staircase that led to the
  2607	platform, I saw a cabin six feet long, in which Conseil and Ned Land,
  2608	enchanted with their repast, were devouring it with avidity.  Then a
  2609	door opened into a kitchen nine feet long, situated between the large
  2610	store-rooms. There electricity, better than gas itself, did all the
  2611	cooking.  The streams under the furnaces gave out to the sponges of
  2612	platina a heat which was regularly kept up and distributed.  They also
  2613	heated a distilling apparatus, which, by evaporation, furnished
  2614	excellent drinkable water.  Near this kitchen was a bathroom
  2615	comfortably furnished, with hot and cold water taps.
  2616	
  2617	Next to the kitchen was the berth-room of the vessel, sixteen feet
  2618	long.  But the door was shut, and I could not see the management of it,
  2619	which might have given me an idea of the number of men employed on
  2620	board the Nautilus.
  2621	
  2622	At the bottom was a fourth partition that separated this office from
  2623	the engine-room. A door opened, and I found myself in the compartment
  2624	where Captain Nemo--certainly an engineer of a very high order--had
  2625	arranged his locomotive machinery.  This engine-room, clearly lighted,
  2626	did not measure less than sixty-five feet in length.  It was divided
  2627	into two parts; the first contained the materials for producing
  2628	electricity, and the second the machinery that connected it with the
  2629	screw.  I examined it with great interest, in order to understand the
  2630	machinery of the Nautilus.
  2631	
  2632	"You see," said the Captain, "I use Bunsen's contrivances, not
  2633	Ruhmkorff's. Those would not have been powerful enough.  Bunsen's are
  2634	fewer in number, but strong and large, which experience proves to be
  2635	the best.  The electricity produced passes forward, where it works, by
  2636	electro-magnets of great size, on a system of levers and cog-wheels
  2637	that transmit the movement to the axle of the screw.  This one, the
  2638	diameter of which is nineteen feet, and the thread twenty-three feet,
  2639	performs about 120 revolutions in a second."
  2640	
  2641	"And you get then?"
  2642	
  2643	"A speed of fifty miles an hour."
  2644	
  2645	"I have seen the Nautilus manoeuvre before the Abraham Lincoln, and I
  2646	have my own ideas as to its speed.  But this is not enough.  We must
  2647	see where we go.  We must be able to direct it to the right, to the
  2648	left, above, below.  How do you get to the great depths, where you find
  2649	an increasing resistance, which is rated by hundreds of atmospheres?
  2650	How do you return to the surface of the ocean?  And how do you maintain
  2651	yourselves in the requisite medium?  Am I asking too much?"
  2652	
  2653	"Not at all, Professor," replied the Captain, with some hesitation;
  2654	"since you may never leave this submarine boat.  Come into the saloon,
  2655	it is our usual study, and there you will learn all you want to know
  2656	about the Nautilus."
  2657	
  2658	
  2659	
  2660	CHAPTER XII
  2661	
  2662	SOME FIGURES
  2663	
  2664	A moment after we were seated on a divan in the saloon smoking.  The
  2665	Captain showed me a sketch that gave the plan, section, and elevation
  2666	of the Nautilus.  Then he began his description in these words:
  2667	
  2668	"Here, M. Aronnax, are the several dimensions of the boat you are in.
  2669	It is an elongated cylinder with conical ends.  It is very like a cigar
  2670	in shape, a shape already adopted in London in several constructions of
  2671	the same sort.  The length of this cylinder, from stem to stern, is
  2672	exactly 232 feet, and its maximum breadth is twenty-six feet.  It is
  2673	not built quite like your long-voyage steamers, but its lines are
  2674	sufficiently long, and its curves prolonged enough, to allow the water
  2675	to slide off easily, and oppose no obstacle to its passage.  These two
  2676	dimensions enable you to obtain by a simple calculation the surface and
  2677	cubic contents of the Nautilus.  Its area measures 6,032 feet; and its
  2678	contents about 1,500 cubic yards; that is to say, when completely
  2679	immersed it displaces 50,000 feet of water, or weighs 1,500 tons.
  2680	
  2681	"When I made the plans for this submarine vessel, I meant that
  2682	nine-tenths should be submerged:  consequently it ought only to
  2683	displace nine-tenths of its bulk, that is to say, only to weigh that
  2684	number of tons.  I ought not, therefore, to have exceeded that weight,
  2685	constructing it on the aforesaid dimensions.
  2686	
  2687	"The Nautilus is composed of two hulls, one inside, the other outside,
  2688	joined by T-shaped irons, which render it very strong.  Indeed, owing
  2689	to this cellular arrangement it resists like a block, as if it were
  2690	solid.  Its sides cannot yield; it coheres spontaneously, and not by
  2691	the closeness of its rivets; and its perfect union of the materials
  2692	enables it to defy the roughest seas.
  2693	
  2694	"These two hulls are composed of steel plates, whose density is from .7
  2695	to .8 that of water.  The first is not less than two inches and a half
  2696	thick and weighs 394 tons.  The second envelope, the keel, twenty
  2697	inches high and ten thick, weighs only sixty-two tons.  The engine, the
  2698	ballast, the several accessories and apparatus appendages, the
  2699	partitions and bulkheads, weigh 961.62 tons.  Do you follow all this?"
  2700	
  2701	"I do."
  2702	
  2703	"Then, when the Nautilus is afloat under these circumstances, one-tenth
  2704	is out of the water.  Now, if I have made reservoirs of a size equal to
  2705	this tenth, or capable of holding 150 tons, and if I fill them with
  2706	water, the boat, weighing then 1,507 tons, will be completely immersed.
  2707	That would happen, Professor.  These reservoirs are in the lower part
  2708	of the Nautilus.  I turn on taps and they fill, and the vessel sinks
  2709	that had just been level with the surface."
  2710	
  2711	"Well, Captain, but now we come to the real difficulty.  I can
  2712	understand your rising to the surface; but, diving below the surface,
  2713	does not your submarine contrivance encounter a pressure, and
  2714	consequently undergo an upward thrust of one atmosphere for every
  2715	thirty feet of water, just about fifteen pounds per square inch?"
  2716	
  2717	"Just so, sir."
  2718	
  2719	"Then, unless you quite fill the Nautilus, I do not see how you can
  2720	draw it down to those depths."
  2721	
  2722	"Professor, you must not confound statics with dynamics or you will be
  2723	exposed to grave errors.  There is very little labour spent in
  2724	attaining the lower regions of the ocean, for all bodies have a
  2725	tendency to sink.  When I wanted to find out the necessary increase of
  2726	weight required to sink the Nautilus, I had only to calculate the
  2727	reduction of volume that sea-water acquires according to the depth."
  2728	
  2729	"That is evident."
  2730	
  2731	"Now, if water is not absolutely incompressible, it is at least capable
  2732	of very slight compression.  Indeed, after the most recent calculations
  2733	this reduction is only .000436 of an atmosphere for each thirty feet of
  2734	depth.  If we want to sink 3,000 feet, I should keep account of the
  2735	reduction of bulk under a pressure equal to that of a column of water
  2736	of a thousand feet.  The calculation is easily verified.  Now, I have
  2737	supplementary reservoirs capable of holding a hundred tons.  Therefore
  2738	I can sink to a considerable depth.  When I wish to rise to the level
  2739	of the sea, I only let off the water, and empty all the reservoirs if I
  2740	want the Nautilus to emerge from the tenth part of her total capacity."
  2741	
  2742	I had nothing to object to these reasonings.
  2743	
  2744	"I admit your calculations, Captain," I replied; "I should be wrong to
  2745	dispute them since daily experience confirms them; but I foresee a real
  2746	difficulty in the way."
  2747	
  2748	"What, sir?"
  2749	
  2750	"When you are about 1,000 feet deep, the walls of the Nautilus bear a
  2751	pressure of 100 atmospheres.  If, then, just now you were to empty the
  2752	supplementary reservoirs, to lighten the vessel, and to go up to the
  2753	surface, the pumps must overcome the pressure of 100 atmospheres, which
  2754	is 1,500 lbs.  per square inch.  From that a power----"
  2755	
  2756	"That electricity alone can give," said the Captain, hastily.  "I
  2757	repeat, sir, that the dynamic power of my engines is almost infinite.
  2758	The pumps of the Nautilus have an enormous power, as you must have
  2759	observed when their jets of water burst like a torrent upon the Abraham
  2760	Lincoln.  Besides, I use subsidiary reservoirs only to attain a mean
  2761	depth of 750 to 1,000 fathoms, and that with a view of managing my
  2762	machines.  Also, when I have a mind to visit the depths of the ocean
  2763	five or six mlles below the surface, I make use of slower but not less
  2764	infallible means."
  2765	
  2766	"What are they, Captain?"
  2767	
  2768	"That involves my telling you how the Nautilus is worked."
  2769	
  2770	"I am impatient to learn."
  2771	
  2772	"To steer this boat to starboard or port, to turn, in a word, following
  2773	a horizontal plan, I use an ordinary rudder fixed on the back of the
  2774	stern-post, and with one wheel and some tackle to steer by.  But I can
  2775	also make the Nautilus rise and sink, and sink and rise, by a vertical
  2776	movement by means of two inclined planes fastened to its sides,
  2777	opposite the centre of flotation, planes that move in every direction,
  2778	and that are worked by powerful levers from the interior.  If the
  2779	planes are kept parallel with the boat, it moves horizontally.  If
  2780	slanted, the Nautilus, according to this inclination, and under the
  2781	influence of the screw, either sinks diagonally or rises diagonally as
  2782	it suits me.  And even if I wish to rise more quickly to the surface, I
  2783	ship the screw, and the pressure of the water causes the Nautilus to
  2784	rise vertically like a balloon filled with hydrogen."
  2785	
  2786	"Bravo, Captain!  But how can the steersman follow the route in the
  2787	middle of the waters?"
  2788	
  2789	"The steersman is placed in a glazed box, that is raised about the hull
  2790	of the Nautilus, and furnished with lenses."
  2791	
  2792	"Are these lenses capable of resisting such pressure?"
  2793	
  2794	"Perfectly.  Glass, which breaks at a blow, is, nevertheless, capable
  2795	of offering considerable resistance.  During some experiments of
  2796	fishing by electric light in 1864 in the Northern Seas, we saw plates
  2797	less than a third of an inch thick resist a pressure of sixteen
  2798	atmospheres.  Now, the glass that I use is not less than thirty times
  2799	thicker."
  2800	
  2801	"Granted.  But, after all, in order to see, the light must exceed the
  2802	darkness, and in the midst of the darkness in the water, how can you
  2803	see?"
  2804	
  2805	"Behind the steersman's cage is placed a powerful electric reflector,
  2806	the rays from which light up the sea for half a mile in front."
  2807	
  2808	"Ah! bravo, bravo, Captain!  Now I can account for this phosphorescence
  2809	in the supposed narwhal that puzzled us so.  I now ask you if the
  2810	boarding of the Nautilus and of the Scotia, that has made such a noise,
  2811	has been the result of a chance rencontre?"
  2812	
  2813	"Quite accidental, sir.  I was sailing only one fathom below the
  2814	surface of the water when the shock came.  It had no bad result."
  2815	
  2816	"None, sir.  But now, about your rencontre with the Abraham Lincoln?"
  2817	
  2818	"Professor, I am sorry for one of the best vessels in the American
  2819	navy; but they attacked me, and I was bound to defend myself.  I
  2820	contented myself, however, with putting the frigate hors de combat; she
  2821	will not have any difficulty in getting repaired at the next port."
  2822	
  2823	"Ah, Commander! your Nautilus is certainly a marvellous boat."
  2824	
  2825	"Yes, Professor; and I love it as if it were part of myself.  If danger
  2826	threatens one of your vessels on the ocean, the first impression is the
  2827	feeling of an abyss above and below.  On the Nautilus men's hearts
  2828	never fail them.  No defects to be afraid of, for the double shell is
  2829	as firm as iron; no rigging to attend to; no sails for the wind to
  2830	carry away; no boilers to burst; no fire to fear, for the vessel is
  2831	made of iron, not of wood; no coal to run short, for electricity is the
  2832	only mechanical agent; no collision to fear, for it alone swims in deep
  2833	water; no tempest to brave, for when it dives below the water it
  2834	reaches absolute tranquillity.  There, sir! that is the perfection of
  2835	vessels!  And if it is true that the engineer has more confidence in
  2836	the vessel than the builder, and the builder than the captain himself,
  2837	you understand the trust I repose in my Nautilus; for I am at once
  2838	captain, builder, and engineer."
  2839	
  2840	"But how could you construct this wonderful Nautilus in secret?"
  2841	
  2842	"Each separate portion, M. Aronnax, was brought from different parts of
  2843	the globe."
  2844	
  2845	"But these parts had to be put together and arranged?"
  2846	
  2847	"Professor, I had set up my workshops upon a desert island in the
  2848	ocean.  There my workmen, that is to say, the brave men that I
  2849	instructed and educated, and myself have put together our Nautilus.
  2850	Then, when the work was finished, fire destroyed all trace of our
  2851	proceedings on this island, that I could have jumped over if I had
  2852	liked."
  2853	
  2854	"Then the cost of this vessel is great?"
  2855	
  2856	"M. Aronnax, an iron vessel costs L145 per ton.  Now the Nautilus
  2857	weighed 1,500. It came therefore to L67,500, and L80,000 more for
  2858	fitting it up, and about L200,000, with the works of art and the
  2859	collections it contains."
  2860	
  2861	"One last question, Captain Nemo."
  2862	
  2863	"Ask it, Professor."
  2864	
  2865	"You are rich?"
  2866	
  2867	"Immensely rich, sir; and I could, without missing it, pay the national
  2868	debt of France."
  2869	
  2870	I stared at the singular person who spoke thus.  Was he playing upon my
  2871	credulity?  The future would decide that.
  2872	
  2873	
  2874	
  2875	CHAPTER XIII
  2876	
  2877	THE BLACK RIVER
  2878	
  2879	The portion of the terrestrial globe which is covered by water is
  2880	estimated at upwards of eighty millions of acres.  This fluid mass
  2881	comprises two billions two hundred and fifty millions of cubic miles,
  2882	forming a spherical body of a diameter of sixty leagues, the weight of
  2883	which would be three quintillions of tons.  To comprehend the meaning
  2884	of these figures, it is necessary to observe that a quintillion is to a
  2885	billion as a billion is to unity; in other words, there are as many
  2886	billions in a quintillion as there are units in a billion.  This mass
  2887	of fluid is equal to about the quantity of water which would be
  2888	discharged by all the rivers of the earth in forty thousand years.
  2889	
  2890	During the geological epochs the ocean originally prevailed everywhere.
  2891	Then by degrees, in the silurian period, the tops of the mountains
  2892	began to appear, the islands emerged, then disappeared in partial
  2893	deluges, reappeared, became settled, formed continents, till at length
  2894	the earth became geographically arranged, as we see in the present day.
  2895	The solid had wrested from the liquid thirty-seven million six hundred
  2896	and fifty-seven square miles, equal to twelve billions nine hundred and
  2897	sixty millions of acres.
  2898	
  2899	The shape of continents allows us to divide the waters into five great
  2900	portions:  the Arctic or Frozen Ocean, the Antarctic, or Frozen Ocean,
  2901	the Indian, the Atlantic, and the Pacific Oceans.
  2902	
  2903	The Pacific Ocean extends from north to south between the two Polar
  2904	Circles, and from east to west between Asia and America, over an extent
  2905	of 145 degrees of longitude.  It is the quietest of seas; its currents
  2906	are broad and slow, it has medium tides, and abundant rain.  Such was
  2907	the ocean that my fate destined me first to travel over under these
  2908	strange conditions.
  2909	
  2910	"Sir," said Captain Nemo, "we will, if you please, take our bearings
  2911	and fix the starting-point of this voyage.  It is a quarter to twelve;
  2912	I will go up again to the surface."
  2913	
  2914	The Captain pressed an electric clock three times.  The pumps began to
  2915	drive the water from the tanks; the needle of the manometer marked by a
  2916	different pressure the ascent of the Nautilus, then it stopped.
  2917	
  2918	"We have arrived," said the Captain.
  2919	
  2920	I went to the central staircase which opened on to the platform,
  2921	clambered up the iron steps, and found myself on the upper part of the
  2922	Nautilus.
  2923	
  2924	The platform was only three feet out of water.  The front and back of
  2925	the Nautilus was of that spindle-shape which caused it justly to be
  2926	compared to a cigar.  I noticed that its iron plates, slightly
  2927	overlaying each other, resembled the shell which clothes the bodies of
  2928	our large terrestrial reptiles.  It explained to me how natural it was,
  2929	in spite of all glasses, that this boat should have been taken for a
  2930	marine animal.
  2931	
  2932	Toward the middle of the platform the longboat, half buried in the hull
  2933	of the vessel, formed a slight excrescence.  Fore and aft rose two
  2934	cages of medium height with inclined sides, and partly closed by thick
  2935	lenticular glasses; one destined for the steersman who directed the
  2936	Nautilus, the other containing a brilliant lantern to give light on the
  2937	road.
  2938	
  2939	The sea was beautiful, the sky pure.  Scarcely could the long vehicle
  2940	feel the broad undulations of the ocean.  A light breeze from the east
  2941	rippled the surface of the waters.  The horizon, free from fog, made
  2942	observation easy.  Nothing was in sight.  Not a quicksand, not an
  2943	island.  A vast desert.
  2944	
  2945	Captain Nemo, by the help of his sextant, took the altitude of the sun,
  2946	which ought also to give the latitude.  He waited for some moments till
