  2947	its disc touched the horizon.  Whilst taking observations not a muscle
  2948	moved, the instrument could not have been more motionless in a hand of
  2949	marble.
  2950	
  2951	"Twelve o'clock, sir," said he.  "When you like----"
  2952	
  2953	I cast a last look upon the sea, slightly yellowed by the Japanese
  2954	coast, and descended to the saloon.
  2955	
  2956	"And now, sir, I leave you to your studies," added the Captain; "our
  2957	course is E.N.E., our depth is twenty-six fathoms.  Here are maps on a
  2958	large scale by which you may follow it.  The saloon is at your
  2959	disposal, and, with your permission, I will retire."  Captain Nemo
  2960	bowed, and I remained alone, lost in thoughts all bearing on the
  2961	commander of the Nautilus.
  2962	
  2963	For a whole hour was I deep in these reflections, seeking to pierce
  2964	this mystery so interesting to me.  Then my eyes fell upon the vast
  2965	planisphere spread upon the table, and I placed my finger on the very
  2966	spot where the given latitude and longitude crossed.
  2967	
  2968	The sea has its large rivers like the continents.  They are special
  2969	currents known by their temperature and their colour.  The most
  2970	remarkable of these is known by the name of the Gulf Stream.  Science
  2971	has decided on the globe the direction of five principal currents: one
  2972	in the North Atlantic, a second in the South, a third in the North
  2973	Pacific, a fourth in the South, and a fifth in the Southern Indian
  2974	Ocean.  It is even probable that a sixth current existed at one time or
  2975	another in the Northern Indian Ocean, when the Caspian and Aral Seas
  2976	formed but one vast sheet of water.
  2977	
  2978	At this point indicated on the planisphere one of these currents was
  2979	rolling, the Kuro-Scivo of the Japanese, the Black River, which,
  2980	leaving the Gulf of Bengal, where it is warmed by the perpendicular
  2981	rays of a tropical sun, crosses the Straits of Malacca along the coast
  2982	of Asia, turns into the North Pacific to the Aleutian Islands, carrying
  2983	with it trunks of camphor-trees and other indigenous productions, and
  2984	edging the waves of the ocean with the pure indigo of its warm water.
  2985	It was this current that the Nautilus was to follow.  I followed it
  2986	with my eye; saw it lose itself in the vastness of the Pacific, and
  2987	felt myself drawn with it, when Ned Land and Conseil appeared at the
  2988	door of the saloon.
  2989	
  2990	My two brave companions remained petrified at the sight of the wonders
  2991	spread before them.
  2992	
  2993	"Where are we, where are we?" exclaimed the Canadian.  "In the museum
  2994	at Quebec?"
  2995	
  2996	"My friends," I answered, making a sign for them to enter, "you are not
  2997	in Canada, but on board the Nautilus, fifty yards below the level of
  2998	the sea."
  2999	
  3000	"But, M. Aronnax," said Ned Land, "can you tell me how many men there
  3001	are on board?  Ten, twenty, fifty, a hundred?"
  3002	
  3003	"I cannot answer you, Mr. Land; it is better to abandon for a time all
  3004	idea of seizing the Nautilus or escaping from it.  This ship is a
  3005	masterpiece of modern industry, and I should be sorry not to have seen
  3006	it.  Many people would accept the situation forced upon us, if only to
  3007	move amongst such wonders.  So be quiet and let us try and see what
  3008	passes around us."
  3009	
  3010	"See!" exclaimed the harpooner, "but we can see nothing in this iron
  3011	prison!  We are walking--we are sailing--blindly."
  3012	
  3013	Ned Land had scarcely pronounced these words when all was suddenly
  3014	darkness.  The luminous ceiling was gone, and so rapidly that my eyes
  3015	received a painful impression.
  3016	
  3017	We remained mute, not stirring, and not knowing what surprise awaited
  3018	us, whether agreeable or disagreeable.  A sliding noise was heard: one
  3019	would have said that panels were working at the sides of the Nautilus.
  3020	
  3021	"It is the end of the end!" said Ned Land.
  3022	
  3023	Suddenly light broke at each side of the saloon, through two oblong
  3024	openings.  The liquid mass appeared vividly lit up by the electric
  3025	gleam.  Two crystal plates separated us from the sea.  At first I
  3026	trembled at the thought that this frail partition might break, but
  3027	strong bands of copper bound them, giving an almost infinite power of
  3028	resistance.
  3029	
  3030	The sea was distinctly visible for a mile all round the Nautilus.  What
  3031	a spectacle!  What pen can describe it?  Who could paint the effects of
  3032	the light through those transparent sheets of water, and the softness
  3033	of the successive gradations from the lower to the superior strata of
  3034	the ocean?
  3035	
  3036	We know the transparency of the sea and that its clearness is far
  3037	beyond that of rock-water. The mineral and organic substances which it
  3038	holds in suspension heightens its transparency.  In certain parts of
  3039	the ocean at the Antilles, under seventy-five fathoms of water, can be
  3040	seen with surprising clearness a bed of sand.  The penetrating power of
  3041	the solar rays does not seem to cease for a depth of one hundred and
  3042	fifty fathoms.  But in this middle fluid travelled over by the
  3043	Nautilus, the electric brightness was produced even in the bosom of the
  3044	waves.  It was no longer luminous water, but liquid light.
  3045	
  3046	On each side a window opened into this unexplored abyss.  The obscurity
  3047	of the saloon showed to advantage the brightness outside, and we looked
  3048	out as if this pure crystal had been the glass of an immense aquarium.
  3049	
  3050	"You wished to see, friend Ned; well, you see now."
  3051	
  3052	"Curious! curious!" muttered the Canadian, who, forgetting his
  3053	ill-temper, seemed to submit to some irresistible attraction; "and one
  3054	would come further than this to admire such a sight!"
  3055	
  3056	"Ah!" thought I to myself, "I understand the life of this man; he has
  3057	made a world apart for himself, in which he treasures all his greatest
  3058	wonders."
  3059	
  3060	For two whole hours an aquatic army escorted the Nautilus.  During
  3061	their games, their bounds, while rivalling each other in beauty,
  3062	brightness, and velocity, I distinguished the green labre; the banded
  3063	mullet, marked by a double line of black; the round-tailed goby, of a
  3064	white colour, with violet spots on the back; the Japanese scombrus, a
  3065	beautiful mackerel of these seas, with a blue body and silvery head;
  3066	the brilliant azurors, whose name alone defies description; some banded
  3067	spares, with variegated fins of blue and yellow; the woodcocks of the
  3068	seas, some specimens of which attain a yard in length; Japanese
  3069	salamanders, spider lampreys, serpents six feet long, with eyes small
  3070	and lively, and a huge mouth bristling with teeth; with many other
  3071	species.
  3072	
  3073	Our imagination was kept at its height, interjections followed quickly
  3074	on each other.  Ned named the fish, and Conseil classed them.  I was in
  3075	ecstasies with the vivacity of their movements and the beauty of their
  3076	forms.  Never had it been given to me to surprise these animals, alive
  3077	and at liberty, in their natural element.  I will not mention all the
  3078	varieties which passed before my dazzled eyes, all the collection of
  3079	the seas of China and Japan.  These fish, more numerous than the birds
  3080	of the air, came, attracted, no doubt, by the brilliant focus of the
  3081	electric light.
  3082	
  3083	Suddenly there was daylight in the saloon, the iron panels closed
  3084	again, and the enchanting vision disappeared.  But for a long time I
  3085	dreamt on, till my eyes fell on the instruments hanging on the
  3086	partition.  The compass still showed the course to be E.N.E., the
  3087	manometer indicated a pressure of five atmospheres, equivalent to a
  3088	depth of twenty five fathoms, and the electric log gave a speed of
  3089	fifteen miles an hour.  I expected Captain Nemo, but he did not appear.
  3090	The clock marked the hour of five.
  3091	
  3092	Ned Land and Conseil returned to their cabin, and I retired to my
  3093	chamber.  My dinner was ready.  It was composed of turtle soup made of
  3094	the most delicate hawks bills, of a surmullet served with puff paste
  3095	(the liver of which, prepared by itself, was most delicious), and
  3096	fillets of the emperor-holocanthus, the savour of which seemed to me
  3097	superior even to salmon.
  3098	
  3099	I passed the evening reading, writing, and thinking.  Then sleep
  3100	overpowered me, and I stretched myself on my couch of zostera, and
  3101	slept profoundly, whilst the Nautilus was gliding rapidly through the
  3102	current of the Black River.
  3103	
  3104	
  3105	
  3106	CHAPTER XIV
  3107	
  3108	A NOTE OF INVITATION
  3109	
  3110	The next day was the 9th of November.  I awoke after a long sleep of
  3111	twelve hours.  Conseil came, according to custom, to know "how I passed
  3112	the night," and to offer his services.  He had left his friend the
  3113	Canadian sleeping like a man who had never done anything else all his
  3114	life.  I let the worthy fellow chatter as he pleased, without caring to
  3115	answer him.  I was preoccupied by the absence of the Captain during our
  3116	sitting of the day before, and hoping to see him to-day.
  3117	
  3118	As soon as I was dressed I went into the saloon.  It was deserted.  I
  3119	plunged into the study of the shell treasures hidden behind the glasses.
  3120	
  3121	The whole day passed without my being honoured by a visit from Captain
  3122	Nemo.  The panels of the saloon did not open.  Perhaps they did not
  3123	wish us to tire of these beautiful things.
  3124	
  3125	The course of the Nautilus was E.N.E., her speed twelve knots, the
  3126	depth below the surface between twenty-five and thirty fathoms.
  3127	
  3128	The next day, 10th of November, the same desertion, the same solitude.
  3129	I did not see one of the ship's crew: Ned and Conseil spent the greater
  3130	part of the day with me.  They were astonished at the puzzling absence
  3131	of the Captain.  Was this singular man ill?--had he altered his
  3132	intentions with regard to us?
  3133	
  3134	After all, as Conseil said, we enjoyed perfect liberty, we were
  3135	delicately and abundantly fed.  Our host kept to his terms of the
  3136	treaty.  We could not complain, and, indeed, the singularity of our
  3137	fate reserved such wonderful compensation for us that we had no right
  3138	to accuse it as yet.
  3139	
  3140	That day I commenced the journal of these adventures which has enabled
  3141	me to relate them with more scrupulous exactitude and minute detail.
  3142	
  3143	11th November, early in the morning.  The fresh air spreading over the
  3144	interior of the Nautilus told me that we had come to the surface of the
  3145	ocean to renew our supply of oxygen.  I directed my steps to the
  3146	central staircase, and mounted the platform.
  3147	
  3148	It was six o'clock, the weather was cloudy, the sea grey, but calm.
  3149	Scarcely a billow.  Captain Nemo, whom I hoped to meet, would he be
  3150	there?  I saw no one but the steersman imprisoned in his glass cage.
  3151	Seated upon the projection formed by the hull of the pinnace, I inhaled
  3152	the salt breeze with delight.
  3153	
  3154	By degrees the fog disappeared under the action of the sun's rays, the
  3155	radiant orb rose from behind the eastern horizon.  The sea flamed under
  3156	its glance like a train of gunpowder.  The clouds scattered in the
  3157	heights were coloured with lively tints of beautiful shades, and
  3158	numerous "mare's tails," which betokened wind for that day.  But what
  3159	was wind to this Nautilus, which tempests could not frighten!
  3160	
  3161	I was admiring this joyous rising of the sun, so gay, and so
  3162	life-giving, when I heard steps approaching the platform.  I was
  3163	prepared to salute Captain Nemo, but it was his second (whom I had
  3164	already seen on the Captain's first visit) who appeared.  He advanced
  3165	on the platform, not seeming to see me.  With his powerful glass to his
  3166	eye, he scanned every point of the horizon with great attention.  This
  3167	examination over, he approached the panel and pronounced a sentence in
  3168	exactly these terms.  I have remembered it, for every morning it was
  3169	repeated under exactly the same conditions.  It was thus worded:
  3170	
  3171	"Nautron respoc lorni virch."
  3172	
  3173	What it meant I could not say.
  3174	
  3175	These words pronounced, the second descended.  I thought that the
  3176	Nautilus was about to return to its submarine navigation.  I regained
  3177	the panel and returned to my chamber.
  3178	
  3179	Five days sped thus, without any change in our situation.  Every
  3180	morning I mounted the platform.  The same phrase was pronounced by the
  3181	same individual.  But Captain Nemo did not appear.
  3182	
  3183	I had made up my mind that I should never see him again, when, on the
  3184	16th November, on returning to my room with Ned and Conseil, I found
  3185	upon my table a note addressed to me.  I opened it impatiently.  It was
  3186	written in a bold, clear hand, the characters rather pointed, recalling
  3187	the German type.  The note was worded as follows:
  3188	
  3189	
  3190	TO PROFESSOR ARONNAX, On board the Nautilus.  16th of November, 1867.
  3191	
  3192	Captain Nemo invites Professor Aronnax to a hunting-party, which will
  3193	take place to-morrow morning in the forests of the Island of Crespo.
  3194	He hopes that nothing will prevent the Professor from being present,
  3195	and he will with pleasure see him joined by his companions.
  3196	
  3197	CAPTAIN NEMO, Commander of the Nautilus.
  3198	
  3199	
  3200	"A hunt!" exclaimed Ned.
  3201	
  3202	"And in the forests of the Island of Crespo!" added Conseil.
  3203	
  3204	"Oh! then the gentleman is going on terra firma?" replied Ned Land.
  3205	
  3206	"That seems to me to be clearly indicated," said I, reading the letter
  3207	once more.
  3208	
  3209	"Well, we must accept," said the Canadian.  "But once more on dry
  3210	ground, we shall know what to do.  Indeed, I shall not be sorry to eat
  3211	a piece of fresh venison."
  3212	
  3213	Without seeking to reconcile what was contradictory between Captain
  3214	Nemo's manifest aversion to islands and continents, and his invitation
  3215	to hunt in a forest, I contented myself with replying:
  3216	
  3217	"Let us first see where the Island of Crespo is."
  3218	
  3219	I consulted the planisphere, and in 32° 40' N. lat. and 157°
  3220	50' W. long., I found a small island, recognised in 1801 by Captain
  3221	Crespo, and marked in the ancient Spanish maps as Rocca de la Plata,
  3222	the meaning of which is The Silver Rock.  We were then about eighteen
  3223	hundred miles from our starting-point, and the course of the Nautilus,
  3224	a little changed, was bringing it back towards the southeast.
  3225	
  3226	I showed this little rock, lost in the midst of the North Pacific, to
  3227	my companions.
  3228	
  3229	"If Captain Nemo does sometimes go on dry ground," said I, "he at least
  3230	chooses desert islands."
  3231	
  3232	Ned Land shrugged his shoulders without speaking, and Conseil and he
  3233	left me.
  3234	
  3235	After supper, which was served by the steward, mute and impassive, I
  3236	went to bed, not without some anxiety.
  3237	
  3238	The next morning, the 17th of November, on awakening, I felt that the
  3239	Nautilus was perfectly still.  I dressed quickly and entered the saloon.
  3240	
  3241	Captain Nemo was there, waiting for me.  He rose, bowed, and asked me
  3242	if it was convenient for me to accompany him.  As he made no allusion
  3243	to his absence during the last eight days, I did not mention it, and
  3244	simply answered that my companions and myself were ready to follow him.
  3245	
  3246	We entered the dining-room, where breakfast was served.
  3247	
  3248	"M. Aronnax," said the Captain, "pray, share my breakfast without
  3249	ceremony; we will chat as we eat.  For, though I promised you a walk in
  3250	the forest, I did not undertake to find hotels there.  So breakfast as
  3251	a man who will most likely not have his dinner till very late."
  3252	
  3253	I did honour to the repast.  It was composed of several kinds of fish,
  3254	and slices of sea-cucumber, and different sorts of seaweed.  Our drink
  3255	consisted of pure water, to which the Captain added some drops of a
  3256	fermented liquor, extracted by the Kamschatcha method from a seaweed
  3257	known under the name of Rhodomenia palmata.  Captain Nemo ate at first
  3258	without saying a word.  Then he began:
  3259	
  3260	"Sir, when I proposed to you to hunt in my submarine forest of Crespo,
  3261	you evidently thought me mad.  Sir, you should never judge lightly of
  3262	any man."
  3263	
  3264	"But Captain, believe me----"
  3265	
  3266	"Be kind enough to listen, and you will then see whether you have any
  3267	cause to accuse me of folly and contradiction."
  3268	
  3269	"I listen."
  3270	
  3271	"You know as well as I do, Professor, that man can live under water,
  3272	providing he carries with him a sufficient supply of breathable air.
  3273	In submarine works, the workman, clad in an impervious dress, with his
  3274	head in a metal helmet, receives air from above by means of forcing
  3275	pumps and regulators."
  3276	
  3277	"That is a diving apparatus," said I.
  3278	
  3279	"Just so, but under these conditions the man is not at liberty; he is
  3280	attached to the pump which sends him air through an india-rubber tube,
  3281	and if we were obliged to be thus held to the Nautilus, we could not go
  3282	far."
  3283	
  3284	"And the means of getting free?"  I asked.
  3285	
  3286	"It is to use the Rouquayrol apparatus, invented by two of your own
  3287	countrymen, which I have brought to perfection for my own use, and
  3288	which will allow you to risk yourself under these new physiological
  3289	conditions without any organ whatever suffering.  It consists of a
  3290	reservoir of thick iron plates, in which I store the air under a
  3291	pressure of fifty atmospheres.  This reservoir is fixed on the back by
  3292	means of braces, like a soldier's knapsack.  Its upper part forms a box
  3293	in which the air is kept by means of a bellows, and therefore cannot
  3294	escape unless at its normal tension.  In the Rouquayrol apparatus such
  3295	as we use, two india rubber pipes leave this box and join a sort of
  3296	tent which holds the nose and mouth; one is to introduce fresh air, the
  3297	other to let out the foul, and the tongue closes one or the other
  3298	according to the wants of the respirator.  But I, in encountering great
  3299	pressures at the bottom of the sea, was obliged to shut my head, like
  3300	that of a diver in a ball of copper; and it is to this ball of copper
  3301	that the two pipes, the inspirator and the expirator, open."
  3302	
  3303	"Perfectly, Captain Nemo; but the air that you carry with you must soon
  3304	be used; when it only contains fifteen per cent.  of oxygen it is no
  3305	longer fit to breathe."
  3306	
  3307	"Right!  But I told you, M. Aronnax, that the pumps of the Nautilus
  3308	allow me to store the air under considerable pressure, and on those
  3309	conditions the reservoir of the apparatus can furnish breathable air
  3310	for nine or ten hours."
  3311	
  3312	"I have no further objections to make," I answered.  "I will only ask
  3313	you one thing, Captain--how can you light your road at the bottom of
  3314	the sea?"
  3315	
  3316	"With the Ruhmkorff apparatus, M. Aronnax; one is carried on the back,
  3317	the other is fastened to the waist.  It is composed of a Bunsen pile,
  3318	which I do not work with bichromate of potash, but with sodium.  A wire
  3319	is introduced which collects the electricity produced, and directs it
  3320	towards a particularly made lantern.  In this lantern is a spiral glass
  3321	which contains a small quantity of carbonic gas.  When the apparatus is
  3322	at work this gas becomes luminous, giving out a white and continuous
  3323	light.  Thus provided, I can breathe and I can see."
  3324	
  3325	"Captain Nemo, to all my objections you make such crushing answers that
  3326	I dare no longer doubt.  But, if I am forced to admit the Rouquayrol
  3327	and Ruhmkorff apparatus, I must be allowed some reservations with
  3328	regard to the gun I am to carry."
  3329	
  3330	"But it is not a gun for powder," answered the Captain.
  3331	
  3332	"Then it is an air-gun."
  3333	
  3334	"Doubtless!  How would you have me manufacture gun powder on board,
  3335	without either saltpetre, sulphur, or charcoal?"
  3336	
  3337	"Besides," I added, "to fire under water in a medium eight hundred and
  3338	fifty-five times denser than the air, we must conquer very considerable
  3339	resistance."
  3340	
  3341	"That would be no difficulty.  There exist guns, according to Fulton,
  3342	perfected in England by Philip Coles and Burley, in France by Furcy,
  3343	and in Italy by Landi, which are furnished with a peculiar system of
  3344	closing, which can fire under these conditions.  But I repeat, having
  3345	no powder, I use air under great pressure, which the pumps of the
  3346	Nautilus furnish abundantly."
  3347	
  3348	"But this air must be rapidly used?"
  3349	
  3350	"Well, have I not my Rouquayrol reservoir, which can furnish it at
  3351	need?  A tap is all that is required.  Besides M. Aronnax, you must see
  3352	yourself that, during our submarine hunt, we can spend but little air
  3353	and but few balls."
  3354	
  3355	"But it seems to me that in this twilight, and in the midst of this
  3356	fluid, which is very dense compared with the atmosphere, shots could
  3357	not go far, nor easily prove mortal."
  3358	
  3359	"Sir, on the contrary, with this gun every blow is mortal; and, however
  3360	lightly the animal is touched, it falls as if struck by a thunderbolt."
  3361	
  3362	"Why?"
  3363	
  3364	"Because the balls sent by this gun are not ordinary balls, but little
  3365	cases of glass.  These glass cases are covered with a case of steel,
  3366	and weighted with a pellet of lead; they are real Leyden bottles, into
  3367	which the electricity is forced to a very high tension.  With the
  3368	slightest shock they are discharged, and the animal, however strong it
  3369	may be, falls dead.  I must tell you that these cases are size number
  3370	four, and that the charge for an ordinary gun would be ten."
  3371	
  3372	"I will argue no longer," I replied, rising from the table.  "I have
  3373	nothing left me but to take my gun.  At all events, I will go where you
  3374	go."
  3375	
  3376	Captain Nemo then led me aft; and in passing before Ned's and Conseil's
  3377	cabin, I called my two companions, who followed promptly.  We then came
  3378	to a cell near the machinery-room, in which we put on our walking-dress.
  3379	
  3380	
  3381	
  3382	CHAPTER XV
  3383	
  3384	A WALK ON THE BOTTOM OF THE SEA
  3385	
  3386	This cell was, to speak correctly, the arsenal and wardrobe of the
  3387	Nautilus.  A dozen diving apparatuses hung from the partition waiting
  3388	our use.
  3389	
  3390	Ned Land, on seeing them, showed evident repugnance to dress himself in
  3391	one.
  3392	
  3393	"But, my worthy Ned, the forests of the Island of Crespo are nothing
  3394	but submarine forests."
  3395	
  3396	"Good!" said the disappointed harpooner, who saw his dreams of fresh
  3397	meat fade away.  "And you, M. Aronnax, are you going to dress yourself
  3398	in those clothes?"
  3399	
  3400	"There is no alternative, Master Ned."
  3401	
  3402	"As you please, sir," replied the harpooner, shrugging his shoulders;
  3403	"but, as for me, unless I am forced, I will never get into one."
  3404	
  3405	"No one will force you, Master Ned," said Captain Nemo.
  3406	
  3407	"Is Conseil going to risk it?" asked Ned.
  3408	
  3409	"I follow my master wherever he goes," replied Conseil.
  3410	
  3411	At the Captain's call two of the ship's crew came to help us dress in
  3412	these heavy and impervious clothes, made of india-rubber without seam,
  3413	and constructed expressly to resist considerable pressure.  One would
  3414	have thought it a suit of armour, both supple and resisting.  This suit
  3415	formed trousers and waistcoat.  The trousers were finished off with
  3416	thick boots, weighted with heavy leaden soles.  The texture of the
  3417	waistcoat was held together by bands of copper, which crossed the
  3418	chest, protecting it from the great pressure of the water, and leaving
  3419	the lungs free to act; the sleeves ended in gloves, which in no way
  3420	restrained the movement of the hands.  There was a vast difference
  3421	noticeable between these consummate apparatuses and the old cork
  3422	breastplates, jackets, and other contrivances in vogue during the
  3423	eighteenth century.
  3424	
  3425	Captain Nemo and one of his companions (a sort of Hercules, who must
  3426	have possessed great strength), Conseil and myself were soon enveloped
  3427	in the dresses.  There remained nothing more to be done but to enclose
  3428	our heads in the metal box.  But, before proceeding to this operation,
  3429	I asked the Captain's permission to examine the guns.
  3430	
  3431	One of the Nautilus men gave me a simple gun, the butt end of which,
  3432	made of steel, hollow in the centre, was rather large.  It served as a
  3433	reservoir for compressed air, which a valve, worked by a spring,
  3434	allowed to escape into a metal tube.  A box of projectiles in a groove
  3435	in the thickness of the butt end contained about twenty of these
  3436	electric balls, which, by means of a spring, were forced into the
  3437	barrel of the gun.  As soon as one shot was fired, another was ready.
  3438	
  3439	"Captain Nemo," said I, "this arm is perfect, and easily handled: I
  3440	only ask to be allowed to try it.  But how shall we gain the bottom of
  3441	the sea?"
  3442	
  3443	"At this moment, Professor, the Nautilus is stranded in five fathoms,
  3444	and we have nothing to do but to start."
  3445	
  3446	"But how shall we get off?"
  3447	
  3448	"You shall see."
  3449	
  3450	Captain Nemo thrust his head into the helmet, Conseil and I did the
  3451	same, not without hearing an ironical "Good sport!" from the Canadian.
  3452	The upper part of our dress terminated in a copper collar upon which
  3453	was screwed the metal helmet.  Three holes, protected by thick glass,
  3454	allowed us to see in all directions, by simply turning our head in the
  3455	interior of the head-dress. As soon as it was in position, the
  3456	Rouquayrol apparatus on our backs began to act; and, for my part, I
  3457	could breathe with ease.
  3458	
  3459	With the Ruhmkorff lamp hanging from my belt, and the gun in my hand, I
  3460	was ready to set out.  But to speak the truth, imprisoned in these
  3461	heavy garments, and glued to the deck by my leaden soles, it was
  3462	impossible for me to take a step.
  3463	
  3464	But this state of things was provided for.  I felt myself being pushed
  3465	into a little room contiguous to the wardrobe room.  My companions
  3466	followed, towed along in the same way.  I heard a water-tight door,
  3467	furnished with stopper plates, close upon us, and we were wrapped in
  3468	profound darkness.
  3469	
  3470	After some minutes, a loud hissing was heard.  I felt the cold mount
  3471	from my feet to my chest.  Evidently from some part of the vessel they
  3472	had, by means of a tap, given entrance to the water, which was invading
  3473	us, and with which the room was soon filled.  A second door cut in the
  3474	side of the Nautilus then opened.  We saw a faint light.  In another
  3475	instant our feet trod the bottom of the sea.
  3476	
  3477	And now, how can I retrace the impression left upon me by that walk
  3478	under the waters?  Words are impotent to relate such wonders!  Captain
  3479	Nemo walked in front, his companion followed some steps behind.
  3480	Conseil and I remained near each other, as if an exchange of words had
  3481	been possible through our metallic cases.  I no longer felt the weight
  3482	of my clothing, or of my shoes, of my reservoir of air, or my thick
  3483	helmet, in the midst of which my head rattled like an almond in its
  3484	shell.
  3485	
  3486	The light, which lit the soil thirty feet below the surface of the
  3487	ocean, astonished me by its power.  The solar rays shone through the
  3488	watery mass easily, and dissipated all colour, and I clearly
  3489	distinguished objects at a distance of a hundred and fifty yards.
  3490	Beyond that the tints darkened into fine gradations of ultramarine, and
  3491	faded into vague obscurity.  Truly this water which surrounded me was
  3492	but another air denser than the terrestrial atmosphere, but almost as
  3493	transparent.  Above me was the calm surface of the sea.  We were
  3494	walking on fine, even sand, not wrinkled, as on a flat shore, which
  3495	retains the impression of the billows.  This dazzling carpet, really a
  3496	reflector, repelled the rays of the sun with wonderful intensity, which
  3497	accounted for the vibration which penetrated every atom of liquid.
  3498	Shall I be believed when I say that, at the depth of thirty feet, I
  3499	could see as if I was in broad daylight?
  3500	
  3501	For a quarter of an hour I trod on this sand, sown with the impalpable
  3502	dust of shells.  The hull of the Nautilus, resembling a long shoal,
  3503	disappeared by degrees; but its lantern, when darkness should overtake
  3504	us in the waters, would help to guide us on board by its distinct rays.
  3505	
  3506	Soon forms of objects outlined in the distance were discernible.  I
  3507	recognised magnificent rocks, hung with a tapestry of zoophytes of the
  3508	most beautiful kind, and I was at first struck by the peculiar effect
  3509	of this medium.
  3510	
  3511	It was then ten in the morning; the rays of the sun struck the surface
  3512	of the waves at rather an oblique angle, and at the touch of their
  3513	light, decomposed by refraction as through a prism, flowers, rocks,
  3514	plants, shells, and polypi were shaded at the edges by the seven solar
  3515	colours.  It was marvellous, a feast for the eyes, this complication of
  3516	coloured tints, a perfect kaleidoscope of green, yellow, orange,
  3517	violet, indigo, and blue; in one word, the whole palette of an
  3518	enthusiastic colourist!  Why could I not communicate to Conseil the
  3519	lively sensations which were mounting to my brain, and rival him in
  3520	expressions of admiration?  For aught I knew, Captain Nemo and his
  3521	companion might be able to exchange thoughts by means of signs
  3522	previously agreed upon.  So, for want of better, I talked to myself; I
  3523	declaimed in the copper box which covered my head, thereby expending
  3524	more air in vain words than was perhaps wise.
  3525	
  3526	Various kinds of isis, clusters of pure tuft-coral, prickly fungi, and
  3527	anemones formed a brilliant garden of flowers, decked with their
  3528	collarettes of blue tentacles, sea-stars studding the sandy bottom.  It
  3529	was a real grief to me to crush under my feet the brilliant specimens
  3530	of molluscs which strewed the ground by thousands, of hammerheads,
  3531	donaciae (veritable bounding shells), of staircases, and red
  3532	helmet-shells, angel-wings, and many others produced by this
  3533	inexhaustible ocean.  But we were bound to walk, so we went on, whilst
  3534	above our heads waved medusae whose umbrellas of opal or rose-pink,
  3535	escalloped with a band of blue, sheltered us from the rays of the sun
  3536	and fiery pelagiae, which, in the darkness, would have strewn our path
  3537	with phosphorescent light.
  3538	
  3539	All these wonders I saw in the space of a quarter of a mile, scarcely
  3540	stopping, and following Captain Nemo, who beckoned me on by signs.
  3541	Soon the nature of the soil changed; to the sandy plain succeeded an
  3542	extent of slimy mud which the Americans call "ooze," composed of equal
  3543	parts of silicious and calcareous shells.  We then travelled over a
  3544	plain of seaweed of wild and luxuriant vegetation.  This sward was of
  3545	close texture, and soft to the feet, and rivalled the softest carpet
  3546	woven by the hand of man.  But whilst verdure was spread at our feet,
  3547	it did not abandon our heads.  A light network of marine plants, of
  3548	that inexhaustible family of seaweeds of which more than two thousand
  3549	kinds are known, grew on the surface of the water.
  3550	
  3551	I noticed that the green plants kept nearer the top of the sea, whilst
  3552	the red were at a greater depth, leaving to the black or brown the care
  3553	of forming gardens and parterres in the remote beds of the ocean.
  3554	
  3555	We had quitted the Nautilus about an hour and a half.  It was near
  3556	noon; I knew by the perpendicularity of the sun's rays, which were no
  3557	longer refracted.  The magical colours disappeared by degrees, and the
  3558	shades of emerald and sapphire were effaced.  We walked with a regular
  3559	step, which rang upon the ground with astonishing intensity; the
  3560	slightest noise was transmitted with a quickness to which the ear is
  3561	unaccustomed on the earth; indeed, water is a better conductor of sound
  3562	than air, in the ratio of four to one.  At this period the earth sloped
  3563	downwards; the light took a uniform tint.  We were at a depth of a
  3564	hundred and five yards and twenty inches, undergoing a pressure of six
  3565	atmospheres.
  3566	
  3567	At this depth I could still see the rays of the sun, though feebly; to
  3568	their intense brilliancy had succeeded a reddish twilight, the lowest
  3569	state between day and night; but we could still see well enough; it was
  3570	not necessary to resort to the Ruhmkorff apparatus as yet.  At this
  3571	moment Captain Nemo stopped; he waited till I joined him, and then
  3572	pointed to an obscure mass, looming in the shadow, at a short distance.
  3573	
  3574	"It is the forest of the Island of Crespo," thought I; and I was not
  3575	mistaken.
  3576	
  3577	
  3578	
  3579	CHAPTER XVI
  3580	
  3581	A SUBMARINE FOREST
  3582	
  3583	We had at last arrived on the borders of this forest, doubtless one of
  3584	the finest of Captain Nemo's immense domains.  He looked upon it as his
  3585	own, and considered he had the same right over it that the first men
  3586	had in the first days of the world.  And, indeed, who would have
  3587	disputed with him the possession of this submarine property?  What
  3588	other hardier pioneer would come, hatchet in hand, to cut down the dark
  3589	copses?
  3590	
  3591	This forest was composed of large tree-plants; and the moment we
  3592	penetrated under its vast arcades, I was struck by the singular
  3593	position of their branches--a position I had not yet observed.
  3594	
  3595	Not an herb which carpeted the ground, not a branch which clothed the
  3596	trees, was either broken or bent, nor did they extend horizontally; all
  3597	stretched up to the surface of the ocean.  Not a filament, not a
  3598	ribbon, however thin they might be, but kept as straight as a rod of
  3599	iron.  The fuci and llianas grew in rigid perpendicular lines, due to
  3600	the density of the element which had produced them.  Motionless yet,
  3601	when bent to one side by the hand, they directly resumed their former
  3602	position.  Truly it was the region of perpendicularity!
  3603	
  3604	I soon accustomed myself to this fantastic position, as well as to the
  3605	comparative darkness which surrounded us.  The soil of the forest
  3606	seemed covered with sharp blocks, difficult to avoid.  The submarine
  3607	flora struck me as being very perfect, and richer even than it would
  3608	have been in the arctic or tropical zones, where these productions are
  3609	not so plentiful.  But for some minutes I involuntarily confounded the
  3610	genera, taking animals for plants; and who would not have been
  3611	mistaken?  The fauna and the flora are too closely allied in this
  3612	submarine world.
  3613	
  3614	These plants are self-propagated, and the principle of their existence
  3615	is in the water, which upholds and nourishes them.  The greater number,
  3616	instead of leaves, shoot forth blades of capricious shapes, comprised
  3617	within a scale of colours pink, carmine, green, olive, fawn, and brown.
  3618	
  3619	"Curious anomaly, fantastic element!" said an ingenious naturalist, "in
  3620	which the animal kingdom blossoms, and the vegetable does not!"
  3621	
  3622	In about an hour Captain Nemo gave the signal to halt; I, for my part,
  3623	was not sorry, and we stretched ourselves under an arbour of alariae,
  3624	the long thin blades of which stood up like arrows.
  3625	
  3626	This short rest seemed delicious to me; there was nothing wanting but
  3627	the charm of conversation; but, impossible to speak, impossible to
  3628	answer, I only put my great copper head to Conseil's.  I saw the worthy
  3629	fellow's eyes glistening with delight, and, to show his satisfaction,
  3630	he shook himself in his breastplate of air, in the most comical way in
  3631	the world.
  3632	
  3633	After four hours of this walking, I was surprised not to find myself
  3634	dreadfully hungry.  How to account for this state of the stomach I
  3635	could not tell.  But instead I felt an insurmountable desire to sleep,
  3636	which happens to all divers.  And my eyes soon closed behind the thick
  3637	glasses, and I fell into a heavy slumber, which the movement alone had
  3638	prevented before.  Captain Nemo and his robust companion, stretched in
  3639	the clear crystal, set us the example.
  3640	
  3641	How long I remained buried in this drowsiness I cannot judge, but, when
  3642	I woke, the sun seemed sinking towards the horizon.  Captain Nemo had
  3643	already risen, and I was beginning to stretch my limbs, when an
  3644	unexpected apparition brought me briskly to my feet.
  3645	
  3646	A few steps off, a monstrous sea-spider, about thirty-eight inches
  3647	high, was watching me with squinting eyes, ready to spring upon me.
  3648	Though my diver's dress was thick enough to defend me from the bite of
  3649	this animal, I could not help shuddering with horror.  Conseil and the
  3650	sailor of the Nautilus awoke at this moment.  Captain Nemo pointed out
  3651	the hideous crustacean, which a blow from the butt end of the gun
  3652	knocked over, and I saw the horrible claws of the monster writhe in
  3653	terrible convulsions.  This incident reminded me that other animals
  3654	more to be feared might haunt these obscure depths, against whose
  3655	attacks my diving-dress would not protect me.  I had never thought of
  3656	it before, but I now resolved to be upon my guard.  Indeed, I thought
  3657	that this halt would mark the termination of our walk; but I was
  3658	mistaken, for, instead of returning to the Nautilus, Captain Nemo
  3659	continued his bold excursion.  The ground was still on the incline, its
  3660	declivity seemed to be getting greater, and to be leading us to greater
  3661	depths.  It must have been about three o'clock when we reached a narrow
  3662	valley, between high perpendicular walls, situated about seventy-five
  3663	fathoms deep.  Thanks to the perfection of our apparatus, we were
  3664	forty-five fathoms below the limit which nature seems to have imposed
  3665	on man as to his submarine excursions.
  3666	
  3667	I say seventy-five fathoms, though I had no instrument by which to
  3668	judge the distance.  But I knew that even in the clearest waters the
  3669	solar rays could not penetrate further.  And accordingly the darkness
  3670	deepened.  At ten paces not an object was visible.  I was groping my
  3671	way, when I suddenly saw a brilliant white light.  Captain Nemo had
  3672	just put his electric apparatus into use; his companion did the same,
  3673	and Conseil and I followed their example.  By turning a screw I
  3674	established a communication between the wire and the spiral glass, and
  3675	the sea, lit by our four lanterns, was illuminated for a circle of
  3676	thirty-six yards.
  3677	
  3678	As we walked I thought the light of our Ruhmkorff apparatus could not
  3679	fail to draw some inhabitant from its dark couch.  But if they did
  3680	approach us, they at least kept at a respectful distance from the
  3681	hunters.  Several times I saw Captain Nemo stop, put his gun to his
  3682	shoulder, and after some moments drop it and walk on.  At last, after
  3683	about four hours, this marvellous excursion came to an end.  A wall of
  3684	superb rocks, in an imposing mass, rose before us, a heap of gigantic
  3685	blocks, an enormous, steep granite shore, forming dark grottos, but
  3686	which presented no practicable slope; it was the prop of the Island of
  3687	Crespo.  It was the earth!  Captain Nemo stopped suddenly.  A gesture
  3688	of his brought us all to a halt; and, however desirous I might be to
  3689	scale the wall, I was obliged to stop.  Here ended Captain Nemo's
  3690	domains.  And he would not go beyond them.  Further on was a portion of
  3691	the globe he might not trample upon.
  3692	
  3693	The return began.  Captain Nemo had returned to the head of his little
  3694	band, directing their course without hesitation.  I thought we were not
  3695	following the same road to return to the Nautilus.  The new road was
  3696	very steep, and consequently very painful.  We approached the surface
  3697	of the sea rapidly.  But this return to the upper strata was not so
  3698	sudden as to cause relief from the pressure too rapidly, which might
  3699	have produced serious disorder in our organisation, and brought on
  3700	internal lesions, so fatal to divers.  Very soon light reappeared and
  3701	grew, and, the sun being low on the horizon, the refraction edged the
  3702	different objects with a spectral ring.  At ten yards and a half deep,
  3703	we walked amidst a shoal of little fishes of all kinds, more numerous
  3704	than the birds of the air, and also more agile; but no aquatic game
  3705	worthy of a shot had as yet met our gaze, when at that moment I saw the
  3706	Captain shoulder his gun quickly, and follow a moving object into the
  3707	shrubs.  He fired; I heard a slight hissing, and a creature fell
  3708	stunned at some distance from us.  It was a magnificent sea-otter, an
  3709	enhydrus, the only exclusively marine quadruped.  This otter was five
  3710	feet long, and must have been very valuable.  Its skin, chestnut-brown
  3711	above and silvery underneath, would have made one of those beautiful
  3712	furs so sought after in the Russian and Chinese markets: the fineness
  3713	and the lustre of its coat would certainly fetch L80.  I admired this
  3714	curious mammal, with its rounded head ornamented with short ears, its
  3715	round eyes, and white whiskers like those of a cat, with webbed feet
  3716	and nails, and tufted tail.  This precious animal, hunted and tracked
  3717	by fishermen, has now become very rare, and taken refuge chiefly in the
  3718	northern parts of the Pacific, or probably its race would soon become
  3719	extinct.
  3720	
  3721	Captain Nemo's companion took the beast, threw it over his shoulder,
  3722	and we continued our journey.  For one hour a plain of sand lay
  3723	stretched before us.  Sometimes it rose to within two yards and some
  3724	inches of the surface of the water.  I then saw our image clearly
  3725	reflected, drawn inversely, and above us appeared an identical group
  3726	reflecting our movements and our actions; in a word, like us in every
  3727	point, except that they walked with their heads downward and their feet
  3728	in the air.
  3729	
  3730	Another effect I noticed, which was the passage of thick clouds which
  3731	formed and vanished rapidly; but on reflection I understood that these
  3732	seeming clouds were due to the varying thickness of the reeds at the
  3733	bottom, and I could even see the fleecy foam which their broken tops
  3734	multiplied on the water, and the shadows of large birds passing above
  3735	our heads, whose rapid flight I could discern on the surface of the sea.
  3736	
  3737	On this occasion I was witness to one of the finest gun shots which
  3738	ever made the nerves of a hunter thrill.  A large bird of great breadth
  3739	of wing, clearly visible, approached, hovering over us.  Captain Nemo's
  3740	companion shouldered his gun and fired, when it was only a few yards
  3741	above the waves.  The creature fell stunned, and the force of its fall
  3742	brought it within the reach of dexterous hunter's grasp.  It was an
  3743	albatross of the finest kind.
  3744	
  3745	Our march had not been interrupted by this incident.  For two hours we
  3746	followed these sandy plains, then fields of algae very disagreeable to
  3747	cross.  Candidly, I could do no more when I saw a glimmer of light,
  3748	which, for a half mile, broke the darkness of the waters.  It was the
  3749	lantern of the Nautilus.  Before twenty minutes were over we should be
  3750	on board, and I should be able to breathe with ease, for it seemed that
  3751	my reservoir supplied air very deficient in oxygen.  But I did not
  3752	reckon on an accidental meeting which delayed our arrival for some time.
  3753	
  3754	I had remained some steps behind, when I presently saw Captain Nemo
  3755	coming hurriedly towards me.  With his strong hand he bent me to the
  3756	ground, his companion doing the same to Conseil.  At first I knew not
  3757	what to think of this sudden attack, but I was soon reassured by seeing
  3758	the Captain lie down beside me, and remain immovable.
  3759	
  3760	I was stretched on the ground, just under the shelter of a bush of
  3761	algae, when, raising my head, I saw some enormous mass, casting
  3762	phosphorescent gleams, pass blusteringly by.
  3763	
  3764	My blood froze in my veins as I recognised two formidable sharks which
  3765	threatened us.  It was a couple of tintoreas, terrible creatures, with
  3766	enormous tails and a dull glassy stare, the phosphorescent matter
  3767	ejected from holes pierced around the muzzle.  Monstrous brutes! which
  3768	would crush a whole man in their iron jaws.  I did not know whether
  3769	Conseil stopped to classify them; for my part, I noticed their silver
  3770	bellies, and their huge mouths bristling with teeth, from a very
  3771	unscientific point of view, and more as a possible victim than as a
  3772	naturalist.
  3773	
  3774	Happily the voracious creatures do not see well.  They passed without
  3775	seeing us, brushing us with their brownish fins, and we escaped by a
  3776	miracle from a danger certainly greater than meeting a tiger full-face
  3777	in the forest.  Half an hour after, guided by the electric light we
  3778	reached the Nautilus.  The outside door had been left open, and Captain
  3779	Nemo closed it as soon as we had entered the first cell.  He then
  3780	pressed a knob.  I heard the pumps working in the midst of the vessel,
  3781	I felt the water sinking from around me, and in a few moments the cell
  3782	was entirely empty.  The inside door then opened, and we entered the
  3783	vestry.
  3784	
  3785	There our diving-dress was taken off, not without some trouble, and,
  3786	fairly worn out from want of food and sleep, I returned to my room, in
  3787	great wonder at this surprising excursion at the bottom of the sea.
  3788	
  3789	
  3790	
  3791	CHAPTER XVII
  3792	
  3793	FOUR THOUSAND LEAGUES UNDER THE PACIFIC
  3794	
  3795	The next morning, the 18th of November, I had quite recovered from my
  3796	fatigues of the day before, and I went up on to the platform, just as
  3797	the second lieutenant was uttering his daily phrase.
  3798	
  3799	I was admiring the magnificent aspect of the ocean when Captain Nemo
  3800	appeared.  He did not seem to be aware of my presence, and began a
  3801	series of astronomical observations.  Then, when he had finished, he
  3802	went and leant on the cage of the watch-light, and gazed abstractedly
  3803	on the ocean.  In the meantime, a number of the sailors of the
  3804	Nautilus, all strong and healthy men, had come up onto the platform.
  3805	They came to draw up the nets that had been laid all night.  These
  3806	sailors were evidently of different nations, although the European type
  3807	was visible in all of them.  I recognised some unmistakable Irishmen,
  3808	Frenchmen, some Sclaves, and a Greek, or a Candiote.  They were civil,
  3809	and only used that odd language among themselves, the origin of which I
  3810	could not guess, neither could I question them.
  3811	
  3812	The nets were hauled in.  They were a large kind of "chaluts," like
  3813	those on the Normandy coasts, great pockets that the waves and a chain
  3814	fixed in the smaller meshes kept open.  These pockets, drawn by iron
  3815	poles, swept through the water, and gathered in everything in their
  3816	way.  That day they brought up curious specimens from those productive
  3817	coasts.
  3818	
  3819	I reckoned that the haul had brought in more than nine hundredweight of
  3820	fish.  It was a fine haul, but not to be wondered at.  Indeed, the nets
  3821	are let down for several hours, and enclose in their meshes an infinite
  3822	variety.  We had no lack of excellent food, and the rapidity of the
  3823	Nautilus and the attraction of the electric light could always renew
  3824	our supply.  These several productions of the sea were immediately
  3825	lowered through the panel to the steward's room, some to be eaten
  3826	fresh, and others pickled.
  3827	
  3828	The fishing ended, the provision of air renewed, I thought that the
  3829	Nautilus was about to continue its submarine excursion, and was
  3830	preparing to return to my room, when, without further preamble, the
  3831	Captain turned to me, saying:
  3832	
  3833	"Professor, is not this ocean gifted with real life?  It has its
  3834	tempers and its gentle moods.  Yesterday it slept as we did, and now it
  3835	has woke after a quiet night.  Look!" he continued, "it wakes under the
  3836	caresses of the sun.  It is going to renew its diurnal existence.  It
  3837	is an interesting study to watch the play of its organisation.  It has
  3838	a pulse, arteries, spasms; and I agree with the learned Maury, who
  3839	discovered in it a circulation as real as the circulation of blood in
  3840	animals.
  3841	
  3842	"Yes, the ocean has indeed circulation, and to promote it, the Creator
  3843	has caused things to multiply in it--caloric, salt, and animalculae."
  3844	
  3845	When Captain Nemo spoke thus, he seemed altogether changed, and aroused
  3846	an extraordinary emotion in me.
  3847	
  3848	"Also," he added, "true existence is there; and I can imagine the
  3849	foundations of nautical towns, clusters of submarine houses, which,
  3850	like the Nautilus, would ascend every morning to breathe at the surface
  3851	of the water, free towns, independent cities.  Yet who knows whether
  3852	some despot----"
  3853	
  3854	Captain Nemo finished his sentence with a violent gesture.  Then,
  3855	addressing me as if to chase away some sorrowful thought:
  3856	
  3857	"M. Aronnax," he asked, "do you know the depth of the ocean?"
  3858	
  3859	"I only know, Captain, what the principal soundings have taught us."
  3860	
  3861	"Could you tell me them, so that I can suit them to my purpose?"
  3862	
  3863	"These are some," I replied, "that I remember.  If I am not mistaken, a
  3864	depth of 8,000 yards has been found in the North Atlantic, and 2,500
  3865	yards in the Mediterranean.  The most remarkable soundings have been
  3866	made in the South Atlantic, near the thirty-fifth parallel, and they
  3867	gave 12,000 yards, 14,000 yards, and 15,000 yards.  To sum up all, it
  3868	is reckoned that if the bottom of the sea were levelled, its mean depth
  3869	would be about one and three-quarter leagues."
  3870	
  3871	"Well, Professor," replied the Captain, "we shall show you better than
  3872	that I hope.  As to the mean depth of this part of the Pacific, I tell
  3873	you it is only 4,000 yards."
  3874	
  3875	Having said this, Captain Nemo went towards the panel, and disappeared
  3876	down the ladder.  I followed him, and went into the large drawing-room.
  3877	The screw was immediately put in motion, and the log gave twenty miles
  3878	an hour.
  3879	
  3880	During the days and weeks that passed, Captain Nemo was very sparing of
  3881	his visits.  I seldom saw him.  The lieutenant pricked the ship's
  3882	course regularly on the chart, so I could always tell exactly the route
  3883	of the Nautilus.
  3884	
  3885	Nearly every day, for some time, the panels of the drawing-room were
  3886	opened, and we were never tired of penetrating the mysteries of the
  3887	submarine world.
  3888	
  3889	The general direction of the Nautilus was south-east, and it kept
  3890	between 100 and 150 yards of depth.  One day, however, I do not know
  3891	why, being drawn diagonally by means of the inclined planes, it touched
  3892	the bed of the sea.  The thermometer indicated a temperature of 4.25
  3893	(cent.): a temperature that at this depth seemed common to all
  3894	latitudes.
  3895	
  3896	At three o'clock in the morning of the 26th of November the Nautilus
  3897	crossed the tropic of Cancer at 172° long.  On 27th instant it
  3898	sighted the Sandwich Islands, where Cook died, February 14, 1779.  We
  3899	had then gone 4,860 leagues from our starting-point. In the morning,
  3900	when I went on the platform, I saw two miles to windward, Hawaii, the
  3901	largest of the seven islands that form the group.  I saw clearly the
  3902	cultivated ranges, and the several mountain-chains that run parallel
  3903	with the side, and the volcanoes that overtop Mouna-Rea, which rise
  3904	5,000 yards above the level of the sea.  Besides other things the nets
  3905	brought up, were several flabellariae and graceful polypi, that are
  3906	peculiar to that part of the ocean.  The direction of the Nautilus was
  3907	still to the south-east. It crossed the equator December 1, in 142°
  3908	long.; and on the 4th of the same month, after crossing rapidly and
  3909	without anything in particular occurring, we sighted the Marquesas
  3910	group.  I saw, three miles off, Martin's peak in Nouka-Hiva, the
  3911	largest of the group that belongs to France.  I only saw the woody
  3912	mountains against the horizon, because Captain Nemo did not wish to
  3913	bring the ship to the wind.  There the nets brought up beautiful
  3914	specimens of fish:  some with azure fins and tails like gold, the flesh
  3915	of which is unrivalled; some nearly destitute of scales, but of
  3916	exquisite flavour; others, with bony jaws, and yellow-tinged gills, as
  3917	good as bonitos; all fish that would be of use to us.  After leaving
  3918	these charming islands protected by the French flag, from the 4th to
  3919	the 11th of December the Nautilus sailed over about 2,000 miles.
  3920	
  3921	During the daytime of the 11th of December I was busy reading in the
  3922	large drawing-room. Ned Land and Conseil watched the luminous water
  3923	through the half-open panels.  The Nautilus was immovable.  While its
  3924	reservoirs were filled, it kept at a depth of 1,000 yards, a region
  3925	rarely visited in the ocean, and in which large fish were seldom seen.
  3926	
  3927	I was then reading a charming book by Jean Mace, The Slaves of the
  3928	Stomach, and I was learning some valuable lessons from it, when Conseil
  3929	interrupted me.
  3930	
  3931	"Will master come here a moment?" he said, in a curious voice.
  3932	
  3933	"What is the matter, Conseil?"
  3934	
  3935	"I want master to look."
  3936	
  3937	I rose, went, and leaned on my elbows before the panes and watched.
  3938	
  3939	In a full electric light, an enormous black mass, quite immovable, was
  3940	suspended in the midst of the waters.  I watched it attentively,
  3941	seeking to find out the nature of this gigantic cetacean.  But a sudden
  3942	thought crossed my mind.  "A vessel!" I said, half aloud.
  3943	
  3944	"Yes," replied the Canadian, "a disabled ship that has sunk
  3945	perpendicularly."
  3946	
  3947	Ned Land was right; we were close to a vessel of which the tattered
  3948	shrouds still hung from their chains.  The keel seemed to be in good
  3949	order, and it had been wrecked at most some few hours.  Three stumps of
  3950	masts, broken off about two feet above the bridge, showed that the
  3951	vessel had had to sacrifice its masts.  But, lying on its side, it had
  3952	filled, and it was heeling over to port.  This skeleton of what it had
  3953	once been was a sad spectacle as it lay lost under the waves, but
  3954	sadder still was the sight of the bridge, where some corpses, bound
  3955	with ropes, were still lying.  I counted five--four men, one of whom
  3956	was standing at the helm, and a woman standing by the poop, holding an
  3957	infant in her arms.  She was quite young.  I could distinguish her
  3958	features, which the water had not decomposed, by the brilliant light
  3959	from the Nautilus.  In one despairing effort, she had raised her infant
  3960	above her head--poor little thing!--whose arms encircled its mother's
  3961	neck.  The attitude of the four sailors was frightful, distorted as
  3962	they were by their convulsive movements, whilst making a last effort to
  3963	free themselves from the cords that bound them to the vessel.  The
  3964	steersman alone, calm, with a grave, clear face, his grey hair glued to
  3965	his forehead, and his hand clutching the wheel of the helm, seemed even
  3966	then to be guiding the three broken masts through the depths of the
  3967	ocean.
  3968	
  3969	What a scene!  We were dumb; our hearts beat fast before this
  3970	shipwreck, taken as it were from life and photographed in its last
  3971	moments.  And I saw already, coming towards it with hungry eyes,
  3972	enormous sharks, attracted by the human flesh.
  3973	
  3974	However, the Nautilus, turning, went round the submerged vessel, and in
  3975	one instant I read on the stern--"The Florida, Sunderland."
  3976	
  3977	
  3978	
  3979	CHAPTER XVIII
  3980	
  3981	VANIKORO
  3982	
  3983	This terrible spectacle was the forerunner of the series of maritime
  3984	catastrophes that the Nautilus was destined to meet with in its route.
  3985	As long as it went through more frequented waters, we often saw the
  3986	hulls of shipwrecked vessels that were rotting in the depths, and
  3987	deeper down cannons, bullets, anchors, chains, and a thousand other
  3988	iron materials eaten up by rust.  However, on the 11th of December we
  3989	sighted the Pomotou Islands, the old "dangerous group" of Bougainville,
  3990	that extend over a space of 500 leagues at E.S.E. to W.N.W., from the
  3991	Island Ducie to that of Lazareff.  This group covers an area of 370
  3992	square leagues, and it is formed of sixty groups of islands, among
  3993	which the Gambier group is remarkable, over which France exercises
  3994	sway.  These are coral islands, slowly raised, but continuous, created
  3995	by the daily work of polypi.  Then this new island will be joined later
  3996	on to the neighboring groups, and a fifth continent will stretch from
  3997	New Zealand and New Caledonia, and from thence to the Marquesas.
  3998	
  3999	One day, when I was suggesting this theory to Captain Nemo, he replied
  4000	coldly:
  4001	
  4002	"The earth does not want new continents, but new men."
  4003	
  4004	Chance had conducted the Nautilus towards the Island of
  4005	Clermont-Tonnere, one of the most curious of the group, that was
  4006	discovered in 1822 by Captain Bell of the Minerva. I could study now
  4007	the madreporal system, to which are due the islands in this ocean.
  4008	
  4009	Madrepores (which must not be mistaken for corals) have a tissue lined
  4010	with a calcareous crust, and the modifications of its structure have
  4011	induced M. Milne Edwards, my worthy master, to class them into five
  4012	sections. The animalcule that the marine polypus secretes live by
  4013	millions at the bottom of their cells. Their calcareous deposits become
  4014	rocks, reefs, and large and small islands. Here they form a ring,
  4015	surrounding a little inland lake, that communicates with the sea by
  4016	means of gaps. There they make barriers of reefs like those on the
  4017	coasts of New Caledonia and the various Pomoton islands. In other
  4018	places, like those at Reunion and at Maurice, they raise fringed reefs,
  4019	high, straight walls, near which the depth of the ocean is considerable.
  4020	
  4021	Some cable-lengths off the shores of the Island of Clermont I admired
  4022	the gigantic work accomplished by these microscopical workers. These
  4023	walls are specially the work of those madrepores known as milleporas,
  4024	porites, madrepores, and astraeas. These polypi are found particularly
  4025	in the rough beds of the sea, near the surface; and consequently it is
  4026	from the upper part that they begin their operations, in which they
  4027	bury themselves by degrees with the debris of the secretions that
  4028	support them. Such is, at least, Darwin's theory, who thus explains the
  4029	formation of the _atolls_, a superior theory (to my mind) to that given
  4030	of the foundation of the madreporical works, summits of mountains or
  4031	volcanoes, that are submerged some feet below the level of the sea.
  4032	
  4033	I could observe closely these curious walls, for perpendicularly they
  4034	were more than 300 yards deep, and our electric sheets lighted up this
  4035	calcareous matter brilliantly. Replying to a question Conseil asked me
  4036	as to the time these colossal barriers took to be raised, I astonished
  4037	him much by telling him that learned men reckoned it about the eighth
  4038	of an inch in a hundred years.
  4039	
  4040	Towards evening Clermont-Tonnerre was lost in the distance, and the
  4041	route of the Nautilus was sensibly changed. After having crossed the
  4042	tropic of Capricorn in 135° longitude, it sailed W.N.W., making
  4043	again for the tropical zone. Although the summer sun was very strong,
  4044	we did not suffer from heat, for at fifteen or twenty fathoms below the
  4045	surface, the temperature did not rise above from ten to twelve degrees.
  4046	
  4047	On 15th of December, we left to the east the bewitching group of the
  4048	Societies and the graceful Tahiti, queen of the Pacific.  I saw in the
  4049	morning, some miles to the windward, the elevated summits of the
  4050	island.  These waters furnished our table with excellent fish,
  4051	mackerel, bonitos, and some varieties of a sea-serpent.
  4052	
  4053	On the 25th of December the Nautilus sailed into the midst of the New
  4054	Hebrides, discovered by Quiros in 1606, and that Bougainville explored
  4055	in 1768, and to which Cook gave its present name in 1773.  This group
  4056	is composed principally of nine large islands, that form a band of 120
  4057	leagues N.N.S. to S.S.W., between 15° and 2° S. lat., and 164
  4058	deg. and 168° long.  We passed tolerably near to the Island of
  4059	Aurou, that at noon looked like a mass of green woods, surmounted by a
  4060	peak of great height.
  4061	
  4062	That day being Christmas Day, Ned Land seemed to regret sorely the
  4063	non-celebration of "Christmas," the family fete of which Protestants
  4064	are so fond.  I had not seen Captain Nemo for a week, when, on the
  4065	morning of the 27th, he came into the large drawing-room, always
  4066	seeming as if he had seen you five minutes before.  I was busily
  4067	tracing the route of the Nautilus on the planisphere.  The Captain came
  4068	up to me, put his finger on one spot on the chart, and said this single
  4069	word.
  4070	
  4071	"Vanikoro."
  4072	
  4073	The effect was magical!  It was the name of the islands on which La
  4074	Perouse had been lost!  I rose suddenly.
  4075	
  4076	"The Nautilus has brought us to Vanikoro?"  I asked.
  4077	
  4078	"Yes, Professor," said the Captain.
  4079	
  4080	"And I can visit the celebrated islands where the Boussole and the
  4081	Astrolabe struck?"
  4082	
  4083	"If you like, Professor."
  4084	
  4085	"When shall we be there?"
  4086	
  4087	"We are there now."
  4088	
  4089	Followed by Captain Nemo, I went up on to the platform, and greedily
  4090	scanned the horizon.
  4091	
  4092	To the N.E. two volcanic islands emerged of unequal size, surrounded by
  4093	a coral reef that measured forty miles in circumference.  We were close
  4094	to Vanikoro, really the one to which Dumont d'Urville gave the name of
  4095	Isle de la Recherche, and exactly facing the little harbour of Vanou,
  4096	situated in 16° 4' S. lat., and 164° 32' E. long.  The earth
  4097	seemed covered with verdure from the shore to the summits in the
  4098	interior, that were crowned by Mount Kapogo, 476 feet high.  The
  4099	Nautilus, having passed the outer belt of rocks by a narrow strait,
  4100	found itself among breakers where the sea was from thirty to forty
  4101	fathoms deep.  Under the verdant shade of some mangroves I perceived
  4102	some savages, who appeared greatly surprised at our approach.  In the
  4103	long black body, moving between wind and water, did they not see some
  4104	formidable cetacean that they regarded with suspicion?
  4105	
  4106	Just then Captain Nemo asked me what I knew about the wreck of La
  4107	Perouse.
  4108	
  4109	"Only what everyone knows, Captain," I replied.
  4110	
  4111	"And could you tell me what everyone knows about it?" he inquired,
  4112	ironically.
  4113	
  4114	"Easily."
  4115	
  4116	I related to him all that the last works of Dumont d'Urville had made
  4117	known--works from which the following is a brief account.
  4118	
  4119	La Perouse, and his second, Captain de Langle, were sent by Louis XVI,
  4120	in 1785, on a voyage of circumnavigation.  They embarked in the
  4121	corvettes Boussole and the Astrolabe, neither of which were again heard
  4122	of.  In 1791, the French Government, justly uneasy as to the fate of
  4123	these two sloops, manned two large merchantmen, the Recherche and the
  4124	Esperance, which left Brest the 28th of September under the command of
  4125	Bruni d'Entrecasteaux.
  4126	
  4127	Two months after, they learned from Bowen, commander of the Albemarle,
  4128	that the debris of shipwrecked vessels had been seen on the coasts of
  4129	New Georgia.  But D'Entrecasteaux, ignoring this communication--rather
  4130	uncertain, besides--directed his course towards the Admiralty Islands,
  4131	mentioned in a report of Captain Hunter's as being the place where La
  4132	Perouse was wrecked.
  4133	
  4134	They sought in vain.  The Esperance and the Recherche passed before
  4135	Vanikoro without stopping there, and, in fact, this voyage was most
  4136	disastrous, as it cost D'Entrecasteaux his life, and those of two of
  4137	his lieutenants, besides several of his crew.
  4138	
  4139	Captain Dillon, a shrewd old Pacific sailor, was the first to find
  4140	unmistakable traces of the wrecks.  On the 15th of May, 1824, his
  4141	vessel, the St. Patrick, passed close to Tikopia, one of the New
  4142	Hebrides.  There a Lascar came alongside in a canoe, sold him the
  4143	handle of a sword in silver that bore the print of characters engraved
  4144	on the hilt.  The Lascar pretended that six years before, during a stay
  4145	at Vanikoro, he had seen two Europeans that belonged to some vessels
  4146	that had run aground on the reefs some years ago.
  4147	
  4148	Dillon guessed that he meant La Perouse, whose disappearance had
  4149	troubled the whole world.  He tried to get on to Vanikoro, where,
  4150	according to the Lascar, he would find numerous debris of the wreck,
  4151	but winds and tides prevented him.
  4152	
  4153	Dillon returned to Calcutta.  There he interested the Asiatic Society
  4154	and the Indian Company in his discovery.  A vessel, to which was given
  4155	the name of the Recherche, was put at his disposal, and he set out,
  4156	23rd January, 1827, accompanied by a French agent.
  4157	
  4158	The Recherche, after touching at several points in the Pacific, cast
  4159	anchor before Vanikoro, 7th July, 1827, in that same harbour of Vanou
  4160	where the Nautilus was at this time.
  4161	
  4162	There it collected numerous relics of the wreck--iron utensils,
  4163	anchors, pulley-strops, swivel-guns, an 18 lb. shot, fragments of
  4164	astronomical instruments, a piece of crown work, and a bronze clock,
  4165	bearing this inscription--"Bazin m'a fait," the mark of the foundry of
  4166	the arsenal at Brest about 1785.  There could be no further doubt.
  4167	
  4168	Dillon, having made all inquiries, stayed in the unlucky place till
  4169	October.  Then he quitted Vanikoro, and directed his course towards New
  4170	Zealand; put into Calcutta, 7th April, 1828, and returned to France,
  4171	where he was warmly welcomed by Charles X.
  4172	
  4173	But at the same time, without knowing Dillon's movements, Dumont
  4174	d'Urville had already set out to find the scene of the wreck.  And they
  4175	had learned from a whaler that some medals and a cross of St. Louis had
  4176	been found in the hands of some savages of Louisiade and New Caledonia.
  4177	Dumont d'Urville, commander of the Astrolabe, had then sailed, and two
  4178	months after Dillon had left Vanikoro he put into Hobart Town.  There
  4179	he learned the results of Dillon's inquiries, and found that a certain
  4180	James Hobbs, second lieutenant of the Union of Calcutta, after landing
  4181	on an island situated 8° 18' S. lat., and 156° 30' E. long.,
  4182	had seen some iron bars and red stuffs used by the natives of these
  4183	parts.  Dumont d'Urville, much perplexed, and not knowing how to credit
  4184	the reports of low-class journals, decided to follow Dillon's track.
  4185	
  4186	On the 10th of February, 1828, the Astrolabe appeared off Tikopia, and
  4187	took as guide and interpreter a deserter found on the island; made his
  4188	way to Vanikoro, sighted it on the 12th inst., lay among the reefs
  4189	until the 14th, and not until the 20th did he cast anchor within the
  4190	barrier in the harbour of Vanou.
  4191	
  4192	On the 23rd, several officers went round the island and brought back
  4193	some unimportant trifles.  The natives, adopting a system of denials
  4194	and evasions, refused to take them to the unlucky place.  This
  4195	ambiguous conduct led them to believe that the natives had ill-treated
  4196	the castaways, and indeed they seemed to fear that Dumont d'Urville had
  4197	come to avenge La Perouse and his unfortunate crew.
  4198	
  4199	However, on the 26th, appeased by some presents, and understanding that
  4200	they had no reprisals to fear, they led M. Jacquireot to the scene of
  4201	the wreck.
  4202	
  4203	There, in three or four fathoms of water, between the reefs of Pacou
  4204	and Vanou, lay anchors, cannons, pigs of lead and iron, embedded in the
  4205	limy concretions.  The large boat and the whaler belonging to the
  4206	Astrolabe were sent to this place, and, not without some difficulty,
  4207	their crews hauled up an anchor weighing 1,800 lbs., a brass gun, some
  4208	pigs of iron, and two copper swivel-guns.
  4209	
  4210	Dumont d'Urville, questioning the natives, learned too that La Perouse,
  4211	after losing both his vessels on the reefs of this island, had
  4212	constructed a smaller boat, only to be lost a second time.  Where, no
  4213	one knew.
  4214	
  4215	But the French Government, fearing that Dumont d'Urville was not
  4216	acquainted with Dillon's movements, had sent the sloop Bayonnaise,
  4217	commanded by Legoarant de Tromelin, to Vanikoro, which had been
  4218	stationed on the west coast of America.  The Bayonnaise cast her anchor
  4219	before Vanikoro some months after the departure of the Astrolabe, but
  4220	found no new document; but stated that the savages had respected the
  4221	monument to La Perouse.  That is the substance of what I told Captain
  4222	Nemo.
  4223	
  4224	"So," he said, "no one knows now where the third vessel perished that
  4225	was constructed by the castaways on the island of Vanikoro?"
  4226	
  4227	"No one knows."
  4228	
  4229	Captain Nemo said nothing, but signed to me to follow him into the
  4230	large saloon.  The Nautilus sank several yards below the waves, and the
  4231	panels were opened.
  4232	
  4233	I hastened to the aperture, and under the crustations of coral, covered
  4234	with fungi, syphonules, alcyons, madrepores, through myriads of
  4235	charming fish--girelles, glyphisidri, pompherides, diacopes, and
  4236	holocentres--I recognised certain debris that the drags had not been
  4237	able to tear up--iron stirrups, anchors, cannons, bullets, capstan
  4238	fittings, the stem of a ship, all objects clearly proving the wreck of
  4239	some vessel, and now carpeted with living flowers. While I was looking
  4240	on this desolate scene, Captain Nemo said, in a sad voice:
  4241	
  4242	"Commander La Perouse set out 7th December, 1785, with his vessels La
  4243	Boussole and the Astrolabe.  He first cast anchor at Botany Bay,
  4244	visited the Friendly Isles, New Caledonia, then directed his course
  4245	towards Santa Cruz, and put into Namouka, one of the Hapai group.  Then
  4246	his vessels struck on the unknown reefs of Vanikoro.  The Boussole,
  4247	which went first, ran aground on the southerly coast.  The Astrolabe
  4248	went to its help, and ran aground too.  The first vessel was destroyed
  4249	almost immediately.  The second, stranded under the wind, resisted some
  4250	days.  The natives made the castaways welcome.  They installed
  4251	themselves in the island, and constructed a smaller boat with the
  4252	debris of the two large ones.  Some sailors stayed willingly at
  4253	Vanikoro; the others, weak and ill, set out with La Perouse.  They
  4254	directed their course towards the Solomon Islands, and there perished,
  4255	with everything, on the westerly coast of the chief island of the
  4256	group, between Capes Deception and Satisfaction."
  4257	
  4258	"How do you know that?"
  4259	
  4260	"By this, that I found on the spot where was the last wreck."
  4261	
  4262	Captain Nemo showed me a tin-plate box, stamped with the French arms,
  4263	and corroded by the salt water.  He opened it, and I saw a bundle of
  4264	papers, yellow but still readable.
  4265	
  4266	They were the instructions of the naval minister to Commander La
  4267	Perouse, annotated in the margin in Louis XVI's handwriting.
  4268	
  4269	"Ah! it is a fine death for a sailor!" said Captain Nemo, at last.  "A
  4270	coral tomb makes a quiet grave; and I trust that I and my comrades will
  4271	find no other."
  4272	
  4273	
  4274	
  4275	CHAPTER XIX
  4276	
  4277	TORRES STRAITS
  4278	
  4279	During the night of the 27th or 28th of December, the Nautilus left the
  4280	shores of Vanikoro with great speed.  Her course was south-westerly,
  4281	and in three days she had gone over the 750 leagues that separated it
  4282	from La Perouse's group and the south-east point of Papua.
  4283	
  4284	Early on the 1st of January, 1863, Conseil joined me on the platform.
  4285	
  4286	"Master, will you permit me to wish you a happy New Year?"
  4287	
  4288	"What!  Conseil; exactly as if I was at Paris in my study at the Jardin
  4289	des Plantes?  Well, I accept your good wishes, and thank you for them.
  4290	Only, I will ask you what you mean by a `Happy New Year' under our
  4291	circumstances?  Do you mean the year that will bring us to the end of
  4292	our imprisonment, or the year that sees us continue this strange
  4293	voyage?"
  4294	
  4295	"Really, I do not know how to answer, master.  We are sure to see
  4296	curious things, and for the last two months we have not had time for
  4297	dullness.  The last marvel is always the most astonishing; and, if we
  4298	continue this progression, I do not know how it will end.  It is my
  4299	opinion that we shall never again see the like.  I think then, with no
  4300	offence to master, that a happy year would be one in which we could see
  4301	everything."
  4302	
  4303	On 2nd January we had made 11,340 miles, or 5,250 French leagues, since
  4304	our starting-point in the Japan Seas.  Before the ship's head stretched
  4305	the dangerous shores of the coral sea, on the north-east coast of
  4306	Australia.  Our boat lay along some miles from the redoubtable bank on
  4307	which Cook's vessel was lost, 10th June, 1770.  The boat in which Cook
  4308	was struck on a rock, and, if it did not sink, it was owing to a piece
  4309	of coral that was broken by the shock, and fixed itself in the broken
  4310	keel.
  4311	
  4312	I had wished to visit the reef, 360 leagues long, against which the
  4313	sea, always rough, broke with great violence, with a noise like
  4314	thunder.  But just then the inclined planes drew the Nautilus down to a
  4315	great depth, and I could see nothing of the high coral walls.  I had to
  4316	content myself with the different specimens of fish brought up by the
