  4317	nets.  I remarked, among others, some germons, a species of mackerel as
  4318	large as a tunny, with bluish sides, and striped with transverse bands,
  4319	that disappear with the animal's life.
  4320	
  4321	These fish followed us in shoals, and furnished us with very delicate
  4322	food. We took also a large number of gilt-heads, about one and a half
  4323	inches long, tasting like dorys; and flying pyrapeds like submarine
  4324	swallows, which, in dark nights, light alternately the air and water
  4325	with their phosphorescent light. Among the molluscs and zoophytes, I
  4326	found in the meshes of the net several species of alcyonarians, echini,
  4327	hammers, spurs, dials, cerites, and hyalleae. The flora was represented
  4328	by beautiful floating seaweeds, laminariae, and macrocystes,
  4329	impregnated with the mucilage that transudes through their pores; and
  4330	among which I gathered an admirable Nemastoma Geliniarois, that was
  4331	classed among the natural curiosities of the museum.
  4332	
  4333	Two days after crossing the coral sea, 4th January, we sighted the
  4334	Papuan coasts.  On this occasion, Captain Nemo informed me that his
  4335	intention was to get into the Indian Ocean by the Strait of Torres.
  4336	His communication ended there.
  4337	
  4338	The Torres Straits are nearly thirty-four leagues wide; but they are
  4339	obstructed by an innumerable quantity of islands, islets, breakers, and
  4340	rocks, that make its navigation almost impracticable; so that Captain
  4341	Nemo took all needful precautions to cross them.  The Nautilus,
  4342	floating betwixt wind and water, went at a moderate pace.  Her screw,
  4343	like a cetacean's tail, beat the waves slowly.
  4344	
  4345	Profiting by this, I and my two companions went up on to the deserted
  4346	platform.  Before us was the steersman's cage, and I expected that
  4347	Captain Nemo was there directing the course of the Nautilus.  I had
  4348	before me the excellent charts of the Straits of Torres, and I
  4349	consulted them attentively.  Round the Nautilus the sea dashed
  4350	furiously.  The course of the waves, that went from south-east to
  4351	north-west at the rate of two and a half miles, broke on the coral that
  4352	showed itself here and there.
  4353	
  4354	"This is a bad sea!" remarked Ned Land.
  4355	
  4356	"Detestable indeed, and one that does not suit a boat like the
  4357	Nautilus."
  4358	
  4359	"The Captain must be very sure of his route, for I see there pieces of
  4360	coral that would do for its keel if it only touched them slightly."
  4361	
  4362	Indeed the situation was dangerous, but the Nautilus seemed to slide
  4363	like magic off these rocks.  It did not follow the routes of the
  4364	Astrolabe and the Zelee exactly, for they proved fatal to Dumont
  4365	d'Urville. It bore more northwards, coasted the Islands of Murray, and
  4366	came back to the south-west towards Cumberland Passage.  I thought it
  4367	was going to pass it by, when, going back to north-west, it went
  4368	through a large quantity of islands and islets little known, towards
  4369	the Island Sound and Canal Mauvais.
  4370	
  4371	I wondered if Captain Nemo, foolishly imprudent, would steer his vessel
  4372	into that pass where Dumont d'Urville's two corvettes touched; when,
  4373	swerving again, and cutting straight through to the west, he steered
  4374	for the Island of Gilboa.
  4375	
  4376	It was then three in the afternoon.  The tide began to recede, being
  4377	quite full.  The Nautilus approached the island, that I still saw, with
  4378	its remarkable border of screw-pines. He stood off it at about two
  4379	miles distant.  Suddenly a shock overthrew me.  The Nautilus just
  4380	touched a rock, and stayed immovable, laying lightly to port side.
  4381	
  4382	When I rose, I perceived Captain Nemo and his lieutenant on the
  4383	platform.  They were examining the situation of the vessel, and
  4384	exchanging words in their incomprehensible dialect.
  4385	
  4386	She was situated thus:  Two miles, on the starboard side, appeared
  4387	Gilboa, stretching from north to west like an immense arm.  Towards the
  4388	south and east some coral showed itself, left by the ebb.  We had run
  4389	aground, and in one of those seas where the tides are middling--a sorry
  4390	matter for the floating of the Nautilus.  However, the vessel had not
  4391	suffered, for her keel was solidly joined.  But, if she could neither
  4392	glide off nor move, she ran the risk of being for ever fastened to
  4393	these rocks, and then Captain Nemo's submarine vessel would be done for.
  4394	
  4395	I was reflecting thus, when the Captain, cool and calm, always master
  4396	of himself, approached me.
  4397	
  4398	"An accident?"  I asked.
  4399	
  4400	"No; an incident."
  4401	
  4402	"But an incident that will oblige you perhaps to become an inhabitant
  4403	of this land from which you flee?"
  4404	
  4405	Captain Nemo looked at me curiously, and made a negative gesture, as
  4406	much as to say that nothing would force him to set foot on terra firma
  4407	again.  Then he said:
  4408	
  4409	"Besides, M. Aronnax, the Nautilus is not lost; it will carry you yet
  4410	into the midst of the marvels of the ocean.  Our voyage is only begun,
  4411	and I do not wish to be deprived so soon of the honour of your company."
  4412	
  4413	"However, Captain Nemo," I replied, without noticing the ironical turn
  4414	of his phrase, "the Nautilus ran aground in open sea.  Now the tides
  4415	are not strong in the Pacific; and, if you cannot lighten the Nautilus,
  4416	I do not see how it will be reinflated."
  4417	
  4418	"The tides are not strong in the Pacific:  you are right there,
  4419	Professor; but in Torres Straits one finds still a difference of a yard
  4420	and a half between the level of high and low seas.  To-day is 4th
  4421	January, and in five days the moon will be full.  Now, I shall be very
  4422	much astonished if that satellite does not raise these masses of water
  4423	sufficiently, and render me a service that I should be indebted to her
  4424	for."
  4425	
  4426	Having said this, Captain Nemo, followed by his lieutenant, redescended
  4427	to the interior of the Nautilus.  As to the vessel, it moved not, and
  4428	was immovable, as if the coralline polypi had already walled it up with
  4429	their in destructible cement.
  4430	
  4431	"Well, sir?" said Ned Land, who came up to me after the departure of
  4432	the Captain.
  4433	
  4434	"Well, friend Ned, we will wait patiently for the tide on the 9th
  4435	instant; for it appears that the moon will have the goodness to put it
  4436	off again."
  4437	
  4438	"Really?"
  4439	
  4440	"Really."
  4441	
  4442	"And this Captain is not going to cast anchor at all since the tide
  4443	will suffice?" said Conseil, simply.
  4444	
  4445	The Canadian looked at Conseil, then shrugged his shoulders.
  4446	
  4447	"Sir, you may believe me when I tell you that this piece of iron will
  4448	navigate neither on nor under the sea again; it is only fit to be sold
  4449	for its weight.  I think, therefore, that the time has come to part
  4450	company with Captain Nemo."
  4451	
  4452	"Friend Ned, I do not despair of this stout Nautilus, as you do; and in
  4453	four days we shall know what to hold to on the Pacific tides.  Besides,
  4454	flight might be possible if we were in sight of the English or
  4455	Provencal coast; but on the Papuan shores, it is another thing; and it
  4456	will be time enough to come to that extremity if the Nautilus does not
  4457	recover itself again, which I look upon as a grave event."
  4458	
  4459	"But do they know, at least, how to act circumspectly?  There is an
  4460	island; on that island there are trees; under those trees, terrestrial
  4461	animals, bearers of cutlets and roast beef, to which I would willingly
  4462	give a trial."
  4463	
  4464	"In this, friend Ned is right," said Conseil, "and I agree with him.
  4465	Could not master obtain permission from his friend Captain Nemo to put
  4466	us on land, if only so as not to lose the habit of treading on the
  4467	solid parts of our planet?"
  4468	
  4469	"I can ask him, but he will refuse."
  4470	
  4471	"Will master risk it?" asked Conseil, "and we shall know how to rely
  4472	upon the Captain's amiability."
  4473	
  4474	To my great surprise, Captain Nemo gave me the permission I asked for,
  4475	and he gave it very agreeably, without even exacting from me a promise
  4476	to return to the vessel; but flight across New Guinea might be very
  4477	perilous, and I should not have counselled Ned Land to attempt it.
  4478	Better to be a prisoner on board the Nautilus than to fall into the
  4479	hands of the natives.
  4480	
  4481	At eight o'clock, armed with guns and hatchets, we got off the
  4482	Nautilus.  The sea was pretty calm; a slight breeze blew on land.
  4483	Conseil and I rowing, we sped along quickly, and Ned steered in the
  4484	straight passage that the breakers left between them.  The boat was
  4485	well handled, and moved rapidly.
  4486	
  4487	Ned Land could not restrain his joy.  He was like a prisoner that had
  4488	escaped from prison, and knew not that it was necessary to re-enter it.
  4489	
  4490	"Meat!  We are going to eat some meat; and what meat!" he replied.
  4491	"Real game! no, bread, indeed."
  4492	
  4493	"I do not say that fish is not good; we must not abuse it; but a piece
  4494	of fresh venison, grilled on live coals, will agreeably vary our
  4495	ordinary course."
  4496	
  4497	"Glutton!" said Conseil, "he makes my mouth water."
  4498	
  4499	"It remains to be seen," I said, "if these forests are full of game,
  4500	and if the game is not such as will hunt the hunter himself."
  4501	
  4502	"Well said, M. Aronnax," replied the Canadian, whose teeth seemed
  4503	sharpened like the edge of a hatchet; "but I will eat tiger--loin of
  4504	tiger--if there is no other quadruped on this island."
  4505	
  4506	"Friend Ned is uneasy about it," said Conseil.
  4507	
  4508	"Whatever it may be," continued Ned Land, "every animal with four paws
  4509	without feathers, or with two paws without feathers, will be saluted by
  4510	my first shot."
  4511	
  4512	"Very well!  Master Land's imprudences are beginning."
  4513	
  4514	"Never fear, M. Aronnax," replied the Canadian; "I do not want
  4515	twenty-five minutes to offer you a dish, of my sort."
  4516	
  4517	At half-past eight the Nautilus boat ran softly aground on a heavy
  4518	sand, after having happily passed the coral reef that surrounds the
  4519	Island of Gilboa.
  4520	
  4521	
  4522	
  4523	CHAPTER XX
  4524	
  4525	A FEW DAYS ON LAND
  4526	
  4527	I was much impressed on touching land.  Ned Land tried the soil with
  4528	his feet, as if to take possession of it.  However, it was only two
  4529	months before that we had become, according to Captain Nemo,
  4530	"passengers on board the Nautilus," but, in reality, prisoners of its
  4531	commander.
  4532	
  4533	In a few minutes we were within musket-shot of the coast.  The whole
  4534	horizon was hidden behind a beautiful curtain of forests.  Enormous
  4535	trees, the trunks of which attained a height of 200 feet, were tied to
  4536	each other by garlands of bindweed, real natural hammocks, which a
  4537	light breeze rocked.  They were mimosas, figs, hibisci, and palm trees,
  4538	mingled together in profusion; and under the shelter of their verdant
  4539	vault grew orchids, leguminous plants, and ferns.
  4540	
  4541	But, without noticing all these beautiful specimens of Papuan flora,
  4542	the Canadian abandoned the agreeable for the useful.  He discovered a
  4543	coco-tree, beat down some of the fruit, broke them, and we drunk the
  4544	milk and ate the nut with a satisfaction that protested against the
  4545	ordinary food on the Nautilus.
  4546	
  4547	"Excellent!" said Ned Land.
  4548	
  4549	"Exquisite!" replied Conseil.
  4550	
  4551	"And I do not think," said the Canadian, "that he would object to our
  4552	introducing a cargo of coco-nuts on board."
  4553	
  4554	"I do not think he would, but he would not taste them."
  4555	
  4556	"So much the worse for him," said Conseil.
  4557	
  4558	"And so much the better for us," replied Ned Land.  "There will be more
  4559	for us."
  4560	
  4561	"One word only, Master Land," I said to the harpooner, who was
  4562	beginning to ravage another coco-nut tree.  "Coco-nuts are good things,
  4563	but before filling the canoe with them it would be wise to reconnoitre
  4564	and see if the island does not produce some substance not less useful.
  4565	Fresh vegetables would be welcome on board the Nautilus."
  4566	
  4567	"Master is right," replied Conseil; "and I propose to reserve three
  4568	places in our vessel, one for fruits, the other for vegetables, and the
  4569	third for the venison, of which I have not yet seen the smallest
  4570	specimen."
  4571	
  4572	"Conseil, we must not despair," said the Canadian.
  4573	
  4574	"Let us continue," I returned, "and lie in wait.  Although the island
  4575	seems uninhabited, it might still contain some individuals that would
  4576	be less hard than we on the nature of game."
  4577	
  4578	"Ho! ho!" said Ned Land, moving his jaws significantly.
  4579	
  4580	"Well, Ned!" said Conseil.
  4581	
  4582	"My word!" returned the Canadian, "I begin to understand the charms of
  4583	anthropophagy."
  4584	
  4585	"Ned!  Ned! what are you saying?  You, a man-eater? I should not feel
  4586	safe with you, especially as I share your cabin.  I might perhaps wake
  4587	one day to find myself half devoured."
  4588	
  4589	"Friend Conseil, I like you much, but not enough to eat you
  4590	unnecessarily."
  4591	
  4592	"I would not trust you," replied Conseil.  "But enough.  We must
  4593	absolutely bring down some game to satisfy this cannibal, or else one
  4594	of these fine mornings, master will find only pieces of his servant to
  4595	serve him."
  4596	
  4597	While we were talking thus, we were penetrating the sombre arches of
  4598	the forest, and for two hours we surveyed it in all directions.
  4599	
  4600	Chance rewarded our search for eatable vegetables, and one of the most
  4601	useful products of the tropical zones furnished us with precious food
  4602	that we missed on board.  I would speak of the bread-fruit tree, very
  4603	abundant in the island of Gilboa; and I remarked chiefly the variety
  4604	destitute of seeds, which bears in Malaya the name of "rima."
  4605	
  4606	Ned Land knew these fruits well.  He had already eaten many during his
  4607	numerous voyages, and he knew how to prepare the eatable substance.
  4608	Moreover, the sight of them excited him, and he could contain himself
  4609	no longer.
  4610	
  4611	"Master," he said, "I shall die if I do not taste a little of this
  4612	bread-fruit pie."
  4613	
  4614	"Taste it, friend Ned--taste it as you want.  We are here to make
  4615	experiments--make them."
  4616	
  4617	"It won't take long," said the Canadian.
  4618	
  4619	And, provided with a lentil, he lighted a fire of dead wood that
  4620	crackled joyously.  During this time, Conseil and I chose the best
  4621	fruits of the bread-fruit. Some had not then attained a sufficient
  4622	degree of maturity; and their thick skin covered a white but rather
  4623	fibrous pulp.  Others, the greater number yellow and gelatinous, waited
  4624	only to be picked.
  4625	
  4626	These fruits enclosed no kernel.  Conseil brought a dozen to Ned Land,
  4627	who placed them on a coal fire, after having cut them in thick slices,
  4628	and while doing this repeating:
  4629	
  4630	"You will see, master, how good this bread is.  More so when one has
  4631	been deprived of it so long.  It is not even bread," added he, "but a
  4632	delicate pastry.  You have eaten none, master?"
  4633	
  4634	"No, Ned."
  4635	
  4636	"Very well, prepare yourself for a juicy thing.  If you do not come for
  4637	more, I am no longer the king of harpooners."
  4638	
  4639	After some minutes, the part of the fruits that was exposed to the fire
  4640	was completely roasted.  The interior looked like a white pasty, a sort
  4641	of soft crumb, the flavour of which was like that of an artichoke.
  4642	
  4643	It must be confessed this bread was excellent, and I ate of it with
  4644	great relish.
  4645	
  4646	"What time is it now?" asked the Canadian.
  4647	
  4648	"Two o'clock at least," replied Conseil.
  4649	
  4650	"How time flies on firm ground!" sighed Ned Land.
  4651	
  4652	"Let us be off," replied Conseil.
  4653	
  4654	We returned through the forest, and completed our collection by a raid
  4655	upon the cabbage-palms, that we gathered from the tops of the trees,
  4656	little beans that I recognised as the "abrou" of the Malays, and yams
  4657	of a superior quality.
  4658	
  4659	We were loaded when we reached the boat.  But Ned Land did not find his
  4660	provisions sufficient.  Fate, however, favoured us.  Just as we were
  4661	pushing off, he perceived several trees, from twenty-five to thirty
  4662	feet high, a species of palm-tree.
  4663	
  4664	At last, at five o'clock in the evening, loaded with our riches, we
  4665	quitted the shore, and half an hour after we hailed the Nautilus.  No
  4666	one appeared on our arrival.  The enormous iron-plated cylinder seemed
  4667	deserted.  The provisions embarked, I descended to my chamber, and
  4668	after supper slept soundly.
  4669	
  4670	The next day, 6th January, nothing new on board.  Not a sound inside,
  4671	not a sign of life.  The boat rested along the edge, in the same place
  4672	in which we had left it.  We resolved to return to the island.  Ned
  4673	Land hoped to be more fortunate than on the day before with regard to
  4674	the hunt, and wished to visit another part of the forest.
  4675	
  4676	At dawn we set off.  The boat, carried on by the waves that flowed to
  4677	shore, reached the island in a few minutes.
  4678	
  4679	We landed, and, thinking that it was better to give in to the Canadian,
  4680	we followed Ned Land, whose long limbs threatened to distance us.  He
  4681	wound up the coast towards the west:  then, fording some torrents, he
  4682	gained the high plain that was bordered with admirable forests.  Some
  4683	kingfishers were rambling along the water-courses, but they would not
  4684	let themselves be approached.  Their circumspection proved to me that
  4685	these birds knew what to expect from bipeds of our species, and I
  4686	concluded that, if the island was not inhabited, at least human beings
  4687	occasionally frequented it.
  4688	
  4689	After crossing a rather large prairie, we arrived at the skirts of a
  4690	little wood that was enlivened by the songs and flight of a large
  4691	number of birds.
  4692	
  4693	"There are only birds," said Conseil.
  4694	
  4695	"But they are eatable," replied the harpooner.
  4696	
  4697	"I do not agree with you, friend Ned, for I see only parrots there."
  4698	
  4699	"Friend Conseil," said Ned, gravely, "the parrot is like pheasant to
  4700	those who have nothing else."
  4701	
  4702	"And," I added, "this bird, suitably prepared, is worth knife and fork."
  4703	
  4704	Indeed, under the thick foliage of this wood, a world of parrots were
  4705	flying from branch to branch, only needing a careful education to speak
  4706	the human language.  For the moment, they were chattering with parrots
  4707	of all colours, and grave cockatoos, who seemed to meditate upon some
  4708	philosophical problem, whilst brilliant red lories passed like a piece
  4709	of bunting carried away by the breeze, papuans, with the finest azure
  4710	colours, and in all a variety of winged things most charming to behold,
  4711	but few eatable.
  4712	
  4713	However, a bird peculiar to these lands, and which has never passed the
  4714	limits of the Arrow and Papuan islands, was wanting in this collection.
  4715	But fortune reserved it for me before long.
  4716	
  4717	After passing through a moderately thick copse, we found a plain
  4718	obstructed with bushes.  I saw then those magnificent birds, the
  4719	disposition of whose long feathers obliges them to fly against the
  4720	wind.  Their undulating flight, graceful aerial curves, and the shading
  4721	of their colours, attracted and charmed one's looks.  I had no trouble
  4722	in recognising them.
  4723	
  4724	"Birds of paradise!"  I exclaimed.
  4725	
  4726	The Malays, who carry on a great trade in these birds with the Chinese,
  4727	have several means that we could not employ for taking them.  Sometimes
  4728	they put snares on the top of high trees that the birds of paradise
  4729	prefer to frequent.  Sometimes they catch them with a viscous birdlime
  4730	that paralyses their movements.  They even go so far as to poison the
  4731	fountains that the birds generally drink from.  But we were obliged to
  4732	fire at them during flight, which gave us few chances to bring them
  4733	down; and, indeed, we vainly exhausted one half our ammunition.
  4734	
  4735	About eleven o'clock in the morning, the first range of mountains that
  4736	form the centre of the island was traversed, and we had killed nothing.
  4737	Hunger drove us on.  The hunters had relied on the products of the
  4738	chase, and they were wrong.  Happily Conseil, to his great surprise,
  4739	made a double shot and secured breakfast.  He brought down a white
  4740	pigeon and a wood-pigeon, which, cleverly plucked and suspended from a
  4741	skewer, was roasted before a red fire of dead wood.  While these
  4742	interesting birds were cooking, Ned prepared the fruit of the
  4743	bread-tree. Then the wood-pigeons were devoured to the bones, and
  4744	declared excellent.  The nutmeg, with which they are in the habit of
  4745	stuffing their crops, flavours their flesh and renders it delicious
  4746	eating.
  4747	
  4748	"Now, Ned, what do you miss now?"
  4749	
  4750	"Some four-footed game, M. Aronnax.  All these pigeons are only
  4751	side-dishes and trifles; and until I have killed an animal with cutlets
  4752	I shall not be content."
  4753	
  4754	"Nor I, Ned, if I do not catch a bird of paradise."
  4755	
  4756	"Let us continue hunting," replied Conseil.  "Let us go towards the
  4757	sea.  We have arrived at the first declivities of the mountains, and I
  4758	think we had better regain the region of forests."
  4759	
  4760	That was sensible advice, and was followed out.  After walking for one
  4761	hour we had attained a forest of sago-trees. Some inoffensive serpents
  4762	glided away from us.  The birds of paradise fled at our approach, and
  4763	truly I despaired of getting near one when Conseil, who was walking in
  4764	front, suddenly bent down, uttered a triumphal cry, and came back to me
  4765	bringing a magnificent specimen.
  4766	
  4767	"Ah! bravo, Conseil!"
  4768	
  4769	"Master is very good."
  4770	
  4771	"No, my boy; you have made an excellent stroke.  Take one of these
  4772	living birds, and carry it in your hand."
  4773	
  4774	"If master will examine it, he will see that I have not deserved great
  4775	merit."
  4776	
  4777	"Why, Conseil?"
  4778	
  4779	"Because this bird is as drunk as a quail."
  4780	
  4781	"Drunk!"
  4782	
  4783	"Yes, sir; drunk with the nutmegs that it devoured under the
  4784	nutmeg-tree, under which I found it.  See, friend Ned, see the
  4785	monstrous effects of intemperance!"
  4786	
  4787	"By Jove!" exclaimed the Canadian, "because I have drunk gin for two
  4788	months, you must needs reproach me!"
  4789	
  4790	However, I examined the curious bird.  Conseil was right.  The bird,
  4791	drunk with the juice, was quite powerless.  It could not fly; it could
  4792	hardly walk.
  4793	
  4794	This bird belonged to the most beautiful of the eight species that are
  4795	found in Papua and in the neighbouring islands.  It was the "large
  4796	emerald bird, the most rare kind." It measured three feet in length.
  4797	Its head was comparatively small, its eyes placed near the opening of
  4798	the beak, and also small.  But the shades of colour were beautiful,
  4799	having a yellow beak, brown feet and claws, nut-coloured wings with
  4800	purple tips, pale yellow at the back of the neck and head, and emerald
  4801	colour at the throat, chestnut on the breast and belly.  Two horned,
  4802	downy nets rose from below the tail, that prolonged the long light
  4803	feathers of admirable fineness, and they completed the whole of this
  4804	marvellous bird, that the natives have poetically named the "bird of
  4805	the sun."
  4806	
  4807	But if my wishes were satisfied by the possession of the bird of
  4808	paradise, the Canadian's were not yet.  Happily, about two o'clock, Ned
  4809	Land brought down a magnificent hog; from the brood of those the
  4810	natives call "bari-outang." The animal came in time for us to procure
  4811	real quadruped meat, and he was well received.  Ned Land was very proud
  4812	of his shot.  The hog, hit by the electric ball, fell stone dead.  The
  4813	Canadian skinned and cleaned it properly, after having taken half a
  4814	dozen cutlets, destined to furnish us with a grilled repast in the
  4815	evening.  Then the hunt was resumed, which was still more marked by Ned
  4816	and Conseil's exploits.
  4817	
  4818	Indeed, the two friends, beating the bushes, roused a herd of kangaroos
  4819	that fled and bounded along on their elastic paws.  But these animals
  4820	did not take to flight so rapidly but what the electric capsule could
  4821	stop their course.
  4822	
  4823	"Ah, Professor!" cried Ned Land, who was carried away by the delights
  4824	of the chase, "what excellent game, and stewed, too!  What a supply for
  4825	the Nautilus!  Two! three! five down!  And to think that we shall eat
  4826	that flesh, and that the idiots on board shall not have a crumb!"
  4827	
  4828	I think that, in the excess of his joy, the Canadian, if he had not
  4829	talked so much, would have killed them all.  But he contented himself
  4830	with a single dozen of these interesting marsupians.  These animals
  4831	were small.  They were a species of those "kangaroo rabbits" that live
  4832	habitually in the hollows of trees, and whose speed is extreme; but
  4833	they are moderately fat, and furnish, at least, estimable food.  We
  4834	were very satisfied with the results of the hunt.  Happy Ned proposed
  4835	to return to this enchanting island the next day, for he wished to
  4836	depopulate it of all the eatable quadrupeds.  But he had reckoned
  4837	without his host.
  4838	
  4839	At six o'clock in the evening we had regained the shore; our boat was
  4840	moored to the usual place.  The Nautilus, like a long rock, emerged
  4841	from the waves two miles from the beach.  Ned Land, without waiting,
  4842	occupied himself about the important dinner business.  He understood
  4843	all about cooking well.  The "bari-outang," grilled on the coals, soon
  4844	scented the air with a delicious odour.
  4845	
  4846	Indeed, the dinner was excellent.  Two wood-pigeons completed this
  4847	extraordinary menu.  The sago pasty, the artocarpus bread, some
  4848	mangoes, half a dozen pineapples, and the liquor fermented from some
  4849	coco-nuts, overjoyed us.  I even think that my worthy companions' ideas
  4850	had not all the plainness desirable.
  4851	
  4852	"Suppose we do not return to the Nautilus this evening?" said Conseil.
  4853	
  4854	"Suppose we never return?" added Ned Land.
  4855	
  4856	Just then a stone fell at our feet and cut short the harpooner's
  4857	proposition.
  4858	
  4859	
  4860	
  4861	CHAPTER XXI
  4862	
  4863	CAPTAIN NEMO'S THUNDERBOLT
  4864	
  4865	We looked at the edge of the forest without rising, my hand stopping in
  4866	the action of putting it to my mouth, Ned Land's completing its office.
  4867	
  4868	"Stones do not fall from the sky," remarked Conseil, "or they would
  4869	merit the name aerolites."
  4870	
  4871	A second stone, carefully aimed, that made a savoury pigeon's leg fall
  4872	from Conseil's hand, gave still more weight to his observation.  We all
  4873	three arose, shouldered our guns, and were ready to reply to any attack.
  4874	
  4875	"Are they apes?" cried Ned Land.
  4876	
  4877	"Very nearly--they are savages."
  4878	
  4879	"To the boat!"  I said, hurrying to the sea.
  4880	
  4881	It was indeed necessary to beat a retreat, for about twenty natives
  4882	armed with bows and slings appeared on the skirts of a copse that
  4883	masked the horizon to the right, hardly a hundred steps from us.
  4884	
  4885	Our boat was moored about sixty feet from us.  The savages approached
  4886	us, not running, but making hostile demonstrations.  Stones and arrows
  4887	fell thickly.
  4888	
  4889	Ned Land had not wished to leave his provisions; and, in spite of his
  4890	imminent danger, his pig on one side and kangaroos on the other, he
  4891	went tolerably fast.  In two minutes we were on the shore.  To load the
  4892	boat with provisions and arms, to push it out to sea, and ship the
  4893	oars, was the work of an instant.  We had not gone two cable-lengths,
  4894	when a hundred savages, howling and gesticulating, entered the water up
  4895	to their waists.  I watched to see if their apparition would attract
  4896	some men from the Nautilus on to the platform.  But no.  The enormous
  4897	machine, lying off, was absolutely deserted.
  4898	
  4899	Twenty minutes later we were on board.  The panels were open.  After
  4900	making the boat fast, we entered into the interior of the Nautilus.
  4901	
  4902	I descended to the drawing-room, from whence I heard some chords.
  4903	Captain Nemo was there, bending over his organ, and plunged in a
  4904	musical ecstasy.
  4905	
  4906	"Captain!"
  4907	
  4908	He did not hear me.
  4909	
  4910	"Captain!"  I said, touching his hand.
  4911	
  4912	He shuddered, and, turning round, said, "Ah! it is you, Professor?
  4913	Well, have you had a good hunt, have you botanised successfully?"
  4914	
  4915	"Yes Captain; but we have unfortunately brought a troop of bipeds,
  4916	whose vicinity troubles me."
  4917	
  4918	"What bipeds?"
  4919	
  4920	"Savages."
  4921	
  4922	"Savages!" he echoed, ironically.  "So you are astonished, Professor,
  4923	at having set foot on a strange land and finding savages?  Savages!
  4924	where are there not any?  Besides, are they worse than others, these
  4925	whom you call savages?"
  4926	
  4927	"But Captain----"
  4928	
  4929	"How many have you counted?"
  4930	
  4931	"A hundred at least."
  4932	
  4933	"M. Aronnax," replied Captain Nemo, placing his fingers on the organ
  4934	stops, "when all the natives of Papua are assembled on this shore, the
  4935	Nautilus will have nothing to fear from their attacks."
  4936	
  4937	The Captain's fingers were then running over the keys of the
  4938	instrument, and I remarked that he touched only the black keys, which
  4939	gave his melodies an essentially Scotch character.  Soon he had
  4940	forgotten my presence, and had plunged into a reverie that I did not
  4941	disturb.  I went up again on to the platform: night had already fallen;
  4942	for, in this low latitude, the sun sets rapidly and without twilight.
  4943	I could only see the island indistinctly; but the numerous fires,
  4944	lighted on the beach, showed that the natives did not think of leaving
  4945	it.  I was alone for several hours, sometimes thinking of the
  4946	natives--but without any dread of them, for the imperturbable
  4947	confidence of the Captain was catching--sometimes forgetting them to
  4948	admire the splendours of the night in the tropics.  My remembrances
  4949	went to France in the train of those zodiacal stars that would shine in
  4950	some hours' time.  The moon shone in the midst of the constellations of
  4951	the zenith.
  4952	
  4953	The night slipped away without any mischance, the islanders frightened
  4954	no doubt at the sight of a monster aground in the bay.  The panels were
  4955	open, and would have offered an easy access to the interior of the
  4956	Nautilus.
  4957	
  4958	At six o'clock in the morning of the 8th January I went up on to the
  4959	platform.  The dawn was breaking.  The island soon showed itself
  4960	through the dissipating fogs, first the shore, then the summits.
  4961	
  4962	The natives were there, more numerous than on the day before--five or
  4963	six hundred perhaps--some of them, profiting by the low water, had come
  4964	on to the coral, at less than two cable-lengths from the Nautilus.  I
  4965	distinguished them easily; they were true Papuans, with athletic
  4966	figures, men of good race, large high foreheads, large, but not broad
  4967	and flat, and white teeth.  Their woolly hair, with a reddish tinge,
  4968	showed off on their black shining bodies like those of the Nubians.
  4969	From the lobes of their ears, cut and distended, hung chaplets of
  4970	bones.  Most of these savages were naked.  Amongst them, I remarked
  4971	some women, dressed from the hips to knees in quite a crinoline of
  4972	herbs, that sustained a vegetable waistband.  Some chiefs had
  4973	ornamented their necks with a crescent and collars of glass beads, red
  4974	and white; nearly all were armed with bows, arrows, and shields and
  4975	carried on their shoulders a sort of net containing those round stones
  4976	which they cast from their slings with great skill.  One of these
  4977	chiefs, rather near to the Nautilus, examined it attentively.  He was,
  4978	perhaps, a "mado" of high rank, for he was draped in a mat of
  4979	banana-leaves, notched round the edges, and set off with brilliant
  4980	colours.
  4981	
  4982	I could easily have knocked down this native, who was within a short
  4983	length; but I thought that it was better to wait for real hostile
  4984	demonstrations.  Between Europeans and savages, it is proper for the
  4985	Europeans to parry sharply, not to attack.
  4986	
  4987	During low water the natives roamed about near the Nautilus, but were
  4988	not troublesome; I heard them frequently repeat the word "Assai," and
  4989	by their gestures I understood that they invited me to go on land, an
  4990	invitation that I declined.
  4991	
  4992	So that, on that day, the boat did not push off, to the great
  4993	displeasure of Master Land, who could not complete his provisions.
  4994	
  4995	This adroit Canadian employed his time in preparing the viands and meat
  4996	that he had brought off the island.  As for the savages, they returned
  4997	to the shore about eleven o'clock in the morning, as soon as the coral
  4998	tops began to disappear under the rising tide; but I saw their numbers
  4999	had increased considerably on the shore.  Probably they came from the
  5000	neighbouring islands, or very likely from Papua.  However, I had not
  5001	seen a single native canoe.  Having nothing better to do, I thought of
  5002	dragging these beautiful limpid waters, under which I saw a profusion
  5003	of shells, zoophytes, and marine plants.  Moreover, it was the last day
  5004	that the Nautilus would pass in these parts, if it float in open sea
  5005	the next day, according to Captain Nemo's promise.
  5006	
  5007	I therefore called Conseil, who brought me a little light drag, very
  5008	like those for the oyster fishery.  Now to work!  For two hours we
  5009	fished unceasingly, but without bringing up any rarities.  The drag was
  5010	filled with midas-ears, harps, melames, and particularly the most
  5011	beautiful hammers I have ever seen.  We also brought up some sea-slugs,
  5012	pearl-oysters, and a dozen little turtles that were reserved for the
  5013	pantry on board.
  5014	
  5015	But just when I expected it least, I put my hand on a wonder, I might
  5016	say a natural deformity, very rarely met with.  Conseil was just
  5017	dragging, and his net came up filled with divers ordinary shells, when,
  5018	all at once, he saw me plunge my arm quickly into the net, to draw out
  5019	a shell, and heard me utter a cry.
  5020	
  5021	"What is the matter, sir?" he asked in surprise.  "Has master been
  5022	bitten?"
  5023	
  5024	"No, my boy; but I would willingly have given a finger for my
  5025	discovery."
  5026	
  5027	"What discovery?"
  5028	
  5029	"This shell," I said, holding up the object of my triumph.
  5030	
  5031	"It is simply an olive porphyry, genus olive, order of the
  5032	pectinibranchidae, class of gasteropods, sub-class mollusca."
  5033	
  5034	"Yes, Conseil; but, instead of being rolled from right to left, this
  5035	olive turns from left to right."
  5036	
  5037	"Is it possible?"
  5038	
  5039	"Yes, my boy; it is a left shell."
  5040	
  5041	Shells are all right-handed, with rare exceptions; and, when by chance
  5042	their spiral is left, amateurs are ready to pay their weight in gold.
  5043	
  5044	Conseil and I were absorbed in the contemplation of our treasure, and I
  5045	was promising myself to enrich the museum with it, when a stone
  5046	unfortunately thrown by a native struck against, and broke, the
  5047	precious object in Conseil's hand.  I uttered a cry of despair!
  5048	Conseil took up his gun, and aimed at a savage who was poising his
  5049	sling at ten yards from him.  I would have stopped him, but his blow
  5050	took effect and broke the bracelet of amulets which encircled the arm
  5051	of the savage.
  5052	
  5053	"Conseil!" cried I. "Conseil!"
  5054	
  5055	"Well, sir! do you not see that the cannibal has commenced the attack?"
  5056	
  5057	"A shell is not worth the life of a man," said I.
  5058	
  5059	"Ah! the scoundrel!" cried Conseil; "I would rather he had broken my
  5060	shoulder!"
  5061	
  5062	Conseil was in earnest, but I was not of his opinion.  However, the
  5063	situation had changed some minutes before, and we had not perceived.  A
  5064	score of canoes surrounded the Nautilus.  These canoes, scooped out of
  5065	the trunk of a tree, long, narrow, well adapted for speed, were
  5066	balanced by means of a long bamboo pole, which floated on the water.
  5067	They were managed by skilful, half-naked paddlers, and I watched their
  5068	advance with some uneasiness.  It was evident that these Papuans had
  5069	already had dealings with the Europeans and knew their ships.  But this
  5070	long iron cylinder anchored in the bay, without masts or chimneys, what
  5071	could they think of it?  Nothing good, for at first they kept at a
  5072	respectful distance.  However, seeing it motionless, by degrees they
  5073	took courage, and sought to familiarise themselves with it.  Now this
  5074	familiarity was precisely what it was necessary to avoid.  Our arms,
  5075	which were noiseless, could only produce a moderate effect on the
  5076	savages, who have little respect for aught but blustering things.  The
  5077	thunderbolt without the reverberations of thunder would frighten man
  5078	but little, though the danger lies in the lightning, not in the noise.
  5079	
  5080	At this moment the canoes approached the Nautilus, and a shower of
  5081	arrows alighted on her.
  5082	
  5083	I went down to the saloon, but found no one there.  I ventured to knock
  5084	at the door that opened into the Captain's room.  "Come in," was the
  5085	answer.
  5086	
  5087	I entered, and found Captain Nemo deep in algebraical calculations of
  5088	_x_ and other quantities.
  5089	
  5090	"I am disturbing you," said I, for courtesy's sake.
  5091	
  5092	"That is true, M. Aronnax," replied the Captain; "but I think you have
  5093	serious reasons for wishing to see me?"
  5094	
  5095	"Very grave ones; the natives are surrounding us in their canoes, and
  5096	in a few minutes we shall certainly be attacked by many hundreds of
  5097	savages."
  5098	
  5099	"Ah!" said Captain Nemo quietly, "they are come with their canoes?"
  5100	
  5101	"Yes, sir."
  5102	
  5103	"Well, sir, we must close the hatches."
  5104	
  5105	"Exactly, and I came to say to you----"
  5106	
  5107	"Nothing can be more simple," said Captain Nemo.  And, pressing an
  5108	electric button, he transmitted an order to the ship's crew.
  5109	
  5110	"It is all done, sir," said he, after some moments.  "The pinnace is
  5111	ready, and the hatches are closed.  You do not fear, I imagine, that
  5112	these gentlemen could stave in walls on which the balls of your frigate
  5113	have had no effect?"
  5114	
  5115	"No, Captain; but a danger still exists."
  5116	
  5117	"What is that, sir?"
  5118	
  5119	"It is that to-morrow, at about this hour, we must open the hatches to
  5120	renew the air of the Nautilus.  Now, if, at this moment, the Papuans
  5121	should occupy the platform, I do not see how you could prevent them
  5122	from entering."
  5123	
  5124	"Then, sir, you suppose that they will board us?"
  5125	
  5126	"I am certain of it."
  5127	
  5128	"Well, sir, let them come.  I see no reason for hindering them.  After
  5129	all, these Papuans are poor creatures, and I am unwilling that my visit
  5130	to the island should cost the life of a single one of these wretches."
  5131	
  5132	Upon that I was going away; But Captain Nemo detained me, and asked me
  5133	to sit down by him.  He questioned me with interest about our
  5134	excursions on shore, and our hunting; and seemed not to understand the
  5135	craving for meat that possessed the Canadian.  Then the conversation
  5136	turned on various subjects, and, without being more communicative,
  5137	Captain Nemo showed himself more amiable.
  5138	
  5139	Amongst other things, we happened to speak of the situation of the
  5140	Nautilus, run aground in exactly the same spot in this strait where
  5141	Dumont d'Urville was nearly lost.  Apropos of this:
  5142	
  5143	"This D'Urville was one of your great sailors," said the Captain to me,
  5144	"one of your most intelligent navigators.  He is the Captain Cook of
  5145	you Frenchmen.  Unfortunate man of science, after having braved the
  5146	icebergs of the South Pole, the coral reefs of Oceania, the cannibals
  5147	of the Pacific, to perish miserably in a railway train!  If this
  5148	energetic man could have reflected during the last moments of his life,
  5149	what must have been uppermost in his last thoughts, do you suppose?"
  5150	
  5151	So speaking, Captain Nemo seemed moved, and his emotion gave me a
  5152	better opinion of him.  Then, chart in hand, we reviewed the travels of
  5153	the French navigator, his voyages of circumnavigation, his double
  5154	detention at the South Pole, which led to the discovery of Adelaide and
  5155	Louis Philippe, and fixing the hydrographical bearings of the principal
  5156	islands of Oceania.
  5157	
  5158	"That which your D'Urville has done on the surface of the seas," said
  5159	Captain Nemo, "that have I done under them, and more easily, more
  5160	completely than he.  The Astrolabe and the Zelee, incessantly tossed
  5161	about by the hurricane, could not be worth the Nautilus, quiet
  5162	repository of labour that she is, truly motionless in the midst of the
  5163	waters.
  5164	
  5165	"To-morrow," added the Captain, rising, "to-morrow, at twenty minutes
  5166	to three p.m., the Nautilus shall float, and leave the Strait of Torres
  5167	uninjured."
  5168	
  5169	Having curtly pronounced these words, Captain Nemo bowed slightly.
  5170	This was to dismiss me, and I went back to my room.
  5171	
  5172	There I found Conseil, who wished to know the result of my interview
  5173	with the Captain.
  5174	
  5175	"My boy," said I, "when I feigned to believe that his Nautilus was
  5176	threatened by the natives of Papua, the Captain answered me very
  5177	sarcastically.  I have but one thing to say to you: Have confidence in
  5178	him, and go to sleep in peace."
  5179	
  5180	"Have you no need of my services, sir?"
  5181	
  5182	"No, my friend.  What is Ned Land doing?"
  5183	
  5184	"If you will excuse me, sir," answered Conseil, "friend Ned is busy
  5185	making a kangaroo-pie which will be a marvel."
  5186	
  5187	I remained alone and went to bed, but slept indifferently.  I heard the
  5188	noise of the savages, who stamped on the platform, uttering deafening
  5189	cries.  The night passed thus, without disturbing the ordinary repose
  5190	of the crew.  The presence of these cannibals affected them no more
  5191	than the soldiers of a masked battery care for the ants that crawl over
  5192	its front.
  5193	
  5194	At six in the morning I rose.  The hatches had not been opened.  The
  5195	inner air was not renewed, but the reservoirs, filled ready for any
  5196	emergency, were now resorted to, and discharged several cubic feet of
  5197	oxygen into the exhausted atmosphere of the Nautilus.
  5198	
  5199	I worked in my room till noon, without having seen Captain Nemo, even
  5200	for an instant.  On board no preparations for departure were visible.
  5201	
  5202	I waited still some time, then went into the large saloon.  The clock
  5203	marked half-past two.  In ten minutes it would be high-tide: and, if
  5204	Captain Nemo had not made a rash promise, the Nautilus would be
  5205	immediately detached.  If not, many months would pass ere she could
  5206	leave her bed of coral.
  5207	
  5208	However, some warning vibrations began to be felt in the vessel.  I
  5209	heard the keel grating against the rough calcareous bottom of the coral
  5210	reef.
  5211	
  5212	At five-and-twenty minutes to three, Captain Nemo appeared in the
  5213	saloon.
  5214	
  5215	"We are going to start," said he.
  5216	
  5217	"Ah!" replied I.
  5218	
  5219	"I have given the order to open the hatches."
  5220	
  5221	"And the Papuans?"
  5222	
  5223	"The Papuans?" answered Captain Nemo, slightly shrugging his shoulders.
  5224	
  5225	"Will they not come inside the Nautilus?"
  5226	
  5227	"How?"
  5228	
  5229	"Only by leaping over the hatches you have opened."
  5230	
  5231	"M. Aronnax," quietly answered Captain Nemo, "they will not enter the
  5232	hatches of the Nautilus in that way, even if they were open."
  5233	
  5234	I looked at the Captain.
  5235	
  5236	"You do not understand?" said he.
  5237	
  5238	"Hardly."
  5239	
  5240	"Well, come and you will see."
  5241	
  5242	I directed my steps towards the central staircase.  There Ned Land and
  5243	Conseil were slyly watching some of the ship's crew, who were opening
  5244	the hatches, while cries of rage and fearful vociferations resounded
  5245	outside.
  5246	
  5247	The port lids were pulled down outside.  Twenty horrible faces
  5248	appeared.  But the first native who placed his hand on the stair-rail,
  5249	struck from behind by some invisible force, I know not what, fled,
  5250	uttering the most fearful cries and making the wildest contortions.
  5251	
  5252	Ten of his companions followed him.  They met with the same fate.
  5253	
  5254	Conseil was in ecstasy.  Ned Land, carried away by his violent
  5255	instincts, rushed on to the staircase.  But the moment he seized the
  5256	rail with both hands, he, in his turn, was overthrown.
  5257	
  5258	"I am struck by a thunderbolt," cried he, with an oath.
  5259	
  5260	This explained all.  It was no rail; but a metallic cable charged with
  5261	electricity from the deck communicating with the platform.  Whoever
  5262	touched it felt a powerful shock--and this shock would have been mortal
  5263	if Captain Nemo had discharged into the conductor the whole force of
  5264	the current.  It might truly be said that between his assailants and
  5265	himself he had stretched a network of electricity which none could pass
  5266	with impunity.
  5267	
  5268	Meanwhile, the exasperated Papuans had beaten a retreat paralysed with
  5269	terror.  As for us, half laughing, we consoled and rubbed the
  5270	unfortunate Ned Land, who swore like one possessed.
  5271	
  5272	But at this moment the Nautilus, raised by the last waves of the tide,
  5273	quitted her coral bed exactly at the fortieth minute fixed by the
  5274	Captain.  Her screw swept the waters slowly and majestically.  Her
  5275	speed increased gradually, and, sailing on the surface of the ocean,
  5276	she quitted safe and sound the dangerous passes of the Straits of
  5277	Torres.
  5278	
  5279	
  5280	
  5281	CHAPTER XXII
  5282	
  5283	"AEGRI SOMNIA"
  5284	
  5285	The following day 10th January, the Nautilus continued her course
  5286	between two seas, but with such remarkable speed that I could not
  5287	estimate it at less than thirty-five miles an hour.  The rapidity of
  5288	her screw was such that I could neither follow nor count its
  5289	revolutions.  When I reflected that this marvellous electric agent,
  5290	after having afforded motion, heat, and light to the Nautilus, still
  5291	protected her from outward attack, and transformed her into an ark of
  5292	safety which no profane hand might touch without being thunderstricken,
  5293	my admiration was unbounded, and from the structure it extended to the
  5294	engineer who had called it into existence.
  5295	
  5296	Our course was directed to the west, and on the 11th of January we
  5297	doubled Cape Wessel, situation in 135° long. and 10° S. lat.,
  5298	which forms the east point of the Gulf of Carpentaria.  The reefs were
  5299	still numerous, but more equalised, and marked on the chart with
  5300	extreme precision.  The Nautilus easily avoided the breakers of Money
  5301	to port and the Victoria reefs to starboard, placed at 130° long.
  5302	and on the 10th parallel, which we strictly followed.
  5303	
  5304	On the 13th of January, Captain Nemo arrived in the Sea of Timor, and
  5305	recognised the island of that name in 122° long.
  5306	
  5307	From this point the direction of the Nautilus inclined towards the
  5308	south-west. Her head was set for the Indian Ocean.  Where would the
  5309	fancy of Captain Nemo carry us next?  Would he return to the coast of
  5310	Asia or would he approach again the shores of Europe?  Improbable
  5311	conjectures both, to a man who fled from inhabited continents.  Then
  5312	would he descend to the south?  Was he going to double the Cape of Good
  5313	Hope, then Cape Horn, and finally go as far as the Antarctic pole?
  5314	Would he come back at last to the Pacific, where his Nautilus could
  5315	sail free and independently?  Time would show.
  5316	
  5317	After having skirted the sands of Cartier, of Hibernia, Seringapatam,
  5318	and Scott, last efforts of the solid against the liquid element, on the
  5319	14th of January we lost sight of land altogether.  The speed of the
  5320	Nautilus was considerably abated, and with irregular course she
  5321	sometimes swam in the bosom of the waters, sometimes floated on their
  5322	surface.
  5323	
  5324	During this period of the voyage, Captain Nemo made some interesting
  5325	experiments on the varied temperature of the sea, in different beds.
  5326	Under ordinary conditions these observations are made by means of
  5327	rather complicated instruments, and with somewhat doubtful results, by
  5328	means of thermometrical sounding-leads, the glasses often breaking
  5329	under the pressure of the water, or an apparatus grounded on the
  5330	variations of the resistance of metals to the electric currents.
  5331	Results so obtained could not be correctly calculated.  On the
  5332	contrary, Captain Nemo went himself to test the temperature in the
  5333	depths of the sea, and his thermometer, placed in communication with
  5334	the different sheets of water, gave him the required degree immediately
  5335	and accurately.
  5336	
  5337	It was thus that, either by overloading her reservoirs or by descending
  5338	obliquely by means of her inclined planes, the Nautilus successively
  5339	attained the depth of three, four, five, seven, nine, and ten thousand
  5340	yards, and the definite result of this experience was that the sea
  5341	preserved an average temperature of four degrees and a half at a depth
  5342	of five thousand fathoms under all latitudes.
  5343	
  5344	On the 16th of January, the Nautilus seemed becalmed only a few yards
  5345	beneath the surface of the waves.  Her electric apparatus remained
  5346	inactive and her motionless screw left her to drift at the mercy of the
  5347	currents.  I supposed that the crew was occupied with interior repairs,
  5348	rendered necessary by the violence of the mechanical movements of the
  5349	machine.
  5350	
  5351	My companions and I then witnessed a curious spectacle.  The hatches of
  5352	the saloon were open, and, as the beacon light of the Nautilus was not
  5353	in action, a dim obscurity reigned in the midst of the waters.  I
  5354	observed the state of the sea, under these conditions, and the largest
  5355	fish appeared to me no more than scarcely defined shadows, when the
  5356	Nautilus found herself suddenly transported into full light.  I thought
  5357	at first that the beacon had been lighted, and was casting its electric
  5358	radiance into the liquid mass.  I was mistaken, and after a rapid
  5359	survey perceived my error.
  5360	
  5361	The Nautilus floated in the midst of a phosphorescent bed which, in
  5362	this obscurity, became quite dazzling.  It was produced by myriads of
  5363	luminous animalculae, whose brilliancy was increased as they glided
  5364	over the metallic hull of the vessel.  I was surprised by lightning in
  5365	the midst of these luminous sheets, as though they had been rivulets of
  5366	lead melted in an ardent furnace or metallic masses brought to a white
  5367	heat, so that, by force of contrast, certain portions of light appeared
  5368	to cast a shade in the midst of the general ignition, from which all
  5369	shade seemed banished.  No; this was not the calm irradiation of our
  5370	ordinary lightning.  There was unusual life and vigour: this was truly
  5371	living light!
  5372	
  5373	In reality, it was an infinite agglomeration of coloured infusoria, of
  5374	veritable globules of jelly, provided with a threadlike tentacle, and
  5375	of which as many as twenty-five thousand have been counted in less than
  5376	two cubic half-inches of water.
  5377	
  5378	During several hours the Nautilus floated in these brilliant waves, and
  5379	our admiration increased as we watched the marine monsters disporting
  5380	themselves like salamanders.  I saw there in the midst of this fire
  5381	that burns not the swift and elegant porpoise (the indefatigable clown
  5382	of the ocean), and some swordfish ten feet long, those prophetic
  5383	heralds of the hurricane whose formidable sword would now and then
  5384	strike the glass of the saloon.  Then appeared the smaller fish, the
  5385	balista, the leaping mackerel, wolf-thorn-tails, and a hundred others
  5386	which striped the luminous atmosphere as they swam.  This dazzling
  5387	spectacle was enchanting!  Perhaps some atmospheric condition increased
  5388	the intensity of this phenomenon.  Perhaps some storm agitated the
  5389	surface of the waves.  But at this depth of some yards, the Nautilus
  5390	was unmoved by its fury and reposed peacefully in still water.
  5391	
  5392	So we progressed, incessantly charmed by some new marvel.  The days
  5393	passed rapidly away, and I took no account of them.  Ned, according to
  5394	habit, tried to vary the diet on board.  Like snails, we were fixed to
  5395	our shells, and I declare it is easy to lead a snail's life.
  5396	
  5397	Thus this life seemed easy and natural, and we thought no longer of the
  5398	life we led on land; but something happened to recall us to the
  5399	strangeness of our situation.
  5400	
  5401	On the 18th of January, the Nautilus was in 105° long. and 15°
  5402	S. lat.  The weather was threatening, the sea rough and rolling.  There
  5403	was a strong east wind.  The barometer, which had been going down for
  5404	some days, foreboded a coming storm.  I went up on to the platform just
  5405	as the second lieutenant was taking the measure of the horary angles,
  5406	and waited, according to habit till the daily phrase was said.  But on
  5407	this day it was exchanged for another phrase not less incomprehensible.
  5408	Almost directly, I saw Captain Nemo appear with a glass, looking
  5409	towards the horizon.
  5410	
  5411	For some minutes he was immovable, without taking his eye off the point
  5412	of observation.  Then he lowered his glass and exchanged a few words
  5413	with his lieutenant.  The latter seemed to be a victim to some emotion
  5414	that he tried in vain to repress.  Captain Nemo, having more command
  5415	over himself, was cool.  He seemed, too, to be making some objections
  5416	to which the lieutenant replied by formal assurances.  At least I
  5417	concluded so by the difference of their tones and gestures.  For
  5418	myself, I had looked carefully in the direction indicated without
  5419	seeing anything.  The sky and water were lost in the clear line of the
  5420	horizon.
  5421	
  5422	However, Captain Nemo walked from one end of the platform to the other,
  5423	without looking at me, perhaps without seeing me.  His step was firm,
  5424	but less regular than usual.  He stopped sometimes, crossed his arms,
  5425	and observed the sea.  What could he be looking for on that immense
  5426	expanse?
  5427	
  5428	The Nautilus was then some hundreds of miles from the nearest coast.
  5429	
  5430	The lieutenant had taken up the glass and examined the horizon
  5431	steadfastly, going and coming, stamping his foot and showing more
  5432	nervous agitation than his superior officer.  Besides, this mystery
  5433	must necessarily be solved, and before long; for, upon an order from
  5434	Captain Nemo, the engine, increasing its propelling power, made the
  5435	screw turn more rapidly.
  5436	
  5437	Just then the lieutenant drew the Captain's attention again.  The
  5438	latter stopped walking and directed his glass towards the place
  5439	indicated.  He looked long.  I felt very much puzzled, and descended to
  5440	the drawing-room, and took out an excellent telescope that I generally
  5441	used.  Then, leaning on the cage of the watch-light that jutted out
  5442	from the front of the platform, set myself to look over all the line of
  5443	the sky and sea.
  5444	
  5445	But my eye was no sooner applied to the glass than it was quickly
  5446	snatched out of my hands.
  5447	
  5448	I turned round.  Captain Nemo was before me, but I did not know him.
  5449	His face was transfigured.  His eyes flashed sullenly; his teeth were
  5450	set; his stiff body, clenched fists, and head shrunk between his
  5451	shoulders, betrayed the violent agitation that pervaded his whole
  5452	frame.  He did not move.  My glass, fallen from his hands, had rolled
  5453	at his feet.
  5454	
  5455	Had I unwittingly provoked this fit of anger?  Did this
  5456	incomprehensible person imagine that I had discovered some forbidden
  5457	secret?  No; I was not the object of this hatred, for he was not
  5458	looking at me; his eye was steadily fixed upon the impenetrable point
  5459	of the horizon.  At last Captain Nemo recovered himself.  His agitation
  5460	subsided.  He addressed some words in a foreign language to his
  5461	lieutenant, then turned to me.  "M. Aronnax," he said, in rather an
  5462	imperious tone, "I require you to keep one of the conditions that bind
  5463	you to me."
  5464	
  5465	"What is it, Captain?"
  5466	
  5467	"You must be confined, with your companions, until I think fit to
  5468	release you."
  5469	
  5470	"You are the master," I replied, looking steadily at him.  "But may I
  5471	ask you one question?"
  5472	
  5473	"None, sir."
  5474	
  5475	There was no resisting this imperious command, it would have been
  5476	useless.  I went down to the cabin occupied by Ned Land and Conseil,
  5477	and told them the Captain's determination.  You may judge how this
  5478	communication was received by the Canadian.
  5479	
  5480	But there was not time for altercation.  Four of the crew waited at the
  5481	door, and conducted us to that cell where we had passed our first night
  5482	on board the Nautilus.
  5483	
  5484	Ned Land would have remonstrated, but the door was shut upon him.
  5485	
  5486	"Will master tell me what this means?" asked Conseil.
  5487	
  5488	I told my companions what had passed.  They were as much astonished as
  5489	I, and equally at a loss how to account for it.
  5490	
  5491	Meanwhile, I was absorbed in my own reflections, and could think of
  5492	nothing but the strange fear depicted in the Captain's countenance.  I
  5493	was utterly at a loss to account for it, when my cogitations were
  5494	disturbed by these words from Ned Land:
  5495	
  5496	"Hallo! breakfast is ready."
  5497	
  5498	And indeed the table was laid.  Evidently Captain Nemo had given this
  5499	order at the same time that he had hastened the speed of the Nautilus.
  5500	
  5501	"Will master permit me to make a recommendation?" asked Conseil.
  5502	
  5503	"Yes, my boy."
  5504	
  5505	"Well, it is that master breakfasts.  It is prudent, for we do not know
  5506	what may happen."
  5507	
  5508	"You are right, Conseil."
  5509	
  5510	"Unfortunately," said Ned Land, "they have only given us the ship's
  5511	fare."
  5512	
  5513	"Friend Ned," asked Conseil, "what would you have said if the breakfast
  5514	had been entirely forgotten?"
  5515	
  5516	This argument cut short the harpooner's recriminations.
  5517	
  5518	We sat down to table.  The meal was eaten in silence.
  5519	
  5520	Just then the luminous globe that lighted the cell went out, and left
  5521	us in total darkness.  Ned Land was soon asleep, and what astonished me
  5522	was that Conseil went off into a heavy slumber.  I was thinking what
  5523	could have caused his irresistible drowsiness, when I felt my brain
  5524	becoming stupefied.  In spite of my efforts to keep my eyes open, they
  5525	would close.  A painful suspicion seized me.  Evidently soporific
  5526	substances had been mixed with the food we had just taken.
  5527	Imprisonment was not enough to conceal Captain Nemo's projects from us,
  5528	sleep was more necessary.  I then heard the panels shut.  The
  5529	undulations of the sea, which caused a slight rolling motion, ceased.
  5530	Had the Nautilus quitted the surface of the ocean?  Had it gone back to
  5531	the motionless bed of water?  I tried to resist sleep.  It was
  5532	impossible.  My breathing grew weak.  I felt a mortal cold freeze my
  5533	stiffened and half-paralysed limbs.  My eye lids, like leaden caps,
  5534	fell over my eyes.  I could not raise them; a morbid sleep, full of
  5535	hallucinations, bereft me of my being.  Then the visions disappeared,
  5536	and left me in complete insensibility.
  5537	
  5538	
  5539	
  5540	CHAPTER XXIII
  5541	
  5542	THE CORAL KINGDOM
  5543	
  5544	The next day I woke with my head singularly clear.  To my great
  5545	surprise, I was in my own room.  My companions, no doubt, had been
  5546	reinstated in their cabin, without having perceived it any more than I.
  5547	Of what had passed during the night they were as ignorant as I was, and
  5548	to penetrate this mystery I only reckoned upon the chances of the
  5549	future.
  5550	
  5551	I then thought of quitting my room.  Was I free again or a prisoner?
  5552	Quite free.  I opened the door, went to the half-deck, went up the
  5553	central stairs.  The panels, shut the evening before, were open.  I
  5554	went on to the platform.
  5555	
  5556	Ned Land and Conseil waited there for me.  I questioned them; they knew
  5557	nothing.  Lost in a heavy sleep in which they had been totally
  5558	unconscious, they had been astonished at finding themselves in their
  5559	cabin.
  5560	
  5561	As for the Nautilus, it seemed quiet and mysterious as ever.  It
  5562	floated on the surface of the waves at a moderate pace.  Nothing seemed
  5563	changed on board.
  5564	
  5565	The second lieutenant then came on to the platform, and gave the usual
  5566	order below.
  5567	
  5568	As for Captain Nemo, he did not appear.
  5569	
  5570	Of the people on board, I only saw the impassive steward, who served me
  5571	with his usual dumb regularity.
  5572	
  5573	About two o'clock, I was in the drawing-room, busied in arranging my
  5574	notes, when the Captain opened the door and appeared.  I bowed.  He
  5575	made a slight inclination in return, without speaking.  I resumed my
  5576	work, hoping that he would perhaps give me some explanation of the
  5577	events of the preceding night.  He made none.  I looked at him.  He
  5578	seemed fatigued; his heavy eyes had not been refreshed by sleep; his
  5579	face looked very sorrowful.  He walked to and fro, sat down and got up
  5580	again, took a chance book, put it down, consulted his instruments
  5581	without taking his habitual notes, and seemed restless and uneasy.  At
  5582	last, he came up to me, and said:
  5583	
  5584	"Are you a doctor, M. Aronnax?"
  5585	
  5586	I so little expected such a question that I stared some time at him
  5587	without answering.
  5588	
  5589	"Are you a doctor?" he repeated.  "Several of your colleagues have
  5590	studied medicine."
  5591	
  5592	"Well," said I, "I am a doctor and resident surgeon to the hospital.  I
  5593	practised several years before entering the museum."
  5594	
  5595	"Very well, sir."
  5596	
  5597	My answer had evidently satisfied the Captain.  But, not knowing what
  5598	he would say next, I waited for other questions, reserving my answers
  5599	according to circumstances.
  5600	
  5601	"M. Aronnax, will you consent to prescribe for one of my men?" he asked.
  5602	
  5603	"Is he ill?"
  5604	
  5605	"Yes."
  5606	
  5607	"I am ready to follow you."
  5608	
  5609	"Come, then."
  5610	
  5611	I own my heart beat, I do not know why.  I saw certain connection
  5612	between the illness of one of the crew and the events of the day
  5613	before; and this mystery interested me at least as much as the sick man.
  5614	
  5615	Captain Nemo conducted me to the poop of the Nautilus, and took me into
  5616	a cabin situated near the sailors' quarters.
  5617	
  5618	There, on a bed, lay a man about forty years of age, with a resolute
  5619	expression of countenance, a true type of an Anglo-Saxon.
  5620	
  5621	I leant over him.  He was not only ill, he was wounded.  His head,
  5622	swathed in bandages covered with blood, lay on a pillow.  I undid the
  5623	bandages, and the wounded man looked at me with his large eyes and gave
  5624	no sign of pain as I did it.  It was a horrible wound.  The skull,
  5625	shattered by some deadly weapon, left the brain exposed, which was much
  5626	injured.  Clots of blood had formed in the bruised and broken mass, in
  5627	colour like the dregs of wine.
  5628	
  5629	There was both contusion and suffusion of the brain.  His breathing was
  5630	slow, and some spasmodic movements of the muscles agitated his face.  I
  5631	felt his pulse.  It was intermittent.  The extremities of the body were
  5632	growing cold already, and I saw death must inevitably ensue.  After
  5633	dressing the unfortunate man's wounds, I readjusted the bandages on his
  5634	head, and turned to Captain Nemo.
  5635	
  5636	"What caused this wound?"  I asked.
  5637	
  5638	"What does it signify?" he replied, evasively.  "A shock has broken one
  5639	of the levers of the engine, which struck myself.  But your opinion as
  5640	to his state?"
  5641	
  5642	I hesitated before giving it.
  5643	
  5644	"You may speak," said the Captain.  "This man does not understand
  5645	French."
  5646	
  5647	I gave a last look at the wounded man.
  5648	
  5649	"He will be dead in two hours."
  5650	
  5651	"Can nothing save him?"
  5652	
  5653	"Nothing."
  5654	
  5655	Captain Nemo's hand contracted, and some tears glistened in his eyes,
  5656	which I thought incapable of shedding any.
  5657	
  5658	For some moments I still watched the dying man, whose life ebbed
  5659	slowly.  His pallor increased under the electric light that was shed
  5660	over his death-bed. I looked at his intelligent forehead, furrowed with
  5661	premature wrinkles, produced probably by misfortune and sorrow.  I
  5662	tried to learn the secret of his life from the last words that escaped
  5663	his lips.
  5664	
  5665	"You can go now, M. Aronnax," said the Captain.
  5666	
  5667	I left him in the dying man's cabin, and returned to my room much
  5668	affected by this scene.  During the whole day, I was haunted by
  5669	uncomfortable suspicions, and at night I slept badly, and between my
  5670	broken dreams I fancied I heard distant sighs like the notes of a
  5671	funeral psalm.  Were they the prayers of the dead, murmured in that
  5672	language that I could not understand?
  5673	
  5674	The next morning I went on to the bridge.  Captain Nemo was there
  5675	before me.  As soon as he perceived me he came to me.
  5676	
  5677	"Professor, will it be convenient to you to make a submarine excursion
  5678	to-day?"
  5679	
  5680	"With my companions?"  I asked.
  5681	
  5682	"If they like."
  5683	
  5684	"We obey your orders, Captain."
  5685	
  5686	"Will you be so good then as to put on your cork jackets?"
  5687	
  5688	It was not a question of dead or dying.  I rejoined Ned Land and
  5689	Conseil, and told them of Captain Nemo's proposition.  Conseil hastened
  5690	to accept it, and this time the Canadian seemed quite willing to follow
  5691	our example.
  5692	
  5693	It was eight o'clock in the morning.  At half-past eight we were
  5694	equipped for this new excursion, and provided with two contrivances for
  5695	light and breathing.  The double door was open; and, accompanied by
  5696	Captain Nemo, who was followed by a dozen of the crew, we set foot, at
  5697	a depth of about thirty feet, on the solid bottom on which the Nautilus
  5698	rested.
  5699	
  5700	A slight declivity ended in an uneven bottom, at fifteen fathoms depth.
  5701	This bottom differed entirely from the one I had visited on my first
  5702	excursion under the waters of the Pacific Ocean.  Here, there was no
  5703	fine sand, no submarine prairies, no sea-forest. I immediately
  5704	recognised that marvellous region in which, on that day, the Captain
  5705	did the honours to us.  It was the coral kingdom.
  5706	
  5707	The light produced a thousand charming varieties, playing in the midst
  5708	of the branches that were so vividly coloured.  I seemed to see the
  5709	membraneous and cylindrical tubes tremble beneath the undulation of the
  5710	waters.  I was tempted to gather their fresh petals, ornamented with
  5711	delicate tentacles, some just blown, the others budding, while a small
  5712	fish, swimming swiftly, touched them slightly, like flights of birds.
  5713	But if my hand approached these living flowers, these animated,
  5714	sensitive plants, the whole colony took alarm.  The white petals
  5715	re-entered their red cases, the flowers faded as I looked, and the bush
  5716	changed into a block of stony knobs.
  5717	
  5718	Chance had thrown me just by the most precious specimens of the
  5719	zoophyte.  This coral was more valuable than that found in the
  5720	Mediterranean, on the coasts of France, Italy and Barbary.  Its tints
  5721	justified the poetical names of "Flower of Blood," and "Froth of
  5722	Blood," that trade has given to its most beautiful productions.  Coral
  5723	is sold for L20 per ounce; and in this place the watery beds would make
  5724	the fortunes of a company of coral-divers. This precious matter, often
  5725	confused with other polypi, formed then the inextricable plots called
  5726	"macciota," and on which I noticed several beautiful specimens of pink
  5727	coral.
  5728	
  5729	But soon the bushes contract, and the arborisations increase. Real
  5730	petrified thickets, long joints of fantastic architecture, were
  5731	disclosed before us. Captain Nemo placed himself under a dark gallery,
  5732	where by a slight declivity we reached a depth of a hundred yards. The
  5733	light from our lamps produced sometimes magical effects, following the
  5734	rough outlines of the natural arches and pendants disposed like
  5735	lustres, that were tipped with points of fire.
  5736	
  5737	At last, after walking two hours, we had attained a depth of about
  5738	three hundred yards, that is to say, the extreme limit on which coral
  5739	begins to form.  But there was no isolated bush, nor modest brushwood,
  5740	at the bottom of lofty trees.  It was an immense forest of large
  5741	mineral vegetations, enormous petrified trees, united by garlands of
  5742	elegant sea-bindweed, all adorned with clouds and reflections.  We
  5743	passed freely under their high branches, lost in the shade of the waves.
  5744	
  5745	Captain Nemo had stopped.  I and my companions halted, and, turning
  5746	round, I saw his men were forming a semi-circle round their chief.
  5747	Watching attentively, I observed that four of them carried on their
  5748	shoulders an object of an oblong shape.
  5749	
  5750	We occupied, in this place, the centre of a vast glade surrounded by
  5751	the lofty foliage of the submarine forest.  Our lamps threw over this
  5752	place a sort of clear twilight that singularly elongated the shadows on
  5753	the ground.  At the end of the glade the darkness increased, and was
  5754	only relieved by little sparks reflected by the points of coral.
  5755	
  5756	Ned Land and Conseil were near me.  We watched, and I thought I was
  5757	going to witness a strange scene.  On observing the ground, I saw that
  5758	it was raised in certain places by slight excrescences encrusted with
  5759	limy deposits, and disposed with a regularity that betrayed the hand of
  5760	man.
  5761	
  5762	In the midst of the glade, on a pedestal of rocks roughly piled up,
  5763	stood a cross of coral that extended its long arms that one might have
  5764	thought were made of petrified blood.  Upon a sign from Captain Nemo
  5765	one of the men advanced; and at some feet from the cross he began to
  5766	dig a hole with a pickaxe that he took from his belt.  I understood
  5767	all!  This glade was a cemetery, this hole a tomb, this oblong object
  5768	the body of the man who had died in the night!  The Captain and his men
  5769	had come to bury their companion in this general resting-place, at the
  5770	bottom of this inaccessible ocean!
  5771	
  5772	The grave was being dug slowly; the fish fled on all sides while their
  5773	retreat was being thus disturbed; I heard the strokes of the pickaxe,
  5774	which sparkled when it hit upon some flint lost at the bottom of the
  5775	waters.  The hole was soon large and deep enough to receive the body.
  5776	Then the bearers approached; the body, enveloped in a tissue of white
  5777	linen, was lowered into the damp grave.  Captain Nemo, with his arms
  5778	crossed on his breast, and all the friends of him who had loved them,
  5779	knelt in prayer.
  5780	
  5781	The grave was then filled in with the rubbish taken from the ground,
  5782	which formed a slight mound.  When this was done, Captain Nemo and his
  5783	men rose; then, approaching the grave, they knelt again, and all
  5784	extended their hands in sign of a last adieu.  Then the funeral
  5785	procession returned to the Nautilus, passing under the arches of the
  5786	forest, in the midst of thickets, along the coral bushes, and still on
  5787	the ascent.  At last the light of the ship appeared, and its luminous
  5788	track guided us to the Nautilus.  At one o'clock we had returned.
  5789	
  5790	As soon as I had changed my clothes I went up on to the platform, and,
  5791	a prey to conflicting emotions, I sat down near the binnacle.  Captain
  5792	Nemo joined me.  I rose and said to him:
  5793	
  5794	"So, as I said he would, this man died in the night?"
  5795	
  5796	"Yes, M. Aronnax."
  5797	
  5798	"And he rests now, near his companions, in the coral cemetery?"
  5799	
  5800	"Yes, forgotten by all else, but not by us.  We dug the grave, and the
  5801	polypi undertake to seal our dead for eternity." And, burying his face
  5802	quickly in his hands, he tried in vain to suppress a sob.  Then he
  5803	added:  "Our peaceful cemetery is there, some hundred feet below the
  5804	surface of the waves."
  5805	
  5806	"Your dead sleep quietly, at least, Captain, out of the reach of
  5807	sharks."
  5808	
  5809	"Yes, sir, of sharks and men," gravely replied the Captain.
  5810	
  5811	
  5812	
  5813	PART TWO
  5814	
  5815	
  5816	
  5817	CHAPTER I
  5818	
  5819	THE INDIAN OCEAN
  5820	
  5821	We now come to the second part of our journey under the sea.  The first
  5822	ended with the moving scene in the coral cemetery which left such a
  5823	deep impression on my mind.  Thus, in the midst of this great sea,
  5824	Captain Nemo's life was passing, even to his grave, which he had
  5825	prepared in one of its deepest abysses.  There, not one of the ocean's
  5826	monsters could trouble the last sleep of the crew of the Nautilus, of
  5827	those friends riveted to each other in death as in life.  "Nor any man,
  5828	either," had added the Captain.  Still the same fierce, implacable
  5829	defiance towards human society!
  5830	
  5831	I could no longer content myself with the theory which satisfied
  5832	Conseil.
  5833	
  5834	That worthy fellow persisted in seeing in the Commander of the Nautilus
  5835	one of those unknown _savants_ who return mankind contempt for
