  5836	indifference.  For him, he was a misunderstood genius who, tired of
  5837	earth's deceptions, had taken refuge in this inaccessible medium, where
  5838	he might follow his instincts freely.  To my mind, this explains but
  5839	one side of Captain Nemo's character.  Indeed, the mystery of that last
  5840	night during which we had been chained in prison, the sleep, and the
  5841	precaution so violently taken by the Captain of snatching from my eyes
  5842	the glass I had raised to sweep the horizon, the mortal wound of the
  5843	man, due to an unaccountable shock of the Nautilus, all put me on a new
  5844	track.  No; Captain Nemo was not satisfied with shunning man.  His
  5845	formidable apparatus not only suited his instinct of freedom, but
  5846	perhaps also the design of some terrible retaliation.
  5847	
  5848	At this moment nothing is clear to me; I catch but a glimpse of light
  5849	amidst all the darkness, and I must confine myself to writing as events
  5850	shall dictate.
  5851	
  5852	That day, the 24th of January, 1868, at noon, the second officer came
  5853	to take the altitude of the sun.  I mounted the platform, lit a cigar,
  5854	and watched the operation.  It seemed to me that the man did not
  5855	understand French; for several times I made remarks in a loud voice,
  5856	which must have drawn from him some involuntary sign of attention, if
  5857	he had understood them; but he remained undisturbed and dumb.
  5858	
  5859	As he was taking observations with the sextant, one of the sailors of
  5860	the Nautilus (the strong man who had accompanied us on our first
  5861	submarine excursion to the Island of Crespo) came to clean the glasses
  5862	of the lantern.  I examined the fittings of the apparatus, the strength
  5863	of which was increased a hundredfold by lenticular rings, placed
  5864	similar to those in a lighthouse, and which projected their brilliance
  5865	in a horizontal plane.  The electric lamp was combined in such a way as
  5866	to give its most powerful light.  Indeed, it was produced in vacuo,
  5867	which insured both its steadiness and its intensity.  This vacuum
  5868	economised the graphite points between which the luminous arc was
  5869	developed--an important point of economy for Captain Nemo, who could
  5870	not easily have replaced them; and under these conditions their waste
  5871	was imperceptible.  When the Nautilus was ready to continue its
  5872	submarine journey, I went down to the saloon.  The panel was closed,
  5873	and the course marked direct west.
  5874	
  5875	We were furrowing the waters of the Indian Ocean, a vast liquid plain,
  5876	with a surface of 1,200,000,000 of acres, and whose waters are so clear
  5877	and transparent that any one leaning over them would turn giddy.  The
  5878	Nautilus usually floated between fifty and a hundred fathoms deep.  We
  5879	went on so for some days.  To anyone but myself, who had a great love
  5880	for the sea, the hours would have seemed long and monotonous; but the
  5881	daily walks on the platform, when I steeped myself in the reviving air
  5882	of the ocean, the sight of the rich waters through the windows of the
  5883	saloon, the books in the library, the compiling of my memoirs, took up
  5884	all my time, and left me not a moment of ennui or weariness.
  5885	
  5886	For some days we saw a great number of aquatic birds, sea-mews or
  5887	gulls.  Some were cleverly killed and, prepared in a certain way, made
  5888	very acceptable water-game. Amongst large-winged birds, carried a long
  5889	distance from all lands and resting upon the waves from the fatigue of
  5890	their flight, I saw some magnificent albatrosses, uttering discordant
  5891	cries like the braying of an ass, and birds belonging to the family of
  5892	the long-wings.
  5893	
  5894	As to the fish, they always provoked our admiration when we surprised
  5895	the secrets of their aquatic life through the open panels.  I saw many
  5896	kinds which I never before had a chance of observing.
  5897	
  5898	I shall notice chiefly ostracions peculiar to the Red Sea, the Indian
  5899	Ocean, and that part which washes the coast of tropical America. These
  5900	fishes, like the tortoise, the armadillo, the sea-hedgehog, and the
  5901	Crustacea, are protected by a breastplate which is neither chalky nor
  5902	stony, but real bone. In some it takes the form of a solid triangle, in
  5903	others of a solid quadrangle. Amongst the triangular I saw some an inch
  5904	and a half in length, with wholesome flesh and a delicious flavour;
  5905	they are brown at the tail, and yellow at the fins, and I recommend
  5906	their introduction into fresh water, to which a certain number of
  5907	sea-fish easily accustom themselves. I would also mention quadrangular
  5908	ostracions, having on the back four large tubercles; some dotted over
  5909	with white spots on the lower part of the body, and which may be tamed
  5910	like birds; trigons provided with spikes formed by the lengthening of
  5911	their bony shell, and which, from their strange gruntings, are called
  5912	"seapigs"; also dromedaries with large humps in the shape of a cone,
  5913	whose flesh is very tough and leathery.
  5914	
  5915	I now borrow from the daily notes of Master Conseil. "Certain fish of
  5916	the genus petrodon peculiar to those seas, with red backs and white
  5917	chests, which are distinguished by three rows of longitudinal
  5918	filaments; and some electrical, seven inches long, decked in the
  5919	liveliest colours.  Then, as specimens of other kinds, some ovoides,
  5920	resembling an egg of a dark brown colour, marked with white bands, and
  5921	without tails; diodons, real sea-porcupines, furnished with spikes, and
  5922	capable of swelling in such a way as to look like cushions bristling
  5923	with darts; hippocampi, common to every ocean; some pegasi with
  5924	lengthened snouts, which their pectoral fins, being much elongated and
  5925	formed in the shape of wings, allow, if not to fly, at least to shoot
  5926	into the air; pigeon spatulae, with tails covered with many rings of
  5927	shell; macrognathi with long jaws, an excellent fish, nine inches long,
  5928	and bright with most agreeable colours; pale-coloured calliomores, with
  5929	rugged heads; and plenty of chaetpdons, with long and tubular muzzles,
  5930	which kill insects by shooting them, as from an air-gun, with a single
  5931	drop of water. These we may call the flycatchers of the seas.
  5932	
  5933	"In the eighty-ninth genus of fishes, classed by Lacepede, belonging to
  5934	the second lower class of bony, characterised by opercules and
  5935	bronchial membranes, I remarked the scorpaena, the head of which is
  5936	furnished with spikes, and which has but one dorsal fin; these
  5937	creatures are covered, or not, with little shells, according to the
  5938	sub-class to which they belong. The second sub-class gives us specimens
  5939	of didactyles fourteen or fifteen inches in length, with yellow rays,
  5940	and heads of a most fantastic appearance. As to the first sub-class, it
  5941	gives several specimens of that singular looking fish appropriately
  5942	called a 'seafrog,' with large head, sometimes pierced with holes,
  5943	sometimes swollen with protuberances, bristling with spikes, and
  5944	covered with tubercles; it has irregular and hideous horns; its body
  5945	and tail are covered with callosities; its sting makes a dangerous
  5946	wound; it is both repugnant and horrible to look at."
  5947	
  5948	From the 21st to the 23rd of January the Nautilus went at the rate of
  5949	two hundred and fifty leagues in twenty-four hours, being five hundred
  5950	and forty miles, or twenty-two miles an hour.  If we recognised so many
  5951	different varieties of fish, it was because, attracted by the electric
  5952	light, they tried to follow us; the greater part, however, were soon
  5953	distanced by our speed, though some kept their place in the waters of
  5954	the Nautilus for a time.  The morning of the 24th, in 12° 5' S.
  5955	lat., and 94° 33' long., we observed Keeling Island, a coral
  5956	formation, planted with magnificent cocos, and which had been visited
  5957	by Mr. Darwin and Captain Fitzroy.  The Nautilus skirted the shores of
  5958	this desert island for a little distance.  Its nets brought up numerous
  5959	specimens of polypi and curious shells of mollusca.  Some precious
  5960	productions of the species of delphinulae enriched the treasures of
  5961	Captain Nemo, to which I added an astraea punctifera, a kind of
  5962	parasite polypus often found fixed to a shell.
  5963	
  5964	Soon Keeling Island disappeared from the horizon, and our course was
  5965	directed to the north-west in the direction of the Indian Peninsula.
  5966	
  5967	From Keeling Island our course was slower and more variable, often
  5968	taking us into great depths.  Several times they made use of the
  5969	inclined planes, which certain internal levers placed obliquely to the
  5970	waterline.  In that way we went about two miles, but without ever
  5971	obtaining the greatest depths of the Indian Sea, which soundings of
  5972	seven thousand fathoms have never reached.  As to the temperature of
  5973	the lower strata, the thermometer invariably indicated 4° above
  5974	zero.  I only observed that in the upper regions the water was always
  5975	colder in the high levels than at the surface of the sea.
  5976	
  5977	On the 25th of January the ocean was entirely deserted; the Nautilus
  5978	passed the day on the surface, beating the waves with its powerful
  5979	screw and making them rebound to a great height.  Who under such
  5980	circumstances would not have taken it for a gigantic cetacean?  Three
  5981	parts of this day I spent on the platform.  I watched the sea.  Nothing
  5982	on the horizon, till about four o'clock a steamer running west on our
  5983	counter.  Her masts were visible for an instant, but she could not see
  5984	the Nautilus, being too low in the water.  I fancied this steamboat
  5985	belonged to the P.O. Company, which runs from Ceylon to Sydney,
  5986	touching at King George's Point and Melbourne.
  5987	
  5988	At five o'clock in the evening, before that fleeting twilight which
  5989	binds night to day in tropical zones, Conseil and I were astonished by
  5990	a curious spectacle.
  5991	
  5992	It was a shoal of argonauts travelling along on the surface of the
  5993	ocean.  We could count several hundreds.  They belonged to the tubercle
  5994	kind which are peculiar to the Indian seas.
  5995	
  5996	These graceful molluscs moved backwards by means of their locomotive
  5997	tube, through which they propelled the water already drawn in.  Of
  5998	their eight tentacles, six were elongated, and stretched out floating
  5999	on the water, whilst the other two, rolled up flat, were spread to the
  6000	wing like a light sail.  I saw their spiral-shaped and fluted shells,
  6001	which Cuvier justly compares to an elegant skiff.  A boat indeed!  It
  6002	bears the creature which secretes it without its adhering to it.
  6003	
  6004	For nearly an hour the Nautilus floated in the midst of this shoal of
  6005	molluscs.  Then I know not what sudden fright they took.  But as if at
  6006	a signal every sail was furled, the arms folded, the body drawn in, the
  6007	shells turned over, changing their centre of gravity, and the whole
  6008	fleet disappeared under the waves.  Never did the ships of a squadron
  6009	manoeuvre with more unity.
  6010	
  6011	At that moment night fell suddenly, and the reeds, scarcely raised by
  6012	the breeze, lay peaceably under the sides of the Nautilus.
  6013	
  6014	The next day, 26th of January, we cut the equator at the eighty-second
  6015	meridian and entered the northern hemisphere.  During the day a
  6016	formidable troop of sharks accompanied us, terrible creatures, which
  6017	multiply in these seas and make them very dangerous.  They were
  6018	"cestracio philippi" sharks, with brown backs and whitish bellies,
  6019	armed with eleven rows of teeth--eyed sharks--their throat being marked
  6020	with a large black spot surrounded with white like an eye.  There were
  6021	also some Isabella sharks, with rounded snouts marked with dark spots.
  6022	These powerful creatures often hurled themselves at the windows of the
  6023	saloon with such violence as to make us feel very insecure.  At such
  6024	times Ned Land was no longer master of himself.  He wanted to go to the
  6025	surface and harpoon the monsters, particularly certain smooth-hound
  6026	sharks, whose mouth is studded with teeth like a mosaic; and large
  6027	tiger-sharks nearly six yards long, the last named of which seemed to
  6028	excite him more particularly.  But the Nautilus, accelerating her
  6029	speed, easily left the most rapid of them behind.
  6030	
  6031	The 27th of January, at the entrance of the vast Bay of Bengal, we met
  6032	repeatedly a forbidding spectacle, dead bodies floating on the surface
  6033	of the water.  They were the dead of the Indian villages, carried by
  6034	the Ganges to the level of the sea, and which the vultures, the only
  6035	undertakers of the country, had not been able to devour.  But the
  6036	sharks did not fail to help them at their funeral work.
  6037	
  6038	About seven o'clock in the evening, the Nautilus, half-immersed, was
  6039	sailing in a sea of milk.  At first sight the ocean seemed lactified.
  6040	Was it the effect of the lunar rays?  No; for the moon, scarcely two
  6041	days old, was still lying hidden under the horizon in the rays of the
  6042	sun.  The whole sky, though lit by the sidereal rays, seemed black by
  6043	contrast with the whiteness of the waters.
  6044	
  6045	Conseil could not believe his eyes, and questioned me as to the cause
  6046	of this strange phenomenon.  Happily I was able to answer him.
  6047	
  6048	"It is called a milk sea," I explained.  "A large extent of white
  6049	wavelets often to be seen on the coasts of Amboyna, and in these parts
  6050	of the sea."
  6051	
  6052	"But, sir," said Conseil, "can you tell me what causes such an effect?
  6053	for I suppose the water is not really turned into milk."
  6054	
  6055	"No, my boy; and the whiteness which surprises you is caused only by
  6056	the presence of myriads of infusoria, a sort of luminous little worm,
  6057	gelatinous and without colour, of the thickness of a hair, and whose
  6058	length is not more than seven-thousandths of an inch.  These insects
  6059	adhere to one another sometimes for several leagues."
  6060	
  6061	"Several leagues!" exclaimed Conseil.
  6062	
  6063	"Yes, my boy; and you need not try to compute the number of these
  6064	infusoria.  You will not be able, for, if I am not mistaken, ships have
  6065	floated on these milk seas for more than forty miles."
  6066	
  6067	Towards midnight the sea suddenly resumed its usual colour; but behind
  6068	us, even to the limits of the horizon, the sky reflected the whitened
  6069	waves, and for a long time seemed impregnated with the vague
  6070	glimmerings of an aurora borealis.
  6071	
  6072	
  6073	
  6074	CHAPTER II
  6075	
  6076	A NOVEL PROPOSAL OF CAPTAIN NEMO'S
  6077	
  6078	On the 28th of February, when at noon the Nautilus came to the surface
  6079	of the sea, in 9° 4' N. lat., there was land in sight about eight
  6080	miles to westward.  The first thing I noticed was a range of mountains
  6081	about two thousand feet high, the shapes of which were most capricious.
  6082	On taking the bearings, I knew that we were nearing the island of
  6083	Ceylon, the pearl which hangs from the lobe of the Indian Peninsula.
  6084	
  6085	Captain Nemo and his second appeared at this moment.  The Captain
  6086	glanced at the map.  Then turning to me, said:
  6087	
  6088	"The Island of Ceylon, noted for its pearl-fisheries. Would you like to
  6089	visit one of them, M. Aronnax?"
  6090	
  6091	"Certainly, Captain."
  6092	
  6093	"Well, the thing is easy.  Though, if we see the fisheries, we shall
  6094	not see the fishermen.  The annual exportation has not yet begun.
  6095	Never mind, I will give orders to make for the Gulf of Manaar, where we
  6096	shall arrive in the night."
  6097	
  6098	The Captain said something to his second, who immediately went out.
  6099	Soon the Nautilus returned to her native element, and the manometer
  6100	showed that she was about thirty feet deep.
  6101	
  6102	"Well, sir," said Captain Nemo, "you and your companions shall visit
  6103	the Bank of Manaar, and if by chance some fisherman should be there, we
  6104	shall see him at work."
  6105	
  6106	"Agreed, Captain!"
  6107	
  6108	"By the bye, M. Aronnax you are not afraid of sharks?"
  6109	
  6110	"Sharks!" exclaimed I.
  6111	
  6112	This question seemed a very hard one.
  6113	
  6114	"Well?" continued Captain Nemo.
  6115	
  6116	"I admit, Captain, that I am not yet very familiar with that kind of
  6117	fish."
  6118	
  6119	"We are accustomed to them," replied Captain Nemo, "and in time you
  6120	will be too.  However, we shall be armed, and on the road we may be
  6121	able to hunt some of the tribe.  It is interesting.  So, till
  6122	to-morrow, sir, and early."
  6123	
  6124	This said in a careless tone, Captain Nemo left the saloon.  Now, if
  6125	you were invited to hunt the bear in the mountains of Switzerland, what
  6126	would you say?
  6127	
  6128	"Very well! to-morrow we will go and hunt the bear." If you were asked
  6129	to hunt the lion in the plains of Atlas, or the tiger in the Indian
  6130	jungles, what would you say?
  6131	
  6132	"Ha! ha! it seems we are going to hunt the tiger or the lion!" But when
  6133	you are invited to hunt the shark in its natural element, you would
  6134	perhaps reflect before accepting the invitation.  As for myself, I
  6135	passed my hand over my forehead, on which stood large drops of cold
  6136	perspiration.  "Let us reflect," said I, "and take our time.  Hunting
  6137	otters in submarine forests, as we did in the Island of Crespo, will
  6138	pass; but going up and down at the bottom of the sea, where one is
  6139	almost certain to meet sharks, is quite another thing!  I know well
  6140	that in certain countries, particularly in the Andaman Islands, the
  6141	negroes never hesitate to attack them with a dagger in one hand and a
  6142	running noose in the other; but I also know that few who affront those
  6143	creatures ever return alive.  However, I am not a negro, and if I were
  6144	I think a little hesitation in this case would not be ill-timed."
  6145	
  6146	At this moment Conseil and the Canadian entered, quite composed, and
  6147	even joyous.  They knew not what awaited them.
  6148	
  6149	"Faith, sir," said Ned Land, "your Captain Nemo--the devil take
  6150	him!--has just made us a very pleasant offer."
  6151	
  6152	"Ah!" said I, "you know?"
  6153	
  6154	"If agreeable to you, sir," interrupted Conseil, "the commander of the
  6155	Nautilus has invited us to visit the magnificent Ceylon fisheries
  6156	to-morrow, in your company; he did it kindly, and behaved like a real
  6157	gentleman."
  6158	
  6159	"He said nothing more?"
  6160	
  6161	"Nothing more, sir, except that he had already spoken to you of this
  6162	little walk."
  6163	
  6164	"Sir," said Conseil, "would you give us some details of the pearl
  6165	fishery?"
  6166	
  6167	"As to the fishing itself," I asked, "or the incidents, which?"
  6168	
  6169	"On the fishing," replied the Canadian; "before entering upon the
  6170	ground, it is as well to know something about it."
  6171	
  6172	"Very well; sit down, my friends, and I will teach you."
  6173	
  6174	Ned and Conseil seated themselves on an ottoman, and the first thing
  6175	the Canadian asked was:
  6176	
  6177	"Sir, what is a pearl?"
  6178	
  6179	"My worthy Ned," I answered, "to the poet, a pearl is a tear of the
  6180	sea; to the Orientals, it is a drop of dew solidified; to the ladies,
  6181	it is a jewel of an oblong shape, of a brilliancy of mother-of-pearl
  6182	substance, which they wear on their fingers, their necks, or their
  6183	ears; for the chemist it is a mixture of phosphate and carbonate of
  6184	lime, with a little gelatine; and lastly, for naturalists, it is simply
  6185	a morbid secretion of the organ that produces the mother-of-pearl
  6186	amongst certain bivalves."
  6187	
  6188	"Branch of molluscs," said Conseil.
  6189	
  6190	"Precisely so, my learned Conseil; and, amongst these testacea the
  6191	earshell, the tridacnae, the turbots, in a word, all those which
  6192	secrete mother-of-pearl, that is, the blue, bluish, violet, or white
  6193	substance which lines the interior of their shells, are capable of
  6194	producing pearls."
  6195	
  6196	"Mussels too?" asked the Canadian.
  6197	
  6198	"Yes, mussels of certain waters in Scotland, Wales, Ireland, Saxony,
  6199	Bohemia, and France."
  6200	
  6201	"Good!  For the future I shall pay attention," replied the Canadian.
  6202	
  6203	"But," I continued, "the particular mollusc which secretes the pearl is
  6204	the pearl-oyster, the meleagrina margaritiferct, that precious
  6205	pintadine. The pearl is nothing but a nacreous formation, deposited in
  6206	a globular form, either adhering to the oyster shell, or buried in the
  6207	folds of the creature. On the shell it is fast; in the flesh it is
  6208	loose; but always has for a kernel a small hard substance, may be a
  6209	barren egg, may be a grain of sand, around which the pearly matter
  6210	deposits itself year after year successively, and by thin concentric
  6211	layers."
  6212	
  6213	"Are many pearls found in the same oyster?" asked Conseil.
  6214	
  6215	"Yes, my boy.  Some are a perfect casket.  One oyster has been
  6216	mentioned, though I allow myself to doubt it, as having contained no
  6217	less than a hundred and fifty sharks."
  6218	
  6219	"A hundred and fifty sharks!" exclaimed Ned Land.
  6220	
  6221	"Did I say sharks?" said I hurriedly.  "I meant to say a hundred and
  6222	fifty pearls.  Sharks would not be sense."
  6223	
  6224	"Certainly not," said Conseil; "but will you tell us now by what means
  6225	they extract these pearls?"
  6226	
  6227	"They proceed in various ways.  When they adhere to the shell, the
  6228	fishermen often pull them off with pincers; but the most common way is
  6229	to lay the oysters on mats of the seaweed which covers the banks.  Thus
  6230	they die in the open air; and at the end of ten days they are in a
  6231	forward state of decomposition.  They are then plunged into large
  6232	reservoirs of sea-water; then they are opened and washed."
  6233	
  6234	"The price of these pearls varies according to their size?" asked
  6235	Conseil.
  6236	
  6237	"Not only according to their size," I answered, "but also according to
  6238	their shape, their water (that is, their colour), and their lustre:
  6239	that is, that bright and diapered sparkle which makes them so charming
  6240	to the eye.  The most beautiful are called virgin pearls, or paragons.
  6241	They are formed alone in the tissue of the mollusc, are white, often
  6242	opaque, and sometimes have the transparency of an opal; they are
  6243	generally round or oval.  The round are made into bracelets, the oval
  6244	into pendants, and, being more precious, are sold singly.  Those
  6245	adhering to the shell of the oyster are more irregular in shape, and
  6246	are sold by weight.  Lastly, in a lower order are classed those small
  6247	pearls known under the name of seed-pearls; they are sold by measure,
  6248	and are especially used in embroidery for church ornaments."
  6249	
  6250	"But," said Conseil, "is this pearl-fishery dangerous?"
  6251	
  6252	"No," I answered, quickly; "particularly if certain precautions are
  6253	taken."
  6254	
  6255	"What does one risk in such a calling?" said Ned Land, "the swallowing
  6256	of some mouthfuls of sea-water?"
  6257	
  6258	"As you say, Ned.  By the bye," said I, trying to take Captain Nemo's
  6259	careless tone, "are you afraid of sharks, brave Ned?"
  6260	
  6261	"I!" replied the Canadian; "a harpooner by profession?  It is my trade
  6262	to make light of them."
  6263	
  6264	"But," said I, "it is not a question of fishing for them with an
  6265	iron-swivel, hoisting them into the vessel, cutting off their tails
  6266	with a blow of a chopper, ripping them up, and throwing their heart
  6267	into the sea!"
  6268	
  6269	"Then, it is a question of----"
  6270	
  6271	"Precisely."
  6272	
  6273	"In the water?"
  6274	
  6275	"In the water."
  6276	
  6277	"Faith, with a good harpoon!  You know, sir, these sharks are
  6278	ill-fashioned beasts.  They turn on their bellies to seize you, and in
  6279	that time----"
  6280	
  6281	Ned Land had a way of saying "seize" which made my blood run cold.
  6282	
  6283	"Well, and you, Conseil, what do you think of sharks?"
  6284	
  6285	"Me!" said Conseil.  "I will be frank, sir."
  6286	
  6287	"So much the better," thought I.
  6288	
  6289	"If you, sir, mean to face the sharks, I do not see why your faithful
  6290	servant should not face them with you."
  6291	
  6292	
  6293	
  6294	CHAPTER III
  6295	
  6296	A PEARL OF TEN MILLIONS
  6297	
  6298	The next morning at four o'clock I was awakened by the steward whom
  6299	Captain Nemo had placed at my service.  I rose hurriedly, dressed, and
  6300	went into the saloon.
  6301	
  6302	Captain Nemo was awaiting me.
  6303	
  6304	"M. Aronnax," said he, "are you ready to start?"
  6305	
  6306	"I am ready."
  6307	
  6308	"Then please to follow me."
  6309	
  6310	"And my companions, Captain?"
  6311	
  6312	"They have been told and are waiting."
  6313	
  6314	"Are we not to put on our diver's dresses?" asked I.
  6315	
  6316	"Not yet.  I have not allowed the Nautilus to come too near this coast,
  6317	and we are some distance from the Manaar Bank; but the boat is ready,
  6318	and will take us to the exact point of disembarking, which will save us
  6319	a long way.  It carries our diving apparatus, which we will put on when
  6320	we begin our submarine journey."
  6321	
  6322	Captain Nemo conducted me to the central staircase, which led on the
  6323	platform.  Ned and Conseil were already there, delighted at the idea of
  6324	the "pleasure party" which was preparing.  Five sailors from the
  6325	Nautilus, with their oars, waited in the boat, which had been made fast
  6326	against the side.
  6327	
  6328	The night was still dark.  Layers of clouds covered the sky, allowing
  6329	but few stars to be seen.  I looked on the side where the land lay, and
  6330	saw nothing but a dark line enclosing three parts of the horizon, from
  6331	south-west to north west.  The Nautilus, having returned during the
  6332	night up the western coast of Ceylon, was now west of the bay, or
  6333	rather gulf, formed by the mainland and the Island of Manaar.  There,
  6334	under the dark waters, stretched the pintadine bank, an inexhaustible
  6335	field of pearls, the length of which is more than twenty miles.
  6336	
  6337	Captain Nemo, Ned Land, Conseil, and I took our places in the stern of
  6338	the boat.  The master went to the tiller; his four companions leaned on
  6339	their oars, the painter was cast off, and we sheered off.
  6340	
  6341	The boat went towards the south; the oarsmen did not hurry.  I noticed
  6342	that their strokes, strong in the water, only followed each other every
  6343	ten seconds, according to the method generally adopted in the navy.
  6344	Whilst the craft was running by its own velocity, the liquid drops
  6345	struck the dark depths of the waves crisply like spats of melted lead.
  6346	A little billow, spreading wide, gave a slight roll to the boat, and
  6347	some samphire reeds flapped before it.
  6348	
  6349	We were silent.  What was Captain Nemo thinking of?  Perhaps of the
  6350	land he was approaching, and which he found too near to him, contrary
  6351	to the Canadian's opinion, who thought it too far off.  As to Conseil,
  6352	he was merely there from curiosity.
  6353	
  6354	About half-past five the first tints on the horizon showed the upper
  6355	line of coast more distinctly.  Flat enough in the east, it rose a
  6356	little to the south.  Five miles still lay between us, and it was
  6357	indistinct owing to the mist on the water.  At six o'clock it became
  6358	suddenly daylight, with that rapidity peculiar to tropical regions,
  6359	which know neither dawn nor twilight.  The solar rays pierced the
  6360	curtain of clouds, piled up on the eastern horizon, and the radiant orb
  6361	rose rapidly.  I saw land distinctly, with a few trees scattered here
  6362	and there.  The boat neared Manaar Island, which was rounded to the
  6363	south.  Captain Nemo rose from his seat and watched the sea.
  6364	
  6365	At a sign from him the anchor was dropped, but the chain scarcely ran,
  6366	for it was little more than a yard deep, and this spot was one of the
  6367	highest points of the bank of pintadines.
  6368	
  6369	"Here we are, M. Aronnax," said Captain Nemo.  "You see that enclosed
  6370	bay?  Here, in a month will be assembled the numerous fishing boats of
  6371	the exporters, and these are the waters their divers will ransack so
  6372	boldly.  Happily, this bay is well situated for that kind of fishing.
  6373	It is sheltered from the strongest winds; the sea is never very rough
  6374	here, which makes it favourable for the diver's work.  We will now put
  6375	on our dresses, and begin our walk."
  6376	
  6377	I did not answer, and, while watching the suspected waves, began with
  6378	the help of the sailors to put on my heavy sea-dress. Captain Nemo and
  6379	my companions were also dressing.  None of the Nautilus men were to
  6380	accompany us on this new excursion.
  6381	
  6382	Soon we were enveloped to the throat in india-rubber clothing; the air
  6383	apparatus fixed to our backs by braces.  As to the Ruhmkorff apparatus,
  6384	there was no necessity for it.  Before putting my head into the copper
  6385	cap, I had asked the question of the Captain.
  6386	
  6387	"They would be useless," he replied.  "We are going to no great depth,
  6388	and the solar rays will be enough to light our walk.  Besides, it would
  6389	not be prudent to carry the electric light in these waters; its
  6390	brilliancy might attract some of the dangerous inhabitants of the coast
  6391	most inopportunely."
  6392	
  6393	As Captain Nemo pronounced these words, I turned to Conseil and Ned
  6394	Land.  But my two friends had already encased their heads in the metal
  6395	cap, and they could neither hear nor answer.
  6396	
  6397	One last question remained to ask of Captain Nemo.
  6398	
  6399	"And our arms?" asked I; "our guns?"
  6400	
  6401	"Guns!  What for?  Do not mountaineers attack the bear with a dagger in
  6402	their hand, and is not steel surer than lead?  Here is a strong blade;
  6403	put it in your belt, and we start."
  6404	
  6405	I looked at my companions; they were armed like us, and, more than
  6406	that, Ned Land was brandishing an enormous harpoon, which he had placed
  6407	in the boat before leaving the Nautilus.
  6408	
  6409	Then, following the Captain's example, I allowed myself to be dressed
  6410	in the heavy copper helmet, and our reservoirs of air were at once in
  6411	activity.  An instant after we were landed, one after the other, in
  6412	about two yards of water upon an even sand.  Captain Nemo made a sign
  6413	with his hand, and we followed him by a gentle declivity till we
  6414	disappeared under the waves.
  6415	
  6416	Over our feet, like coveys of snipe in a bog, rose shoals of fish, of
  6417	the genus monoptera, which have no other fins but their tail. I
  6418	recognized the Javanese, a real serpent two and a half feet long, of a
  6419	livid colour underneath, and which might easily be mistaken for a
  6420	conger eel if it were not for the golden stripes on its side. In the
  6421	genus stromateus, whose bodies are very flat and oval, I saw some of
  6422	the most brilliant colours, carrying their dorsal fin like a scythe; an
  6423	excellent eating fish, which, dried and pickled, is known by the name
  6424	of Karawade; then some tranquebars, belonging to the genus
  6425	apsiphoroides, whose body is covered with a shell cuirass of eight
  6426	longitudinal plates.
  6427	
  6428	The heightening sun lit the mass of waters more and more. The soil
  6429	changed by degrees. To the fine sand succeeded a perfect causeway of
  6430	boulders, covered with a carpet of molluscs and zoophytes. Amongst the
  6431	specimens of these branches I noticed some placenae, with thin unequal
  6432	shells, a kind of ostracion peculiar to the Red Sea and the Indian
  6433	Ocean; some orange lucinae with rounded shells; rockfish three feet and
  6434	a half long, which raised themselves under the waves like hands ready
  6435	to seize one. There were also some panopyres, slightly luminous; and
  6436	lastly, some oculines, like magnificent fans, forming one of the
  6437	richest vegetations of these seas.
  6438	
  6439	In the midst of these living plants, and under the arbours of the
  6440	hydrophytes, were layers of clumsy articulates, particularly some
  6441	raninae, whose carapace formed a slightly rounded triangle; and some
  6442	horrible looking parthenopes.
  6443	
  6444	At about seven o'clock we found ourselves at last surveying the
  6445	oyster-banks on which the pearl-oysters are reproduced by millions.
  6446	
  6447	Captain Nemo pointed with his hand to the enormous heap of oysters; and
  6448	I could well understand that this mine was inexhaustible, for Nature's
  6449	creative power is far beyond man's instinct of destruction.  Ned Land,
  6450	faithful to his instinct, hastened to fill a net which he carried by
  6451	his side with some of the finest specimens.  But we could not stop.  We
  6452	must follow the Captain, who seemed to guide him self by paths known
  6453	only to himself.  The ground was sensibly rising, and sometimes, on
  6454	holding up my arm, it was above the surface of the sea.  Then the level
  6455	of the bank would sink capriciously.  Often we rounded high rocks
  6456	scarped into pyramids.  In their dark fractures huge crustacea, perched
  6457	upon their high claws like some war-machine, watched us with fixed
  6458	eyes, and under our feet crawled various kinds of annelides.
  6459	
  6460	At this moment there opened before us a large grotto dug in a
  6461	picturesque heap of rocks and carpeted with all the thick warp of the
  6462	submarine flora.  At first it seemed very dark to me.  The solar rays
  6463	seemed to be extinguished by successive gradations, until its vague
  6464	transparency became nothing more than drowned light.  Captain Nemo
  6465	entered; we followed.  My eyes soon accustomed themselves to this
  6466	relative state of darkness.  I could distinguish the arches springing
  6467	capriciously from natural pillars, standing broad upon their granite
  6468	base, like the heavy columns of Tuscan architecture.  Why had our
  6469	incomprehensible guide led us to the bottom of this submarine crypt?  I
  6470	was soon to know.  After descending a rather sharp declivity, our feet
  6471	trod the bottom of a kind of circular pit.  There Captain Nemo stopped,
  6472	and with his hand indicated an object I had not yet perceived.  It was
  6473	an oyster of extraordinary dimensions, a gigantic tridacne, a goblet
  6474	which could have contained a whole lake of holy-water, a basin the
  6475	breadth of which was more than two yards and a half, and consequently
  6476	larger than that ornamenting the saloon of the Nautilus.  I approached
  6477	this extraordinary mollusc.  It adhered by its filaments to a table of
  6478	granite, and there, isolated, it developed itself in the calm waters of
  6479	the grotto.  I estimated the weight of this tridacne at 600 lb.  Such
  6480	an oyster would contain 30 lb. of meat; and one must have the stomach
  6481	of a Gargantua to demolish some dozens of them.
  6482	
  6483	Captain Nemo was evidently acquainted with the existence of this
  6484	bivalve, and seemed to have a particular motive in verifying the actual
  6485	state of this tridacne.  The shells were a little open; the Captain
  6486	came near and put his dagger between to prevent them from closing; then
  6487	with his hand he raised the membrane with its fringed edges, which
  6488	formed a cloak for the creature.  There, between the folded plaits, I
  6489	saw a loose pearl, whose size equalled that of a coco-nut. Its globular
  6490	shape, perfect clearness, and admirable lustre made it altogether a
  6491	jewel of inestimable value.  Carried away by my curiosity, I stretched
  6492	out my hand to seize it, weigh it, and touch it; but the Captain
  6493	stopped me, made a sign of refusal, and quickly withdrew his dagger,
  6494	and the two shells closed suddenly.  I then understood Captain Nemo's
  6495	intention.  In leaving this pearl hidden in the mantle of the tridacne
  6496	he was allowing it to grow slowly.  Each year the secretions of the
  6497	mollusc would add new concentric circles.  I estimated its value at
  6498	L500,000 at least.
  6499	
  6500	After ten minutes Captain Nemo stopped suddenly.  I thought he had
  6501	halted previously to returning.  No; by a gesture he bade us crouch
  6502	beside him in a deep fracture of the rock, his hand pointed to one part
  6503	of the liquid mass, which I watched attentively.
  6504	
  6505	About five yards from me a shadow appeared, and sank to the ground.
  6506	The disquieting idea of sharks shot through my mind, but I was
  6507	mistaken; and once again it was not a monster of the ocean that we had
  6508	anything to do with.
  6509	
  6510	It was a man, a living man, an Indian, a fisherman, a poor devil who, I
  6511	suppose, had come to glean before the harvest.  I could see the bottom
  6512	of his canoe anchored some feet above his head.  He dived and went up
  6513	successively.  A stone held between his feet, cut in the shape of a
  6514	sugar loaf, whilst a rope fastened him to his boat, helped him to
  6515	descend more rapidly.  This was all his apparatus.  Reaching the
  6516	bottom, about five yards deep, he went on his knees and filled his bag
  6517	with oysters picked up at random.  Then he went up, emptied it, pulled
  6518	up his stone, and began the operation once more, which lasted thirty
  6519	seconds.
  6520	
  6521	The diver did not see us.  The shadow of the rock hid us from sight.
  6522	And how should this poor Indian ever dream that men, beings like
  6523	himself, should be there under the water watching his movements and
  6524	losing no detail of the fishing?  Several times he went up in this way,
  6525	and dived again.  He did not carry away more than ten at each plunge,
  6526	for he was obliged to pull them from the bank to which they adhered by
  6527	means of their strong byssus.  And how many of those oysters for which
  6528	he risked his life had no pearl in them!  I watched him closely; his
  6529	manoeuvres were regular; and for the space of half an hour no danger
  6530	appeared to threaten him.
  6531	
  6532	I was beginning to accustom myself to the sight of this interesting
  6533	fishing, when suddenly, as the Indian was on the ground, I saw him make
  6534	a gesture of terror, rise, and make a spring to return to the surface
  6535	of the sea.
  6536	
  6537	I understood his dread.  A gigantic shadow appeared just above the
  6538	unfortunate diver.  It was a shark of enormous size advancing
  6539	diagonally, his eyes on fire, and his jaws open.  I was mute with
  6540	horror and unable to move.
  6541	
  6542	The voracious creature shot towards the Indian, who threw himself on
  6543	one side to avoid the shark's fins; but not its tail, for it struck his
  6544	chest and stretched him on the ground.
  6545	
  6546	This scene lasted but a few seconds:  the shark returned, and, turning
  6547	on his back, prepared himself for cutting the Indian in two, when I saw
  6548	Captain Nemo rise suddenly, and then, dagger in hand, walk straight to
  6549	the monster, ready to fight face to face with him.  The very moment the
  6550	shark was going to snap the unhappy fisherman in two, he perceived his
  6551	new adversary, and, turning over, made straight towards him.
  6552	
  6553	I can still see Captain Nemo's position.  Holding himself well
  6554	together, he waited for the shark with admirable coolness; and, when it
  6555	rushed at him, threw himself on one side with wonderful quickness,
  6556	avoiding the shock, and burying his dagger deep into its side.  But it
  6557	was not all over.  A terrible combat ensued.
  6558	
  6559	The shark had seemed to roar, if I might say so.  The blood rushed in
  6560	torrents from its wound.  The sea was dyed red, and through the opaque
  6561	liquid I could distinguish nothing more.  Nothing more until the moment
  6562	when, like lightning, I saw the undaunted Captain hanging on to one of
  6563	the creature's fins, struggling, as it were, hand to hand with the
  6564	monster, and dealing successive blows at his enemy, yet still unable to
  6565	give a decisive one.
  6566	
  6567	The shark's struggles agitated the water with such fury that the
  6568	rocking threatened to upset me.
  6569	
  6570	I wanted to go to the Captain's assistance, but, nailed to the spot
  6571	with horror, I could not stir.
  6572	
  6573	I saw the haggard eye; I saw the different phases of the fight.  The
  6574	Captain fell to the earth, upset by the enormous mass which leant upon
  6575	him.  The shark's jaws opened wide, like a pair of factory shears, and
  6576	it would have been all over with the Captain; but, quick as thought,
  6577	harpoon in hand, Ned Land rushed towards the shark and struck it with
  6578	its sharp point.
  6579	
  6580	The waves were impregnated with a mass of blood.  They rocked under the
  6581	shark's movements, which beat them with indescribable fury.  Ned Land
  6582	had not missed his aim.  It was the monster's death-rattle.  Struck to
  6583	the heart, it struggled in dreadful convulsions, the shock of which
  6584	overthrew Conseil.
  6585	
  6586	But Ned Land had disentangled the Captain, who, getting up without any
  6587	wound, went straight to the Indian, quickly cut the cord which held him
  6588	to his stone, took him in his arms, and, with a sharp blow of his heel,
  6589	mounted to the surface.
  6590	
  6591	We all three followed in a few seconds, saved by a miracle, and reached
  6592	the fisherman's boat.
  6593	
  6594	Captain Nemo's first care was to recall the unfortunate man to life
  6595	again.  I did not think he could succeed.  I hoped so, for the poor
  6596	creature's immersion was not long; but the blow from the shark's tail
  6597	might have been his death-blow.
  6598	
  6599	Happily, with the Captain's and Conseil's sharp friction, I saw
  6600	consciousness return by degrees.  He opened his eyes.  What was his
  6601	surprise, his terror even, at seeing four great copper heads leaning
  6602	over him!  And, above all, what must he have thought when Captain Nemo,
  6603	drawing from the pocket of his dress a bag of pearls, placed it in his
  6604	hand!  This munificent charity from the man of the waters to the poor
  6605	Cingalese was accepted with a trembling hand.  His wondering eyes
  6606	showed that he knew not to what super-human beings he owed both fortune
  6607	and life.
  6608	
  6609	At a sign from the Captain we regained the bank, and, following the
  6610	road already traversed, came in about half an hour to the anchor which
  6611	held the canoe of the Nautilus to the earth.
  6612	
  6613	Once on board, we each, with the help of the sailors, got rid of the
  6614	heavy copper helmet.
  6615	
  6616	Captain Nemo's first word was to the Canadian.
  6617	
  6618	"Thank you, Master Land," said he.
  6619	
  6620	"It was in revenge, Captain," replied Ned Land.  "I owed you that."
  6621	
  6622	A ghastly smile passed across the Captain's lips, and that was all.
  6623	
  6624	"To the Nautilus," said he.
  6625	
  6626	The boat flew over the waves.  Some minutes after we met the shark's
  6627	dead body floating.  By the black marking of the extremity of its fins,
  6628	I recognised the terrible melanopteron of the Indian Seas, of the
  6629	species of shark so properly called.  It was more than twenty-five feet
  6630	long; its enormous mouth occupied one-third of its body.  It was an
  6631	adult, as was known by its six rows of teeth placed in an isosceles
  6632	triangle in the upper jaw.
  6633	
  6634	Whilst I was contemplating this inert mass, a dozen of these voracious
  6635	beasts appeared round the boat; and, without noticing us, threw
  6636	themselves upon the dead body and fought with one another for the
  6637	pieces.
  6638	
  6639	At half-past eight we were again on board the Nautilus.  There I
  6640	reflected on the incidents which had taken place in our excursion to
  6641	the Manaar Bank.
  6642	
  6643	Two conclusions I must inevitably draw from it--one bearing upon the
  6644	unparalleled courage of Captain Nemo, the other upon his devotion to a
  6645	human being, a representative of that race from which he fled beneath
  6646	the sea.  Whatever he might say, this strange man had not yet succeeded
  6647	in entirely crushing his heart.
  6648	
  6649	When I made this observation to him, he answered in a slightly moved
  6650	tone:
  6651	
  6652	"That Indian, sir, is an inhabitant of an oppressed country; and I am
  6653	still, and shall be, to my last breath, one of them!"
  6654	
  6655	
  6656	
  6657	CHAPTER IV
  6658	
  6659	THE RED SEA
  6660	
  6661	In the course of the day of the 29th of January, the island of Ceylon
  6662	disappeared under the horizon, and the Nautilus, at a speed of twenty
  6663	miles an hour, slid into the labyrinth of canals which separate the
  6664	Maldives from the Laccadives.  It coasted even the Island of Kiltan, a
  6665	land originally coraline, discovered by Vasco da Gama in 1499, and one
  6666	of the nineteen principal islands of the Laccadive Archipelago,
  6667	situated between 10° and 14° 30' N. lat., and 69° 50' 72"
  6668	E. long.
  6669	
  6670	We had made 16,220 miles, or 7,500 (French) leagues from our
  6671	starting-point in the Japanese Seas.
  6672	
  6673	The next day (30th January), when the Nautilus went to the surface of
  6674	the ocean there was no land in sight.  Its course was N.N.E., in the
  6675	direction of the Sea of Oman, between Arabia and the Indian Peninsula,
  6676	which serves as an outlet to the Persian Gulf.  It was evidently a
  6677	block without any possible egress.  Where was Captain Nemo taking us
  6678	to?  I could not say.  This, however, did not satisfy the Canadian, who
  6679	that day came to me asking where we were going.
  6680	
  6681	"We are going where our Captain's fancy takes us, Master Ned."
  6682	
  6683	"His fancy cannot take us far, then," said the Canadian.  "The Persian
  6684	Gulf has no outlet:  and, if we do go in, it will not be long before we
  6685	are out again."
  6686	
  6687	"Very well, then, we will come out again, Master Land; and if, after
  6688	the Persian Gulf, the Nautilus would like to visit the Red Sea, the
  6689	Straits of Bab-el-mandeb are there to give us entrance."
  6690	
  6691	"I need not tell you, sir," said Ned Land, "that the Red Sea is as much
  6692	closed as the Gulf, as the Isthmus of Suez is not yet cut; and, if it
  6693	was, a boat as mysterious as ours would not risk itself in a canal cut
  6694	with sluices.  And again, the Red Sea is not the road to take us back
  6695	to Europe."
  6696	
  6697	"But I never said we were going back to Europe."
  6698	
  6699	"What do you suppose, then?"
  6700	
  6701	"I suppose that, after visiting the curious coasts of Arabia and Egypt,
  6702	the Nautilus will go down the Indian Ocean again, perhaps cross the
  6703	Channel of Mozambique, perhaps off the Mascarenhas, so as to gain the
  6704	Cape of Good Hope."
  6705	
  6706	"And once at the Cape of Good Hope?" asked the Canadian, with peculiar
  6707	emphasis.
  6708	
  6709	"Well, we shall penetrate into that Atlantic which we do not yet know.
  6710	Ah! friend Ned, you are getting tired of this journey under the sea;
  6711	you are surfeited with the incessantly varying spectacle of submarine
  6712	wonders.  For my part, I shall be sorry to see the end of a voyage
  6713	which it is given to so few men to make."
  6714	
  6715	For four days, till the 3rd of February, the Nautilus scoured the Sea
  6716	of Oman, at various speeds and at various depths.  It seemed to go at
  6717	random, as if hesitating as to which road it should follow, but we
  6718	never passed the Tropic of Cancer.
  6719	
  6720	In quitting this sea we sighted Muscat for an instant, one of the most
  6721	important towns of the country of Oman.  I admired its strange aspect,
  6722	surrounded by black rocks upon which its white houses and forts stood
  6723	in relief.  I saw the rounded domes of its mosques, the elegant points
  6724	of its minarets, its fresh and verdant terraces.  But it was only a
  6725	vision!  The Nautilus soon sank under the waves of that part of the sea.
  6726	
  6727	We passed along the Arabian coast of Mahrah and Hadramaut, for a
  6728	distance of six miles, its undulating line of mountains being
  6729	occasionally relieved by some ancient ruin.  The 5th of February we at
  6730	last entered the Gulf of Aden, a perfect funnel introduced into the
  6731	neck of Bab-el-mandeb, through which the Indian waters entered the Red
  6732	Sea.
  6733	
  6734	The 6th of February, the Nautilus floated in sight of Aden, perched
  6735	upon a promontory which a narrow isthmus joins to the mainland, a kind
  6736	of inaccessible Gibraltar, the fortifications of which were rebuilt by
  6737	the English after taking possession in 1839.  I caught a glimpse of the
  6738	octagon minarets of this town, which was at one time the richest
  6739	commercial magazine on the coast.
  6740	
  6741	I certainly thought that Captain Nemo, arrived at this point, would
  6742	back out again; but I was mistaken, for he did no such thing, much to
  6743	my surprise.
  6744	
  6745	The next day, the 7th of February, we entered the Straits of
  6746	Bab-el-mandeb, the name of which, in the Arab tongue, means The Gate of
  6747	Tears.
  6748	
  6749	To twenty miles in breadth, it is only thirty-two in length.  And for
  6750	the Nautilus, starting at full speed, the crossing was scarcely the
  6751	work of an hour.  But I saw nothing, not even the Island of Perim, with
  6752	which the British Government has fortified the position of Aden.  There
  6753	were too many English or French steamers of the line of Suez to Bombay,
  6754	Calcutta to Melbourne, and from Bourbon to the Mauritius, furrowing
  6755	this narrow passage, for the Nautilus to venture to show itself.  So it
  6756	remained prudently below.  At last about noon, we were in the waters of
  6757	the Red Sea.
  6758	
  6759	I would not even seek to understand the caprice which had decided
  6760	Captain Nemo upon entering the gulf.  But I quite approved of the
  6761	Nautilus entering it.  Its speed was lessened:  sometimes it kept on
  6762	the surface, sometimes it dived to avoid a vessel, and thus I was able
  6763	to observe the upper and lower parts of this curious sea.
  6764	
  6765	The 8th of February, from the first dawn of day, Mocha came in sight,
  6766	now a ruined town, whose walls would fall at a gunshot, yet which
  6767	shelters here and there some verdant date-trees; once an important
  6768	city, containing six public markets, and twenty-six mosques, and whose
  6769	walls, defended by fourteen forts, formed a girdle of two miles in
  6770	circumference.
  6771	
  6772	The Nautilus then approached the African shore, where the depth of the
  6773	sea was greater.  There, between two waters clear as crystal, through
  6774	the open panels we were allowed to contemplate the beautiful bushes of
  6775	brilliant coral and large blocks of rock clothed with a splendid fur of
  6776	green variety of sites and landscapes along these sandbanks and algae
  6777	and fuci.  What an indescribable spectacle, and what variety of sites
  6778	and landscapes along these sandbanks and volcanic islands which bound
  6779	the Libyan coast!  But where these shrubs appeared in all their beauty
  6780	was on the eastern coast, which the Nautilus soon gained.  It was on
  6781	the coast of Tehama, for there not only did this display of zoophytes
  6782	flourish beneath the level of the sea, but they also formed picturesque
  6783	interlacings which unfolded themselves about sixty feet above the
  6784	surface, more capricious but less highly coloured than those whose
  6785	freshness was kept up by the vital power of the waters.
  6786	
  6787	What charming hours I passed thus at the window of the saloon!  What
  6788	new specimens of submarine flora and fauna did I admire under the
  6789	brightness of our electric lantern!
  6790	
  6791	The 9th of February the Nautilus floated in the broadest part of the
  6792	Red Sea, which is comprised between Souakin, on the west coast, and
  6793	Komfidah, on the east coast, with a diameter of ninety miles.
  6794	
  6795	That day at noon, after the bearings were taken, Captain Nemo mounted
  6796	the platform, where I happened to be, and I was determined not to let
  6797	him go down again without at least pressing him regarding his ulterior
  6798	projects.  As soon as he saw me he approached and graciously offered me
  6799	a cigar.
  6800	
  6801	"Well, sir, does this Red Sea please you?  Have you sufficiently
  6802	observed the wonders it covers, its fishes, its zoophytes, its
  6803	parterres of sponges, and its forests of coral?  Did you catch a
  6804	glimpse of the towns on its borders?"
  6805	
  6806	"Yes, Captain Nemo," I replied; "and the Nautilus is wonderfully fitted
  6807	for such a study.  Ah! it is an intelligent boat!"
  6808	
  6809	"Yes, sir, intelligent and invulnerable.  It fears neither the terrible
  6810	tempests of the Red Sea, nor its currents, nor its sandbanks."
  6811	
  6812	"Certainly," said I, "this sea is quoted as one of the worst, and in
  6813	the time of the ancients, if I am not mistaken, its reputation was
  6814	detestable."
  6815	
  6816	"Detestable, M. Aronnax.  The Greek and Latin historians do not speak
  6817	favourably of it, and Strabo says it is very dangerous during the
  6818	Etesian winds and in the rainy season.  The Arabian Edrisi portrays it
  6819	under the name of the Gulf of Colzoum, and relates that vessels
  6820	perished there in great numbers on the sandbanks and that no one would
  6821	risk sailing in the night.  It is, he pretends, a sea subject to
  6822	fearful hurricanes, strewn with inhospitable islands, and `which offers
  6823	nothing good either on its surface or in its depths.'"
  6824	
  6825	"One may see," I replied, "that these historians never sailed on board
  6826	the Nautilus."
  6827	
  6828	"Just so," replied the Captain, smiling; "and in that respect moderns
  6829	are not more advanced than the ancients.  It required many ages to find
  6830	out the mechanical power of steam.  Who knows if, in another hundred
  6831	years, we may not see a second Nautilus?  Progress is slow, M. Aronnax."
  6832	
  6833	"It is true," I answered; "your boat is at least a century before its
  6834	time, perhaps an era.  What a misfortune that the secret of such an
  6835	invention should die with its inventor!"
  6836	
  6837	Captain Nemo did not reply.  After some minutes' silence he continued:
  6838	
  6839	"You were speaking of the opinions of ancient historians upon the
  6840	dangerous navigation of the Red Sea."
  6841	
  6842	"It is true," said I; "but were not their fears exaggerated?"
  6843	
  6844	"Yes and no, M. Aronnax," replied Captain Nemo, who seemed to know the
  6845	Red Sea by heart.  "That which is no longer dangerous for a modern
  6846	vessel, well rigged, strongly built, and master of its own course,
  6847	thanks to obedient steam, offered all sorts of perils to the ships of
  6848	the ancients.  Picture to yourself those first navigators venturing in
  6849	ships made of planks sewn with the cords of the palmtree, saturated
  6850	with the grease of the seadog, and covered with powdered resin!  They
  6851	had not even instruments wherewith to take their bearings, and they
  6852	went by guess amongst currents of which they scarcely knew anything.
  6853	Under such conditions shipwrecks were, and must have been, numerous.
  6854	But in our time, steamers running between Suez and the South Seas have
  6855	nothing more to fear from the fury of this gulf, in spite of contrary
  6856	trade-winds. The captain and passengers do not prepare for their
  6857	departure by offering propitiatory sacrifices; and, on their return,
  6858	they no longer go ornamented with wreaths and gilt fillets to thank the
  6859	gods in the neighbouring temple."
  6860	
  6861	"I agree with you," said I; "and steam seems to have killed all
  6862	gratitude in the hearts of sailors.  But, Captain, since you seem to
  6863	have especially studied this sea, can you tell me the origin of its
  6864	name?"
  6865	
  6866	"There exist several explanations on the subject, M. Aronnax.  Would
  6867	you like to know the opinion of a chronicler of the fourteenth century?"
  6868	
  6869	"Willingly."
  6870	
  6871	"This fanciful writer pretends that its name was given to it after the
  6872	passage of the Israelites, when Pharaoh perished in the waves which
  6873	closed at the voice of Moses."
  6874	
  6875	"A poet's explanation, Captain Nemo," I replied; "but I cannot content
  6876	myself with that.  I ask you for your personal opinion."
  6877	
  6878	"Here it is, M. Aronnax.  According to my idea, we must see in this
  6879	appellation of the Red Sea a translation of the Hebrew word `Edom'; and
  6880	if the ancients gave it that name, it was on account of the particular
  6881	colour of its waters."
  6882	
  6883	"But up to this time I have seen nothing but transparent waves and
  6884	without any particular colour."
  6885	
  6886	"Very likely; but as we advance to the bottom of the gulf, you will see
  6887	this singular appearance.  I remember seeing the Bay of Tor entirely
  6888	red, like a sea of blood."
  6889	
  6890	"And you attribute this colour to the presence of a microscopic
  6891	seaweed?"
  6892	
  6893	"Yes."
  6894	
  6895	"So, Captain Nemo, it is not the first time you have overrun the Red
  6896	Sea on board the Nautilus?"
  6897	
  6898	"No, sir."
  6899	
  6900	"As you spoke a while ago of the passage of the Israelites and of the
  6901	catastrophe to the Egyptians, I will ask whether you have met with the
  6902	traces under the water of this great historical fact?"
  6903	
  6904	"No, sir; and for a good reason."
  6905	
  6906	"What is it?"
  6907	
  6908	"It is that the spot where Moses and his people passed is now so
  6909	blocked up with sand that the camels can barely bathe their legs there.
  6910	You can well understand that there would not be water enough for my
  6911	Nautilus."
  6912	
  6913	"And the spot?"  I asked.
  6914	
  6915	"The spot is situated a little above the Isthmus of Suez, in the arm
  6916	which formerly made a deep estuary, when the Red Sea extended to the
  6917	Salt Lakes.  Now, whether this passage were miraculous or not, the
  6918	Israelites, nevertheless, crossed there to reach the Promised Land, and
  6919	Pharaoh's army perished precisely on that spot; and I think that
  6920	excavations made in the middle of the sand would bring to light a large
  6921	number of arms and instruments of Egyptian origin."
  6922	
  6923	"That is evident," I replied; "and for the sake of archaeologists let
  6924	us hope that these excavations will be made sooner or later, when new
  6925	towns are established on the isthmus, after the construction of the
  6926	Suez Canal; a canal, however, very useless to a vessel like the
  6927	Nautilus."
  6928	
  6929	"Very likely; but useful to the whole world," said Captain Nemo.  "The
  6930	ancients well understood the utility of a communication between the Red
  6931	Sea and the Mediterranean for their commercial affairs: but they did
  6932	not think of digging a canal direct, and took the Nile as an
  6933	intermediate.  Very probably the canal which united the Nile to the Red
  6934	Sea was begun by Sesostris, if we may believe tradition.  One thing is
  6935	certain, that in the year 615 before Jesus Christ, Necos undertook the
  6936	works of an alimentary canal to the waters of the Nile across the plain
  6937	of Egypt, looking towards Arabia.  It took four days to go up this
  6938	canal, and it was so wide that two triremes could go abreast.  It was
  6939	carried on by Darius, the son of Hystaspes, and probably finished by
  6940	Ptolemy II.  Strabo saw it navigated:  but its decline from the point
  6941	of departure, near Bubastes, to the Red Sea was so slight that it was
  6942	only navigable for a few months in the year.  This canal answered all
  6943	commercial purposes to the age of Antonius, when it was abandoned and
  6944	blocked up with sand.  Restored by order of the Caliph Omar, it was
  6945	definitely destroyed in 761 or 762 by Caliph Al-Mansor, who wished to
  6946	prevent the arrival of provisions to Mohammed-ben-Abdallah, who had
  6947	revolted against him.  During the expedition into Egypt, your General
  6948	Bonaparte discovered traces of the works in the Desert of Suez; and,
  6949	surprised by the tide, he nearly perished before regaining Hadjaroth,
  6950	at the very place where Moses had encamped three thousand years before
  6951	him."
  6952	
  6953	"Well, Captain, what the ancients dared not undertake, this junction
  6954	between the two seas, which will shorten the road from Cadiz to India,
  6955	M. Lesseps has succeeded in doing; and before long he will have changed
  6956	Africa into an immense island."
  6957	
  6958	"Yes, M. Aronnax; you have the right to be proud of your countryman.
  6959	Such a man brings more honour to a nation than great captains.  He
  6960	began, like so many others, with disgust and rebuffs; but he has
  6961	triumphed, for he has the genius of will.  And it is sad to think that
  6962	a work like that, which ought to have been an international work and
  6963	which would have sufficed to make a reign illustrious, should have
  6964	succeeded by the energy of one man.  All honour to M. Lesseps!"
  6965	
  6966	"Yes! honour to the great citizen," I replied, surprised by the manner
  6967	in which Captain Nemo had just spoken.
  6968	
  6969	"Unfortunately," he continued, "I cannot take you through the Suez
  6970	Canal; but you will be able to see the long jetty of Port Said after
  6971	to-morrow, when we shall be in the Mediterranean."
  6972	
  6973	"The Mediterranean!"  I exclaimed.
  6974	
  6975	"Yes, sir; does that astonish you?"
  6976	
  6977	"What astonishes me is to think that we shall be there the day after
  6978	to-morrow."
  6979	
  6980	"Indeed?"
  6981	
  6982	"Yes, Captain, although by this time I ought to have accustomed myself
  6983	to be surprised at nothing since I have been on board your boat."
  6984	
  6985	"But the cause of this surprise?"
  6986	
  6987	"Well! it is the fearful speed you will have to put on the Nautilus, if
  6988	the day after to-morrow she is to be in the Mediterranean, having made
  6989	the round of Africa, and doubled the Cape of Good Hope!"
  6990	
  6991	"Who told you that she would make the round of Africa and double the
  6992	Cape of Good Hope, sir?"
  6993	
  6994	"Well, unless the Nautilus sails on dry land, and passes above the
  6995	isthmus----"
  6996	
  6997	"Or beneath it, M. Aronnax."
  6998	
  6999	"Beneath it?"
  7000	
  7001	"Certainly," replied Captain Nemo quietly.  "A long time ago Nature
  7002	made under this tongue of land what man has this day made on its
  7003	surface."
  7004	
  7005	"What! such a passage exists?"
  7006	
  7007	"Yes; a subterranean passage, which I have named the Arabian Tunnel.
  7008	It takes us beneath Suez and opens into the Gulf of Pelusium."
  7009	
  7010	"But this isthmus is composed of nothing but quick sands?"
  7011	
  7012	"To a certain depth.  But at fifty-five yards only there is a solid
  7013	layer of rock."
  7014	
  7015	"Did you discover this passage by chance?"  I asked more and more
  7016	surprised.
  7017	
  7018	"Chance and reasoning, sir; and by reasoning even more than by chance.
  7019	Not only does this passage exist, but I have profited by it several
  7020	times.  Without that I should not have ventured this day into the
  7021	impassable Red Sea.  I noticed that in the Red Sea and in the
  7022	Mediterranean there existed a certain number of fishes of a kind
  7023	perfectly identical.  Certain of the fact, I asked myself was it
  7024	possible that there was no communication between the two seas?  If
  7025	there was, the subterranean current must necessarily run from the Red
  7026	Sea to the Mediterranean, from the sole cause of difference of level.
  7027	I caught a large number of fishes in the neighbourhood of Suez.  I
  7028	passed a copper ring through their tails, and threw them back into the
  7029	sea.  Some months later, on the coast of Syria, I caught some of my
  7030	fish ornamented with the ring.  Thus the communication between the two
  7031	was proved.  I then sought for it with my Nautilus; I discovered it,
  7032	ventured into it, and before long, sir, you too will have passed
  7033	through my Arabian tunnel!"
  7034	
  7035	
  7036	
  7037	CHAPTER V
  7038	
  7039	THE ARABIAN TUNNEL
  7040	
  7041	That same evening, in 21° 30' N. lat., the Nautilus floated on the
  7042	surface of the sea, approaching the Arabian coast.  I saw Djeddah, the
  7043	most important counting-house of Egypt, Syria, Turkey, and India.  I
  7044	distinguished clearly enough its buildings, the vessels anchored at the
  7045	quays, and those whose draught of water obliged them to anchor in the
  7046	roads.  The sun, rather low on the horizon, struck full on the houses
  7047	of the town, bringing out their whiteness.  Outside, some wooden
  7048	cabins, and some made of reeds, showed the quarter inhabited by the
  7049	Bedouins.  Soon Djeddah was shut out from view by the shadows of night,
  7050	and the Nautilus found herself under water slightly phosphorescent.
  7051	
  7052	The next day, the 10th of February, we sighted several ships running to
  7053	windward.  The Nautilus returned to its submarine navigation; but at
  7054	noon, when her bearings were taken, the sea being deserted, she rose
  7055	again to her waterline.
  7056	
  7057	Accompanied by Ned and Conseil, I seated myself on the platform.  The
  7058	coast on the eastern side looked like a mass faintly printed upon a
  7059	damp fog.
  7060	
  7061	We were leaning on the sides of the pinnace, talking of one thing and
  7062	another, when Ned Land, stretching out his hand towards a spot on the
  7063	sea, said:
  7064	
  7065	"Do you see anything there, sir?"
  7066	
  7067	"No, Ned," I replied; "but I have not your eyes, you know."
  7068	
  7069	"Look well," said Ned, "there, on the starboard beam, about the height
  7070	of the lantern!  Do you not see a mass which seems to move?"
  7071	
  7072	"Certainly," said I, after close attention; "I see something like a
  7073	long black body on the top of the water."
  7074	
  7075	And certainly before long the black object was not more than a mile
  7076	from us.  It looked like a great sandbank deposited in the open sea.
  7077	It was a gigantic dugong!
  7078	
  7079	Ned Land looked eagerly.  His eyes shone with covetousness at the sight
  7080	of the animal.  His hand seemed ready to harpoon it.  One would have
  7081	thought he was awaiting the moment to throw himself into the sea and
  7082	attack it in its element.
  7083	
  7084	At this instant Captain Nemo appeared on the platform.  He saw the
  7085	dugong, understood the Canadian's attitude, and, addressing him, said:
  7086	
  7087	"If you held a harpoon just now, Master Land, would it not burn your
  7088	hand?"
  7089	
  7090	"Just so, sir."
  7091	
  7092	"And you would not be sorry to go back, for one day, to your trade of a
  7093	fisherman and to add this cetacean to the list of those you have
  7094	already killed?"
  7095	
  7096	"I should not, sir."
  7097	
  7098	"Well, you can try."
  7099	
  7100	"Thank you, sir," said Ned Land, his eyes flaming.
  7101	
  7102	"Only," continued the Captain, "I advise you for your own sake not to
  7103	miss the creature."
  7104	
  7105	"Is the dugong dangerous to attack?"  I asked, in spite of the
  7106	Canadian's shrug of the shoulders.
  7107	
  7108	"Yes," replied the Captain; "sometimes the animal turns upon its
  7109	assailants and overturns their boat.  But for Master Land this danger
  7110	is not to be feared.  His eye is prompt, his arm sure."
  7111	
  7112	At this moment seven men of the crew, mute and immovable as ever,
  7113	mounted the platform.  One carried a harpoon and a line similar to
  7114	those employed in catching whales.  The pinnace was lifted from the
  7115	bridge, pulled from its socket, and let down into the sea.  Six oarsmen
  7116	took their seats, and the coxswain went to the tiller.  Ned, Conseil,
  7117	and I went to the back of the boat.
  7118	
  7119	"You are not coming, Captain?"  I asked.
  7120	
  7121	"No, sir; but I wish you good sport."
  7122	
  7123	The boat put off, and, lifted by the six rowers, drew rapidly towards
  7124	the dugong, which floated about two miles from the Nautilus.
  7125	
  7126	Arrived some cables-length from the cetacean, the speed slackened, and
  7127	the oars dipped noiselessly into the quiet waters.  Ned Land, harpoon
  7128	in hand, stood in the fore part of the boat.  The harpoon used for
  7129	striking the whale is generally attached to a very long cord which runs
  7130	out rapidly as the wounded creature draws it after him.  But here the
  7131	cord was not more than ten fathoms long, and the extremity was attached
  7132	to a small barrel which, by floating, was to show the course the dugong
  7133	took under the water.
  7134	
  7135	I stood and carefully watched the Canadian's adversary.  This dugong,
  7136	which also bears the name of the halicore, closely resembles the
  7137	manatee; its oblong body terminated in a lengthened tail, and its
  7138	lateral fins in perfect fingers.  Its difference from the manatee
  7139	consisted in its upper jaw, which was armed with two long and pointed
  7140	teeth which formed on each side diverging tusks.
  7141	
  7142	This dugong which Ned Land was preparing to attack was of colossal
  7143	dimensions; it was more than seven yards long.  It did not move, and
  7144	seemed to be sleeping on the waves, which circumstance made it easier
  7145	to capture.
  7146	
  7147	The boat approached within six yards of the animal.  The oars rested on
  7148	the rowlocks.  I half rose.  Ned Land, his body thrown a little back,
  7149	brandished the harpoon in his experienced hand.
  7150	
  7151	Suddenly a hissing noise was heard, and the dugong disappeared.  The
  7152	harpoon, although thrown with great force; had apparently only struck
  7153	the water.
  7154	
  7155	"Curse it!" exclaimed the Canadian furiously; "I have missed it!"
  7156	
  7157	"No," said I; "the creature is wounded--look at the blood; but your
  7158	weapon has not stuck in his body."
  7159	
  7160	"My harpoon! my harpoon!" cried Ned Land.
  7161	
  7162	The sailors rowed on, and the coxswain made for the floating barrel.
  7163	The harpoon regained, we followed in pursuit of the animal.
  7164	
  7165	The latter came now and then to the surface to breathe.  Its wound had
  7166	not weakened it, for it shot onwards with great rapidity.
  7167	
  7168	The boat, rowed by strong arms, flew on its track.  Several times it
  7169	approached within some few yards, and the Canadian was ready to strike,
  7170	but the dugong made off with a sudden plunge, and it was impossible to
  7171	reach it.
  7172	
  7173	Imagine the passion which excited impatient Ned Land!  He hurled at the
  7174	unfortunate creature the most energetic expletives in the English
  7175	tongue.  For my part, I was only vexed to see the dugong escape all our
  7176	attacks.
  7177	
  7178	We pursued it without relaxation for an hour, and I began to think it
  7179	would prove difficult to capture, when the animal, possessed with the
  7180	perverse idea of vengeance of which he had cause to repent, turned upon
  7181	the pinnace and assailed us in its turn.
  7182	
  7183	This manoeuvre did not escape the Canadian.
  7184	
  7185	"Look out!" he cried.
  7186	
  7187	The coxswain said some words in his outlandish tongue, doubtless
  7188	warning the men to keep on their guard.
  7189	
  7190	The dugong came within twenty feet of the boat, stopped, sniffed the
  7191	air briskly with its large nostrils (not pierced at the extremity, but
  7192	in the upper part of its muzzle). Then, taking a spring, he threw
  7193	himself upon us.
  7194	
  7195	The pinnace could not avoid the shock, and half upset, shipped at least
  7196	two tons of water, which had to be emptied; but, thanks to the
  7197	coxswain, we caught it sideways, not full front, so we were not quite
  7198	overturned.  While Ned Land, clinging to the bows, belaboured the
  7199	gigantic animal with blows from his harpoon, the creature's teeth were
  7200	buried in the gunwale, and it lifted the whole thing out of the water,
  7201	as a lion does a roebuck.  We were upset over one another, and I know
  7202	not how the adventure would have ended, if the Canadian, still enraged
  7203	with the beast, had not struck it to the heart.
  7204	
  7205	I heard its teeth grind on the iron plate, and the dugong disappeared,
  7206	carrying the harpoon with him.  But the barrel soon returned to the
  7207	surface, and shortly after the body of the animal, turned on its back.
  7208	The boat came up with it, took it in tow, and made straight for the
  7209	Nautilus.
  7210	
  7211	It required tackle of enormous strength to hoist the dugong on to the
  7212	platform.  It weighed 10,000 lb.
  7213	
  7214	The next day, 11th February, the larder of the Nautilus was enriched by
  7215	some more delicate game.  A flight of sea-swallows rested on the
  7216	Nautilus.  It was a species of the Sterna nilotica, peculiar to Egypt;
  7217	its beak is black, head grey and pointed, the eye surrounded by white
  7218	spots, the back, wings, and tail of a greyish colour, the belly and
  7219	throat white, and claws red.  They also took some dozen of Nile ducks,
  7220	a wild bird of high flavour, its throat and upper part of the head
  7221	white with black spots.
  7222	
  7223	About five o'clock in the evening we sighted to the north the Cape of
  7224	Ras-Mohammed. This cape forms the extremity of Arabia Petraea,
  7225	comprised between the Gulf of Suez and the Gulf of Acabah.
  7226	
  7227	The Nautilus penetrated into the Straits of Jubal, which leads to the
  7228	Gulf of Suez.  I distinctly saw a high mountain, towering between the
  7229	two gulfs of Ras-Mohammed. It was Mount Horeb, that Sinai at the top of
  7230	which Moses saw God face to face.
  7231	
  7232	At six o'clock the Nautilus, sometimes floating, sometimes immersed,
  7233	passed some distance from Tor, situated at the end of the bay, the
  7234	waters of which seemed tinted with red, an observation already made by
  7235	Captain Nemo.  Then night fell in the midst of a heavy silence,
  7236	sometimes broken by the cries of the pelican and other night-birds, and
  7237	the noise of the waves breaking upon the shore, chafing against the
  7238	rocks, or the panting of some far-off steamer beating the waters of the
  7239	Gulf with its noisy paddles.
  7240	
  7241	From eight to nine o'clock the Nautilus remained some fathoms under the
  7242	water.  According to my calculation we must have been very near Suez.
  7243	Through the panel of the saloon I saw the bottom of the rocks
  7244	brilliantly lit up by our electric lamp.  We seemed to be leaving the
  7245	Straits behind us more and more.
  7246	
  7247	At a quarter-past nine, the vessel having returned to the surface, I
  7248	mounted the platform.  Most impatient to pass through Captain Nemo's
  7249	tunnel, I could not stay in one place, so came to breathe the fresh
  7250	night air.
  7251	
  7252	Soon in the shadow I saw a pale light, half discoloured by the fog,
  7253	shining about a mile from us.
  7254	
  7255	"A floating lighthouse!" said someone near me.
  7256	
  7257	I turned, and saw the Captain.
  7258	
  7259	"It is the floating light of Suez," he continued.  "It will not be long
  7260	before we gain the entrance of the tunnel."
  7261	
  7262	"The entrance cannot be easy?"
  7263	
  7264	"No, sir; for that reason I am accustomed to go into the steersman's
  7265	cage and myself direct our course.  And now, if you will go down, M.
  7266	Aronnax, the Nautilus is going under the waves, and will not return to
  7267	the surface until we have passed through the Arabian Tunnel."
  7268	
  7269	Captain Nemo led me towards the central staircase; half way down he
  7270	opened a door, traversed the upper deck, and landed in the pilot's
  7271	cage, which it may be remembered rose at the extremity of the platform.
  7272	It was a cabin measuring six feet square, very much like that occupied
