  7273	by the pilot on the steamboats of the Mississippi or Hudson.  In the
  7274	midst worked a wheel, placed vertically, and caught to the tiller-rope,
  7275	which ran to the back of the Nautilus.  Four light-ports with
  7276	lenticular glasses, let in a groove in the partition of the cabin,
  7277	allowed the man at the wheel to see in all directions.
  7278	
  7279	This cabin was dark; but soon my eyes accustomed themselves to the
  7280	obscurity, and I perceived the pilot, a strong man, with his hands
  7281	resting on the spokes of the wheel.  Outside, the sea appeared vividly
  7282	lit up by the lantern, which shed its rays from the back of the cabin
  7283	to the other extremity of the platform.
  7284	
  7285	"Now," said Captain Nemo, "let us try to make our passage."
  7286	
  7287	Electric wires connected the pilot's cage with the machinery room, and
  7288	from there the Captain could communicate simultaneously to his Nautilus
  7289	the direction and the speed.  He pressed a metal knob, and at once the
  7290	speed of the screw diminished.
  7291	
  7292	I looked in silence at the high straight wall we were running by at
  7293	this moment, the immovable base of a massive sandy coast.  We followed
  7294	it thus for an hour only some few yards off.
  7295	
  7296	Captain Nemo did not take his eye from the knob, suspended by its two
  7297	concentric circles in the cabin.  At a simple gesture, the pilot
  7298	modified the course of the Nautilus every instant.
  7299	
  7300	I had placed myself at the port-scuttle, and saw some magnificent
  7301	substructures of coral, zoophytes, seaweed, and fucus, agitating their
  7302	enormous claws, which stretched out from the fissures of the rock.
  7303	
  7304	At a quarter-past ten, the Captain himself took the helm.  A large
  7305	gallery, black and deep, opened before us.  The Nautilus went boldly
  7306	into it.  A strange roaring was heard round its sides.  It was the
  7307	waters of the Red Sea, which the incline of the tunnel precipitated
  7308	violently towards the Mediterranean.  The Nautilus went with the
  7309	torrent, rapid as an arrow, in spite of the efforts of the machinery,
  7310	which, in order to offer more effective resistance, beat the waves with
  7311	reversed screw.
  7312	
  7313	On the walls of the narrow passage I could see nothing but brilliant
  7314	rays, straight lines, furrows of fire, traced by the great speed, under
  7315	the brilliant electric light.  My heart beat fast.
  7316	
  7317	At thirty-five minutes past ten, Captain Nemo quitted the helm, and,
  7318	turning to me, said:
  7319	
  7320	"The Mediterranean!"
  7321	
  7322	In less than twenty minutes, the Nautilus, carried along by the
  7323	torrent, had passed through the Isthmus of Suez.
  7324	
  7325	
  7326	
  7327	CHAPTER VI
  7328	
  7329	THE GRECIAN ARCHIPELAGO
  7330	
  7331	The next day, the 12th of February, at the dawn of day, the Nautilus
  7332	rose to the surface.  I hastened on to the platform.  Three miles to
  7333	the south the dim outline of Pelusium was to be seen.  A torrent had
  7334	carried us from one sea to another.  About seven o'clock Ned and
  7335	Conseil joined me.
  7336	
  7337	"Well, Sir Naturalist," said the Canadian, in a slightly jovial tone,
  7338	"and the Mediterranean?"
  7339	
  7340	"We are floating on its surface, friend Ned."
  7341	
  7342	"What!" said Conseil, "this very night."
  7343	
  7344	"Yes, this very night; in a few minutes we have passed this impassable
  7345	isthmus."
  7346	
  7347	"I do not believe it," replied the Canadian.
  7348	
  7349	"Then you are wrong, Master Land," I continued; "this low coast which
  7350	rounds off to the south is the Egyptian coast.  And you who have such
  7351	good eyes, Ned, you can see the jetty of Port Said stretching into the
  7352	sea."
  7353	
  7354	The Canadian looked attentively.
  7355	
  7356	"Certainly you are right, sir, and your Captain is a first-rate man.
  7357	We are in the Mediterranean.  Good!  Now, if you please, let us talk of
  7358	our own little affair, but so that no one hears us."
  7359	
  7360	I saw what the Canadian wanted, and, in any case, I thought it better
  7361	to let him talk, as he wished it; so we all three went and sat down
  7362	near the lantern, where we were less exposed to the spray of the blades.
  7363	
  7364	"Now, Ned, we listen; what have you to tell us?"
  7365	
  7366	"What I have to tell you is very simple.  We are in Europe; and before
  7367	Captain Nemo's caprices drag us once more to the bottom of the Polar
  7368	Seas, or lead us into Oceania, I ask to leave the Nautilus."
  7369	
  7370	I wished in no way to shackle the liberty of my companions, but I
  7371	certainly felt no desire to leave Captain Nemo.
  7372	
  7373	Thanks to him, and thanks to his apparatus, I was each day nearer the
  7374	completion of my submarine studies; and I was rewriting my book of
  7375	submarine depths in its very element.  Should I ever again have such an
  7376	opportunity of observing the wonders of the ocean?  No, certainly not!
  7377	And I could not bring myself to the idea of abandoning the Nautilus
  7378	before the cycle of investigation was accomplished.
  7379	
  7380	"Friend Ned, answer me frankly, are you tired of being on board?  Are
  7381	you sorry that destiny has thrown us into Captain Nemo's hands?"
  7382	
  7383	The Canadian remained some moments without answering.  Then, crossing
  7384	his arms, he said:
  7385	
  7386	"Frankly, I do not regret this journey under the seas.  I shall be glad
  7387	to have made it; but, now that it is made, let us have done with it.
  7388	That is my idea."
  7389	
  7390	"It will come to an end, Ned."
  7391	
  7392	"Where and when?"
  7393	
  7394	"Where I do not know--when I cannot say; or, rather, I suppose it will
  7395	end when these seas have nothing more to teach us."
  7396	
  7397	"Then what do you hope for?" demanded the Canadian.
  7398	
  7399	"That circumstances may occur as well six months hence as now by which
  7400	we may and ought to profit."
  7401	
  7402	"Oh!" said Ned Land, "and where shall we be in six months, if you
  7403	please, Sir Naturalist?"
  7404	
  7405	"Perhaps in China; you know the Nautilus is a rapid traveller.  It goes
  7406	through water as swallows through the air, or as an express on the
  7407	land.  It does not fear frequented seas; who can say that it may not
  7408	beat the coasts of France, England, or America, on which flight may be
  7409	attempted as advantageously as here."
  7410	
  7411	"M. Aronnax," replied the Canadian, "your arguments are rotten at the
  7412	foundation.  You speak in the future, `We shall be there!  we shall be
  7413	here!'  I speak in the present, `We are here, and we must profit by
  7414	it.'"
  7415	
  7416	Ned Land's logic pressed me hard, and I felt myself beaten on that
  7417	ground.  I knew not what argument would now tell in my favour.
  7418	
  7419	"Sir," continued Ned, "let us suppose an impossibility: if Captain Nemo
  7420	should this day offer you your liberty; would you accept it?"
  7421	
  7422	"I do not know," I answered.
  7423	
  7424	"And if," he added, "the offer made you this day was never to be
  7425	renewed, would you accept it?"
  7426	
  7427	"Friend Ned, this is my answer.  Your reasoning is against me.  We must
  7428	not rely on Captain Nemo's good-will. Common prudence forbids him to
  7429	set us at liberty.  On the other side, prudence bids us profit by the
  7430	first opportunity to leave the Nautilus."
  7431	
  7432	"Well, M. Aronnax, that is wisely said."
  7433	
  7434	"Only one observation--just one.  The occasion must be serious, and our
  7435	first attempt must succeed; if it fails, we shall never find another,
  7436	and Captain Nemo will never forgive us."
  7437	
  7438	"All that is true," replied the Canadian.  "But your observation
  7439	applies equally to all attempts at flight, whether in two years' time,
  7440	or in two days'. But the question is still this: If a favourable
  7441	opportunity presents itself, it must be seized."
  7442	
  7443	"Agreed!  And now, Ned, will you tell me what you mean by a favourable
  7444	opportunity?"
  7445	
  7446	"It will be that which, on a dark night, will bring the Nautilus a
  7447	short distance from some European coast."
  7448	
  7449	"And you will try and save yourself by swimming?"
  7450	
  7451	"Yes, if we were near enough to the bank, and if the vessel was
  7452	floating at the time.  Not if the bank was far away, and the boat was
  7453	under the water."
  7454	
  7455	"And in that case?"
  7456	
  7457	"In that case, I should seek to make myself master of the pinnace.  I
  7458	know how it is worked.  We must get inside, and the bolts once drawn,
  7459	we shall come to the surface of the water, without even the pilot, who
  7460	is in the bows, perceiving our flight."
  7461	
  7462	"Well, Ned, watch for the opportunity; but do not forget that a hitch
  7463	will ruin us."
  7464	
  7465	"I will not forget, sir."
  7466	
  7467	"And now, Ned, would you like to know what I think of your project?"
  7468	
  7469	"Certainly, M. Aronnax."
  7470	
  7471	"Well, I think--I do not say I hope--I think that this favourable
  7472	opportunity will never present itself."
  7473	
  7474	"Why not?"
  7475	
  7476	"Because Captain Nemo cannot hide from himself that we have not given
  7477	up all hope of regaining our liberty, and he will be on his guard,
  7478	above all, in the seas and in the sight of European coasts."
  7479	
  7480	"We shall see," replied Ned Land, shaking his head determinedly.
  7481	
  7482	"And now, Ned Land," I added, "let us stop here.  Not another word on
  7483	the subject.  The day that you are ready, come and let us know, and we
  7484	will follow you.  I rely entirely upon you."
  7485	
  7486	Thus ended a conversation which, at no very distant time, led to such
  7487	grave results.  I must say here that facts seemed to confirm my
  7488	foresight, to the Canadian's great despair.  Did Captain Nemo distrust
  7489	us in these frequented seas? or did he only wish to hide himself from
  7490	the numerous vessels, of all nations, which ploughed the Mediterranean?
  7491	I could not tell; but we were oftener between waters and far from the
  7492	coast.  Or, if the Nautilus did emerge, nothing was to be seen but the
  7493	pilot's cage; and sometimes it went to great depths, for, between the
  7494	Grecian Archipelago and Asia Minor we could not touch the bottom by
  7495	more than a thousand fathoms.
  7496	
  7497	Thus I only knew we were near the Island of Carpathos, one of the
  7498	Sporades, by Captain Nemo reciting these lines from Virgil:
  7499	
  7500	    "Est Carpathio Neptuni gurgite vates,
  7501	     Caeruleus Proteus,"
  7502	
  7503	as he pointed to a spot on the planisphere.
  7504	
  7505	It was indeed the ancient abode of Proteus, the old shepherd of
  7506	Neptune's flocks, now the Island of Scarpanto, situated between Rhodes
  7507	and Crete.  I saw nothing but the granite base through the glass panels
  7508	of the saloon.
  7509	
  7510	The next day, the 14th of February, I resolved to employ some hours in
  7511	studying the fishes of the Archipelago; but for some reason or other
  7512	the panels remained hermetically sealed.  Upon taking the course of the
  7513	Nautilus, I found that we were going towards Candia, the ancient Isle
  7514	of Crete.  At the time I embarked on the Abraham Lincoln, the whole of
  7515	this island had risen in insurrection against the despotism of the
  7516	Turks.  But how the insurgents had fared since that time I was
  7517	absolutely ignorant, and it was not Captain Nemo, deprived of all land
  7518	communications, who could tell me.
  7519	
  7520	I made no allusion to this event when that night I found myself alone
  7521	with him in the saloon.  Besides, he seemed to be taciturn and
  7522	preoccupied.  Then, contrary to his custom, he ordered both panels to
  7523	be opened, and, going from one to the other, observed the mass of
  7524	waters attentively.  To what end I could not guess; so, on my side, I
  7525	employed my time in studying the fish passing before my eyes.
  7526	
  7527	In the midst of the waters a man appeared, a diver, carrying at his
  7528	belt a leathern purse.  It was not a body abandoned to the waves; it
  7529	was a living man, swimming with a strong hand, disappearing
  7530	occasionally to take breath at the surface.
  7531	
  7532	I turned towards Captain Nemo, and in an agitated voice exclaimed:
  7533	
  7534	"A man shipwrecked!  He must be saved at any price!"
  7535	
  7536	The Captain did not answer me, but came and leaned against the panel.
  7537	
  7538	The man had approached, and, with his face flattened against the glass,
  7539	was looking at us.
  7540	
  7541	To my great amazement, Captain Nemo signed to him.  The diver answered
  7542	with his hand, mounted immediately to the surface of the water, and did
  7543	not appear again.
  7544	
  7545	"Do not be uncomfortable," said Captain Nemo.  "It is Nicholas of Cape
  7546	Matapan, surnamed Pesca.  He is well known in all the Cyclades.  A bold
  7547	diver! water is his element, and he lives more in it than on land,
  7548	going continually from one island to another, even as far as Crete."
  7549	
  7550	"You know him, Captain?"
  7551	
  7552	"Why not, M. Aronnax?"
  7553	
  7554	Saying which, Captain Nemo went towards a piece of furniture standing
  7555	near the left panel of the saloon.  Near this piece of furniture, I saw
  7556	a chest bound with iron, on the cover of which was a copper plate,
  7557	bearing the cypher of the Nautilus with its device.
  7558	
  7559	At that moment, the Captain, without noticing my presence, opened the
  7560	piece of furniture, a sort of strong box, which held a great many
  7561	ingots.
  7562	
  7563	They were ingots of gold.  From whence came this precious metal, which
  7564	represented an enormous sum?  Where did the Captain gather this gold
  7565	from? and what was he going to do with it?
  7566	
  7567	I did not say one word.  I looked.  Captain Nemo took the ingots one by
  7568	one, and arranged them methodically in the chest, which he filled
  7569	entirely.  I estimated the contents at more than 4,000 lb. weight of
  7570	gold, that is to say, nearly L200,000.
  7571	
  7572	The chest was securely fastened, and the Captain wrote an address on
  7573	the lid, in characters which must have belonged to Modern Greece.
  7574	
  7575	This done, Captain Nemo pressed a knob, the wire of which communicated
  7576	with the quarters of the crew.  Four men appeared, and, not without
  7577	some trouble, pushed the chest out of the saloon.  Then I heard them
  7578	hoisting it up the iron staircase by means of pulleys.
  7579	
  7580	At that moment, Captain Nemo turned to me.
  7581	
  7582	"And you were saying, sir?" said he.
  7583	
  7584	"I was saying nothing, Captain."
  7585	
  7586	"Then, sir, if you will allow me, I will wish you good night."
  7587	
  7588	Whereupon he turned and left the saloon.
  7589	
  7590	I returned to my room much troubled, as one may believe.  I vainly
  7591	tried to sleep--I sought the connecting link between the apparition of
  7592	the diver and the chest filled with gold.  Soon, I felt by certain
  7593	movements of pitching and tossing that the Nautilus was leaving the
  7594	depths and returning to the surface.
  7595	
  7596	Then I heard steps upon the platform; and I knew they were unfastening
  7597	the pinnace and launching it upon the waves.  For one instant it struck
  7598	the side of the Nautilus, then all noise ceased.
  7599	
  7600	Two hours after, the same noise, the same going and coming was renewed;
  7601	the boat was hoisted on board, replaced in its socket, and the Nautilus
  7602	again plunged under the waves.
  7603	
  7604	So these millions had been transported to their address.  To what point
  7605	of the continent?  Who was Captain Nemo's correspondent?
  7606	
  7607	The next day I related to Conseil and the Canadian the events of the
  7608	night, which had excited my curiosity to the highest degree.  My
  7609	companions were not less surprised than myself.
  7610	
  7611	"But where does he take his millions to?" asked Ned Land.
  7612	
  7613	To that there was no possible answer.  I returned to the saloon after
  7614	having breakfast and set to work.  Till five o'clock in the evening I
  7615	employed myself in arranging my notes.  At that moment--(ought I to
  7616	attribute it to some peculiar idiosyncrasy)--I felt so great a heat
  7617	that I was obliged to take off my coat.  It was strange, for we were
  7618	under low latitudes; and even then the Nautilus, submerged as it was,
  7619	ought to experience no change of temperature.  I looked at the
  7620	manometer; it showed a depth of sixty feet, to which atmospheric heat
  7621	could never attain.
  7622	
  7623	I continued my work, but the temperature rose to such a pitch as to be
  7624	intolerable.
  7625	
  7626	"Could there be fire on board?"  I asked myself.
  7627	
  7628	I was leaving the saloon, when Captain Nemo entered; he approached the
  7629	thermometer, consulted it, and, turning to me, said:
  7630	
  7631	"Forty-two degrees."
  7632	
  7633	"I have noticed it, Captain," I replied; "and if it gets much hotter we
  7634	cannot bear it."
  7635	
  7636	"Oh, sir, it will not get better if we do not wish it."
  7637	
  7638	"You can reduce it as you please, then?"
  7639	
  7640	"No; but I can go farther from the stove which produces it."
  7641	
  7642	"It is outward, then!"
  7643	
  7644	"Certainly; we are floating in a current of boiling water."
  7645	
  7646	"Is it possible!"  I exclaimed.
  7647	
  7648	"Look."
  7649	
  7650	The panels opened, and I saw the sea entirely white all round.  A
  7651	sulphurous smoke was curling amid the waves, which boiled like water in
  7652	a copper.  I placed my hand on one of the panes of glass, but the heat
  7653	was so great that I quickly took it off again.
  7654	
  7655	"Where are we?"  I asked.
  7656	
  7657	"Near the Island of Santorin, sir," replied the Captain.  "I wished to
  7658	give you a sight of the curious spectacle of a submarine eruption."
  7659	
  7660	"I thought," said I, "that the formation of these new islands was
  7661	ended."
  7662	
  7663	"Nothing is ever ended in the volcanic parts of the sea," replied
  7664	Captain Nemo; "and the globe is always being worked by subterranean
  7665	fires.  Already, in the nineteenth year of our era, according to
  7666	Cassiodorus and Pliny, a new island, Theia (the divine), appeared in
  7667	the very place where these islets have recently been formed.  Then they
  7668	sank under the waves, to rise again in the year 69, when they again
  7669	subsided.  Since that time to our days the Plutonian work has been
  7670	suspended.  But on the 3rd of February, 1866, a new island, which they
  7671	named George Island, emerged from the midst of the sulphurous vapour
  7672	near Nea Kamenni, and settled again the 6th of the same month.  Seven
  7673	days after, the 13th of February, the Island of Aphroessa appeared,
  7674	leaving between Nea Kamenni and itself a canal ten yards broad.  I was
  7675	in these seas when the phenomenon occurred, and I was able therefore to
  7676	observe all the different phases.  The Island of Aphroessa, of round
  7677	form, measured 300 feet in diameter, and 30 feet in height.  It was
  7678	composed of black and vitreous lava, mixed with fragments of felspar.
  7679	And lastly, on the 10th of March, a smaller island, called Reka, showed
  7680	itself near Nea Kamenni, and since then these three have joined
  7681	together, forming but one and the same island."
  7682	
  7683	"And the canal in which we are at this moment?"  I asked.
  7684	
  7685	"Here it is," replied Captain Nemo, showing me a map of the
  7686	Archipelago.  "You see, I have marked the new islands."
  7687	
  7688	I returned to the glass.  The Nautilus was no longer moving, the heat
  7689	was becoming unbearable.  The sea, which till now had been white, was
  7690	red, owing to the presence of salts of iron.  In spite of the ship's
  7691	being hermetically sealed, an insupportable smell of sulphur filled the
  7692	saloon, and the brilliancy of the electricity was entirely extinguished
  7693	by bright scarlet flames.  I was in a bath, I was choking, I was
  7694	broiled.
  7695	
  7696	"We can remain no longer in this boiling water," said I to the Captain.
  7697	
  7698	"It would not be prudent," replied the impassive Captain Nemo.
  7699	
  7700	An order was given; the Nautilus tacked about and left the furnace it
  7701	could not brave with impunity.  A quarter of an hour after we were
  7702	breathing fresh air on the surface.  The thought then struck me that,
  7703	if Ned Land had chosen this part of the sea for our flight, we should
  7704	never have come alive out of this sea of fire.
  7705	
  7706	The next day, the 16th of February, we left the basin which, between
  7707	Rhodes and Alexandria, is reckoned about 1,500 fathoms in depth, and
  7708	the Nautilus, passing some distance from Cerigo, quitted the Grecian
  7709	Archipelago after having doubled Cape Matapan.
  7710	
  7711	
  7712	
  7713	CHAPTER VII
  7714	
  7715	THE MEDITERRANEAN IN FORTY-EIGHT HOURS
  7716	
  7717	The Mediterranean, the blue sea par excellence, "the great sea" of the
  7718	Hebrews, "the sea" of the Greeks, the "mare nostrum" of the Romans,
  7719	bordered by orange-trees, aloes, cacti, and sea-pines; embalmed with
  7720	the perfume of the myrtle, surrounded by rude mountains, saturated with
  7721	pure and transparent air, but incessantly worked by underground fires;
  7722	a perfect battlefield in which Neptune and Pluto still dispute the
  7723	empire of the world!
  7724	
  7725	It is upon these banks, and on these waters, says Michelet, that man is
  7726	renewed in one of the most powerful climates of the globe.  But,
  7727	beautiful as it was, I could only take a rapid glance at the basin
  7728	whose superficial area is two million of square yards.  Even Captain
  7729	Nemo's knowledge was lost to me, for this puzzling person did not
  7730	appear once during our passage at full speed.  I estimated the course
  7731	which the Nautilus took under the waves of the sea at about six hundred
  7732	leagues, and it was accomplished in forty-eight hours.  Starting on the
  7733	morning of the 16th of February from the shores of Greece, we had
  7734	crossed the Straits of Gibraltar by sunrise on the 18th.
  7735	
  7736	It was plain to me that this Mediterranean, enclosed in the midst of
  7737	those countries which he wished to avoid, was distasteful to Captain
  7738	Nemo.  Those waves and those breezes brought back too many
  7739	remembrances, if not too many regrets.  Here he had no longer that
  7740	independence and that liberty of gait which he had when in the open
  7741	seas, and his Nautilus felt itself cramped between the close shores of
  7742	Africa and Europe.
  7743	
  7744	Our speed was now twenty-five miles an hour.  It may be well understood
  7745	that Ned Land, to his great disgust, was obliged to renounce his
  7746	intended flight.  He could not launch the pinnace, going at the rate of
  7747	twelve or thirteen yards every second.  To quit the Nautilus under such
  7748	conditions would be as bad as jumping from a train going at full
  7749	speed--an imprudent thing, to say the least of it.  Besides, our vessel
  7750	only mounted to the surface of the waves at night to renew its stock of
  7751	air; it was steered entirely by the compass and the log.
  7752	
  7753	I saw no more of the interior of this Mediterranean than a traveller by
  7754	express train perceives of the landscape which flies before his eyes;
  7755	that is to say, the distant horizon, and not the nearer objects which
  7756	pass like a flash of lightning.
  7757	
  7758	We were then passing between Sicily and the coast of Tunis.  In the
  7759	narrow space between Cape Bon and the Straits of Messina the bottom of
  7760	the sea rose almost suddenly.  There was a perfect bank, on which there
  7761	was not more than nine fathoms of water, whilst on either side the
  7762	depth was ninety fathoms.
  7763	
  7764	The Nautilus had to manoeuvre very carefully so as not to strike
  7765	against this submarine barrier.
  7766	
  7767	I showed Conseil, on the map of the Mediterranean, the spot occupied by
  7768	this reef.
  7769	
  7770	"But if you please, sir," observed Conseil, "it is like a real isthmus
  7771	joining Europe to Africa."
  7772	
  7773	"Yes, my boy, it forms a perfect bar to the Straits of Lybia, and the
  7774	soundings of Smith have proved that in former times the continents
  7775	between Cape Boco and Cape Furina were joined."
  7776	
  7777	"I can well believe it," said Conseil.
  7778	
  7779	"I will add," I continued, "that a similar barrier exists between
  7780	Gibraltar and Ceuta, which in geological times formed the entire
  7781	Mediterranean."
  7782	
  7783	"What if some volcanic burst should one day raise these two barriers
  7784	above the waves?"
  7785	
  7786	"It is not probable, Conseil."
  7787	
  7788	"Well, but allow me to finish, please, sir; if this phenomenon should
  7789	take place, it will be troublesome for M. Lesseps, who has taken so
  7790	much pains to pierce the isthmus."
  7791	
  7792	"I agree with you; but I repeat, Conseil, this phenomenon will never
  7793	happen.  The violence of subterranean force is ever diminishing.
  7794	Volcanoes, so plentiful in the first days of the world, are being
  7795	extinguished by degrees; the internal heat is weakened, the temperature
  7796	of the lower strata of the globe is lowered by a perceptible quantity
  7797	every century to the detriment of our globe, for its heat is its life."
  7798	
  7799	"But the sun?"
  7800	
  7801	"The sun is not sufficient, Conseil.  Can it give heat to a dead body?"
  7802	
  7803	"Not that I know of."
  7804	
  7805	"Well, my friend, this earth will one day be that cold corpse; it will
  7806	become uninhabitable and uninhabited like the moon, which has long
  7807	since lost all its vital heat."
  7808	
  7809	"In how many centuries?"
  7810	
  7811	"In some hundreds of thousands of years, my boy."
  7812	
  7813	"Then," said Conseil, "we shall have time to finish our journey--that
  7814	is, if Ned Land does not interfere with it."
  7815	
  7816	And Conseil, reassured, returned to the study of the bank, which the
  7817	Nautilus was skirting at a moderate speed.
  7818	
  7819	During the night of the 16th and 17th February we had entered the
  7820	second Mediterranean basin, the greatest depth of which was 1,450
  7821	fathoms.  The Nautilus, by the action of its crew, slid down the
  7822	inclined planes and buried itself in the lowest depths of the sea.
  7823	
  7824	On the 18th of February, about three o'clock in the morning, we were at
  7825	the entrance of the Straits of Gibraltar.  There once existed two
  7826	currents: an upper one, long since recognised, which conveys the waters
  7827	of the ocean into the basin of the Mediterranean; and a lower
  7828	counter-current, which reasoning has now shown to exist.  Indeed, the
  7829	volume of water in the Mediterranean, incessantly added to by the waves
  7830	of the Atlantic and by rivers falling into it, would each year raise
  7831	the level of this sea, for its evaporation is not sufficient to restore
  7832	the equilibrium.  As it is not so, we must necessarily admit the
  7833	existence of an under-current, which empties into the basin of the
  7834	Atlantic through the Straits of Gibraltar the surplus waters of the
  7835	Mediterranean.  A fact indeed; and it was this counter-current by which
  7836	the Nautilus profited.  It advanced rapidly by the narrow pass.  For
  7837	one instant I caught a glimpse of the beautiful ruins of the temple of
  7838	Hercules, buried in the ground, according to Pliny, and with the low
  7839	island which supports it; and a few minutes later we were floating on
  7840	the Atlantic.
  7841	
  7842	
  7843	
  7844	CHAPTER VIII
  7845	
  7846	VIGO BAY
  7847	
  7848	The Atlantic! a vast sheet of water whose superficial area covers
  7849	twenty-five millions of square miles, the length of which is nine
  7850	thousand miles, with a mean breadth of two thousand seven hundred--an
  7851	ocean whose parallel winding shores embrace an immense circumference,
  7852	watered by the largest rivers of the world, the St. Lawrence, the
  7853	Mississippi, the Amazon, the Plata, the Orinoco, the Niger, the
  7854	Senegal, the Elbe, the Loire, and the Rhine, which carry water from the
  7855	most civilised, as well as from the most savage, countries!
  7856	Magnificent field of water, incessantly ploughed by vessels of every
  7857	nation, sheltered by the flags of every nation, and which terminates in
  7858	those two terrible points so dreaded by mariners, Cape Horn and the
  7859	Cape of Tempests.
  7860	
  7861	The Nautilus was piercing the water with its sharp spur, after having
  7862	accomplished nearly ten thousand leagues in three months and a half, a
  7863	distance greater than the great circle of the earth.  Where were we
  7864	going now, and what was reserved for the future?  The Nautilus, leaving
  7865	the Straits of Gibraltar, had gone far out.  It returned to the surface
  7866	of the waves, and our daily walks on the platform were restored to us.
  7867	
  7868	I mounted at once, accompanied by Ned Land and Conseil.  At a distance
  7869	of about twelve miles, Cape St. Vincent was dimly to be seen, forming
  7870	the south-western point of the Spanish peninsula.  A strong southerly
  7871	gale was blowing.  The sea was swollen and billowy; it made the
  7872	Nautilus rock violently.  It was almost impossible to keep one's foot
  7873	on the platform, which the heavy rolls of the sea beat over every
  7874	instant.  So we descended after inhaling some mouthfuls of fresh air.
  7875	
  7876	I returned to my room, Conseil to his cabin; but the Canadian, with a
  7877	preoccupied air, followed me.  Our rapid passage across the
  7878	Mediterranean had not allowed him to put his project into execution,
  7879	and he could not help showing his disappointment.  When the door of my
  7880	room was shut, he sat down and looked at me silently.
  7881	
  7882	"Friend Ned," said I, "I understand you; but you cannot reproach
  7883	yourself.  To have attempted to leave the Nautilus under the
  7884	circumstances would have been folly."
  7885	
  7886	Ned Land did not answer; his compressed lips and frowning brow showed
  7887	with him the violent possession this fixed idea had taken of his mind.
  7888	
  7889	"Let us see," I continued; "we need not despair yet.  We are going up
  7890	the coast of Portugal again; France and England are not far off, where
  7891	we can easily find refuge.  Now if the Nautilus, on leaving the Straits
  7892	of Gibraltar, had gone to the south, if it had carried us towards
  7893	regions where there were no continents, I should share your uneasiness.
  7894	But we know now that Captain Nemo does not fly from civilised seas, and
  7895	in some days I think you can act with security."
  7896	
  7897	Ned Land still looked at me fixedly; at length his fixed lips parted,
  7898	and he said, "It is for to-night."
  7899	
  7900	I drew myself up suddenly.  I was, I admit, little prepared for this
  7901	communication.  I wanted to answer the Canadian, but words would not
  7902	come.
  7903	
  7904	"We agreed to wait for an opportunity," continued Ned Land, "and the
  7905	opportunity has arrived.  This night we shall be but a few miles from
  7906	the Spanish coast.  It is cloudy.  The wind blows freely.  I have your
  7907	word, M. Aronnax, and I rely upon you."
  7908	
  7909	As I was silent, the Canadian approached me.
  7910	
  7911	"To-night, at nine o'clock," said he.  "I have warned Conseil.  At that
  7912	moment Captain Nemo will be shut up in his room, probably in bed.
  7913	Neither the engineers nor the ship's crew can see us.  Conseil and I
  7914	will gain the central staircase, and you, M. Aronnax, will remain in
  7915	the library, two steps from us, waiting my signal.  The oars, the mast,
  7916	and the sail are in the canoe.  I have even succeeded in getting some
  7917	provisions.  I have procured an English wrench, to unfasten the bolts
  7918	which attach it to the shell of the Nautilus.  So all is ready, till
  7919	to-night."
  7920	
  7921	"The sea is bad."
  7922	
  7923	"That I allow," replied the Canadian; "but we must risk that.  Liberty
  7924	is worth paying for; besides, the boat is strong, and a few miles with
  7925	a fair wind to carry us is no great thing.  Who knows but by to-morrow
  7926	we may be a hundred leagues away?  Let circumstances only favour us,
  7927	and by ten or eleven o'clock we shall have landed on some spot of terra
  7928	firma, alive or dead.  But adieu now till to-night."
  7929	
  7930	With these words the Canadian withdrew, leaving me almost dumb.  I had
  7931	imagined that, the chance gone, I should have time to reflect and
  7932	discuss the matter.  My obstinate companion had given me no time; and,
  7933	after all, what could I have said to him?  Ned Land was perfectly
  7934	right.  There was almost the opportunity to profit by.  Could I retract
  7935	my word, and take upon myself the responsibility of compromising the
  7936	future of my companions?  To-morrow Captain Nemo might take us far from
  7937	all land.
  7938	
  7939	At that moment a rather loud hissing noise told me that the reservoirs
  7940	were filling, and that the Nautilus was sinking under the waves of the
  7941	Atlantic.
  7942	
  7943	A sad day I passed, between the desire of regaining my liberty of
  7944	action and of abandoning the wonderful Nautilus, and leaving my
  7945	submarine studies incomplete.
  7946	
  7947	What dreadful hours I passed thus!  Sometimes seeing myself and
  7948	companions safely landed, sometimes wishing, in spite of my reason,
  7949	that some unforeseen circumstance, would prevent the realisation of Ned
  7950	Land's project.
  7951	
  7952	Twice I went to the saloon.  I wished to consult the compass.  I wished
  7953	to see if the direction the Nautilus was taking was bringing us nearer
  7954	or taking us farther from the coast.  But no; the Nautilus kept in
  7955	Portuguese waters.
  7956	
  7957	I must therefore take my part and prepare for flight.  My luggage was
  7958	not heavy; my notes, nothing more.
  7959	
  7960	As to Captain Nemo, I asked myself what he would think of our escape;
  7961	what trouble, what wrong it might cause him and what he might do in
  7962	case of its discovery or failure.  Certainly I had no cause to complain
  7963	of him; on the contrary, never was hospitality freer than his.  In
  7964	leaving him I could not be taxed with ingratitude.  No oath bound us to
  7965	him.  It was on the strength of circumstances he relied, and not upon
  7966	our word, to fix us for ever.
  7967	
  7968	I had not seen the Captain since our visit to the Island of Santorin.
  7969	Would chance bring me to his presence before our departure?  I wished
  7970	it, and I feared it at the same time.  I listened if I could hear him
  7971	walking the room contiguous to mine.  No sound reached my ear.  I felt
  7972	an unbearable uneasiness.  This day of waiting seemed eternal.  Hours
  7973	struck too slowly to keep pace with my impatience.
  7974	
  7975	My dinner was served in my room as usual.  I ate but little; I was too
  7976	preoccupied.  I left the table at seven o'clock. A hundred and twenty
  7977	minutes (I counted them) still separated me from the moment in which I
  7978	was to join Ned Land.  My agitation redoubled.  My pulse beat
  7979	violently.  I could not remain quiet.  I went and came, hoping to calm
  7980	my troubled spirit by constant movement.  The idea of failure in our
  7981	bold enterprise was the least painful of my anxieties; but the thought
  7982	of seeing our project discovered before leaving the Nautilus, of being
  7983	brought before Captain Nemo, irritated, or (what was worse) saddened,
  7984	at my desertion, made my heart beat.
  7985	
  7986	I wanted to see the saloon for the last time.  I descended the stairs
  7987	and arrived in the museum, where I had passed so many useful and
  7988	agreeable hours.  I looked at all its riches, all its treasures, like a
  7989	man on the eve of an eternal exile, who was leaving never to return.
  7990	
  7991	These wonders of Nature, these masterpieces of art, amongst which for
  7992	so many days my life had been concentrated, I was going to abandon them
  7993	for ever!  I should like to have taken a last look through the windows
  7994	of the saloon into the waters of the Atlantic:  but the panels were
  7995	hermetically closed, and a cloak of steel separated me from that ocean
  7996	which I had not yet explored.
  7997	
  7998	In passing through the saloon, I came near the door let into the angle
  7999	which opened into the Captain's room.  To my great surprise, this door
  8000	was ajar.  I drew back involuntarily.  If Captain Nemo should be in his
  8001	room, he could see me.  But, hearing no sound, I drew nearer.  The room
  8002	was deserted.  I pushed open the door and took some steps forward.
  8003	Still the same monklike severity of aspect.
  8004	
  8005	Suddenly the clock struck eight.  The first beat of the hammer on the
  8006	bell awoke me from my dreams.  I trembled as if an invisible eye had
  8007	plunged into my most secret thoughts, and I hurried from the room.
  8008	
  8009	There my eye fell upon the compass.  Our course was still north.  The
  8010	log indicated moderate speed, the manometer a depth of about sixty feet.
  8011	
  8012	I returned to my room, clothed myself warmly--sea boots, an otterskin
  8013	cap, a great coat of byssus, lined with sealskin; I was ready, I was
  8014	waiting.  The vibration of the screw alone broke the deep silence which
  8015	reigned on board.  I listened attentively.  Would no loud voice
  8016	suddenly inform me that Ned Land had been surprised in his projected
  8017	flight.  A mortal dread hung over me, and I vainly tried to regain my
  8018	accustomed coolness.
  8019	
  8020	At a few minutes to nine, I put my ear to the Captain's door.  No
  8021	noise.  I left my room and returned to the saloon, which was half in
  8022	obscurity, but deserted.
  8023	
  8024	I opened the door communicating with the library.  The same
  8025	insufficient light, the same solitude.  I placed myself near the door
  8026	leading to the central staircase, and there waited for Ned Land's
  8027	signal.
  8028	
  8029	At that moment the trembling of the screw sensibly diminished, then it
  8030	stopped entirely.  The silence was now only disturbed by the beatings
  8031	of my own heart.  Suddenly a slight shock was felt; and I knew that the
  8032	Nautilus had stopped at the bottom of the ocean.  My uneasiness
  8033	increased.  The Canadian's signal did not come.  I felt inclined to
  8034	join Ned Land and beg of him to put off his attempt.  I felt that we
  8035	were not sailing under our usual conditions.
  8036	
  8037	At this moment the door of the large saloon opened, and Captain Nemo
  8038	appeared.  He saw me, and without further preamble began in an amiable
  8039	tone of voice:
  8040	
  8041	"Ah, sir!  I have been looking for you.  Do you know the history of
  8042	Spain?"
  8043	
  8044	Now, one might know the history of one's own country by heart; but in
  8045	the condition I was at the time, with troubled mind and head quite
  8046	lost, I could not have said a word of it.
  8047	
  8048	"Well," continued Captain Nemo, "you heard my question!  Do you know
  8049	the history of Spain?"
  8050	
  8051	"Very slightly," I answered.
  8052	
  8053	"Well, here are learned men having to learn," said the Captain.  "Come,
  8054	sit down, and I will tell you a curious episode in this history.  Sir,
  8055	listen well," said he; "this history will interest you on one side, for
  8056	it will answer a question which doubtless you have not been able to
  8057	solve."
  8058	
  8059	"I listen, Captain," said I, not knowing what my interlocutor was
  8060	driving at, and asking myself if this incident was bearing on our
  8061	projected flight.
  8062	
  8063	"Sir, if you have no objection, we will go back to 1702.  You cannot be
  8064	ignorant that your king, Louis XIV, thinking that the gesture of a
  8065	potentate was sufficient to bring the Pyrenees under his yoke, had
  8066	imposed the Duke of Anjou, his grandson, on the Spaniards.  This prince
  8067	reigned more or less badly under the name of Philip V, and had a strong
  8068	party against him abroad.  Indeed, the preceding year, the royal houses
  8069	of Holland, Austria, and England had concluded a treaty of alliance at
  8070	the Hague, with the intention of plucking the crown of Spain from the
  8071	head of Philip V, and placing it on that of an archduke to whom they
  8072	prematurely gave the title of Charles III.
  8073	
  8074	"Spain must resist this coalition; but she was almost entirely
  8075	unprovided with either soldiers or sailors.  However, money would not
  8076	fail them, provided that their galleons, laden with gold and silver
  8077	from America, once entered their ports.  And about the end of 1702 they
  8078	expected a rich convoy which France was escorting with a fleet of
  8079	twenty-three vessels, commanded by Admiral Chateau-Renaud, for the
  8080	ships of the coalition were already beating the Atlantic.  This convoy
  8081	was to go to Cadiz, but the Admiral, hearing that an English fleet was
  8082	cruising in those waters, resolved to make for a French port.
  8083	
  8084	"The Spanish commanders of the convoy objected to this decision.  They
  8085	wanted to be taken to a Spanish port, and, if not to Cadiz, into Vigo
  8086	Bay, situated on the northwest coast of Spain, and which was not
  8087	blocked.
  8088	
  8089	"Admiral Chateau-Renaud had the rashness to obey this injunction, and
  8090	the galleons entered Vigo Bay.
  8091	
  8092	"Unfortunately, it formed an open road which could not be defended in
  8093	any way.  They must therefore hasten to unload the galleons before the
  8094	arrival of the combined fleet; and time would not have failed them had
  8095	not a miserable question of rivalry suddenly arisen.
  8096	
  8097	"You are following the chain of events?" asked Captain Nemo.
  8098	
  8099	"Perfectly," said I, not knowing the end proposed by this historical
  8100	lesson.
  8101	
  8102	"I will continue.  This is what passed.  The merchants of Cadiz had a
  8103	privilege by which they had the right of receiving all merchandise
  8104	coming from the West Indies.  Now, to disembark these ingots at the
  8105	port of Vigo was depriving them of their rights.  They complained at
  8106	Madrid, and obtained the consent of the weak-minded Philip that the
  8107	convoy, without discharging its cargo, should remain sequestered in the
  8108	roads of Vigo until the enemy had disappeared.
  8109	
  8110	"But whilst coming to this decision, on the 22nd of October, 1702, the
  8111	English vessels arrived in Vigo Bay, when Admiral Chateau-Renaud, in
  8112	spite of inferior forces, fought bravely.  But, seeing that the
  8113	treasure must fall into the enemy's hands, he burnt and scuttled every
  8114	galleon, which went to the bottom with their immense riches."
  8115	
  8116	Captain Nemo stopped.  I admit I could not see yet why this history
  8117	should interest me.
  8118	
  8119	"Well?"  I asked.
  8120	
  8121	"Well, M. Aronnax," replied Captain Nemo, "we are in that Vigo Bay; and
  8122	it rests with yourself whether you will penetrate its mysteries."
  8123	
  8124	The Captain rose, telling me to follow him.  I had had time to recover.
  8125	I obeyed.  The saloon was dark, but through the transparent glass the
  8126	waves were sparkling.  I looked.
  8127	
  8128	For half a mile around the Nautilus, the waters seemed bathed in
  8129	electric light.  The sandy bottom was clean and bright.  Some of the
  8130	ship's crew in their diving-dresses were clearing away half-rotten
  8131	barrels and empty cases from the midst of the blackened wrecks.  From
  8132	these cases and from these barrels escaped ingots of gold and silver,
  8133	cascades of piastres and jewels.  The sand was heaped up with them.
  8134	Laden with their precious booty, the men returned to the Nautilus,
  8135	disposed of their burden, and went back to this inexhaustible fishery
  8136	of gold and silver.
  8137	
  8138	I understood now.  This was the scene of the battle of the 22nd of
  8139	October, 1702.  Here on this very spot the galleons laden for the
  8140	Spanish Government had sunk.  Here Captain Nemo came, according to his
  8141	wants, to pack up those millions with which he burdened the Nautilus.
  8142	It was for him and him alone America had given up her precious metals.
  8143	He was heir direct, without anyone to share, in those treasures torn
  8144	from the Incas and from the conquered of Ferdinand Cortez.
  8145	
  8146	"Did you know, sir," he asked, smiling, "that the sea contained such
  8147	riches?"
  8148	
  8149	"I knew," I answered, "that they value money held in suspension in
  8150	these waters at two millions."
  8151	
  8152	"Doubtless; but to extract this money the expense would be greater than
  8153	the profit.  Here, on the contrary, I have but to pick up what man has
  8154	lost--and not only in Vigo Bay, but in a thousand other ports where
  8155	shipwrecks have happened, and which are marked on my submarine map.
  8156	Can you understand now the source of the millions I am worth?"
  8157	
  8158	"I understand, Captain.  But allow me to tell you that in exploring
  8159	Vigo Bay you have only been beforehand with a rival society."
  8160	
  8161	"And which?"
  8162	
  8163	"A society which has received from the Spanish Government the privilege
  8164	of seeking those buried galleons.  The shareholders are led on by the
  8165	allurement of an enormous bounty, for they value these rich shipwrecks
  8166	at five hundred millions."
  8167	
  8168	"Five hundred millions they were," answered Captain Nemo, "but they are
  8169	so no longer."
  8170	
  8171	"Just so," said I; "and a warning to those shareholders would be an act
  8172	of charity.  But who knows if it would be well received?  What gamblers
  8173	usually regret above all is less the loss of their money than of their
  8174	foolish hopes.  After all, I pity them less than the thousands of
  8175	unfortunates to whom so much riches well-distributed would have been
  8176	profitable, whilst for them they will be for ever barren."
  8177	
  8178	I had no sooner expressed this regret than I felt that it must have
  8179	wounded Captain Nemo.
  8180	
  8181	"Barren!" he exclaimed, with animation.  "Do you think then, sir, that
  8182	these riches are lost because I gather them?  Is it for myself alone,
  8183	according to your idea, that I take the trouble to collect these
  8184	treasures?  Who told you that I did not make a good use of it?  Do you
  8185	think I am ignorant that there are suffering beings and oppressed races
  8186	on this earth, miserable creatures to console, victims to avenge?  Do
  8187	you not understand?"
  8188	
  8189	Captain Nemo stopped at these last words, regretting perhaps that he
  8190	had spoken so much.  But I had guessed that, whatever the motive which
  8191	had forced him to seek independence under the sea, it had left him
  8192	still a man, that his heart still beat for the sufferings of humanity,
  8193	and that his immense charity was for oppressed races as well as
  8194	individuals.  And I then understood for whom those millions were
  8195	destined which were forwarded by Captain Nemo when the Nautilus was
  8196	cruising in the waters of Crete.
  8197	
  8198	
  8199	
  8200	CHAPTER IX
  8201	
  8202	A VANISHED CONTINENT
  8203	
  8204	The next morning, the 19th of February, I saw the Canadian enter my
  8205	room.  I expected this visit.  He looked very disappointed.
  8206	
  8207	"Well, sir?" said he.
  8208	
  8209	"Well, Ned, fortune was against us yesterday."
  8210	
  8211	"Yes; that Captain must needs stop exactly at the hour we intended
  8212	leaving his vessel."
  8213	
  8214	"Yes, Ned, he had business at his bankers."
  8215	
  8216	"His bankers!"
  8217	
  8218	"Or rather his banking-house; by that I mean the ocean, where his
  8219	riches are safer than in the chests of the State."
  8220	
  8221	I then related to the Canadian the incidents of the preceding night,
  8222	hoping to bring him back to the idea of not abandoning the Captain; but
  8223	my recital had no other result than an energetically expressed regret
  8224	from Ned that he had not been able to take a walk on the battlefield of
  8225	Vigo on his own account.
  8226	
  8227	"However," said he, "all is not ended.  It is only a blow of the
  8228	harpoon lost.  Another time we must succeed; and to-night, if
  8229	necessary----"
  8230	
  8231	"In what direction is the Nautilus going?"  I asked.
  8232	
  8233	"I do not know," replied Ned.
  8234	
  8235	"Well, at noon we shall see the point."
  8236	
  8237	The Canadian returned to Conseil.  As soon as I was dressed, I went
  8238	into the saloon.  The compass was not reassuring.  The course of the
  8239	Nautilus was S.S.W. We were turning our backs on Europe.
  8240	
  8241	I waited with some impatience till the ship's place was pricked on the
  8242	chart.  At about half-past eleven the reservoirs were emptied, and our
  8243	vessel rose to the surface of the ocean.  I rushed towards the
  8244	platform.  Ned Land had preceded me.  No more land in sight.  Nothing
  8245	but an immense sea.  Some sails on the horizon, doubtless those going
  8246	to San Roque in search of favourable winds for doubling the Cape of
  8247	Good Hope.  The weather was cloudy.  A gale of wind was preparing.  Ned
  8248	raved, and tried to pierce the cloudy horizon.  He still hoped that
  8249	behind all that fog stretched the land he so longed for.
  8250	
  8251	At noon the sun showed itself for an instant.  The second profited by
  8252	this brightness to take its height.  Then, the sea becoming more
  8253	billowy, we descended, and the panel closed.
  8254	
  8255	An hour after, upon consulting the chart, I saw the position of the
  8256	Nautilus was marked at 16° 17' long., and 33° 22' lat., at 150
  8257	leagues from the nearest coast.  There was no means of flight, and I
  8258	leave you to imagine the rage of the Canadian when I informed him of
  8259	our situation.
  8260	
  8261	For myself, I was not particularly sorry.  I felt lightened of the load
  8262	which had oppressed me, and was able to return with some degree of
  8263	calmness to my accustomed work.
  8264	
  8265	That night, about eleven o'clock, I received a most unexpected visit
  8266	from Captain Nemo.  He asked me very graciously if I felt fatigued from
  8267	my watch of the preceding night.  I answered in the negative.
  8268	
  8269	"Then, M. Aronnax, I propose a curious excursion."
  8270	
  8271	"Propose, Captain?"
  8272	
  8273	"You have hitherto only visited the submarine depths by daylight, under
  8274	the brightness of the sun.  Would it suit you to see them in the
  8275	darkness of the night?"
  8276	
  8277	"Most willingly."
  8278	
  8279	"I warn you, the way will be tiring.  We shall have far to walk, and
  8280	must climb a mountain.  The roads are not well kept."
  8281	
  8282	"What you say, Captain, only heightens my curiosity; I am ready to
  8283	follow you."
  8284	
  8285	"Come then, sir, we will put on our diving-dresses."
  8286	
  8287	Arrived at the robing-room, I saw that neither of my companions nor any
  8288	of the ship's crew were to follow us on this excursion.  Captain Nemo
  8289	had not even proposed my taking with me either Ned or Conseil.
  8290	
  8291	In a few moments we had put on our diving-dresses; they placed on our
  8292	backs the reservoirs, abundantly filled with air, but no electric lamps
  8293	were prepared.  I called the Captain's attention to the fact.
  8294	
  8295	"They will be useless," he replied.
  8296	
  8297	I thought I had not heard aright, but I could not repeat my
  8298	observation, for the Captain's head had already disappeared in its
  8299	metal case.  I finished harnessing myself.  I felt them put an
  8300	iron-pointed stick into my hand, and some minutes later, after going
  8301	through the usual form, we set foot on the bottom of the Atlantic at a
  8302	depth of 150 fathoms.  Midnight was near.  The waters were profoundly
  8303	dark, but Captain Nemo pointed out in the distance a reddish spot, a
  8304	sort of large light shining brilliantly about two miles from the
  8305	Nautilus.  What this fire might be, what could feed it, why and how it
  8306	lit up the liquid mass, I could not say.  In any case, it did light our
  8307	way, vaguely, it is true, but I soon accustomed myself to the peculiar
  8308	darkness, and I understood, under such circumstances, the uselessness
  8309	of the Ruhmkorff apparatus.
  8310	
  8311	As we advanced, I heard a kind of pattering above my head.  The noise
  8312	redoubling, sometimes producing a continual shower, I soon understood
  8313	the cause.  It was rain falling violently, and crisping the surface of
  8314	the waves.  Instinctively the thought flashed across my mind that I
  8315	should be wet through!  By the water! in the midst of the water!  I
  8316	could not help laughing at the odd idea.  But, indeed, in the thick
  8317	diving-dress, the liquid element is no longer felt, and one only seems
  8318	to be in an atmosphere somewhat denser than the terrestrial atmosphere.
  8319	Nothing more.
  8320	
  8321	After half an hour's walk the soil became stony.  Medusae, microscopic
  8322	crustacea, and pennatules lit it slightly with their phosphorescent
  8323	gleam.  I caught a glimpse of pieces of stone covered with millions of
  8324	zoophytes and masses of sea weed.  My feet often slipped upon this
  8325	sticky carpet of sea weed, and without my iron-tipped stick I should
  8326	have fallen more than once.  In turning round, I could still see the
  8327	whitish lantern of the Nautilus beginning to pale in the distance.
  8328	
  8329	But the rosy light which guided us increased and lit up the horizon.
  8330	The presence of this fire under water puzzled me in the highest degree.
  8331	Was I going towards a natural phenomenon as yet unknown to the _savants_
  8332	of the earth?  Or even (for this thought crossed my brain) had the hand
  8333	of man aught to do with this conflagration?  Had he fanned this flame?
  8334	Was I to meet in these depths companions and friends of Captain Nemo
  8335	whom he was going to visit, and who, like him, led this strange
  8336	existence?  Should I find down there a whole colony of exiles who,
  8337	weary of the miseries of this earth, had sought and found independence
  8338	in the deep ocean?  All these foolish and unreasonable ideas pursued
  8339	me.  And in this condition of mind, over-excited by the succession of
  8340	wonders continually passing before my eyes, I should not have been
  8341	surprised to meet at the bottom of the sea one of those submarine towns
  8342	of which Captain Nemo dreamed.
  8343	
  8344	Our road grew lighter and lighter.  The white glimmer came in rays from
  8345	the summit of a mountain about 800 feet high.  But what I saw was
  8346	simply a reflection, developed by the clearness of the waters.  The
  8347	source of this inexplicable light was a fire on the opposite side of
  8348	the mountain.
  8349	
  8350	In the midst of this stony maze furrowing the bottom of the Atlantic,
  8351	Captain Nemo advanced without hesitation.  He knew this dreary road.
  8352	Doubtless he had often travelled over it, and could not lose himself.
  8353	I followed him with unshaken confidence.  He seemed to me like a genie
  8354	of the sea; and, as he walked before me, I could not help admiring his
  8355	stature, which was outlined in black on the luminous horizon.
  8356	
  8357	It was one in the morning when we arrived at the first slopes of the
  8358	mountain; but to gain access to them we must venture through the
  8359	difficult paths of a vast copse.
  8360	
  8361	Yes; a copse of dead trees, without leaves, without sap, trees
  8362	petrified by the action of the water and here and there overtopped by
  8363	gigantic pines.  It was like a coal-pit still standing, holding by the
  8364	roots to the broken soil, and whose branches, like fine black paper
  8365	cuttings, showed distinctly on the watery ceiling.  Picture to yourself
  8366	a forest in the Hartz hanging on to the sides of the mountain, but a
  8367	forest swallowed up.  The paths were encumbered with seaweed and fucus,
  8368	between which grovelled a whole world of crustacea.  I went along,
  8369	climbing the rocks, striding over extended trunks, breaking the sea
  8370	bind-weed which hung from one tree to the other; and frightening the
  8371	fishes, which flew from branch to branch.  Pressing onward, I felt no
  8372	fatigue.  I followed my guide, who was never tired.  What a spectacle!
  8373	How can I express it? how paint the aspect of those woods and rocks in
  8374	this medium--their under parts dark and wild, the upper coloured with
  8375	red tints, by that light which the reflecting powers of the waters
  8376	doubled?  We climbed rocks which fell directly after with gigantic
  8377	bounds and the low growling of an avalanche.  To right and left ran
  8378	long, dark galleries, where sight was lost.  Here opened vast glades
  8379	which the hand of man seemed to have worked; and I sometimes asked
  8380	myself if some inhabitant of these submarine regions would not suddenly
  8381	appear to me.
  8382	
  8383	But Captain Nemo was still mounting.  I could not stay behind.  I
  8384	followed boldly.  My stick gave me good help.  A false step would have
  8385	been dangerous on the narrow passes sloping down to the sides of the
  8386	gulfs; but I walked with firm step, without feeling any giddiness.  Now
  8387	I jumped a crevice, the depth of which would have made me hesitate had
  8388	it been among the glaciers on the land; now I ventured on the unsteady
  8389	trunk of a tree thrown across from one abyss to the other, without
  8390	looking under my feet, having only eyes to admire the wild sites of
  8391	this region.
  8392	
  8393	There, monumental rocks, leaning on their regularly-cut bases, seemed
  8394	to defy all laws of equilibrium.  From between their stony knees trees
  8395	sprang, like a jet under heavy pressure, and upheld others which upheld
  8396	them.  Natural towers, large scarps, cut perpendicularly, like a
  8397	"curtain," inclined at an angle which the laws of gravitation could
  8398	never have tolerated in terrestrial regions.
  8399	
  8400	Two hours after quitting the Nautilus we had crossed the line of trees,
  8401	and a hundred feet above our heads rose the top of the mountain, which
  8402	cast a shadow on the brilliant irradiation of the opposite slope.  Some
  8403	petrified shrubs ran fantastically here and there.  Fishes got up under
  8404	our feet like birds in the long grass.  The massive rocks were rent
  8405	with impenetrable fractures, deep grottos, and unfathomable holes, at
  8406	the bottom of which formidable creatures might be heard moving.  My
  8407	blood curdled when I saw enormous antennae blocking my road, or some
  8408	frightful claw closing with a noise in the shadow of some cavity.
  8409	Millions of luminous spots shone brightly in the midst of the darkness.
  8410	They were the eyes of giant crustacea crouched in their holes; giant
  8411	lobsters setting themselves up like halberdiers, and moving their claws
  8412	with the clicking sound of pincers; titanic crabs, pointed like a gun
  8413	on its carriage; and frightful-looking poulps, interweaving their
  8414	tentacles like a living nest of serpents.
  8415	
  8416	We had now arrived on the first platform, where other surprises awaited
  8417	me.  Before us lay some picturesque ruins, which betrayed the hand of
  8418	man and not that of the Creator.  There were vast heaps of stone,
  8419	amongst which might be traced the vague and shadowy forms of castles
  8420	and temples, clothed with a world of blossoming zoophytes, and over
  8421	which, instead of ivy, sea-weed and fucus threw a thick vegetable
  8422	mantle.  But what was this portion of the globe which had been
  8423	swallowed by cataclysms?  Who had placed those rocks and stones like
  8424	cromlechs of prehistoric times?  Where was I?  Whither had Captain
  8425	Nemo's fancy hurried me?
  8426	
  8427	I would fain have asked him; not being able to, I stopped him--I seized
  8428	his arm.  But, shaking his head, and pointing to the highest point of
  8429	the mountain, he seemed to say:
  8430	
  8431	"Come, come along; come higher!"
  8432	
  8433	I followed, and in a few minutes I had climbed to the top, which for a
  8434	circle of ten yards commanded the whole mass of rock.
  8435	
  8436	I looked down the side we had just climbed.  The mountain did not rise
  8437	more than seven or eight hundred feet above the level of the plain; but
  8438	on the opposite side it commanded from twice that height the depths of
  8439	this part of the Atlantic.  My eyes ranged far over a large space lit
  8440	by a violent fulguration.  In fact, the mountain was a volcano.
  8441	
  8442	At fifty feet above the peak, in the midst of a rain of stones and
  8443	scoriae, a large crater was vomiting forth torrents of lava which fell
  8444	in a cascade of fire into the bosom of the liquid mass.  Thus situated,
  8445	this volcano lit the lower plain like an immense torch, even to the
  8446	extreme limits of the horizon.  I said that the submarine crater threw
  8447	up lava, but no flames.  Flames require the oxygen of the air to feed
  8448	upon and cannot be developed under water; but streams of lava, having
  8449	in themselves the principles of their incandescence, can attain a white
  8450	heat, fight vigorously against the liquid element, and turn it to
  8451	vapour by contact.
  8452	
  8453	Rapid currents bearing all these gases in diffusion and torrents of
  8454	lava slid to the bottom of the mountain like an eruption of Vesuvius on
  8455	another Terra del Greco.
  8456	
  8457	There indeed under my eyes, ruined, destroyed, lay a town--its roofs
  8458	open to the sky, its temples fallen, its arches dislocated, its columns
  8459	lying on the ground, from which one would still recognise the massive
  8460	character of Tuscan architecture.  Further on, some remains of a
  8461	gigantic aqueduct; here the high base of an Acropolis, with the
  8462	floating outline of a Parthenon; there traces of a quay, as if an
  8463	ancient port had formerly abutted on the borders of the ocean, and
  8464	disappeared with its merchant vessels and its war-galleys. Farther on
  8465	again, long lines of sunken walls and broad, deserted streets--a
  8466	perfect Pompeii escaped beneath the waters.  Such was the sight that
  8467	Captain Nemo brought before my eyes!
  8468	
  8469	Where was I?  Where was I?  I must know at any cost.  I tried to speak,
  8470	but Captain Nemo stopped me by a gesture, and, picking up a piece of
  8471	chalk-stone, advanced to a rock of black basalt, and traced the one
  8472	word:
  8473	
  8474	
  8475	ATLANTIS
  8476	
  8477	
  8478	What a light shot through my mind!  Atlantis! the Atlantis of Plato,
  8479	that continent denied by Origen and Humbolt, who placed its
  8480	disappearance amongst the legendary tales.  I had it there now before
  8481	my eyes, bearing upon it the unexceptionable testimony of its
  8482	catastrophe.  The region thus engulfed was beyond Europe, Asia, and
  8483	Lybia, beyond the columns of Hercules, where those powerful people, the
  8484	Atlantides, lived, against whom the first wars of ancient Greeks were
  8485	waged.
  8486	
  8487	Thus, led by the strangest destiny, I was treading under foot the
  8488	mountains of this continent, touching with my hand those ruins a
  8489	thousand generations old and contemporary with the geological epochs.
  8490	I was walking on the very spot where the contemporaries of the first
  8491	man had walked.
  8492	
  8493	Whilst I was trying to fix in my mind every detail of this grand
  8494	landscape, Captain Nemo remained motionless, as if petrified in mute
  8495	ecstasy, leaning on a mossy stone.  Was he dreaming of those
  8496	generations long since disappeared?  Was he asking them the secret of
  8497	human destiny?  Was it here this strange man came to steep himself in
  8498	historical recollections, and live again this ancient life--he who
  8499	wanted no modern one?  What would I not have given to know his
  8500	thoughts, to share them, to understand them!  We remained for an hour
  8501	at this place, contemplating the vast plains under the brightness of
  8502	the lava, which was some times wonderfully intense.  Rapid tremblings
  8503	ran along the mountain caused by internal bubblings, deep noise,
  8504	distinctly transmitted through the liquid medium were echoed with
  8505	majestic grandeur.  At this moment the moon appeared through the mass
  8506	of waters and threw her pale rays on the buried continent.  It was but
  8507	a gleam, but what an indescribable effect!  The Captain rose, cast one
  8508	last look on the immense plain, and then bade me follow him.
  8509	
  8510	We descended the mountain rapidly, and, the mineral forest once passed,
  8511	I saw the lantern of the Nautilus shining like a star.  The Captain
  8512	walked straight to it, and we got on board as the first rays of light
  8513	whitened the surface of the ocean.
  8514	
  8515	
  8516	
  8517	CHAPTER X
  8518	
  8519	THE SUBMARINE COAL-MINES
  8520	
  8521	The next day, the 20th of February, I awoke very late:  the fatigues of
  8522	the previous night had prolonged my sleep until eleven o'clock. I
  8523	dressed quickly, and hastened to find the course the Nautilus was
  8524	taking.  The instruments showed it to be still toward the south, with a
  8525	speed of twenty miles an hour and a depth of fifty fathoms.
  8526	
  8527	The species of fishes here did not differ much from those already
  8528	noticed.  There were rays of giant size, five yards long, and endowed
  8529	with great muscular strength, which enabled them to shoot above the
  8530	waves; sharks of many kinds; amongst others, one fifteen feet long,
  8531	with triangular sharp teeth, and whose transparency rendered it almost
  8532	invisible in the water.
  8533	
  8534	Amongst bony fish Conseil noticed some about three yards long, armed at
  8535	the upper jaw with a piercing sword; other bright-coloured creatures,
  8536	known in the time of Aristotle by the name of the sea-dragon, which are
  8537	dangerous to capture on account of the spikes on their back.
  8538	
  8539	About four o'clock, the soil, generally composed of a thick mud mixed
  8540	with petrified wood, changed by degrees, and it became more stony, and
  8541	seemed strewn with conglomerate and pieces of basalt, with a sprinkling
  8542	of lava.  I thought that a mountainous region was succeeding the long
  8543	plains; and accordingly, after a few evolutions of the Nautilus, I saw
  8544	the southerly horizon blocked by a high wall which seemed to close all
  8545	exit.  Its summit evidently passed the level of the ocean.  It must be
  8546	a continent, or at least an island--one of the Canaries, or of the Cape
  8547	Verde Islands.  The bearings not being yet taken, perhaps designedly, I
  8548	was ignorant of our exact position.  In any case, such a wall seemed to
  8549	me to mark the limits of that Atlantis, of which we had in reality
  8550	passed over only the smallest part.
  8551	
  8552	Much longer should I have remained at the window admiring the beauties
  8553	of sea and sky, but the panels closed.  At this moment the Nautilus
  8554	arrived at the side of this high, perpendicular wall.  What it would
  8555	do, I could not guess.  I returned to my room; it no longer moved.  I
  8556	laid myself down with the full intention of waking after a few hours'
  8557	sleep; but it was eight o'clock the next day when I entered the saloon.
  8558	I looked at the manometer.  It told me that the Nautilus was floating
  8559	on the surface of the ocean.  Besides, I heard steps on the platform.
  8560	I went to the panel.  It was open; but, instead of broad daylight, as I
  8561	expected, I was surrounded by profound darkness.  Where were we?  Was I
  8562	mistaken?  Was it still night?  No; not a star was shining and night
  8563	has not that utter darkness.
  8564	
  8565	I knew not what to think, when a voice near me said:
  8566	
  8567	"Is that you, Professor?"
  8568	
  8569	"Ah!  Captain," I answered, "where are we?"
  8570	
  8571	"Underground, sir."
  8572	
  8573	"Underground!"  I exclaimed.  "And the Nautilus floating still?"
  8574	
  8575	"It always floats."
  8576	
  8577	"But I do not understand."
  8578	
  8579	"Wait a few minutes, our lantern will be lit, and, if you like light
  8580	places, you will be satisfied."
  8581	
  8582	I stood on the platform and waited.  The darkness was so complete that
  8583	I could not even see Captain Nemo; but, looking to the zenith, exactly
  8584	above my head, I seemed to catch an undecided gleam, a kind of twilight
  8585	filling a circular hole.  At this instant the lantern was lit, and its
  8586	vividness dispelled the faint light.  I closed my dazzled eyes for an
  8587	instant, and then looked again.  The Nautilus was stationary, floating
  8588	near a mountain which formed a sort of quay.  The lake, then,
  8589	supporting it was a lake imprisoned by a circle of walls, measuring two
  8590	miles in diameter and six in circumference.  Its level (the manometer
  8591	showed) could only be the same as the outside level, for there must
  8592	necessarily be a communication between the lake and the sea.  The high
  8593	partitions, leaning forward on their base, grew into a vaulted roof
  8594	bearing the shape of an immense funnel turned upside down, the height
  8595	being about five or six hundred yards.  At the summit was a circular
  8596	orifice, by which I had caught the slight gleam of light, evidently
  8597	daylight.
  8598	
  8599	"Where are we?"  I asked.
  8600	
  8601	"In the very heart of an extinct volcano, the interior of which has
  8602	been invaded by the sea, after some great convulsion of the earth.
  8603	Whilst you were sleeping, Professor, the Nautilus penetrated to this
  8604	lagoon by a natural canal, which opens about ten yards beneath the
  8605	surface of the ocean.  This is its harbour of refuge, a sure,
  8606	commodious, and mysterious one, sheltered from all gales.  Show me, if
  8607	you can, on the coasts of any of your continents or islands, a road
  8608	which can give such perfect refuge from all storms."
  8609	
  8610	"Certainly," I replied, "you are in safety here, Captain Nemo.  Who
  8611	could reach you in the heart of a volcano?  But did I not see an
  8612	opening at its summit?"
  8613	
  8614	"Yes; its crater, formerly filled with lava, vapour, and flames, and
  8615	which now gives entrance to the life-giving air we breathe."
  8616	
  8617	"But what is this volcanic mountain?"
  8618	
  8619	"It belongs to one of the numerous islands with which this sea is
  8620	strewn--to vessels a simple sandbank--to us an immense cavern.  Chance
  8621	led me to discover it, and chance served me well."
  8622	
  8623	"But of what use is this refuge, Captain?  The Nautilus wants no port."
  8624	
  8625	"No, sir; but it wants electricity to make it move, and the wherewithal
  8626	to make the electricity--sodium to feed the elements, coal from which
  8627	to get the sodium, and a coal-mine to supply the coal.  And exactly on
  8628	this spot the sea covers entire forests embedded during the geological
  8629	periods, now mineralised and transformed into coal; for me they are an
  8630	inexhaustible mine."
  8631	
  8632	"Your men follow the trade of miners here, then, Captain?"
  8633	
  8634	"Exactly so.  These mines extend under the waves like the mines of
  8635	Newcastle.  Here, in their diving-dresses, pick axe and shovel in hand,
  8636	my men extract the coal, which I do not even ask from the mines of the
  8637	earth.  When I burn this combustible for the manufacture of sodium, the
  8638	smoke, escaping from the crater of the mountain, gives it the
  8639	appearance of a still-active volcano."
  8640	
  8641	"And we shall see your companions at work?"
  8642	
  8643	"No; not this time at least; for I am in a hurry to continue our
  8644	submarine tour of the earth.  So I shall content myself with drawing
  8645	from the reserve of sodium I already possess.  The time for loading is
  8646	one day only, and we continue our voyage.  So, if you wish to go over
  8647	the cavern and make the round of the lagoon, you must take advantage of
  8648	to-day, M. Aronnax."
  8649	
  8650	I thanked the Captain and went to look for my companions, who had not
  8651	yet left their cabin.  I invited them to follow me without saying where
  8652	we were.  They mounted the platform.  Conseil, who was astonished at
  8653	nothing, seemed to look upon it as quite natural that he should wake
  8654	under a mountain, after having fallen asleep under the waves.  But Ned
  8655	Land thought of nothing but finding whether the cavern had any exit.
  8656	After breakfast, about ten o'clock, we went down on to the mountain.
  8657	
  8658	"Here we are, once more on land," said Conseil.
  8659	
  8660	"I do not call this land," said the Canadian.  "And besides, we are not
  8661	on it, but beneath it."
  8662	
  8663	Between the walls of the mountains and the waters of the lake lay a
  8664	sandy shore which, at its greatest breadth, measured five hundred feet.
  8665	On this soil one might easily make the tour of the lake.  But the base
  8666	of the high partitions was stony ground, with volcanic locks and
  8667	enormous pumice-stones lying in picturesque heaps.  All these detached
  8668	masses, covered with enamel, polished by the action of the
  8669	subterraneous fires, shone resplendent by the light of our electric
  8670	lantern.  The mica dust from the shore, rising under our feet, flew
  8671	like a cloud of sparks.  The bottom now rose sensibly, and we soon
  8672	arrived at long circuitous slopes, or inclined planes, which took us
  8673	higher by degrees; but we were obliged to walk carefully among these
  8674	conglomerates, bound by no cement, the feet slipping on the glassy
  8675	crystal, felspar, and quartz.
  8676	
  8677	The volcanic nature of this enormous excavation was confirmed on all
  8678	sides, and I pointed it out to my companions.
  8679	
  8680	"Picture to yourselves," said I, "what this crater must have been when
  8681	filled with boiling lava, and when the level of the incandescent liquid
  8682	rose to the orifice of the mountain, as though melted on the top of a
  8683	hot plate."
  8684	
  8685	"I can picture it perfectly," said Conseil.  "But, sir, will you tell
  8686	me why the Great Architect has suspended operations, and how it is that
  8687	the furnace is replaced by the quiet waters of the lake?"
  8688	
  8689	"Most probably, Conseil, because some convulsion beneath the ocean
  8690	produced that very opening which has served as a passage for the
  8691	Nautilus.  Then the waters of the Atlantic rushed into the interior of
  8692	the mountain.  There must have been a terrible struggle between the two
  8693	elements, a struggle which ended in the victory of Neptune.  But many
  8694	ages have run out since then, and the submerged volcano is now a
  8695	peaceable grotto."
  8696	
  8697	"Very well," replied Ned Land; "I accept the explanation, sir; but, in
  8698	our own interests, I regret that the opening of which you speak was not
  8699	made above the level of the sea."
  8700	
  8701	"But, friend Ned," said Conseil, "if the passage had not been under the
  8702	sea, the Nautilus could not have gone through it."
  8703	
  8704	We continued ascending.  The steps became more and more perpendicular
  8705	and narrow.  Deep excavations, which we were obliged to cross, cut them
  8706	here and there; sloping masses had to be turned.  We slid upon our
  8707	knees and crawled along.  But Conseil's dexterity and the Canadian's
  8708	strength surmounted all obstacles.  At a height of about 31 feet the
  8709	nature of the ground changed without becoming more practicable.  To the
  8710	conglomerate and trachyte succeeded black basalt, the first dispread in
  8711	layers full of bubbles, the latter forming regular prisms, placed like
  8712	a colonnade supporting the spring of the immense vault, an admirable
  8713	specimen of natural architecture.  Between the blocks of basalt wound
  8714	long streams of lava, long since grown cold, encrusted with bituminous
  8715	rays; and in some places there were spread large carpets of sulphur.  A
  8716	more powerful light shone through the upper crater, shedding a vague
  8717	glimmer over these volcanic depressions for ever buried in the bosom of
  8718	this extinguished mountain.  But our upward march was soon stopped at a
  8719	height of about two hundred and fifty feet by impassable obstacles.
  8720	There was a complete vaulted arch overhanging us, and our ascent was
