  8721	changed to a circular walk.  At the last change vegetable life began to
  8722	struggle with the mineral.  Some shrubs, and even some trees, grew from
  8723	the fractures of the walls.  I recognised some euphorbias, with the
  8724	caustic sugar coming from them; heliotropes, quite incapable of
  8725	justifying their name, sadly drooped their clusters of flowers, both
  8726	their colour and perfume half gone.  Here and there some chrysanthemums
  8727	grew timidly at the foot of an aloe with long, sickly-looking leaves.
  8728	But between the streams of lava, I saw some little violets still
  8729	slightly perfumed, and I admit that I smelt them with delight.  Perfume
  8730	is the soul of the flower, and sea-flowers have no soul.
  8731	
  8732	We had arrived at the foot of some sturdy dragon-trees, which had
  8733	pushed aside the rocks with their strong roots, when Ned Land exclaimed:
  8734	
  8735	"Ah! sir, a hive! a hive!"
  8736	
  8737	"A hive!"  I replied, with a gesture of incredulity.
  8738	
  8739	"Yes, a hive," repeated the Canadian, "and bees humming round it."
  8740	
  8741	I approached, and was bound to believe my own eyes.  There at a hole
  8742	bored in one of the dragon-trees were some thousands of these ingenious
  8743	insects, so common in all the Canaries, and whose produce is so much
  8744	esteemed.  Naturally enough, the Canadian wished to gather the honey,
  8745	and I could not well oppose his wish.  A quantity of dry leaves, mixed
  8746	with sulphur, he lit with a spark from his flint, and he began to smoke
  8747	out the bees.  The humming ceased by degrees, and the hive eventually
  8748	yielded several pounds of the sweetest honey, with which Ned Land
  8749	filled his haversack.
  8750	
  8751	"When I have mixed this honey with the paste of the bread-fruit," said
  8752	he, "I shall be able to offer you a succulent cake."
  8753	
  8754	[Transcriber's Note: 'bread-fruit' has been substituted for
  8755	'artocarpus' in this ed.]
  8756	
  8757	"'Pon my word," said Conseil, "it will be gingerbread."
  8758	
  8759	"Never mind the gingerbread," said I; "let us continue our interesting
  8760	walk."
  8761	
  8762	At every turn of the path we were following, the lake appeared in all
  8763	its length and breadth.  The lantern lit up the whole of its peaceable
  8764	surface, which knew neither ripple nor wave.  The Nautilus remained
  8765	perfectly immovable.  On the platform, and on the mountain, the ship's
  8766	crew were working like black shadows clearly carved against the
  8767	luminous atmosphere.  We were now going round the highest crest of the
  8768	first layers of rock which upheld the roof.  I then saw that bees were
  8769	not the only representatives of the animal kingdom in the interior of
  8770	this volcano.  Birds of prey hovered here and there in the shadows, or
  8771	fled from their nests on the top of the rocks.  There were sparrow
  8772	hawks, with white breasts, and kestrels, and down the slopes scampered,
  8773	with their long legs, several fine fat bustards.  I leave anyone to
  8774	imagine the covetousness of the Canadian at the sight of this savoury
  8775	game, and whether he did not regret having no gun.  But he did his best
  8776	to replace the lead by stones, and, after several fruitless attempts,
  8777	he succeeded in wounding a magnificent bird.  To say that he risked his
  8778	life twenty times before reaching it is but the truth; but he managed
  8779	so well that the creature joined the honey-cakes in his bag.  We were
  8780	now obliged to descend toward the shore, the crest becoming
  8781	impracticable.  Above us the crater seemed to gape like the mouth of a
  8782	well.  From this place the sky could be clearly seen, and clouds,
  8783	dissipated by the west wind, leaving behind them, even on the summit of
  8784	the mountain, their misty remnants--certain proof that they were only
  8785	moderately high, for the volcano did not rise more than eight hundred
  8786	feet above the level of the ocean.  Half an hour after the Canadian's
  8787	last exploit we had regained the inner shore.  Here the flora was
  8788	represented by large carpets of marine crystal, a little umbelliferous
  8789	plant very good to pickle, which also bears the name of pierce-stone
  8790	and sea-fennel. Conseil gathered some bundles of it.  As to the fauna,
  8791	it might be counted by thousands of crustacea of all sorts, lobsters,
  8792	crabs, spider-crabs, chameleon shrimps, and a large number of shells,
  8793	rockfish, and limpets.  Three-quarters of an hour later we had finished
  8794	our circuitous walk and were on board.  The crew had just finished
  8795	loading the sodium, and the Nautilus could have left that instant.  But
  8796	Captain Nemo gave no order.  Did he wish to wait until night, and leave
  8797	the submarine passage secretly?  Perhaps so.  Whatever it might be, the
  8798	next day, the Nautilus, having left its port, steered clear of all land
  8799	at a few yards beneath the waves of the Atlantic.
  8800	
  8801	
  8802	
  8803	CHAPTER XI
  8804	
  8805	THE SARGASSO SEA
  8806	
  8807	That day the Nautilus crossed a singular part of the Atlantic Ocean.
  8808	No one can be ignorant of the existence of a current of warm water
  8809	known by the name of the Gulf Stream.  After leaving the Gulf of
  8810	Florida, we went in the direction of Spitzbergen.  But before entering
  8811	the Gulf of Mexico, about 45° of N. lat., this current divides into
  8812	two arms, the principal one going towards the coast of Ireland and
  8813	Norway, whilst the second bends to the south about the height of the
  8814	Azores; then, touching the African shore, and describing a lengthened
  8815	oval, returns to the Antilles.  This second arm--it is rather a collar
  8816	than an arm--surrounds with its circles of warm water that portion of
  8817	the cold, quiet, immovable ocean called the Sargasso Sea, a perfect
  8818	lake in the open Atlantic: it takes no less than three years for the
  8819	great current to pass round it.  Such was the region the Nautilus was
  8820	now visiting, a perfect meadow, a close carpet of seaweed, fucus, and
  8821	tropical berries, so thick and so compact that the stem of a vessel
  8822	could hardly tear its way through it.  And Captain Nemo, not wishing to
  8823	entangle his screw in this herbaceous mass, kept some yards beneath the
  8824	surface of the waves.  The name Sargasso comes from the Spanish word
  8825	"sargazzo" which signifies kelp.  This kelp, or berry-plant, is the
  8826	principal formation of this immense bank.  And this is the reason why
  8827	these plants unite in the peaceful basin of the Atlantic.  The only
  8828	explanation which can be given, he says, seems to me to result from the
  8829	experience known to all the world.  Place in a vase some fragments of
  8830	cork or other floating body, and give to the water in the vase a
  8831	circular movement, the scattered fragments will unite in a group in the
  8832	centre of the liquid surface, that is to say, in the part least
  8833	agitated.  In the phenomenon we are considering, the Atlantic is the
  8834	vase, the Gulf Stream the circular current, and the Sargasso Sea the
  8835	central point at which the floating bodies unite.
  8836	
  8837	I share Maury's opinion, and I was able to study the phenomenon in the
  8838	very midst, where vessels rarely penetrate.  Above us floated products
  8839	of all kinds, heaped up among these brownish plants; trunks of trees
  8840	torn from the Andes or the Rocky Mountains, and floated by the Amazon
  8841	or the Mississippi; numerous wrecks, remains of keels, or ships'
  8842	bottoms, side-planks stove in, and so weighted with shells and
  8843	barnacles that they could not again rise to the surface.  And time will
  8844	one day justify Maury's other opinion, that these substances thus
  8845	accumulated for ages will become petrified by the action of the water
  8846	and will then form inexhaustible coal-mines--a precious reserve
  8847	prepared by far-seeing Nature for the moment when men shall have
  8848	exhausted the mines of continents.
  8849	
  8850	In the midst of this inextricable mass of plants and sea weed, I
  8851	noticed some charming pink halcyons and actiniae, with their long
  8852	tentacles trailing after them, and medusae, green, red, and blue.
  8853	
  8854	All the day of the 22nd of February we passed in the Sargasso Sea,
  8855	where such fish as are partial to marine plants find abundant
  8856	nourishment.  The next, the ocean had returned to its accustomed
  8857	aspect.  From this time for nineteen days, from the 23rd of February to
  8858	the 12th of March, the Nautilus kept in the middle of the Atlantic,
  8859	carrying us at a constant speed of a hundred leagues in twenty-four
  8860	hours.  Captain Nemo evidently intended accomplishing his submarine
  8861	programme, and I imagined that he intended, after doubling Cape Horn,
  8862	to return to the Australian seas of the Pacific.  Ned Land had cause
  8863	for fear.  In these large seas, void of islands, we could not attempt
  8864	to leave the boat.  Nor had we any means of opposing Captain Nemo's
  8865	will.  Our only course was to submit; but what we could neither gain by
  8866	force nor cunning, I liked to think might be obtained by persuasion.
  8867	This voyage ended, would he not consent to restore our liberty, under
  8868	an oath never to reveal his existence?--an oath of honour which we
  8869	should have religiously kept.  But we must consider that delicate
  8870	question with the Captain.  But was I free to claim this liberty?  Had
  8871	he not himself said from the beginning, in the firmest manner, that the
  8872	secret of his life exacted from him our lasting imprisonment on board
  8873	the Nautilus?  And would not my four months' silence appear to him a
  8874	tacit acceptance of our situation?  And would not a return to the
  8875	subject result in raising suspicions which might be hurtful to our
  8876	projects, if at some future time a favourable opportunity offered to
  8877	return to them?
  8878	
  8879	During the nineteen days mentioned above, no incident of any kind
  8880	happened to signalise our voyage.  I saw little of the Captain; he was
  8881	at work.  In the library I often found his books left open, especially
  8882	those on natural history.  My work on submarine depths, conned over by
  8883	him, was covered with marginal notes, often contradicting my theories
  8884	and systems; but the Captain contented himself with thus purging my
  8885	work; it was very rare for him to discuss it with me.  Sometimes I
  8886	heard the melancholy tones of his organ; but only at night, in the
  8887	midst of the deepest obscurity, when the Nautilus slept upon the
  8888	deserted ocean.  During this part of our voyage we sailed whole days on
  8889	the surface of the waves.  The sea seemed abandoned.  A few
  8890	sailing-vessels, on the road to India, were making for the Cape of Good
  8891	Hope.  One day we were followed by the boats of a whaler, who, no
  8892	doubt, took us for some enormous whale of great price; but Captain Nemo
  8893	did not wish the worthy fellows to lose their time and trouble, so
  8894	ended the chase by plunging under the water.  Our navigation continued
  8895	until the 13th of March; that day the Nautilus was employed in taking
  8896	soundings, which greatly interested me.  We had then made about 13,000
  8897	leagues since our departure from the high seas of the Pacific.  The
  8898	bearings gave us 45° 37' S. lat., and 37° 53' W. long.  It was
  8899	the same water in which Captain Denham of the Herald sounded 7,000
  8900	fathoms without finding the bottom.  There, too, Lieutenant Parker, of
  8901	the American frigate Congress, could not touch the bottom with 15,140
  8902	fathoms.  Captain Nemo intended seeking the bottom of the ocean by a
  8903	diagonal sufficiently lengthened by means of lateral planes placed at
  8904	an angle of 45° with the water-line of the Nautilus.  Then the
  8905	screw set to work at its maximum speed, its four blades beating the
  8906	waves with in describable force.  Under this powerful pressure, the
  8907	hull of the Nautilus quivered like a sonorous chord and sank regularly
  8908	under the water.
  8909	
  8910	At 7,000 fathoms I saw some blackish tops rising from the midst of the
  8911	waters; but these summits might belong to high mountains like the
  8912	Himalayas or Mont Blanc, even higher; and the depth of the abyss
  8913	remained incalculable.  The Nautilus descended still lower, in spite of
  8914	the great pressure.  I felt the steel plates tremble at the fastenings
  8915	of the bolts; its bars bent, its partitions groaned; the windows of the
  8916	saloon seemed to curve under the pressure of the waters.  And this firm
  8917	structure would doubtless have yielded, if, as its Captain had said, it
  8918	had not been capable of resistance like a solid block.  We had attained
  8919	a depth of 16,000 yards (four leagues), and the sides of the Nautilus
  8920	then bore a pressure of 1,600 atmospheres, that is to say, 3,200 lb.
  8921	to each square two-fifths of an inch of its surface.
  8922	
  8923	"What a situation to be in!"  I exclaimed.  "To overrun these deep
  8924	regions where man has never trod!  Look, Captain, look at these
  8925	magnificent rocks, these uninhabited grottoes, these lowest receptacles
  8926	of the globe, where life is no longer possible!  What unknown sights
  8927	are here!  Why should we be unable to preserve a remembrance of them?"
  8928	
  8929	"Would you like to carry away more than the remembrance?" said Captain
  8930	Nemo.
  8931	
  8932	"What do you mean by those words?"
  8933	
  8934	"I mean to say that nothing is easier than to make a photographic view
  8935	of this submarine region."
  8936	
  8937	I had not time to express my surprise at this new proposition, when, at
  8938	Captain Nemo's call, an objective was brought into the saloon.  Through
  8939	the widely-opened panel, the liquid mass was bright with electricity,
  8940	which was distributed with such uniformity that not a shadow, not a
  8941	gradation, was to be seen in our manufactured light.  The Nautilus
  8942	remained motionless, the force of its screw subdued by the inclination
  8943	of its planes: the instrument was propped on the bottom of the oceanic
  8944	site, and in a few seconds we had obtained a perfect negative.
  8945	
  8946	But, the operation being over, Captain Nemo said, "Let us go up; we
  8947	must not abuse our position, nor expose the Nautilus too long to such
  8948	great pressure."
  8949	
  8950	"Go up again!"  I exclaimed.
  8951	
  8952	"Hold well on."
  8953	
  8954	I had not time to understand why the Captain cautioned me thus, when I
  8955	was thrown forward on to the carpet.  At a signal from the Captain, its
  8956	screw was shipped, and its blades raised vertically; the Nautilus shot
  8957	into the air like a balloon, rising with stunning rapidity, and cutting
  8958	the mass of waters with a sonorous agitation.  Nothing was visible; and
  8959	in four minutes it had shot through the four leagues which separated it
  8960	from the ocean, and, after emerging like a flying-fish, fell, making
  8961	the waves rebound to an enormous height.
  8962	
  8963	
  8964	
  8965	CHAPTER XII
  8966	
  8967	CACHALOTS AND WHALES
  8968	
  8969	During the nights of the 13th and 14th of March, the Nautilus returned
  8970	to its southerly course.  I fancied that, when on a level with Cape
  8971	Horn, he would turn the helm westward, in order to beat the Pacific
  8972	seas, and so complete the tour of the world.  He did nothing of the
  8973	kind, but continued on his way to the southern regions.  Where was he
  8974	going to?  To the pole?  It was madness!  I began to think that the
  8975	Captain's temerity justified Ned Land's fears.  For some time past the
  8976	Canadian had not spoken to me of his projects of flight; he was less
  8977	communicative, almost silent.  I could see that this lengthened
  8978	imprisonment was weighing upon him, and I felt that rage was burning
  8979	within him.  When he met the Captain, his eyes lit up with suppressed
  8980	anger; and I feared that his natural violence would lead him into some
  8981	extreme.  That day, the 14th of March, Conseil and he came to me in my
  8982	room.  I inquired the cause of their visit.
  8983	
  8984	"A simple question to ask you, sir," replied the Canadian.
  8985	
  8986	"Speak, Ned."
  8987	
  8988	"How many men are there on board the Nautilus, do you think?"
  8989	
  8990	"I cannot tell, my friend."
  8991	
  8992	"I should say that its working does not require a large crew."
  8993	
  8994	"Certainly, under existing conditions, ten men, at the most, ought to
  8995	be enough."
  8996	
  8997	"Well, why should there be any more?"
  8998	
  8999	"Why?"  I replied, looking fixedly at Ned Land, whose meaning was easy
  9000	to guess.  "Because," I added, "if my surmises are correct, and if I
  9001	have well understood the Captain's existence, the Nautilus is not only
  9002	a vessel: it is also a place of refuge for those who, like its
  9003	commander, have broken every tie upon earth."
  9004	
  9005	"Perhaps so," said Conseil; "but, in any case, the Nautilus can only
  9006	contain a certain number of men.  Could not you, sir, estimate their
  9007	maximum?"
  9008	
  9009	"How, Conseil?"
  9010	
  9011	"By calculation; given the size of the vessel, which you know, sir, and
  9012	consequently the quantity of air it contains, knowing also how much
  9013	each man expends at a breath, and comparing these results with the fact
  9014	that the Nautilus is obliged to go to the surface every twenty-four
  9015	hours."
  9016	
  9017	Conseil had not finished the sentence before I saw what he was driving
  9018	at.
  9019	
  9020	"I understand," said I; "but that calculation, though simple enough,
  9021	can give but a very uncertain result."
  9022	
  9023	"Never mind," said Ned Land urgently.
  9024	
  9025	"Here it is, then," said I. "In one hour each man consumes the oxygen
  9026	contained in twenty gallons of air; and in twenty-four, that contained
  9027	in 480 gallons.  We must, therefore find how many times 480 gallons of
  9028	air the Nautilus contains."
  9029	
  9030	"Just so," said Conseil.
  9031	
  9032	"Or," I continued, "the size of the Nautilus being 1,500 tons; and one
  9033	ton holding 200 gallons, it contains 300,000 gallons of air, which,
  9034	divided by 480, gives a quotient of 625.  Which means to say, strictly
  9035	speaking, that the air contained in the Nautilus would suffice for 625
  9036	men for twenty-four hours."
  9037	
  9038	"Six hundred and twenty-five!" repeated Ned.
  9039	
  9040	"But remember that all of us, passengers, sailors, and officers
  9041	included, would not form a tenth part of that number."
  9042	
  9043	"Still too many for three men," murmured Conseil.
  9044	
  9045	The Canadian shook his head, passed his hand across his forehead, and
  9046	left the room without answering.
  9047	
  9048	"Will you allow me to make one observation, sir?" said Conseil.  "Poor
  9049	Ned is longing for everything that he can not have.  His past life is
  9050	always present to him; everything that we are forbidden he regrets.
  9051	His head is full of old recollections.  And we must understand him.
  9052	What has he to do here?  Nothing; he is not learned like you, sir; and
  9053	has not the same taste for the beauties of the sea that we have.  He
  9054	would risk everything to be able to go once more into a tavern in his
  9055	own country."
  9056	
  9057	Certainly the monotony on board must seem intolerable to the Canadian,
  9058	accustomed as he was to a life of liberty and activity.  Events were
  9059	rare which could rouse him to any show of spirit; but that day an event
  9060	did happen which recalled the bright days of the harpooner.  About
  9061	eleven in the morning, being on the surface of the ocean, the Nautilus
  9062	fell in with a troop of whales--an encounter which did not astonish me,
  9063	knowing that these creatures, hunted to death, had taken refuge in high
  9064	latitudes.
  9065	
  9066	We were seated on the platform, with a quiet sea.  The month of October
  9067	in those latitudes gave us some lovely autumnal days.  It was the
  9068	Canadian--he could not be mistaken--who signalled a whale on the
  9069	eastern horizon.  Looking attentively, one might see its black back
  9070	rise and fall with the waves five miles from the Nautilus.
  9071	
  9072	"Ah!" exclaimed Ned Land, "if I was on board a whaler, now such a
  9073	meeting would give me pleasure.  It is one of large size.  See with
  9074	what strength its blow-holes throw up columns of air an steam!
  9075	Confound it, why am I bound to these steel plates?"
  9076	
  9077	"What, Ned," said I, "you have not forgotten your old ideas of fishing?"
  9078	
  9079	"Can a whale-fisher ever forget his old trade, sir?  Can he ever tire
  9080	of the emotions caused by such a chase?"
  9081	
  9082	"You have never fished in these seas, Ned?"
  9083	
  9084	"Never, sir; in the northern only, and as much in Behring as in Davis
  9085	Straits."
  9086	
  9087	"Then the southern whale is still unknown to you.  It is the Greenland
  9088	whale you have hunted up to this time, and that would not risk passing
  9089	through the warm waters of the equator.  Whales are localised,
  9090	according to their kinds, in certain seas which they never leave.  And
  9091	if one of these creatures went from Behring to Davis Straits, it must
  9092	be simply because there is a passage from one sea to the other, either
  9093	on the American or the Asiatic side."
  9094	
  9095	"In that case, as I have never fished in these seas, I do not know the
  9096	kind of whale frequenting them!"
  9097	
  9098	"I have told you, Ned."
  9099	
  9100	"A greater reason for making their acquaintance," said Conseil.
  9101	
  9102	"Look! look!" exclaimed the Canadian, "they approach: they aggravate
  9103	me; they know that I cannot get at them!"
  9104	
  9105	Ned stamped his feet.  His hand trembled, as he grasped an imaginary
  9106	harpoon.
  9107	
  9108	"Are these cetaceans as large as those of the northern seas?" asked he.
  9109	
  9110	"Very nearly, Ned."
  9111	
  9112	"Because I have seen large whales, sir, whales measuring a hundred
  9113	feet.  I have even been told that those of Hullamoch and Umgallick, of
  9114	the Aleutian Islands, are sometimes a hundred and fifty feet long."
  9115	
  9116	"That seems to me exaggeration. These creatures are only
  9117	balaeaopterons, provided with dorsal fins; and, like the cachalots, are
  9118	generally much smaller than the Greenland whale."
  9119	
  9120	"Ah!" exclaimed the Canadian, whose eyes had never left the ocean,
  9121	"they are coming nearer; they are in the same water as the Nautilus."
  9122	
  9123	Then, returning to the conversation, he said:
  9124	
  9125	"You spoke of the cachalot as a small creature.  I have heard of
  9126	gigantic ones.  They are intelligent cetacea.  It is said of some that
  9127	they cover themselves with seaweed and fucus, and then are taken for
  9128	islands.  People encamp upon them, and settle there; lights a fire----"
  9129	
  9130	"And build houses," said Conseil.
  9131	
  9132	"Yes, joker," said Ned Land.  "And one fine day the creature plunges,
  9133	carrying with it all the inhabitants to the bottom of the sea."
  9134	
  9135	"Something like the travels of Sinbad the Sailor," I replied, laughing.
  9136	
  9137	"Ah!" suddenly exclaimed Ned Land, "it is not one whale; there are
  9138	ten--there are twenty--it is a whole troop!  And I not able to do
  9139	anything! hands and feet tied!"
  9140	
  9141	"But, friend Ned," said Conseil, "why do you not ask Captain Nemo's
  9142	permission to chase them?"
  9143	
  9144	Conseil had not finished his sentence when Ned Land had lowered himself
  9145	through the panel to seek the Captain.  A few minutes afterwards the
  9146	two appeared together on the platform.
  9147	
  9148	Captain Nemo watched the troop of cetacea playing on the waters about a
  9149	mile from the Nautilus.
  9150	
  9151	"They are southern whales," said he; "there goes the fortune of a whole
  9152	fleet of whalers."
  9153	
  9154	"Well, sir," asked the Canadian, "can I not chase them, if only to
  9155	remind me of my old trade of harpooner?"
  9156	
  9157	"And to what purpose?" replied Captain Nemo; "only to destroy!  We have
  9158	nothing to do with the whale-oil on board."
  9159	
  9160	"But, sir," continued the Canadian, "in the Red Sea you allowed us to
  9161	follow the dugong."
  9162	
  9163	"Then it was to procure fresh meat for my crew.  Here it would be
  9164	killing for killing's sake.  I know that is a privilege reserved for
  9165	man, but I do not approve of such murderous pastime.  In destroying the
  9166	southern whale (like the Greenland whale, an inoffensive creature),
  9167	your traders do a culpable action, Master Land.  They have already
  9168	depopulated the whole of Baffin's Bay, and are annihilating a class of
  9169	useful animals.  Leave the unfortunate cetacea alone.  They have plenty
  9170	of natural enemies--cachalots, swordfish, and sawfish--without you
  9171	troubling them."
  9172	
  9173	The Captain was right.  The barbarous and inconsiderate greed of these
  9174	fishermen will one day cause the disappearance of the last whale in the
  9175	ocean.  Ned Land whistled "Yankee-doodle" between his teeth, thrust his
  9176	hands into his pockets, and turned his back upon us.  But Captain Nemo
  9177	watched the troop of cetacea, and, addressing me, said:
  9178	
  9179	"I was right in saying that whales had natural enemies enough, without
  9180	counting man.  These will have plenty to do before long.  Do you see,
  9181	M. Aronnax, about eight miles to leeward, those blackish moving points?"
  9182	
  9183	"Yes, Captain," I replied.
  9184	
  9185	"Those are cachalots--terrible animals, which I have met in troops of
  9186	two or three hundred.  As to those, they are cruel, mischievous
  9187	creatures; they would be right in exterminating them."
  9188	
  9189	The Canadian turned quickly at the last words.
  9190	
  9191	"Well, Captain," said he, "it is still time, in the interest of the
  9192	whales."
  9193	
  9194	"It is useless to expose one's self, Professor.  The Nautilus will
  9195	disperse them.  It is armed with a steel spur as good as Master Land's
  9196	harpoon, I imagine."
  9197	
  9198	The Canadian did not put himself out enough to shrug his shoulders.
  9199	Attack cetacea with blows of a spur!  Who had ever heard of such a
  9200	thing?
  9201	
  9202	"Wait, M. Aronnax," said Captain Nemo.  "We will show you something you
  9203	have never yet seen.  We have no pity for these ferocious creatures.
  9204	They are nothing but mouth and teeth."
  9205	
  9206	Mouth and teeth!  No one could better describe the macrocephalous
  9207	cachalot, which is sometimes more than seventy-five feet long.  Its
  9208	enormous head occupies one-third of its entire body.  Better armed than
  9209	the whale, whose upper jaw is furnished only with whalebone, it is
  9210	supplied with twenty-five large tusks, about eight inches long,
  9211	cylindrical and conical at the top, each weighing two pounds.  It is in
  9212	the upper part of this enormous head, in great cavities divided by
  9213	cartilages, that is to be found from six to eight hundred pounds of
  9214	that precious oil called spermaceti.  The cachalot is a disagreeable
  9215	creature, more tadpole than fish, according to Fredol's description.
  9216	It is badly formed, the whole of its left side being (if we may say
  9217	it), a "failure," and being only able to see with its right eye.  But
  9218	the formidable troop was nearing us.  They had seen the whales and were
  9219	preparing to attack them.  One could judge beforehand that the
  9220	cachalots would be victorious, not only because they were better built
  9221	for attack than their inoffensive adversaries, but also because they
  9222	could remain longer under water without coming to the surface.  There
  9223	was only just time to go to the help of the whales.  The Nautilus went
  9224	under water.  Conseil, Ned Land, and I took our places before the
  9225	window in the saloon, and Captain Nemo joined the pilot in his cage to
  9226	work his apparatus as an engine of destruction.  Soon I felt the
  9227	beatings of the screw quicken, and our speed increased.  The battle
  9228	between the cachalots and the whales had already begun when the
  9229	Nautilus arrived.  They did not at first show any fear at the sight of
  9230	this new monster joining in the conflict.  But they soon had to guard
  9231	against its blows.  What a battle!  The Nautilus was nothing but a
  9232	formidable harpoon, brandished by the hand of its Captain.  It hurled
  9233	itself against the fleshy mass, passing through from one part to the
  9234	other, leaving behind it two quivering halves of the animal.  It could
  9235	not feel the formidable blows from their tails upon its sides, nor the
  9236	shock which it produced itself, much more.  One cachalot killed, it ran
  9237	at the next, tacked on the spot that it might not miss its prey, going
  9238	forwards and backwards, answering to its helm, plunging when the
  9239	cetacean dived into the deep waters, coming up with it when it returned
  9240	to the surface, striking it front or sideways, cutting or tearing in
  9241	all directions and at any pace, piercing it with its terrible spur.
  9242	What carnage!  What a noise on the surface of the waves!  What sharp
  9243	hissing, and what snorting peculiar to these enraged animals!  In the
  9244	midst of these waters, generally so peaceful, their tails made perfect
  9245	billows.  For one hour this wholesale massacre continued, from which
  9246	the cachalots could not escape.  Several times ten or twelve united
  9247	tried to crush the Nautilus by their weight.  From the window we could
  9248	see their enormous mouths, studded with tusks, and their formidable
  9249	eyes.  Ned Land could not contain himself; he threatened and swore at
  9250	them.  We could feel them clinging to our vessel like dogs worrying a
  9251	wild boar in a copse.  But the Nautilus, working its screw, carried
  9252	them here and there, or to the upper levels of the ocean, without
  9253	caring for their enormous weight, nor the powerful strain on the
  9254	vessel.  At length the mass of cachalots broke up, the waves became
  9255	quiet, and I felt that we were rising to the surface.  The panel
  9256	opened, and we hurried on to the platform.  The sea was covered with
  9257	mutilated bodies.  A formidable explosion could not have divided and
  9258	torn this fleshy mass with more violence.  We were floating amid
  9259	gigantic bodies, bluish on the back and white underneath, covered with
  9260	enormous protuberances.  Some terrified cachalots were flying towards
  9261	the horizon.  The waves were dyed red for several miles, and the
  9262	Nautilus floated in a sea of blood:  Captain Nemo joined us.
  9263	
  9264	"Well, Master Land?" said he.
  9265	
  9266	"Well, sir," replied the Canadian, whose enthusiasm had somewhat
  9267	calmed; "it is a terrible spectacle, certainly.  But I am not a
  9268	butcher.  I am a hunter, and I call this a butchery."
  9269	
  9270	"It is a massacre of mischievous creatures," replied the Captain; "and
  9271	the Nautilus is not a butcher's knife."
  9272	
  9273	"I like my harpoon better," said the Canadian.
  9274	
  9275	"Every one to his own," answered the Captain, looking fixedly at Ned
  9276	Land.
  9277	
  9278	I feared he would commit some act of violence, which would end in sad
  9279	consequences.  But his anger was turned by the sight of a whale which
  9280	the Nautilus had just come up with.  The creature had not quite escaped
  9281	from the cachalot's teeth.  I recognised the southern whale by its flat
  9282	head, which is entirely black.  Anatomically, it is distinguished from
  9283	the white whale and the North Cape whale by the seven cervical
  9284	vertebrae, and it has two more ribs than its congeners.  The
  9285	unfortunate cetacean was lying on its side, riddled with holes from the
  9286	bites, and quite dead.  From its mutilated fin still hung a young whale
  9287	which it could not save from the massacre.  Its open mouth let the
  9288	water flow in and out, murmuring like the waves breaking on the shore.
  9289	Captain Nemo steered close to the corpse of the creature.  Two of his
  9290	men mounted its side, and I saw, not without surprise, that they were
  9291	drawing from its breasts all the milk which they contained, that is to
  9292	say, about two or three tons.  The Captain offered me a cup of the
  9293	milk, which was still warm.  I could not help showing my repugnance to
  9294	the drink; but he assured me that it was excellent, and not to be
  9295	distinguished from cow's milk.  I tasted it, and was of his opinion.
  9296	It was a useful reserve to us, for in the shape of salt butter or
  9297	cheese it would form an agreeable variety from our ordinary food.  From
  9298	that day I noticed with uneasiness that Ned Land's ill-will towards
  9299	Captain Nemo increased, and I resolved to watch the Canadian's gestures
  9300	closely.
  9301	
  9302	
  9303	
  9304	CHAPTER XIII
  9305	
  9306	THE ICEBERG
  9307	
  9308	The Nautilus was steadily pursuing its southerly course, following the
  9309	fiftieth meridian with considerable speed.  Did he wish to reach the
  9310	pole?  I did not think so, for every attempt to reach that point had
  9311	hitherto failed.  Again, the season was far advanced, for in the
  9312	Antarctic regions the 13th of March corresponds with the 13th of
  9313	September of northern regions, which begin at the equinoctial season.
  9314	On the 14th of March I saw floating ice in latitude 55°, merely
  9315	pale bits of debris from twenty to twenty-five feet long, forming banks
  9316	over which the sea curled.  The Nautilus remained on the surface of the
  9317	ocean.  Ned Land, who had fished in the Arctic Seas, was familiar with
  9318	its icebergs; but Conseil and I admired them for the first time.  In
  9319	the atmosphere towards the southern horizon stretched a white dazzling
  9320	band.  English whalers have given it the name of "ice blink."  However
  9321	thick the clouds may be, it is always visible, and announces the
  9322	presence of an ice pack or bank.  Accordingly, larger blocks soon
  9323	appeared, whose brilliancy changed with the caprices of the fog.  Some
  9324	of these masses showed green veins, as if long undulating lines had
  9325	been traced with sulphate of copper; others resembled enormous
  9326	amethysts with the light shining through them.  Some reflected the
  9327	light of day upon a thousand crystal facets.  Others shaded with vivid
  9328	calcareous reflections resembled a perfect town of marble.  The more we
  9329	neared the south the more these floating islands increased both in
  9330	number and importance.
  9331	
  9332	At 60° lat. every pass had disappeared.  But, seeking carefully,
  9333	Captain Nemo soon found a narrow opening, through which he boldly
  9334	slipped, knowing, however, that it would close behind him.  Thus,
  9335	guided by this clever hand, the Nautilus passed through all the ice
  9336	with a precision which quite charmed Conseil; icebergs or mountains,
  9337	ice-fields or smooth plains, seeming to have no limits, drift-ice or
  9338	floating ice-packs, plains broken up, called palchs when they are
  9339	circular, and streams when they are made up of long strips.  The
  9340	temperature was very low; the thermometer exposed to the air marked 2
  9341	deg. or 3° below zero, but we were warmly clad with fur, at the
  9342	expense of the sea-bear and seal.  The interior of the Nautilus, warmed
  9343	regularly by its electric apparatus, defied the most intense cold.
  9344	Besides, it would only have been necessary to go some yards beneath the
  9345	waves to find a more bearable temperature.  Two months earlier we
  9346	should have had perpetual daylight in these latitudes; but already we
  9347	had had three or four hours of night, and by and by there would be six
  9348	months of darkness in these circumpolar regions.  On the 15th of March
  9349	we were in the latitude of New Shetland and South Orkney.  The Captain
  9350	told me that formerly numerous tribes of seals inhabited them; but that
  9351	English and American whalers, in their rage for destruction, massacred
  9352	both old and young; thus, where there was once life and animation, they
  9353	had left silence and death.
  9354	
  9355	About eight o'clock on the morning of the 16th of March the Nautilus,
  9356	following the fifty-fifth meridian, cut the Antarctic polar circle.
  9357	Ice surrounded us on all sides, and closed the horizon.  But Captain
  9358	Nemo went from one opening to another, still going higher.  I cannot
  9359	express my astonishment at the beauties of these new regions.  The ice
  9360	took most surprising forms.  Here the grouping formed an oriental town,
  9361	with innumerable mosques and minarets; there a fallen city thrown to
  9362	the earth, as it were, by some convulsion of nature.  The whole aspect
  9363	was constantly changed by the oblique rays of the sun, or lost in the
  9364	greyish fog amidst hurricanes of snow.  Detonations and falls were
  9365	heard on all sides, great overthrows of icebergs, which altered the
  9366	whole landscape like a diorama.  Often seeing no exit, I thought we
  9367	were definitely prisoners; but, instinct guiding him at the slightest
  9368	indication, Captain Nemo would discover a new pass.  He was never
  9369	mistaken when he saw the thin threads of bluish water trickling along
  9370	the ice-fields; and I had no doubt that he had already ventured into
  9371	the midst of these Antarctic seas before.  On the 16th of March,
  9372	however, the ice-fields absolutely blocked our road.  It was not the
  9373	iceberg itself, as yet, but vast fields cemented by the cold.  But this
  9374	obstacle could not stop Captain Nemo: he hurled himself against it with
  9375	frightful violence.  The Nautilus entered the brittle mass like a
  9376	wedge, and split it with frightful crackings.  It was the battering ram
  9377	of the ancients hurled by infinite strength.  The ice, thrown high in
  9378	the air, fell like hail around us.  By its own power of impulsion our
  9379	apparatus made a canal for itself; some times carried away by its own
  9380	impetus, it lodged on the ice-field, crushing it with its weight, and
  9381	sometimes buried beneath it, dividing it by a simple pitching movement,
  9382	producing large rents in it.  Violent gales assailed us at this time,
  9383	accompanied by thick fogs, through which, from one end of the platform
  9384	to the other, we could see nothing.  The wind blew sharply from all
  9385	parts of the compass, and the snow lay in such hard heaps that we had
  9386	to break it with blows of a pickaxe.  The temperature was always at 5
  9387	deg. below zero; every outward part of the Nautilus was covered with
  9388	ice.  A rigged vessel would have been entangled in the blocked up
  9389	gorges.  A vessel without sails, with electricity for its motive power,
  9390	and wanting no coal, could alone brave such high latitudes.  At length,
  9391	on the 18th of March, after many useless assaults, the Nautilus was
  9392	positively blocked.  It was no longer either streams, packs, or
  9393	ice-fields, but an interminable and immovable barrier, formed by
  9394	mountains soldered together.
  9395	
  9396	"An iceberg!" said the Canadian to me.
  9397	
  9398	I knew that to Ned Land, as well as to all other navigators who had
  9399	preceded us, this was an inevitable obstacle.  The sun appearing for an
  9400	instant at noon, Captain Nemo took an observation as near as possible,
  9401	which gave our situation at 51° 30' long. and 67° 39' of S.
  9402	lat.  We had advanced one degree more in this Antarctic region.  Of the
  9403	liquid surface of the sea there was no longer a glimpse.  Under the
  9404	spur of the Nautilus lay stretched a vast plain, entangled with
  9405	confused blocks.  Here and there sharp points and slender needles
  9406	rising to a height of 200 feet; further on a steep shore, hewn as it
  9407	were with an axe and clothed with greyish tints; huge mirrors,
  9408	reflecting a few rays of sunshine, half drowned in the fog.  And over
  9409	this desolate face of nature a stern silence reigned, scarcely broken
  9410	by the flapping of the wings of petrels and puffins.  Everything was
  9411	frozen--even the noise.  The Nautilus was then obliged to stop in its
  9412	adventurous course amid these fields of ice.  In spite of our efforts,
  9413	in spite of the powerful means employed to break up the ice, the
  9414	Nautilus remained immovable.  Generally, when we can proceed no
  9415	further, we have return still open to us; but here return was as
  9416	impossible as advance, for every pass had closed behind us; and for the
  9417	few moments when we were stationary, we were likely to be entirely
  9418	blocked, which did indeed happen about two o'clock in the afternoon,
  9419	the fresh ice forming around its sides with astonishing rapidity.  I
  9420	was obliged to admit that Captain Nemo was more than imprudent.  I was
  9421	on the platform at that moment.  The Captain had been observing our
  9422	situation for some time past, when he said to me:
  9423	
  9424	"Well, sir, what do you think of this?"
  9425	
  9426	"I think that we are caught, Captain."
  9427	
  9428	"So, M. Aronnax, you really think that the Nautilus cannot disengage
  9429	itself?"
  9430	
  9431	"With difficulty, Captain; for the season is already too far advanced
  9432	for you to reckon on the breaking of the ice."
  9433	
  9434	"Ah! sir," said Captain Nemo, in an ironical tone, "you will always be
  9435	the same.  You see nothing but difficulties and obstacles.  I affirm
  9436	that not only can the Nautilus disengage itself, but also that it can
  9437	go further still."
  9438	
  9439	"Further to the South?"  I asked, looking at the Captain.
  9440	
  9441	"Yes, sir; it shall go to the pole."
  9442	
  9443	"To the pole!"  I exclaimed, unable to repress a gesture of incredulity.
  9444	
  9445	"Yes," replied the Captain, coldly, "to the Antarctic pole--to that
  9446	unknown point from whence springs every meridian of the globe.  You
  9447	know whether I can do as I please with the Nautilus!"
  9448	
  9449	Yes, I knew that.  I knew that this man was bold, even to rashness.
  9450	But to conquer those obstacles which bristled round the South Pole,
  9451	rendering it more inaccessible than the North, which had not yet been
  9452	reached by the boldest navigators--was it not a mad enterprise, one
  9453	which only a maniac would have conceived?  It then came into my head to
  9454	ask Captain Nemo if he had ever discovered that pole which had never
  9455	yet been trodden by a human creature?
  9456	
  9457	"No, sir," he replied; "but we will discover it together.  Where others
  9458	have failed, I will not fail.  I have never yet led my Nautilus so far
  9459	into southern seas; but, I repeat, it shall go further yet."
  9460	
  9461	"I can well believe you, Captain," said I, in a slightly ironical tone.
  9462	"I believe you!  Let us go ahead!  There are no obstacles for us!  Let
  9463	us smash this iceberg!  Let us blow it up; and, if it resists, let us
  9464	give the Nautilus wings to fly over it!"
  9465	
  9466	"Over it, sir!" said Captain Nemo, quietly; "no, not over it, but under
  9467	it!"
  9468	
  9469	"Under it!"  I exclaimed, a sudden idea of the Captain's projects
  9470	flashing upon my mind.  I understood; the wonderful qualities of the
  9471	Nautilus were going to serve us in this superhuman enterprise.
  9472	
  9473	"I see we are beginning to understand one another, sir," said the
  9474	Captain, half smiling.  "You begin to see the possibility--I should say
  9475	the success--of this attempt.  That which is impossible for an ordinary
  9476	vessel is easy to the Nautilus.  If a continent lies before the pole,
  9477	it must stop before the continent; but if, on the contrary, the pole is
  9478	washed by open sea, it will go even to the pole."
  9479	
  9480	"Certainly," said I, carried away by the Captain's reasoning; "if the
  9481	surface of the sea is solidified by the ice, the lower depths are free
  9482	by the Providential law which has placed the maximum of density of the
  9483	waters of the ocean one degree higher than freezing-point; and, if I am
  9484	not mistaken, the portion of this iceberg which is above the water is
  9485	as one to four to that which is below."
  9486	
  9487	"Very nearly, sir; for one foot of iceberg above the sea there are
  9488	three below it.  If these ice mountains are not more than 300 feet
  9489	above the surface, they are not more than 900 beneath.  And what are
  9490	900 feet to the Nautilus?"
  9491	
  9492	"Nothing, sir."
  9493	
  9494	"It could even seek at greater depths that uniform temperature of
  9495	sea-water, and there brave with impunity the thirty or forty degrees of
  9496	surface cold."
  9497	
  9498	"Just so, sir--just so," I replied, getting animated.
  9499	
  9500	"The only difficulty," continued Captain Nemo, "is that of remaining
  9501	several days without renewing our provision of air."
  9502	
  9503	"Is that all?  The Nautilus has vast reservoirs; we can fill them, and
  9504	they will supply us with all the oxygen we want."
  9505	
  9506	"Well thought of, M. Aronnax," replied the Captain, smiling.  "But, not
  9507	wishing you to accuse me of rashness, I will first give you all my
  9508	objections."
  9509	
  9510	"Have you any more to make?"
  9511	
  9512	"Only one.  It is possible, if the sea exists at the South Pole, that
  9513	it may be covered; and, consequently, we shall be unable to come to the
  9514	surface."
  9515	
  9516	"Good, sir! but do you forget that the Nautilus is armed with a
  9517	powerful spur, and could we not send it diagonally against these fields
  9518	of ice, which would open at the shocks."
  9519	
  9520	"Ah! sir, you are full of ideas to-day."
  9521	
  9522	"Besides, Captain," I added, enthusiastically, "why should we not find
  9523	the sea open at the South Pole as well as at the North?  The frozen
  9524	poles of the earth do not coincide, either in the southern or in the
  9525	northern regions; and, until it is proved to the contrary, we may
  9526	suppose either a continent or an ocean free from ice at these two
  9527	points of the globe."
  9528	
  9529	"I think so too, M. Aronnax," replied Captain Nemo.  "I only wish you
  9530	to observe that, after having made so many objections to my project,
  9531	you are now crushing me with arguments in its favour!"
  9532	
  9533	The preparations for this audacious attempt now began.  The powerful
  9534	pumps of the Nautilus were working air into the reservoirs and storing
  9535	it at high pressure.  About four o'clock, Captain Nemo announced the
  9536	closing of the panels on the platform.  I threw one last look at the
  9537	massive iceberg which we were going to cross.  The weather was clear,
  9538	the atmosphere pure enough, the cold very great, being 12° below
  9539	zero; but, the wind having gone down, this temperature was not so
  9540	unbearable.  About ten men mounted the sides of the Nautilus, armed
  9541	with pickaxes to break the ice around the vessel, which was soon free.
  9542	The operation was quickly performed, for the fresh ice was still very
  9543	thin.  We all went below.  The usual reservoirs were filled with the
  9544	newly-liberated water, and the Nautilus soon descended.  I had taken my
  9545	place with Conseil in the saloon; through the open window we could see
  9546	the lower beds of the Southern Ocean.  The thermometer went up, the
  9547	needle of the compass deviated on the dial.  At about 900 feet, as
  9548	Captain Nemo had foreseen, we were floating beneath the undulating
  9549	bottom of the iceberg.  But the Nautilus went lower still--it went to
  9550	the depth of four hundred fathoms.  The temperature of the water at the
  9551	surface showed twelve degrees, it was now only ten; we had gained two.
  9552	I need not say the temperature of the Nautilus was raised by its
  9553	heating apparatus to a much higher degree; every manoeuvre was
  9554	accomplished with wonderful precision.
  9555	
  9556	"We shall pass it, if you please, sir," said Conseil.
  9557	
  9558	"I believe we shall," I said, in a tone of firm conviction.
  9559	
  9560	In this open sea, the Nautilus had taken its course direct to the pole,
  9561	without leaving the fifty-second meridian.  From 67° 30' to 90
  9562	deg., twenty-two degrees and a half of latitude remained to travel;
  9563	that is, about five hundred leagues.  The Nautilus kept up a mean speed
  9564	of twenty-six miles an hour--the speed of an express train.  If that
  9565	was kept up, in forty hours we should reach the pole.
  9566	
  9567	For a part of the night the novelty of the situation kept us at the
  9568	window.  The sea was lit with the electric lantern; but it was
  9569	deserted; fishes did not sojourn in these imprisoned waters; they only
  9570	found there a passage to take them from the Antarctic Ocean to the open
  9571	polar sea.  Our pace was rapid; we could feel it by the quivering of
  9572	the long steel body.  About two in the morning I took some hours'
  9573	repose, and Conseil did the same.  In crossing the waist I did not meet
  9574	Captain Nemo: I supposed him to be in the pilot's cage.  The next
  9575	morning, the 19th of March, I took my post once more in the saloon.
  9576	The electric log told me that the speed of the Nautilus had been
  9577	slackened.  It was then going towards the surface; but prudently
  9578	emptying its reservoirs very slowly.  My heart beat fast.  Were we
  9579	going to emerge and regain the open polar atmosphere?  No!  A shock
  9580	told me that the Nautilus had struck the bottom of the iceberg, still
  9581	very thick, judging from the deadened sound.  We had in deed "struck,"
  9582	to use a sea expression, but in an inverse sense, and at a thousand
  9583	feet deep.  This would give three thousand feet of ice above us; one
  9584	thousand being above the water-mark. The iceberg was then higher than
  9585	at its borders--not a very reassuring fact.  Several times that day the
  9586	Nautilus tried again, and every time it struck the wall which lay like
  9587	a ceiling above it.  Sometimes it met with but 900 yards, only 200 of
  9588	which rose above the surface.  It was twice the height it was when the
  9589	Nautilus had gone under the waves.  I carefully noted the different
  9590	depths, and thus obtained a submarine profile of the chain as it was
  9591	developed under the water.  That night no change had taken place in our
  9592	situation.  Still ice between four and five hundred yards in depth!  It
  9593	was evidently diminishing, but, still, what a thickness between us and
  9594	the surface of the ocean!  It was then eight.  According to the daily
  9595	custom on board the Nautilus, its air should have been renewed four
  9596	hours ago; but I did not suffer much, although Captain Nemo had not yet
  9597	made any demand upon his reserve of oxygen.  My sleep was painful that
  9598	night; hope and fear besieged me by turns: I rose several times.  The
  9599	groping of the Nautilus continued.  About three in the morning, I
  9600	noticed that the lower surface of the iceberg was only about fifty feet
  9601	deep.  One hundred and fifty feet now separated us from the surface of
  9602	the waters.  The iceberg was by degrees becoming an ice-field, the
  9603	mountain a plain.  My eyes never left the manometer.  We were still
  9604	rising diagonally to the surface, which sparkled under the electric
  9605	rays.  The iceberg was stretching both above and beneath into
  9606	lengthening slopes; mile after mile it was getting thinner.  At length,
  9607	at six in the morning of that memorable day, the 19th of March, the
  9608	door of the saloon opened, and Captain Nemo appeared.
  9609	
  9610	"The sea is open!!" was all he said.
  9611	
  9612	
  9613	
  9614	CHAPTER XIV
  9615	
  9616	THE SOUTH POLE
  9617	
  9618	I rushed on to the platform.  Yes! the open sea, with but a few
  9619	scattered pieces of ice and moving icebergs--a long stretch of sea; a
  9620	world of birds in the air, and myriads of fishes under those waters,
  9621	which varied from intense blue to olive green, according to the bottom.
  9622	The thermometer marked 3° C. above zero.  It was comparatively
  9623	spring, shut up as we were behind this iceberg, whose lengthened mass
  9624	was dimly seen on our northern horizon.
  9625	
  9626	"Are we at the pole?"  I asked the Captain, with a beating heart.
  9627	
  9628	"I do not know," he replied.  "At noon I will take our bearings."
  9629	
  9630	"But will the sun show himself through this fog?" said I, looking at
  9631	the leaden sky.
  9632	
  9633	"However little it shows, it will be enough," replied the Captain.
  9634	
  9635	About ten miles south a solitary island rose to a height of one hundred
  9636	and four yards.  We made for it, but carefully, for the sea might be
  9637	strewn with banks.  One hour afterwards we had reached it, two hours
  9638	later we had made the round of it.  It measured four or five miles in
  9639	circumference.  A narrow canal separated it from a considerable stretch
  9640	of land, perhaps a continent, for we could not see its limits.  The
  9641	existence of this land seemed to give some colour to Maury's theory.
  9642	The ingenious American has remarked that, between the South Pole and
  9643	the sixtieth parallel, the sea is covered with floating ice of enormous
  9644	size, which is never met with in the North Atlantic.  From this fact he
  9645	has drawn the conclusion that the Antarctic Circle encloses
  9646	considerable continents, as icebergs cannot form in open sea, but only
  9647	on the coasts.  According to these calculations, the mass of ice
  9648	surrounding the southern pole forms a vast cap, the circumference of
  9649	which must be, at least, 2,500 miles.  But the Nautilus, for fear of
  9650	running aground, had stopped about three cable-lengths from a strand
  9651	over which reared a superb heap of rocks.  The boat was launched; the
  9652	Captain, two of his men, bearing instruments, Conseil, and myself were
  9653	in it.  It was ten in the morning.  I had not seen Ned Land.  Doubtless
  9654	the Canadian did not wish to admit the presence of the South Pole.  A
  9655	few strokes of the oar brought us to the sand, where we ran ashore.
  9656	Conseil was going to jump on to the land, when I held him back.
  9657	
  9658	"Sir," said I to Captain Nemo, "to you belongs the honour of first
  9659	setting foot on this land."
  9660	
  9661	"Yes, sir," said the Captain, "and if I do not hesitate to tread this
  9662	South Pole, it is because, up to this time, no human being has left a
  9663	trace there."
  9664	
  9665	Saying this, he jumped lightly on to the sand.  His heart beat with
  9666	emotion.  He climbed a rock, sloping to a little promontory, and there,
  9667	with his arms crossed, mute and motionless, and with an eager look, he
  9668	seemed to take possession of these southern regions.  After five
  9669	minutes passed in this ecstasy, he turned to us.
  9670	
  9671	"When you like, sir."
  9672	
  9673	I landed, followed by Conseil, leaving the two men in the boat.  For a
  9674	long way the soil was composed of a reddish sandy stone, something like
  9675	crushed brick, scoriae, streams of lava, and pumice-stones. One could
  9676	not mistake its volcanic origin.  In some parts, slight curls of smoke
  9677	emitted a sulphurous smell, proving that the internal fires had lost
  9678	nothing of their expansive powers, though, having climbed a high
  9679	acclivity, I could see no volcano for a radius of several miles.  We
  9680	know that in those Antarctic countries, James Ross found two craters,
  9681	the Erebus and Terror, in full activity, on the 167th meridian,
  9682	latitude 77° 32'. The vegetation of this desolate continent seemed
  9683	to me much restricted.  Some lichens lay upon the black rocks; some
  9684	microscopic plants, rudimentary diatomas, a kind of cells placed
  9685	between two quartz shells; long purple and scarlet weed, supported on
  9686	little swimming bladders, which the breaking of the waves brought to
  9687	the shore.  These constituted the meagre flora of this region.  The
  9688	shore was strewn with molluscs, little mussels, and limpets.  I also
  9689	saw myriads of northern clios, one-and-a-quarter inches long, of which
  9690	a whale would swallow a whole world at a mouthful; and some perfect
  9691	sea-butterflies, animating the waters on the skirts of the shore.
  9692	
  9693	There appeared on the high bottoms some coral shrubs, of the kind
  9694	which, according to James Ross, live in the Antarctic seas to the depth
  9695	of more than 1,000 yards.  Then there were little kingfishers and
  9696	starfish studding the soil.  But where life abounded most was in the
  9697	air.  There thousands of birds fluttered and flew of all kinds,
  9698	deafening us with their cries; others crowded the rock, looking at us
  9699	as we passed by without fear, and pressing familiarly close by our
  9700	feet.  There were penguins, so agile in the water, heavy and awkward as
  9701	they are on the ground; they were uttering harsh cries, a large
  9702	assembly, sober in gesture, but extravagant in clamour.  Albatrosses
  9703	passed in the air, the expanse of their wings being at least four yards
  9704	and a half, and justly called the vultures of the ocean; some gigantic
  9705	petrels, and some damiers, a kind of small duck, the underpart of whose
  9706	body is black and white; then there were a whole series of petrels,
  9707	some whitish, with brown-bordered wings, others blue, peculiar to the
  9708	Antarctic seas, and so oily, as I told Conseil, that the inhabitants of
  9709	the Ferroe Islands had nothing to do before lighting them but to put a
  9710	wick in.
  9711	
  9712	"A little more," said Conseil, "and they would be perfect lamps!  After
  9713	that, we cannot expect Nature to have previously furnished them with
  9714	wicks!"
  9715	
  9716	About half a mile farther on the soil was riddled with ruffs' nests, a
  9717	sort of laying-ground, out of which many birds were issuing.  Captain
  9718	Nemo had some hundreds hunted.  They uttered a cry like the braying of
  9719	an ass, were about the size of a goose, slate-colour on the body, white
  9720	beneath, with a yellow line round their throats; they allowed
  9721	themselves to be killed with a stone, never trying to escape.  But the
  9722	fog did not lift, and at eleven the sun had not yet shown itself.  Its
  9723	absence made me uneasy.  Without it no observations were possible.
  9724	How, then, could we decide whether we had reached the pole?  When I
  9725	rejoined Captain Nemo, I found him leaning on a piece of rock, silently
  9726	watching the sky.  He seemed impatient and vexed.  But what was to be
  9727	done?  This rash and powerful man could not command the sun as he did
  9728	the sea.  Noon arrived without the orb of day showing itself for an
  9729	instant.  We could not even tell its position behind the curtain of
  9730	fog; and soon the fog turned to snow.
  9731	
  9732	"Till to-morrow," said the Captain, quietly, and we returned to the
  9733	Nautilus amid these atmospheric disturbances.
  9734	
  9735	The tempest of snow continued till the next day.  It was impossible to
  9736	remain on the platform.  From the saloon, where I was taking notes of
  9737	incidents happening during this excursion to the polar continent, I
  9738	could hear the cries of petrels and albatrosses sporting in the midst
  9739	of this violent storm.  The Nautilus did not remain motionless, but
  9740	skirted the coast, advancing ten miles more to the south in the
  9741	half-light left by the sun as it skirted the edge of the horizon.  The
  9742	next day, the 20th of March, the snow had ceased.  The cold was a
  9743	little greater, the thermometer showing 2° below zero.  The fog
  9744	was rising, and I hoped that that day our observations might be taken.
  9745	Captain Nemo not having yet appeared, the boat took Conseil and myself
  9746	to land.  The soil was still of the same volcanic nature; everywhere
  9747	were traces of lava, scoriae, and basalt; but the crater which had
  9748	vomited them I could not see.  Here, as lower down, this continent was
  9749	alive with myriads of birds.  But their rule was now divided with large
  9750	troops of sea-mammals, looking at us with their soft eyes.  There were
  9751	several kinds of seals, some stretched on the earth, some on flakes of
  9752	ice, many going in and out of the sea.  They did not flee at our
  9753	approach, never having had anything to do with man; and I reckoned that
  9754	there were provisions there for hundreds of vessels.
  9755	
  9756	"Sir," said Conseil, "will you tell me the names of these creatures?"
  9757	
  9758	"They are seals and morses."
  9759	
  9760	It was now eight in the morning.  Four hours remained to us before the
  9761	sun could be observed with advantage.  I directed our steps towards a
  9762	vast bay cut in the steep granite shore.  There, I can aver that earth
  9763	and ice were lost to sight by the numbers of sea-mammals covering them,
  9764	and I involuntarily sought for old Proteus, the mythological shepherd
  9765	who watched these immense flocks of Neptune.  There were more seals
  9766	than anything else, forming distinct groups, male and female, the
  9767	father watching over his family, the mother suckling her little ones,
  9768	some already strong enough to go a few steps.  When they wished to
  9769	change their place, they took little jumps, made by the contraction of
  9770	their bodies, and helped awkwardly enough by their imperfect fin,
  9771	which, as with the lamantin, their cousins, forms a perfect forearm.  I
  9772	should say that, in the water, which is their element--the spine of
  9773	these creatures is flexible; with smooth and close skin and webbed
  9774	feet--they swim admirably.  In resting on the earth they take the most
  9775	graceful attitudes.  Thus the ancients, observing their soft and
  9776	expressive looks, which cannot be surpassed by the most beautiful look
  9777	a woman can give, their clear voluptuous eyes, their charming
  9778	positions, and the poetry of their manners, metamorphosed them, the
  9779	male into a triton and the female into a mermaid.  I made Conseil
  9780	notice the considerable development of the lobes of the brain in these
  9781	interesting cetaceans.  No mammal, except man, has such a quantity of
  9782	brain matter; they are also capable of receiving a certain amount of
  9783	education, are easily domesticated, and I think, with other
  9784	naturalists, that if properly taught they would be of great service as
  9785	fishing-dogs.  The greater part of them slept on the rocks or on the
  9786	sand.  Amongst these seals, properly so called, which have no external
  9787	ears (in which they differ from the otter, whose ears are prominent), I
  9788	noticed several varieties of seals about three yards long, with a white
  9789	coat, bulldog heads, armed with teeth in both jaws, four incisors at
  9790	the top and four at the bottom, and two large canine teeth in the shape
  9791	of a fleur-de-lis. Amongst them glided sea-elephants, a kind of seal,
  9792	with short, flexible trunks.  The giants of this species measured
  9793	twenty feet round and ten yards and a half in length; but they did not
  9794	move as we approached.
  9795	
  9796	"These creatures are not dangerous?" asked Conseil.
  9797	
  9798	"No; not unless you attack them.  When they have to defend their young
  9799	their rage is terrible, and it is not uncommon for them to break the
  9800	fishing-boats to pieces."
  9801	
  9802	"They are quite right," said Conseil.
  9803	
  9804	"I do not say they are not."
  9805	
  9806	Two miles farther on we were stopped by the promontory which shelters
  9807	the bay from the southerly winds.  Beyond it we heard loud bellowings
  9808	such as a troop of ruminants would produce.
  9809	
  9810	"Good!" said Conseil; "a concert of bulls!"
  9811	
  9812	"No; a concert of morses."
  9813	
  9814	"They are fighting!"
  9815	
  9816	"They are either fighting or playing."
  9817	
  9818	We now began to climb the blackish rocks, amid unforeseen stumbles, and
  9819	over stones which the ice made slippery.  More than once I rolled over
  9820	at the expense of my loins.  Conseil, more prudent or more steady, did
  9821	not stumble, and helped me up, saying:
  9822	
  9823	"If, sir, you would have the kindness to take wider steps, you would
  9824	preserve your equilibrium better."
  9825	
  9826	Arrived at the upper ridge of the promontory, I saw a vast white plain
  9827	covered with morses.  They were playing amongst themselves, and what we
  9828	heard were bellowings of pleasure, not of anger.
  9829	
  9830	As I passed these curious animals I could examine them leisurely, for
  9831	they did not move.  Their skins were thick and rugged, of a yellowish
  9832	tint, approaching to red; their hair was short and scant.  Some of them
  9833	were four yards and a quarter long.  Quieter and less timid than their
  9834	cousins of the north, they did not, like them, place sentinels round
  9835	the outskirts of their encampment.  After examining this city of
  9836	morses, I began to think of returning.  It was eleven o'clock, and, if
  9837	Captain Nemo found the conditions favourable for observations, I wished
  9838	to be present at the operation.  We followed a narrow pathway running
  9839	along the summit of the steep shore.  At half-past eleven we had
  9840	reached the place where we landed.  The boat had run aground, bringing
  9841	the Captain.  I saw him standing on a block of basalt, his instruments
  9842	near him, his eyes fixed on the northern horizon, near which the sun
  9843	was then describing a lengthened curve.  I took my place beside him,
  9844	and waited without speaking.  Noon arrived, and, as before, the sun did
  9845	not appear.  It was a fatality.  Observations were still wanting.  If
  9846	not accomplished to-morrow, we must give up all idea of taking any.  We
  9847	were indeed exactly at the 20th of March.  To-morrow, the 21st, would
  9848	be the equinox; the sun would disappear behind the horizon for six
  9849	months, and with its disappearance the long polar night would begin.
  9850	Since the September equinox it had emerged from the northern horizon,
  9851	rising by lengthened spirals up to the 21st of December.  At this
  9852	period, the summer solstice of the northern regions, it had begun to
  9853	descend; and to-morrow was to shed its last rays upon them.  I
  9854	communicated my fears and observations to Captain Nemo.
  9855	
  9856	"You are right, M. Aronnax," said he; "if to-morrow I cannot take the
  9857	altitude of the sun, I shall not be able to do it for six months.  But
  9858	precisely because chance has led me into these seas on the 21st of
  9859	March, my bearings will be easy to take, if at twelve we can see the
  9860	sun."
  9861	
  9862	"Why, Captain?"
  9863	
  9864	"Because then the orb of day described such lengthened curves that it
  9865	is difficult to measure exactly its height above the horizon, and grave
  9866	errors may be made with instruments."
  9867	
  9868	"What will you do then?"
  9869	
  9870	"I shall only use my chronometer," replied Captain Nemo.  "If
  9871	to-morrow, the 21st of March, the disc of the sun, allowing for
  9872	refraction, is exactly cut by the northern horizon, it will show that I
  9873	am at the South Pole."
  9874	
  9875	"Just so," said I. "But this statement is not mathematically correct,
  9876	because the equinox does not necessarily begin at noon."
  9877	
  9878	"Very likely, sir; but the error will not be a hundred yards and we do
  9879	not want more.  Till to-morrow, then!"
  9880	
  9881	Captain Nemo returned on board.  Conseil and I remained to survey the
  9882	shore, observing and studying until five o'clock. Then I went to bed,
  9883	not, however, without invoking, like the Indian, the favour of the
  9884	radiant orb.  The next day, the 21st of March, at five in the morning,
  9885	I mounted the platform.  I found Captain Nemo there.
  9886	
  9887	"The weather is lightening a little," said he.  "I have some hope.
  9888	After breakfast we will go on shore and choose a post for observation."
  9889	
  9890	That point settled, I sought Ned Land.  I wanted to take him with me.
  9891	But the obstinate Canadian refused, and I saw that his taciturnity and
  9892	his bad humour grew day by day.  After all, I was not sorry for his
  9893	obstinacy under the circumstances.  Indeed, there were too many seals
  9894	on shore, and we ought not to lay such temptation in this unreflecting
  9895	fisherman's way.  Breakfast over, we went on shore.  The Nautilus had
  9896	gone some miles further up in the night.  It was a whole league from
  9897	the coast, above which reared a sharp peak about five hundred yards
  9898	high.  The boat took with me Captain Nemo, two men of the crew, and the
  9899	instruments, which consisted of a chronometer, a telescope, and a
  9900	barometer.  While crossing, I saw numerous whales belonging to the
  9901	three kinds peculiar to the southern seas; the whale, or the English
  9902	"right whale," which has no dorsal fin; the "humpback," with reeved
  9903	chest and large, whitish fins, which, in spite of its name, do not form
  9904	wings; and the fin-back, of a yellowish brown, the liveliest of all the
  9905	cetacea.  This powerful creature is heard a long way off when he throws
  9906	to a great height columns of air and vapour, which look like whirlwinds
  9907	of smoke.  These different mammals were disporting themselves in troops
  9908	in the quiet waters; and I could see that this basin of the Antarctic
  9909	Pole serves as a place of refuge to the cetacea too closely tracked by
  9910	the hunters.  I also noticed large medusae floating between the reeds.
  9911	
  9912	At nine we landed; the sky was brightening, the clouds were flying to
  9913	the south, and the fog seemed to be leaving the cold surface of the
  9914	waters.  Captain Nemo went towards the peak, which he doubtless meant
  9915	to be his observatory.  It was a painful ascent over the sharp lava and
  9916	the pumice-stones, in an atmosphere often impregnated with a sulphurous
  9917	smell from the smoking cracks.  For a man unaccustomed to walk on land,
  9918	the Captain climbed the steep slopes with an agility I never saw
  9919	equalled and which a hunter would have envied.  We were two hours
  9920	getting to the summit of this peak, which was half porphyry and half
  9921	basalt.  From thence we looked upon a vast sea which, towards the
  9922	north, distinctly traced its boundary line upon the sky.  At our feet
  9923	lay fields of dazzling whiteness.  Over our heads a pale azure, free
  9924	from fog.  To the north the disc of the sun seemed like a ball of fire,
  9925	already horned by the cutting of the horizon.  From the bosom of the
  9926	water rose sheaves of liquid jets by hundreds.  In the distance lay the
  9927	Nautilus like a cetacean asleep on the water.  Behind us, to the south
  9928	and east, an immense country and a chaotic heap of rocks and ice, the
  9929	limits of which were not visible.  On arriving at the summit Captain
  9930	Nemo carefully took the mean height of the barometer, for he would have
  9931	to consider that in taking his observations.  At a quarter to twelve
  9932	the sun, then seen only by refraction, looked like a golden disc
  9933	shedding its last rays upon this deserted continent and seas which
  9934	never man had yet ploughed.  Captain Nemo, furnished with a lenticular
  9935	glass which, by means of a mirror, corrected the refraction, watched
  9936	the orb sinking below the horizon by degrees, following a lengthened
  9937	diagonal.  I held the chronometer.  My heart beat fast.  If the
  9938	disappearance of the half-disc of the sun coincided with twelve o'clock
  9939	on the chronometer, we were at the pole itself.
  9940	
  9941	"Twelve!"  I exclaimed.
  9942	
  9943	"The South Pole!" replied Captain Nemo, in a grave voice, handing me
  9944	the glass, which showed the orb cut in exactly equal parts by the
  9945	horizon.
  9946	
  9947	I looked at the last rays crowning the peak, and the shadows mounting
  9948	by degrees up its slopes.  At that moment Captain Nemo, resting with
  9949	his hand on my shoulder, said:
  9950	
  9951	"I, Captain Nemo, on this 21st day of March, 1868, have reached the
  9952	South Pole on the ninetieth degree; and I take possession of this part
  9953	of the globe, equal to one-sixth of the known continents."
  9954	
  9955	"In whose name, Captain?"
  9956	
  9957	"In my own, sir!"
  9958	
  9959	Saying which, Captain Nemo unfurled a black banner, bearing an "N" in
  9960	gold quartered on its bunting.  Then, turning towards the orb of day,
  9961	whose last rays lapped the horizon of the sea, he exclaimed:
  9962	
  9963	"Adieu, sun!  Disappear, thou radiant orb! rest beneath this open sea,
  9964	and let a night of six months spread its shadows over my new domains!"
  9965	
  9966	
  9967	
  9968	CHAPTER XV
  9969	
  9970	ACCIDENT OR INCIDENT?
  9971	
  9972	The next day, the 22nd of March, at six in the morning, preparations
  9973	for departure were begun.  The last gleams of twilight were melting
  9974	into night.  The cold was great, the constellations shone with
  9975	wonderful intensity.  In the zenith glittered that wondrous Southern
  9976	Cross--the polar bear of Antarctic regions.  The thermometer showed 120
  9977	below zero, and when the wind freshened it was most biting.  Flakes of
  9978	ice increased on the open water.  The sea seemed everywhere alike.
  9979	Numerous blackish patches spread on the surface, showing the formation
  9980	of fresh ice.  Evidently the southern basin, frozen during the six
  9981	winter months, was absolutely inaccessible.  What became of the whales
  9982	in that time?  Doubtless they went beneath the icebergs, seeking more
  9983	practicable seas.  As to the seals and morses, accustomed to live in a
  9984	hard climate, they remained on these icy shores.  These creatures have
  9985	the instinct to break holes in the ice-field and to keep them open.  To
  9986	these holes they come for breath; when the birds, driven away by the
  9987	cold, have emigrated to the north, these sea mammals remain sole
  9988	masters of the polar continent.  But the reservoirs were filling with
  9989	water, and the Nautilus was slowly descending.  At 1,000 feet deep it
  9990	stopped; its screw beat the waves, and it advanced straight towards the
  9991	north at a speed of fifteen miles an hour.  Towards night it was
  9992	already floating under the immense body of the iceberg.  At three in
  9993	the morning I was awakened by a violent shock.  I sat up in my bed and
  9994	listened in the darkness, when I was thrown into the middle of the
  9995	room.  The Nautilus, after having struck, had rebounded violently.  I
  9996	groped along the partition, and by the staircase to the saloon, which
  9997	was lit by the luminous ceiling.  The furniture was upset.  Fortunately
  9998	the windows were firmly set, and had held fast.  The pictures on the
  9999	starboard side, from being no longer vertical, were clinging to the
 10000	paper, whilst those of the port side were hanging at least a foot from
 10001	the wall.  The Nautilus was lying on its starboard side perfectly
 10002	motionless.  I heard footsteps, and a confusion of voices; but Captain
 10003	Nemo did not appear.  As I was leaving the saloon, Ned Land and Conseil
 10004	entered.
 10005	
 10006	"What is the matter?" said I, at once.
 10007	
 10008	"I came to ask you, sir," replied Conseil.
 10009	
 10010	"Confound it!" exclaimed the Canadian, "I know well enough!  The
 10011	Nautilus has struck; and, judging by the way she lies, I do not think
 10012	she will right herself as she did the first time in Torres Straits."
 10013	
 10014	"But," I asked, "has she at least come to the surface of the sea?"
 10015	
 10016	"We do not know," said Conseil.
 10017	
 10018	"It is easy to decide," I answered.  I consulted the manometer.  To my
 10019	great surprise, it showed a depth of more than 180 fathoms.  "What does
 10020	that mean?"  I exclaimed.
 10021	
 10022	"We must ask Captain Nemo," said Conseil.
 10023	
 10024	"But where shall we find him?" said Ned Land.
 10025	
 10026	"Follow me," said I, to my companions.
 10027	
 10028	We left the saloon.  There was no one in the library.  At the centre
 10029	staircase, by the berths of the ship's crew, there was no one.  I
 10030	thought that Captain Nemo must be in the pilot's cage.  It was best to
 10031	wait.  We all returned to the saloon.  For twenty minutes we remained
 10032	thus, trying to hear the slightest noise which might be made on board
 10033	the Nautilus, when Captain Nemo entered.  He seemed not to see us; his
 10034	face, generally so impassive, showed signs of uneasiness.  He watched
 10035	the compass silently, then the manometer; and, going to the
