 10036	planisphere, placed his finger on a spot representing the southern
 10037	seas.  I would not interrupt him; but, some minutes later, when he
 10038	turned towards me, I said, using one of his own expressions in the
 10039	Torres Straits:
 10040	
 10041	"An incident, Captain?"
 10042	
 10043	"No, sir; an accident this time."
 10044	
 10045	"Serious?"
 10046	
 10047	"Perhaps."
 10048	
 10049	"Is the danger immediate?"
 10050	
 10051	"No."
 10052	
 10053	"The Nautilus has stranded?"
 10054	
 10055	"Yes."
 10056	
 10057	"And this has happened--how?"
 10058	
 10059	"From a caprice of nature, not from the ignorance of man.  Not a
 10060	mistake has been made in the working.  But we cannot prevent
 10061	equilibrium from producing its effects.  We may brave human laws, but
 10062	we cannot resist natural ones."
 10063	
 10064	Captain Nemo had chosen a strange moment for uttering this
 10065	philosophical reflection.  On the whole, his answer helped me little.
 10066	
 10067	"May I ask, sir, the cause of this accident?"
 10068	
 10069	"An enormous block of ice, a whole mountain, has turned over," he
 10070	replied.  "When icebergs are undermined at their base by warmer water
 10071	or reiterated shocks their centre of gravity rises, and the whole thing
 10072	turns over.  This is what has happened; one of these blocks, as it
 10073	fell, struck the Nautilus, then, gliding under its hull, raised it with
 10074	irresistible force, bringing it into beds which are not so thick, where
 10075	it is lying on its side."
 10076	
 10077	"But can we not get the Nautilus off by emptying its reservoirs, that
 10078	it might regain its equilibrium?"
 10079	
 10080	"That, sir, is being done at this moment.  You can hear the pump
 10081	working.  Look at the needle of the manometer; it shows that the
 10082	Nautilus is rising, but the block of ice is floating with it; and,
 10083	until some obstacle stops its ascending motion, our position cannot be
 10084	altered."
 10085	
 10086	Indeed, the Nautilus still held the same position to starboard;
 10087	doubtless it would right itself when the block stopped.  But at this
 10088	moment who knows if we may not be frightfully crushed between the two
 10089	glassy surfaces?  I reflected on all the consequences of our position.
 10090	Captain Nemo never took his eyes off the manometer.  Since the fall of
 10091	the iceberg, the Nautilus had risen about a hundred and fifty feet, but
 10092	it still made the same angle with the perpendicular.  Suddenly a slight
 10093	movement was felt in the hold.  Evidently it was righting a little.
 10094	Things hanging in the saloon were sensibly returning to their normal
 10095	position.  The partitions were nearing the upright.  No one spoke.
 10096	With beating hearts we watched and felt the straightening.  The boards
 10097	became horizontal under our feet.  Ten minutes passed.
 10098	
 10099	"At last we have righted!"  I exclaimed.
 10100	
 10101	"Yes," said Captain Nemo, going to the door of the saloon.
 10102	
 10103	"But are we floating?"  I asked.
 10104	
 10105	"Certainly," he replied; "since the reservoirs are not empty; and, when
 10106	empty, the Nautilus must rise to the surface of the sea."
 10107	
 10108	We were in open sea; but at a distance of about ten yards, on either
 10109	side of the Nautilus, rose a dazzling wall of ice.  Above and beneath
 10110	the same wall.  Above, because the lower surface of the iceberg
 10111	stretched over us like an immense ceiling.  Beneath, because the
 10112	overturned block, having slid by degrees, had found a resting-place on
 10113	the lateral walls, which kept it in that position.  The Nautilus was
 10114	really imprisoned in a perfect tunnel of ice more than twenty yards in
 10115	breadth, filled with quiet water.  It was easy to get out of it by
 10116	going either forward or backward, and then make a free passage under
 10117	the iceberg, some hundreds of yards deeper.  The luminous ceiling had
 10118	been extinguished, but the saloon was still resplendent with intense
 10119	light.  It was the powerful reflection from the glass partition sent
 10120	violently back to the sheets of the lantern.  I cannot describe the
 10121	effect of the voltaic rays upon the great blocks so capriciously cut;
 10122	upon every angle, every ridge, every facet was thrown a different
 10123	light, according to the nature of the veins running through the ice; a
 10124	dazzling mine of gems, particularly of sapphires, their blue rays
 10125	crossing with the green of the emerald.  Here and there were opal
 10126	shades of wonderful softness, running through bright spots like
 10127	diamonds of fire, the brilliancy of which the eye could not bear.  The
 10128	power of the lantern seemed increased a hundredfold, like a lamp
 10129	through the lenticular plates of a first-class lighthouse.
 10130	
 10131	"How beautiful! how beautiful!" cried Conseil.
 10132	
 10133	"Yes," I said, "it is a wonderful sight.  Is it not, Ned?"
 10134	
 10135	"Yes, confound it!  Yes," answered Ned Land, "it is superb!  I am mad
 10136	at being obliged to admit it.  No one has ever seen anything like it;
 10137	but the sight may cost us dear.  And, if I must say all, I think we are
 10138	seeing here things which God never intended man to see."
 10139	
 10140	Ned was right, it was too beautiful.  Suddenly a cry from Conseil made
 10141	me turn.
 10142	
 10143	"What is it?"  I asked.
 10144	
 10145	"Shut your eyes, sir!  Do not look, sir!"  Saying which, Conseil
 10146	clapped his hands over his eyes.
 10147	
 10148	"But what is the matter, my boy?"
 10149	
 10150	"I am dazzled, blinded."
 10151	
 10152	My eyes turned involuntarily towards the glass, but I could not stand
 10153	the fire which seemed to devour them.  I understood what had happened.
 10154	The Nautilus had put on full speed.  All the quiet lustre of the
 10155	ice-walls was at once changed into flashes of lightning.  The fire from
 10156	these myriads of diamonds was blinding.  It required some time to calm
 10157	our troubled looks.  At last the hands were taken down.
 10158	
 10159	"Faith, I should never have believed it," said Conseil.
 10160	
 10161	It was then five in the morning; and at that moment a shock was felt at
 10162	the bows of the Nautilus.  I knew that its spur had struck a block of
 10163	ice.  It must have been a false manoeuvre, for this submarine tunnel,
 10164	obstructed by blocks, was not very easy navigation.  I thought that
 10165	Captain Nemo, by changing his course, would either turn these obstacles
 10166	or else follow the windings of the tunnel.  In any case, the road
 10167	before us could not be entirely blocked.  But, contrary to my
 10168	expectations, the Nautilus took a decided retrograde motion.
 10169	
 10170	"We are going backwards?" said Conseil.
 10171	
 10172	"Yes," I replied.  "This end of the tunnel can have no egress."
 10173	
 10174	"And then?"
 10175	
 10176	"Then," said I, "the working is easy.  We must go back again, and go
 10177	out at the southern opening.  That is all."
 10178	
 10179	In speaking thus, I wished to appear more confident than I really was.
 10180	But the retrograde motion of the Nautilus was increasing; and,
 10181	reversing the screw, it carried us at great speed.
 10182	
 10183	"It will be a hindrance," said Ned.
 10184	
 10185	"What does it matter, some hours more or less, provided we get out at
 10186	last?"
 10187	
 10188	"Yes," repeated Ned Land, "provided we do get out at last!"
 10189	
 10190	For a short time I walked from the saloon to the library.  My
 10191	companions were silent.  I soon threw myself on an ottoman, and took a
 10192	book, which my eyes overran mechanically.  A quarter of an hour after,
 10193	Conseil, approaching me, said, "Is what you are reading very
 10194	interesting, sir?"
 10195	
 10196	"Very interesting!"  I replied.
 10197	
 10198	"I should think so, sir.  It is your own book you are reading."
 10199	
 10200	"My book?"
 10201	
 10202	And indeed I was holding in my hand the work on the Great Submarine
 10203	Depths.  I did not even dream of it.  I closed the book and returned to
 10204	my walk.  Ned and Conseil rose to go.
 10205	
 10206	"Stay here, my friends," said I, detaining them.  "Let us remain
 10207	together until we are out of this block."
 10208	
 10209	"As you please, sir," Conseil replied.
 10210	
 10211	Some hours passed.  I often looked at the instruments hanging from the
 10212	partition.  The manometer showed that the Nautilus kept at a constant
 10213	depth of more than three hundred yards; the compass still pointed to
 10214	south; the log indicated a speed of twenty miles an hour, which, in
 10215	such a cramped space, was very great.  But Captain Nemo knew that he
 10216	could not hasten too much, and that minutes were worth ages to us.  At
 10217	twenty-five minutes past eight a second shock took place, this time
 10218	from behind.  I turned pale.  My companions were close by my side.  I
 10219	seized Conseil's hand.  Our looks expressed our feelings better than
 10220	words.  At this moment the Captain entered the saloon.  I went up to
 10221	him.
 10222	
 10223	"Our course is barred southward?"  I asked.
 10224	
 10225	"Yes, sir.  The iceberg has shifted and closed every outlet."
 10226	
 10227	"We are blocked up then?"
 10228	
 10229	"Yes."
 10230	
 10231	
 10232	
 10233	CHAPTER XVI
 10234	
 10235	WANT OF AIR
 10236	
 10237	Thus around the Nautilus, above and below, was an impenetrable wall of
 10238	ice.  We were prisoners to the iceberg.  I watched the Captain.  His
 10239	countenance had resumed its habitual imperturbability.
 10240	
 10241	"Gentlemen," he said calmly, "there are two ways of dying in the
 10242	circumstances in which we are placed."  (This puzzling person had the
 10243	air of a mathematical professor lecturing to his pupils.) "The first is
 10244	to be crushed; the second is to die of suffocation.  I do not speak of
 10245	the possibility of dying of hunger, for the supply of provisions in the
 10246	Nautilus will certainly last longer than we shall.  Let us, then,
 10247	calculate our chances."
 10248	
 10249	"As to suffocation, Captain," I replied, "that is not to be feared,
 10250	because our reservoirs are full."
 10251	
 10252	"Just so; but they will only yield two days' supply of air.  Now, for
 10253	thirty-six hours we have been hidden under the water, and already the
 10254	heavy atmosphere of the Nautilus requires renewal.  In forty-eight
 10255	hours our reserve will be exhausted."
 10256	
 10257	"Well, Captain, can we be delivered before forty-eight hours?"
 10258	
 10259	"We will attempt it, at least, by piercing the wall that surrounds us."
 10260	
 10261	"On which side?"
 10262	
 10263	"Sound will tell us.  I am going to run the Nautilus aground on the
 10264	lower bank, and my men will attack the iceberg on the side that is
 10265	least thick."
 10266	
 10267	Captain Nemo went out.  Soon I discovered by a hissing noise that the
 10268	water was entering the reservoirs.  The Nautilus sank slowly, and
 10269	rested on the ice at a depth of 350 yards, the depth at which the lower
 10270	bank was immersed.
 10271	
 10272	"My friends," I said, "our situation is serious, but I rely on your
 10273	courage and energy."
 10274	
 10275	"Sir," replied the Canadian, "I am ready to do anything for the general
 10276	safety."
 10277	
 10278	"Good!  Ned," and I held out my hand to the Canadian.
 10279	
 10280	"I will add," he continued, "that, being as handy with the pickaxe as
 10281	with the harpoon, if I can be useful to the Captain, he can command my
 10282	services."
 10283	
 10284	"He will not refuse your help.  Come, Ned!"
 10285	
 10286	I led him to the room where the crew of the Nautilus were putting on
 10287	their cork-jackets. I told the Captain of Ned's proposal, which he
 10288	accepted.  The Canadian put on his sea-costume, and was ready as soon
 10289	as his companions.  When Ned was dressed, I re-entered the
 10290	drawing-room, where the panes of glass were open, and, posted near
 10291	Conseil, I examined the ambient beds that supported the Nautilus.  Some
 10292	instants after, we saw a dozen of the crew set foot on the bank of ice,
 10293	and among them Ned Land, easily known by his stature.  Captain Nemo was
 10294	with them.  Before proceeding to dig the walls, he took the soundings,
 10295	to be sure of working in the right direction.  Long sounding lines were
 10296	sunk in the side walls, but after fifteen yards they were again stopped
 10297	by the thick wall.  It was useless to attack it on the ceiling-like
 10298	surface, since the iceberg itself measured more than 400 yards in
 10299	height.  Captain Nemo then sounded the lower surface.  There ten yards
 10300	of wall separated us from the water, so great was the thickness of the
 10301	ice-field. It was necessary, therefore, to cut from it a piece equal in
 10302	extent to the waterline of the Nautilus.  There were about 6,000 cubic
 10303	yards to detach, so as to dig a hole by which we could descend to the
 10304	ice-field. The work had begun immediately and carried on with
 10305	indefatigable energy.  Instead of digging round the Nautilus which
 10306	would have involved greater difficulty, Captain Nemo had an immense
 10307	trench made at eight yards from the port-quarter. Then the men set to
 10308	work simultaneously with their screws on several points of its
 10309	circumference.  Presently the pickaxe attacked this compact matter
 10310	vigorously, and large blocks were detached from the mass.  By a curious
 10311	effect of specific gravity, these blocks, lighter than water, fled, so
 10312	to speak, to the vault of the tunnel, that increased in thickness at
 10313	the top in proportion as it diminished at the base.  But that mattered
 10314	little, so long as the lower part grew thinner.  After two hours' hard
 10315	work, Ned Land came in exhausted.  He and his comrades were replaced by
 10316	new workers, whom Conseil and I joined.  The second lieutenant of the
 10317	Nautilus superintended us.  The water seemed singularly cold, but I
 10318	soon got warm handling the pickaxe.  My movements were free enough,
 10319	although they were made under a pressure of thirty atmospheres.  When I
 10320	re-entered, after working two hours, to take some food and rest, I
 10321	found a perceptible difference between the pure fluid with which the
 10322	Rouquayrol engine supplied me and the atmosphere of the Nautilus,
 10323	already charged with carbonic acid.  The air had not been renewed for
 10324	forty-eight hours, and its vivifying qualities were considerably
 10325	enfeebled.  However, after a lapse of twelve hours, we had only raised
 10326	a block of ice one yard thick, on the marked surface, which was about
 10327	600 cubic yards!  Reckoning that it took twelve hours to accomplish
 10328	this much it would take five nights and four days to bring this
 10329	enterprise to a satisfactory conclusion.  Five nights and four days!
 10330	And we have only air enough for two days in the reservoirs!  "Without
 10331	taking into account," said Ned, "that, even if we get out of this
 10332	infernal prison, we shall also be imprisoned under the iceberg, shut
 10333	out from all possible communication with the atmosphere." True enough!
 10334	Who could then foresee the minimum of time necessary for our
 10335	deliverance?  We might be suffocated before the Nautilus could regain
 10336	the surface of the waves?  Was it destined to perish in this ice-tomb,
 10337	with all those it enclosed?  The situation was terrible.  But everyone
 10338	had looked the danger in the face, and each was determined to do his
 10339	duty to the last.
 10340	
 10341	As I expected, during the night a new block a yard square was carried
 10342	away, and still further sank the immense hollow.  But in the morning
 10343	when, dressed in my cork-jacket, I traversed the slushy mass at a
 10344	temperature of six or seven degrees below zero, I remarked that the
 10345	side walls were gradually closing in.  The beds of water farthest from
 10346	the trench, that were not warmed by the men's work, showed a tendency
 10347	to solidification.  In presence of this new and imminent danger, what
 10348	would become of our chances of safety, and how hinder the
 10349	solidification of this liquid medium, that would burst the partitions
 10350	of the Nautilus like glass?
 10351	
 10352	I did not tell my companions of this new danger.  What was the good of
 10353	damping the energy they displayed in the painful work of escape?  But
 10354	when I went on board again, I told Captain Nemo of this grave
 10355	complication.
 10356	
 10357	"I know it," he said, in that calm tone which could counteract the most
 10358	terrible apprehensions.  "It is one danger more; but I see no way of
 10359	escaping it; the only chance of safety is to go quicker than
 10360	solidification.  We must be beforehand with it, that is all."
 10361	
 10362	On this day for several hours I used my pickaxe vigorously.  The work
 10363	kept me up.  Besides, to work was to quit the Nautilus, and breathe
 10364	directly the pure air drawn from the reservoirs, and supplied by our
 10365	apparatus, and to quit the impoverished and vitiated atmosphere.
 10366	Towards evening the trench was dug one yard deeper.  When I returned on
 10367	board, I was nearly suffocated by the carbonic acid with which the air
 10368	was filled--ah! if we had only the chemical means to drive away this
 10369	deleterious gas.  We had plenty of oxygen; all this water contained a
 10370	considerable quantity, and by dissolving it with our powerful piles, it
 10371	would restore the vivifying fluid.  I had thought well over it; but of
 10372	what good was that, since the carbonic acid produced by our respiration
 10373	had invaded every part of the vessel?  To absorb it, it was necessary
 10374	to fill some jars with caustic potash, and to shake them incessantly.
 10375	Now this substance was wanting on board, and nothing could replace it.
 10376	On that evening, Captain Nemo ought to open the taps of his reservoirs,
 10377	and let some pure air into the interior of the Nautilus; without this
 10378	precaution we could not get rid of the sense of suffocation.  The next
 10379	day, March 26th, I resumed my miner's work in beginning the fifth yard.
 10380	The side walls and the lower surface of the iceberg thickened visibly.
 10381	It was evident that they would meet before the Nautilus was able to
 10382	disengage itself.  Despair seized me for an instant; my pickaxe nearly
 10383	fell from my hands.  What was the good of digging if I must be
 10384	suffocated, crushed by the water that was turning into stone?--a
 10385	punishment that the ferocity of the savages even would not have
 10386	invented!  Just then Captain Nemo passed near me.  I touched his hand
 10387	and showed him the walls of our prison.  The wall to port had advanced
 10388	to at least four yards from the hull of the Nautilus.  The Captain
 10389	understood me, and signed me to follow him.  We went on board.  I took
 10390	off my cork-jacket and accompanied him into the drawing-room.
 10391	
 10392	"M. Aronnax, we must attempt some desperate means, or we shall be
 10393	sealed up in this solidified water as in cement."
 10394	
 10395	"Yes; but what is to be done?"
 10396	
 10397	"Ah! if my Nautilus were strong enough to bear this pressure without
 10398	being crushed!"
 10399	
 10400	"Well?"  I asked, not catching the Captain's idea.
 10401	
 10402	"Do you not understand," he replied, "that this congelation of water
 10403	will help us?  Do you not see that by its solidification, it would
 10404	burst through this field of ice that imprisons us, as, when it freezes,
 10405	it bursts the hardest stones?  Do you not perceive that it would be an
 10406	agent of safety instead of destruction?"
 10407	
 10408	"Yes, Captain, perhaps.  But, whatever resistance to crushing the
 10409	Nautilus possesses, it could not support this terrible pressure, and
 10410	would be flattened like an iron plate."
 10411	
 10412	"I know it, sir.  Therefore we must not reckon on the aid of nature,
 10413	but on our own exertions.  We must stop this solidification.  Not only
 10414	will the side walls be pressed together; but there is not ten feet of
 10415	water before or behind the Nautilus.  The congelation gains on us on
 10416	all sides."
 10417	
 10418	"How long will the air in the reservoirs last for us to breathe on
 10419	board?"
 10420	
 10421	The Captain looked in my face.  "After to-morrow they will be empty!"
 10422	
 10423	A cold sweat came over me.  However, ought I to have been astonished at
 10424	the answer?  On March 22, the Nautilus was in the open polar seas.  We
 10425	were at 26°. For five days we had lived on the reserve on board.
 10426	And what was left of the respirable air must be kept for the workers.
 10427	Even now, as I write, my recollection is still so vivid that an
 10428	involuntary terror seizes me and my lungs seem to be without air.
 10429	Meanwhile, Captain Nemo reflected silently, and evidently an idea had
 10430	struck him; but he seemed to reject it.  At last, these words escaped
 10431	his lips:
 10432	
 10433	"Boiling water!" he muttered.
 10434	
 10435	"Boiling water?"  I cried.
 10436	
 10437	"Yes, sir.  We are enclosed in a space that is relatively confined.
 10438	Would not jets of boiling water, constantly injected by the pumps,
 10439	raise the temperature in this part and stay the congelation?"
 10440	
 10441	"Let us try it," I said resolutely.
 10442	
 10443	"Let us try it, Professor."
 10444	
 10445	The thermometer then stood at 7° outside.  Captain Nemo took me to
 10446	the galleys, where the vast distillatory machines stood that furnished
 10447	the drinkable water by evaporation.  They filled these with water, and
 10448	all the electric heat from the piles was thrown through the worms
 10449	bathed in the liquid.  In a few minutes this water reached 100°. It
 10450	was directed towards the pumps, while fresh water replaced it in
 10451	proportion.  The heat developed by the troughs was such that cold
 10452	water, drawn up from the sea after only having gone through the
 10453	machines, came boiling into the body of the pump.  The injection was
 10454	begun, and three hours after the thermometer marked 6° below zero
 10455	outside.  One degree was gained.  Two hours later the thermometer only
 10456	marked 4°.
 10457	
 10458	"We shall succeed," I said to the Captain, after having anxiously
 10459	watched the result of the operation.
 10460	
 10461	"I think," he answered, "that we shall not be crushed.  We have no more
 10462	suffocation to fear."
 10463	
 10464	During the night the temperature of the water rose to 1° below
 10465	zero.  The injections could not carry it to a higher point.  But, as
 10466	the congelation of the sea-water produces at least 2°, I was at
 10467	least reassured against the dangers of solidification.
 10468	
 10469	The next day, March 27th, six yards of ice had been cleared, twelve
 10470	feet only remaining to be cleared away.  There was yet forty-eight
 10471	hours' work.  The air could not be renewed in the interior of the
 10472	Nautilus.  And this day would make it worse.  An intolerable weight
 10473	oppressed me.  Towards three o'clock in the evening this feeling rose
 10474	to a violent degree.  Yawns dislocated my jaws.  My lungs panted as
 10475	they inhaled this burning fluid, which became rarefied more and more.
 10476	A moral torpor took hold of me.  I was powerless, almost unconscious.
 10477	My brave Conseil, though exhibiting the same symptoms and suffering in
 10478	the same manner, never left me.  He took my hand and encouraged me, and
 10479	I heard him murmur, "Oh! if I could only not breathe, so as to leave
 10480	more air for my master!"
 10481	
 10482	Tears came into my eyes on hearing him speak thus.  If our situation to
 10483	all was intolerable in the interior, with what haste and gladness would
 10484	we put on our cork-jackets to work in our turn!  Pickaxes sounded on
 10485	the frozen ice-beds. Our arms ached, the skin was torn off our hands.
 10486	But what were these fatigues, what did the wounds matter?  Vital air
 10487	came to the lungs!  We breathed! we breathed!
 10488	
 10489	All this time no one prolonged his voluntary task beyond the prescribed
 10490	time.  His task accomplished, each one handed in turn to his panting
 10491	companions the apparatus that supplied him with life.  Captain Nemo set
 10492	the example, and submitted first to this severe discipline.  When the
 10493	time came, he gave up his apparatus to another and returned to the
 10494	vitiated air on board, calm, unflinching, unmurmuring.
 10495	
 10496	On that day the ordinary work was accomplished with unusual vigour.
 10497	Only two yards remained to be raised from the surface.  Two yards only
 10498	separated us from the open sea.  But the reservoirs were nearly emptied
 10499	of air.  The little that remained ought to be kept for the workers; not
 10500	a particle for the Nautilus.  When I went back on board, I was half
 10501	suffocated.  What a night!  I know not how to describe it.  The next
 10502	day my breathing was oppressed.  Dizziness accompanied the pain in my
 10503	head and made me like a drunken man.  My companions showed the same
 10504	symptoms.  Some of the crew had rattling in the throat.
 10505	
 10506	On that day, the sixth of our imprisonment, Captain Nemo, finding the
 10507	pickaxes work too slowly, resolved to crush the ice-bed that still
 10508	separated us from the liquid sheet.  This man's coolness and energy
 10509	never forsook him.  He subdued his physical pains by moral force.
 10510	
 10511	By his orders the vessel was lightened, that is to say, raised from the
 10512	ice-bed by a change of specific gravity.  When it floated they towed it
 10513	so as to bring it above the immense trench made on the level of the
 10514	water-line. Then, filling his reservoirs of water, he descended and
 10515	shut himself up in the hole.
 10516	
 10517	Just then all the crew came on board, and the double door of
 10518	communication was shut.  The Nautilus then rested on the bed of ice,
 10519	which was not one yard thick, and which the sounding leads had
 10520	perforated in a thousand places.  The taps of the reservoirs were then
 10521	opened, and a hundred cubic yards of water was let in, increasing the
 10522	weight of the Nautilus to 1,800 tons.  We waited, we listened,
 10523	forgetting our sufferings in hope.  Our safety depended on this last
 10524	chance.  Notwithstanding the buzzing in my head, I soon heard the
 10525	humming sound under the hull of the Nautilus.  The ice cracked with a
 10526	singular noise, like tearing paper, and the Nautilus sank.
 10527	
 10528	"We are off!" murmured Conseil in my ear.
 10529	
 10530	I could not answer him.  I seized his hand, and pressed it
 10531	convulsively.  All at once, carried away by its frightful overcharge,
 10532	the Nautilus sank like a bullet under the waters, that is to say, it
 10533	fell as if it was in a vacuum.  Then all the electric force was put on
 10534	the pumps, that soon began to let the water out of the reservoirs.
 10535	After some minutes, our fall was stopped.  Soon, too, the manometer
 10536	indicated an ascending movement.  The screw, going at full speed, made
 10537	the iron hull tremble to its very bolts and drew us towards the north.
 10538	But if this floating under the iceberg is to last another day before we
 10539	reach the open sea, I shall be dead first.
 10540	
 10541	Half stretched upon a divan in the library, I was suffocating.  My face
 10542	was purple, my lips blue, my faculties suspended.  I neither saw nor
 10543	heard.  All notion of time had gone from my mind.  My muscles could not
 10544	contract.  I do not know how many hours passed thus, but I was
 10545	conscious of the agony that was coming over me.  I felt as if I was
 10546	going to die.  Suddenly I came to.  Some breaths of air penetrated my
 10547	lungs.  Had we risen to the surface of the waves?  Were we free of the
 10548	iceberg?  No!  Ned and Conseil, my two brave friends, were sacrificing
 10549	themselves to save me.  Some particles of air still remained at the
 10550	bottom of one apparatus.  Instead of using it, they had kept it for me,
 10551	and, while they were being suffocated, they gave me life, drop by drop.
 10552	I wanted to push back the thing; they held my hands, and for some
 10553	moments I breathed freely.  I looked at the clock; it was eleven in the
 10554	morning.  It ought to be the 28th of March.  The Nautilus went at a
 10555	frightful pace, forty miles an hour.  It literally tore through the
 10556	water.  Where was Captain Nemo?  Had he succumbed?  Were his companions
 10557	dead with him?  At the moment the manometer indicated that we were not
 10558	more than twenty feet from the surface.  A mere plate of ice separated
 10559	us from the atmosphere.  Could we not break it?  Perhaps.  In any case
 10560	the Nautilus was going to attempt it.  I felt that it was in an oblique
 10561	position, lowering the stern, and raising the bows.  The introduction
 10562	of water had been the means of disturbing its equilibrium.  Then,
 10563	impelled by its powerful screw, it attacked the ice-field from beneath
 10564	like a formidable battering-ram.  It broke it by backing and then
 10565	rushing forward against the field, which gradually gave way; and at
 10566	last, dashing suddenly against it, shot forwards on the ice-field, that
 10567	crushed beneath its weight.  The panel was opened--one might say torn
 10568	off--and the pure air came in in abundance to all parts of the Nautilus.
 10569	
 10570	
 10571	
 10572	CHAPTER XVII
 10573	
 10574	FROM CAPE HORN TO THE AMAZON
 10575	
 10576	How I got on to the platform, I have no idea; perhaps the Canadian had
 10577	carried me there.  But I breathed, I inhaled the vivifying sea-air.  My
 10578	two companions were getting drunk with the fresh particles.  The other
 10579	unhappy men had been so long without food, that they could not with
 10580	impunity indulge in the simplest aliments that were given them.  We, on
 10581	the contrary, had no end to restrain ourselves; we could draw this air
 10582	freely into our lungs, and it was the breeze, the breeze alone, that
 10583	filled us with this keen enjoyment.
 10584	
 10585	"Ah!" said Conseil, "how delightful this oxygen is!  Master need not
 10586	fear to breathe it.  There is enough for everybody."
 10587	
 10588	Ned Land did not speak, but he opened his jaws wide enough to frighten
 10589	a shark.  Our strength soon returned, and, when I looked round me, I
 10590	saw we were alone on the platform.  The foreign seamen in the Nautilus
 10591	were contented with the air that circulated in the interior; none of
 10592	them had come to drink in the open air.
 10593	
 10594	The first words I spoke were words of gratitude and thankfulness to my
 10595	two companions.  Ned and Conseil had prolonged my life during the last
 10596	hours of this long agony.  All my gratitude could not repay such
 10597	devotion.
 10598	
 10599	"My friends," said I, "we are bound one to the other for ever, and I am
 10600	under infinite obligations to you."
 10601	
 10602	"Which I shall take advantage of," exclaimed the Canadian.
 10603	
 10604	"What do you mean?" said Conseil.
 10605	
 10606	"I mean that I shall take you with me when I leave this infernal
 10607	Nautilus."
 10608	
 10609	"Well," said Conseil, "after all this, are we going right?"
 10610	
 10611	"Yes," I replied, "for we are going the way of the sun, and here the
 10612	sun is in the north."
 10613	
 10614	"No doubt," said Ned Land; "but it remains to be seen whether he will
 10615	bring the ship into the Pacific or the Atlantic Ocean, that is, into
 10616	frequented or deserted seas."
 10617	
 10618	I could not answer that question, and I feared that Captain Nemo would
 10619	rather take us to the vast ocean that touches the coasts of Asia and
 10620	America at the same time.  He would thus complete the tour round the
 10621	submarine world, and return to those waters in which the Nautilus could
 10622	sail freely.  We ought, before long, to settle this important point.
 10623	The Nautilus went at a rapid pace.  The polar circle was soon passed,
 10624	and the course shaped for Cape Horn.  We were off the American point,
 10625	March 31st, at seven o'clock in the evening.  Then all our past
 10626	sufferings were forgotten.  The remembrance of that imprisonment in the
 10627	ice was effaced from our minds.  We only thought of the future.
 10628	Captain Nemo did not appear again either in the drawing-room or on the
 10629	platform.  The point shown each day on the planisphere, and, marked by
 10630	the lieutenant, showed me the exact direction of the Nautilus.  Now, on
 10631	that evening, it was evident, to, my great satisfaction, that we were
 10632	going back to the North by the Atlantic.  The next day, April 1st, when
 10633	the Nautilus ascended to the surface some minutes before noon, we
 10634	sighted land to the west.  It was Terra del Fuego, which the first
 10635	navigators named thus from seeing the quantity of smoke that rose from
 10636	the natives' huts.  The coast seemed low to me, but in the distance
 10637	rose high mountains.  I even thought I had a glimpse of Mount
 10638	Sarmiento, that rises 2,070 yards above the level of the sea, with a
 10639	very pointed summit, which, according as it is misty or clear, is a
 10640	sign of fine or of wet weather.  At this moment the peak was clearly
 10641	defined against the sky.  The Nautilus, diving again under the water,
 10642	approached the coast, which was only some few miles off.  From the
 10643	glass windows in the drawing-room, I saw long seaweeds and gigantic
 10644	fuci and varech, of which the open polar sea contains so many
 10645	specimens, with their sharp polished filaments; they measured about 300
 10646	yards in length--real cables, thicker than one's thumb; and, having
 10647	great tenacity, they are often used as ropes for vessels.  Another weed
 10648	known as velp, with leaves four feet long, buried in the coral
 10649	concretions, hung at the bottom.  It served as nest and food for
 10650	myriads of crustacea and molluscs, crabs, and cuttlefish.  There seals
 10651	and otters had splendid repasts, eating the flesh of fish with
 10652	sea-vegetables, according to the English fashion.  Over this fertile
 10653	and luxuriant ground the Nautilus passed with great rapidity.  Towards
 10654	evening it approached the Falkland group, the rough summits of which I
 10655	recognised the following day.  The depth of the sea was moderate.  On
 10656	the shores our nets brought in beautiful specimens of sea weed, and
 10657	particularly a certain fucus, the roots of which were filled with the
 10658	best mussels in the world.  Geese and ducks fell by dozens on the
 10659	platform, and soon took their places in the pantry on board.
 10660	
 10661	When the last heights of the Falklands had disappeared from the
 10662	horizon, the Nautilus sank to between twenty and twenty-five yards, and
 10663	followed the American coast.  Captain Nemo did not show himself.  Until
 10664	the 3rd of April we did not quit the shores of Patagonia, sometimes
 10665	under the ocean, sometimes at the surface.  The Nautilus passed beyond
 10666	the large estuary formed by the Uraguay.  Its direction was northwards,
 10667	and followed the long windings of the coast of South America.  We had
 10668	then made 1,600 miles since our embarkation in the seas of Japan.
 10669	About eleven o'clock in the morning the Tropic of Capricorn was crossed
 10670	on the thirty-seventh meridian, and we passed Cape Frio standing out to
 10671	sea.  Captain Nemo, to Ned Land's great displeasure, did not like the
 10672	neighbourhood of the inhabited coasts of Brazil, for we went at a giddy
 10673	speed.  Not a fish, not a bird of the swiftest kind could follow us,
 10674	and the natural curiosities of these seas escaped all observation.
 10675	
 10676	This speed was kept up for several days, and in the evening of the 9th
 10677	of April we sighted the most westerly point of South America that forms
 10678	Cape San Roque.  But then the Nautilus swerved again, and sought the
 10679	lowest depth of a submarine valley which is between this Cape and
 10680	Sierra Leone on the African coast.  This valley bifurcates to the
 10681	parallel of the Antilles, and terminates at the mouth by the enormous
 10682	depression of 9,000 yards.  In this place, the geological basin of the
 10683	ocean forms, as far as the Lesser Antilles, a cliff to three and a half
 10684	miles perpendicular in height, and, at the parallel of the Cape Verde
 10685	Islands, an other wall not less considerable, that encloses thus all
 10686	the sunk continent of the Atlantic.  The bottom of this immense valley
 10687	is dotted with some mountains, that give to these submarine places a
 10688	picturesque aspect.  I speak, moreover, from the manuscript charts that
 10689	were in the library of the Nautilus--charts evidently due to Captain
 10690	Nemo's hand, and made after his personal observations.  For two days
 10691	the desert and deep waters were visited by means of the inclined
 10692	planes.  The Nautilus was furnished with long diagonal broadsides which
 10693	carried it to all elevations.  But on the 11th of April it rose
 10694	suddenly, and land appeared at the mouth of the Amazon River, a vast
 10695	estuary, the embouchure of which is so considerable that it freshens
 10696	the sea-water for the distance of several leagues.
 10697	
 10698	The equator was crossed. Twenty miles to the west were the Guianas, a
 10699	French territory, on which we could have found an easy refuge; but a
 10700	stiff breeze was blowing, and the furious waves would not have allowed
 10701	a single boat to face them. Ned Land understood that, no doubt, for he
 10702	spoke not a word about it. For my part, I made no allusion to his
 10703	schemes of flight, for I would not urge him to make an attempt that
 10704	must inevitably fail. I made the time pass pleasantly by interesting
 10705	studies.  During the days of April 11th and 12th, the Nautilus did not
 10706	leave the surface of the sea, and the net brought in a marvellous haul
 10707	of Zoophytes, fish and reptiles. Some zoophytes had been fished up by
 10708	the chain of the nets; they were for the most part beautiful
 10709	phyctallines, belonging to the actinidian family, and among other
 10710	species the phyctalis protexta, peculiar to that part of the ocean,
 10711	with a little cylindrical trunk, ornamented With vertical lines,
 10712	speckled with red dots, crowning a marvellous blossoming of tentacles.
 10713	As to the molluscs, they consisted of some I had already
 10714	observed--turritellas, olive porphyras, with regular lines
 10715	intercrossed, with red spots standing out plainly against the flesh;
 10716	odd pteroceras, like petrified scorpions; translucid hyaleas,
 10717	argonauts, cuttle-fish (excellent eating), and certain species of
 10718	calmars that naturalists of antiquity have classed amongst the
 10719	flying-fish, and that serve principally for bait for cod-fishing. I had
 10720	now an opportunity of studying several species of fish on these shores.
 10721	Amongst the cartilaginous ones, petromyzons-pricka, a sort of eel,
 10722	fifteen inches long, with a greenish head, violet fins, grey-blue back,
 10723	brown belly, silvered and sown with bright spots, the pupil of the eye
 10724	encircled with gold--a curious animal, that the current of the Amazon
 10725	had drawn to the sea, for they inhabit fresh waters--tuberculated
 10726	streaks, with pointed snouts, and a long loose tail, armed with a long
 10727	jagged sting; little sharks, a yard long, grey and whitish skin, and
 10728	several rows of teeth, bent back, that are generally known by the name
 10729	of pantouffles; vespertilios, a kind of red isosceles triangle, half a
 10730	yard long, to which pectorals are attached by fleshy prolongations that
 10731	make them look like bats, but that their horny appendage, situated near
 10732	the nostrils, has given them the name of sea-unicorns; lastly, some
 10733	species of balistae, the curassavian, whose spots were of a brilliant
 10734	gold colour, and the capriscus of clear violet, and with varying shades
 10735	like a pigeon's throat.
 10736	
 10737	I end here this catalogue, which is somewhat dry perhaps, but very
 10738	exact, with a series of bony fish that I observed in passing belonging
 10739	to the apteronotes, and whose snout is white as snow, the body of a
 10740	beautiful black, marked with a very long loose fleshy strip;
 10741	odontognathes, armed with spikes; sardines nine inches long, glittering
 10742	with a bright silver light; a species of mackerel provided with two
 10743	anal fins; centronotes of a blackish tint, that are fished for with
 10744	torches, long fish, two yards in length, with fat flesh, white and
 10745	firm, which, when they arc fresh, taste like eel, and when dry, like
 10746	smoked salmon; labres, half red, covered with scales only at the bottom
 10747	of the dorsal and anal fins; chrysoptera, on which gold and silver
 10748	blend their brightness with that of the ruby and topaz; golden-tailed
 10749	spares, the flesh of which is extremely delicate, and whose
 10750	phosphorescent properties betray them in the midst of the waters;
 10751	orange-coloured spares with long tongues; maigres, with gold caudal
 10752	fins, dark thorn-tails, anableps of Surinam, etc.
 10753	
 10754	Notwithstanding this "et cetera," I must not omit to mention fish that
 10755	Conseil will long remember, and with good reason. One of our nets had
 10756	hauled up a sort of very flat ray fish, which, with the tail cut off,
 10757	formed a perfect disc, and weighed twenty ounces. It was white
 10758	underneath, red above, with large round spots of dark blue encircled
 10759	with black, very glossy skin, terminating in a bilobed fin. Laid out on
 10760	the platform, it struggled, tried to turn itself by convulsive
 10761	movements, and made so many efforts, that one last turn had nearly sent
 10762	it into the sea. But Conseil, not wishing to let the fish go, rushed to
 10763	it, and, before I could prevent him, had seized it with both hands. In
 10764	a moment he was overthrown, his legs in the air, and half his body
 10765	paralysed, crying--
 10766	
 10767	"Oh! master, master! help me!"
 10768	
 10769	It was the first time the poor boy had spoken to me so familiarly.  The
 10770	Canadian and I took him up, and rubbed his contracted arms till he
 10771	became sensible. The unfortunate Conseil had attacked a cramp-fish of
 10772	the most dangerous kind, the cumana. This odd animal, in a medium
 10773	conductor like water, strikes fish at several yards' distance, so great
 10774	is the power of its electric organ, the two principal surfaces of which
 10775	do not measure less than twenty-seven square feet. The next day, April
 10776	12th, the Nautilus approached the Dutch coast, near the mouth of the
 10777	Maroni. There several groups of sea-cows herded together; they were
 10778	manatees, that, like the dugong and the stellera, belong to the skenian
 10779	order. These beautiful animals, peaceable and inoffensive, from
 10780	eighteen to twenty-one feet in length, weigh at least sixteen
 10781	hundredweight. I told Ned Land and Conseil that provident nature had
 10782	assigned an important role to these mammalia. Indeed, they, like the
 10783	seals, are designed to graze on the submarine prairies, and thus
 10784	destroy the accumulation of weed that obstructs the tropical rivers.
 10785	
 10786	"And do you know," I added, "what has been the result since men have
 10787	almost entirely annihilated this useful race? That the putrefied weeds
 10788	have poisoned the air, and the poisoned air causes the yellow fever,
 10789	that desolates these beautiful countries. Enormous vegetations are
 10790	multiplied under the torrid seas, and the evil is irresistibly
 10791	developed from the mouth of the Rio de la Plata to Florida. If we are
 10792	to believe Toussenel, this plague is nothing to what it would be if the
 10793	seas were cleaned of whales and seals. Then, infested with poulps,
 10794	medusae, and cuttle-fish, they would become immense centres of
 10795	infection, since their waves would not possess 'these vast stomachs
 10796	that God had charged to infest the surface of the seas.'"
 10797	
 10798	
 10799	
 10800	CHAPTER XVIII
 10801	
 10802	THE POULPS
 10803	
 10804	For several days the Nautilus kept off from the American coast.
 10805	Evidently it did not wish to risk the tides of the Gulf of Mexico or of
 10806	the sea of the Antilles.  April 16th, we sighted Martinique and
 10807	Guadaloupe from a distance of about thirty miles.  I saw their tall
 10808	peaks for an instant.  The Canadian, who counted on carrying out his
 10809	projects in the Gulf, by either landing or hailing one of the numerous
 10810	boats that coast from one island to another, was quite disheartened.
 10811	Flight would have been quite practicable, if Ned Land had been able to
 10812	take possession of the boat without the Captain's knowledge.  But in
 10813	the open sea it could not be thought of.  The Canadian, Conseil, and I
 10814	had a long conversation on this subject.  For six months we had been
 10815	prisoners on board the Nautilus.  We had travelled 17,000 leagues; and,
 10816	as Ned Land said, there was no reason why it should come to an end.  We
 10817	could hope nothing from the Captain of the Nautilus, but only from
 10818	ourselves.  Besides, for some time past he had become graver, more
 10819	retired, less sociable.  He seemed to shun me.  I met him rarely.
 10820	Formerly he was pleased to explain the submarine marvels to me; now he
 10821	left me to my studies, and came no more to the saloon.  What change had
 10822	come over him?  For what cause?  For my part, I did not wish to bury
 10823	with me my curious and novel studies.  I had now the power to write the
 10824	true book of the sea; and this book, sooner or later, I wished to see
 10825	daylight.  The land nearest us was the archipelago of the Bahamas.
 10826	There rose high submarine cliffs covered with large weeds.  It was
 10827	about eleven o'clock when Ned Land drew my attention to a formidable
 10828	pricking, like the sting of an ant, which was produced by means of
 10829	large seaweeds.
 10830	
 10831	"Well," I said, "these are proper caverns for poulps, and I should not
 10832	be astonished to see some of these monsters."
 10833	
 10834	"What!" said Conseil; "cuttlefish, real cuttlefish of the cephalopod
 10835	class?"
 10836	
 10837	"No," I said, "poulps of huge dimensions."
 10838	
 10839	"I will never believe that such animals exist," said Ned.
 10840	
 10841	"Well," said Conseil, with the most serious air in the world, "I
 10842	remember perfectly to have seen a large vessel drawn under the waves by
 10843	an octopus's arm."
 10844	
 10845	"You saw that?" said the Canadian.
 10846	
 10847	"Yes, Ned."
 10848	
 10849	"With your own eyes?"
 10850	
 10851	"With my own eyes."
 10852	
 10853	"Where, pray, might that be?"
 10854	
 10855	"At St. Malo," answered Conseil.
 10856	
 10857	"In the port?" said Ned, ironically.
 10858	
 10859	"No; in a church," replied Conseil.
 10860	
 10861	"In a church!" cried the Canadian.
 10862	
 10863	"Yes; friend Ned.  In a picture representing the poulp in question."
 10864	
 10865	"Good!" said Ned Land, bursting out laughing.
 10866	
 10867	"He is quite right," I said.  "I have heard of this picture; but the
 10868	subject represented is taken from a legend, and you know what to think
 10869	of legends in the matter of natural history.  Besides, when it is a
 10870	question of monsters, the imagination is apt to run wild.  Not only is
 10871	it supposed that these poulps can draw down vessels, but a certain
 10872	Olaus Magnus speaks of an octopus a mile long that is more like an
 10873	island than an animal.  It is also said that the Bishop of Nidros was
 10874	building an altar on an immense rock.  Mass finished, the rock began to
 10875	walk, and returned to the sea.  The rock was a poulp.  Another Bishop,
 10876	Pontoppidan, speaks also of a poulp on which a regiment of cavalry
 10877	could manoeuvre.  Lastly, the ancient naturalists speak of monsters
 10878	whose mouths were like gulfs, and which were too large to pass through
 10879	the Straits of Gibraltar."
 10880	
 10881	"But how much is true of these stories?" asked Conseil.
 10882	
 10883	"Nothing, my friends; at least of that which passes the limit of truth
 10884	to get to fable or legend.  Nevertheless, there must be some ground for
 10885	the imagination of the story-tellers. One cannot deny that poulps and
 10886	cuttlefish exist of a large species, inferior, however, to the
 10887	cetaceans.  Aristotle has stated the dimensions of a cuttlefish as five
 10888	cubits, or nine feet two inches.  Our fishermen frequently see some
 10889	that are more than four feet long.  Some skeletons of poulps are
 10890	preserved in the museums of Trieste and Montpelier, that measure two
 10891	yards in length.  Besides, according to the calculations of some
 10892	naturalists, one of these animals only six feet long would have
 10893	tentacles twenty-seven feet long.  That would suffice to make a
 10894	formidable monster."
 10895	
 10896	"Do they fish for them in these days?" asked Ned.
 10897	
 10898	"If they do not fish for them, sailors see them at least.  One of my
 10899	friends, Captain Paul Bos of Havre, has often affirmed that he met one
 10900	of these monsters of colossal dimensions in the Indian seas.  But the
 10901	most astonishing fact, and which does not permit of the denial of the
 10902	existence of these gigantic animals, happened some years ago, in 1861."
 10903	
 10904	"What is the fact?" asked Ned Land.
 10905	
 10906	"This is it.  In 1861, to the north-east of Teneriffe, very nearly in
 10907	the same latitude we are in now, the crew of the despatch-boat Alector
 10908	perceived a monstrous cuttlefish swimming in the waters.  Captain
 10909	Bouguer went near to the animal, and attacked it with harpoon and guns,
 10910	without much success, for balls and harpoons glided over the soft
 10911	flesh.  After several fruitless attempts the crew tried to pass a
 10912	slip-knot round the body of the mollusc.  The noose slipped as far as
 10913	the tail fins and there stopped.  They tried then to haul it on board,
 10914	but its weight was so considerable that the tightness of the cord
 10915	separated the tail from the body, and, deprived of this ornament, he
 10916	disappeared under the water."
 10917	
 10918	"Indeed! is that a fact?"
 10919	
 10920	"An indisputable fact, my good Ned.  They proposed to name this poulp
 10921	`Bouguer's cuttlefish.'"
 10922	
 10923	"What length was it?" asked the Canadian.
 10924	
 10925	"Did it not measure about six yards?" said Conseil, who, posted at the
 10926	window, was examining again the irregular windings of the cliff.
 10927	
 10928	"Precisely," I replied.
 10929	
 10930	"Its head," rejoined Conseil, "was it not crowned with eight tentacles,
 10931	that beat the water like a nest of serpents?"
 10932	
 10933	"Precisely."
 10934	
 10935	"Had not its eyes, placed at the back of its head, considerable
 10936	development?"
 10937	
 10938	"Yes, Conseil."
 10939	
 10940	"And was not its mouth like a parrot's beak?"
 10941	
 10942	"Exactly, Conseil."
 10943	
 10944	"Very well! no offence to master," he replied, quietly; "if this is not
 10945	Bouguer's cuttlefish, it is, at least, one of its brothers."
 10946	
 10947	I looked at Conseil.  Ned Land hurried to the window.
 10948	
 10949	"What a horrible beast!" he cried.
 10950	
 10951	I looked in my turn, and could not repress a gesture of disgust.
 10952	Before my eyes was a horrible monster worthy to figure in the legends
 10953	of the marvellous.  It was an immense cuttlefish, being eight yards
 10954	long.  It swam crossways in the direction of the Nautilus with great
 10955	speed, watching us with its enormous staring green eyes.  Its eight
 10956	arms, or rather feet, fixed to its head, that have given the name of
 10957	cephalopod to these animals, were twice as long as its body, and were
 10958	twisted like the furies' hair.  One could see the 250 air holes on the
 10959	inner side of the tentacles.  The monster's mouth, a horned beak like a
 10960	parrot's, opened and shut vertically.  Its tongue, a horned substance,
 10961	furnished with several rows of pointed teeth, came out quivering from
 10962	this veritable pair of shears.  What a freak of nature, a bird's beak
 10963	on a mollusc!  Its spindle-like body formed a fleshy mass that might
 10964	weigh 4,000 to 5,000 lb.; the, varying colour changing with great
 10965	rapidity, according to the irritation of the animal, passed
 10966	successively from livid grey to reddish brown.  What irritated this
 10967	mollusc?  No doubt the presence of the Nautilus, more formidable than
 10968	itself, and on which its suckers or its jaws had no hold.  Yet, what
 10969	monsters these poulps are! what vitality the Creator has given them!
 10970	what vigour in their movements! and they possess three hearts!  Chance
 10971	had brought us in presence of this cuttlefish, and I did not wish to
 10972	lose the opportunity of carefully studying this specimen of
 10973	cephalopods.  I overcame the horror that inspired me, and, taking a
 10974	pencil, began to draw it.
 10975	
 10976	"Perhaps this is the same which the Alector saw," said Conseil.
 10977	
 10978	"No," replied the Canadian; "for this is whole, and the other had lost
 10979	its tail."
 10980	
 10981	"That is no reason," I replied.  "The arms and tails of these animals
 10982	are re-formed by renewal; and in seven years the tail of Bouguer's
 10983	cuttlefish has no doubt had time to grow."
 10984	
 10985	By this time other poulps appeared at the port light.  I counted seven.
 10986	They formed a procession after the Nautilus, and I heard their beaks
 10987	gnashing against the iron hull.  I continued my work.  These monsters
 10988	kept in the water with such precision that they seemed immovable.
 10989	Suddenly the Nautilus stopped.  A shock made it tremble in every plate.
 10990	
 10991	"Have we struck anything?"  I asked.
 10992	
 10993	"In any case," replied the Canadian, "we shall be free, for we are
 10994	floating."
 10995	
 10996	The Nautilus was floating, no doubt, but it did not move.  A minute
 10997	passed.  Captain Nemo, followed by his lieutenant, entered the
 10998	drawing-room. I had not seen him for some time.  He seemed dull.
 10999	Without noticing or speaking to us, he went to the panel, looked at the
 11000	poulps, and said something to his lieutenant.  The latter went out.
 11001	Soon the panels were shut.  The ceiling was lighted.  I went towards
 11002	the Captain.
 11003	
 11004	"A curious collection of poulps?"  I said.
 11005	
 11006	"Yes, indeed, Mr. Naturalist," he replied; "and we are going to fight
 11007	them, man to beast."
 11008	
 11009	I looked at him.  I thought I had not heard aright.
 11010	
 11011	"Man to beast?"  I repeated.
 11012	
 11013	"Yes, sir.  The screw is stopped.  I think that the horny jaws of one
 11014	of the cuttlefish is entangled in the blades.  That is what prevents
 11015	our moving."
 11016	
 11017	"What are you going to do?"
 11018	
 11019	"Rise to the surface, and slaughter this vermin."
 11020	
 11021	"A difficult enterprise."
 11022	
 11023	"Yes, indeed.  The electric bullets are powerless against the soft
 11024	flesh, where they do not find resistance enough to go off.  But we
 11025	shall attack them with the hatchet."
 11026	
 11027	"And the harpoon, sir," said the Canadian, "if you do not refuse my
 11028	help."
 11029	
 11030	"I will accept it, Master Land."
 11031	
 11032	"We will follow you," I said, and, following Captain Nemo, we went
 11033	towards the central staircase.
 11034	
 11035	There, about ten men with boarding-hatchets were ready for the attack.
 11036	Conseil and I took two hatchets; Ned Land seized a harpoon.  The
 11037	Nautilus had then risen to the surface.  One of the sailors, posted on
 11038	the top ladderstep, unscrewed the bolts of the panels.  But hardly were
 11039	the screws loosed, when the panel rose with great violence, evidently
 11040	drawn by the suckers of a poulp's arm.  Immediately one of these arms
 11041	slid like a serpent down the opening and twenty others were above.
 11042	With one blow of the axe, Captain Nemo cut this formidable tentacle,
 11043	that slid wriggling down the ladder.  Just as we were pressing one on
 11044	the other to reach the platform, two other arms, lashing the air, came
 11045	down on the seaman placed before Captain Nemo, and lifted him up with
 11046	irresistible power.  Captain Nemo uttered a cry, and rushed out.  We
 11047	hurried after him.
 11048	
 11049	What a scene!  The unhappy man, seized by the tentacle and fixed to the
 11050	suckers, was balanced in the air at the caprice of this enormous trunk.
 11051	He rattled in his throat, he was stifled, he cried, "Help! help!"
 11052	These words, spoken in French, startled me!  I had a fellow-countryman
 11053	on board, perhaps several!  That heart-rending cry!  I shall hear it
 11054	all my life.  The unfortunate man was lost.  Who could rescue him from
 11055	that powerful pressure?  However, Captain Nemo had rushed to the poulp,
 11056	and with one blow of the axe had cut through one arm.  His lieutenant
 11057	struggled furiously against other monsters that crept on the flanks of
 11058	the Nautilus.  The crew fought with their axes.  The Canadian, Conseil,
 11059	and I buried our weapons in the fleshy masses; a strong smell of musk
 11060	penetrated the atmosphere.  It was horrible!
 11061	
 11062	For one instant, I thought the unhappy man, entangled with the poulp,
 11063	would be torn from its powerful suction.  Seven of the eight arms had
 11064	been cut off.  One only wriggled in the air, brandishing the victim
 11065	like a feather.  But just as Captain Nemo and his lieutenant threw
 11066	themselves on it, the animal ejected a stream of black liquid.  We were
 11067	blinded with it.  When the cloud dispersed, the cuttlefish had
 11068	disappeared, and my unfortunate countryman with it.  Ten or twelve
 11069	poulps now invaded the platform and sides of the Nautilus.  We rolled
 11070	pell-mell into the midst of this nest of serpents, that wriggled on the
 11071	platform in the waves of blood and ink.  It seemed as though these
 11072	slimy tentacles sprang up like the hydra's heads.  Ned Land's harpoon,
 11073	at each stroke, was plunged into the staring eyes of the cuttle fish.
 11074	But my bold companion was suddenly overturned by the tentacles of a
 11075	monster he had not been able to avoid.
 11076	
 11077	Ah! how my heart beat with emotion and horror!  The formidable beak of
 11078	a cuttlefish was open over Ned Land.  The unhappy man would be cut in
 11079	two.  I rushed to his succour.  But Captain Nemo was before me; his axe
 11080	disappeared between the two enormous jaws, and, miraculously saved, the
 11081	Canadian, rising, plunged his harpoon deep into the triple heart of the
 11082	poulp.
 11083	
 11084	"I owed myself this revenge!" said the Captain to the Canadian.
 11085	
 11086	Ned bowed without replying.  The combat had lasted a quarter of an
 11087	hour.  The monsters, vanquished and mutilated, left us at last, and
 11088	disappeared under the waves.  Captain Nemo, covered with blood, nearly
 11089	exhausted, gazed upon the sea that had swallowed up one of his
 11090	companions, and great tears gathered in his eyes.
 11091	
 11092	
 11093	
 11094	CHAPTER XIX
 11095	
 11096	THE GULF STREAM
 11097	
 11098	This terrible scene of the 20th of April none of us can ever forget.  I
 11099	have written it under the influence of violent emotion.  Since then I
 11100	have revised the recital; I have read it to Conseil and to the
 11101	Canadian.  They found it exact as to facts, but insufficient as to
 11102	effect.  To paint such pictures, one must have the pen of the most
 11103	illustrious of our poets, the author of The Toilers of the Deep.
 11104	
 11105	I have said that Captain Nemo wept while watching the waves; his grief
 11106	was great.  It was the second companion he had lost since our arrival
 11107	on board, and what a death!  That friend, crushed, stifled, bruised by
 11108	the dreadful arms of a poulp, pounded by his iron jaws, would not rest
 11109	with his comrades in the peaceful coral cemetery!  In the midst of the
 11110	struggle, it was the despairing cry uttered by the unfortunate man that
 11111	had torn my heart.  The poor Frenchman, forgetting his conventional
 11112	language, had taken to his own mother tongue, to utter a last appeal!
 11113	Amongst the crew of the Nautilus, associated with the body and soul of
 11114	the Captain, recoiling like him from all contact with men, I had a
 11115	fellow-countryman. Did he alone represent France in this mysterious
 11116	association, evidently composed of individuals of divers nationalities?
 11117	It was one of these insoluble problems that rose up unceasingly before
 11118	my mind!
 11119	
 11120	Captain Nemo entered his room, and I saw him no more for some time.
 11121	But that he was sad and irresolute I could see by the vessel, of which
 11122	he was the soul, and which received all his impressions.  The Nautilus
 11123	did not keep on in its settled course; it floated about like a corpse
 11124	at the will of the waves.  It went at random.  He could not tear
 11125	himself away from the scene of the last struggle, from this sea that
 11126	had devoured one of his men.  Ten days passed thus.  It was not till
 11127	the 1st of May that the Nautilus resumed its northerly course, after
 11128	having sighted the Bahamas at the mouth of the Bahama Canal.  We were
 11129	then following the current from the largest river to the sea, that has
 11130	its banks, its fish, and its proper temperatures.  I mean the Gulf
 11131	Stream.  It is really a river, that flows freely to the middle of the
 11132	Atlantic, and whose waters do not mix with the ocean waters.  It is a
 11133	salt river, salter than the surrounding sea.  Its mean depth is 1,500
 11134	fathoms, its mean breadth ten miles.  In certain places the current
 11135	flows with the speed of two miles and a half an hour.  The body of its
 11136	waters is more considerable than that of all the rivers in the globe.
 11137	It was on this ocean river that the Nautilus then sailed.
 11138	
 11139	I must add that, during the night, the phosphorescent waters of the
 11140	Gulf Stream rivalled the electric power of our watch-light, especially
 11141	in the stormy weather that threatened us so frequently.  May 8th, we
 11142	were still crossing Cape Hatteras, at the height of the North Caroline.
 11143	The width of the Gulf Stream there is seventy-five miles, and its depth
 11144	210 yards.  The Nautilus still went at random; all supervision seemed
 11145	abandoned.  I thought that, under these circumstances, escape would be
 11146	possible.  Indeed, the inhabited shores offered anywhere an easy
 11147	refuge.  The sea was incessantly ploughed by the steamers that ply
 11148	between New York or Boston and the Gulf of Mexico, and overrun day and
 11149	night by the little schooners coasting about the several parts of the
 11150	American coast.  We could hope to be picked up.  It was a favourable
 11151	opportunity, notwithstanding the thirty miles that separated the
 11152	Nautilus from the coasts of the Union.  One unfortunate circumstance
 11153	thwarted the Canadian's plans.  The weather was very bad.  We were
 11154	nearing those shores where tempests are so frequent, that country of
 11155	waterspouts and cyclones actually engendered by the current of the Gulf
 11156	Stream.  To tempt the sea in a frail boat was certain destruction.  Ned
 11157	Land owned this himself.  He fretted, seized with nostalgia that flight
 11158	only could cure.
 11159	
 11160	"Master," he said that day to me, "this must come to an end.  I must
 11161	make a clean breast of it.  This Nemo is leaving land and going up to
 11162	the north.  But I declare to you that I have had enough of the South
 11163	Pole, and I will not follow him to the North."
 11164	
 11165	"What is to be done, Ned, since flight is impracticable just now?"
 11166	
 11167	"We must speak to the Captain," said he; "you said nothing when we were
 11168	in your native seas.  I will speak, now we are in mine.  When I think
 11169	that before long the Nautilus will be by Nova Scotia, and that there
 11170	near New foundland is a large bay, and into that bay the St. Lawrence
 11171	empties itself, and that the St. Lawrence is my river, the river by
 11172	Quebec, my native town--when I think of this, I feel furious, it makes
 11173	my hair stand on end.  Sir, I would rather throw myself into the sea!
 11174	I will not stay here!  I am stifled!"
 11175	
 11176	The Canadian was evidently losing all patience.  His vigorous nature
 11177	could not stand this prolonged imprisonment.  His face altered daily;
 11178	his temper became more surly.  I knew what he must suffer, for I was
 11179	seized with home-sickness myself.  Nearly seven months had passed
 11180	without our having had any news from land; Captain Nemo's isolation,
 11181	his altered spirits, especially since the fight with the poulps, his
 11182	taciturnity, all made me view things in a different light.
 11183	
 11184	"Well, sir?" said Ned, seeing I did not reply.
 11185	
 11186	"Well, Ned, do you wish me to ask Captain Nemo his intentions
 11187	concerning us?"
 11188	
 11189	"Yes, sir."
 11190	
 11191	"Although he has already made them known?"
 11192	
 11193	"Yes; I wish it settled finally.  Speak for me, in my name only, if you
 11194	like."
 11195	
 11196	"But I so seldom meet him.  He avoids me."
 11197	
 11198	"That is all the more reason for you to go to see him."
 11199	
 11200	I went to my room.  From thence I meant to go to Captain Nemo's.  It
 11201	would not do to let this opportunity of meeting him slip.  I knocked at
 11202	the door.  No answer.  I knocked again, then turned the handle.  The
 11203	door opened, I went in.  The Captain was there.  Bending over his
 11204	work-table, he had not heard me.  Resolved not to go without having
 11205	spoken, I approached him.  He raised his head quickly, frowned, and
 11206	said roughly, "You here!  What do you want?"
 11207	
 11208	"To speak to you, Captain."
 11209	
 11210	"But I am busy, sir; I am working.  I leave you at liberty to shut
 11211	yourself up; cannot I be allowed the same?"
 11212	
 11213	This reception was not encouraging; but I was determined to hear and
 11214	answer everything.
 11215	
 11216	"Sir," I said coldly, "I have to speak to you on a matter that admits
 11217	of no delay."
 11218	
 11219	"What is that, sir?" he replied, ironically.  "Have you discovered
 11220	something that has escaped me, or has the sea delivered up any new
 11221	secrets?"
 11222	
 11223	We were at cross-purposes. But, before I could reply, he showed me an
 11224	open manuscript on his table, and said, in a more serious tone, "Here,
 11225	M. Aronnax, is a manuscript written in several languages.  It contains
 11226	the sum of my studies of the sea; and, if it please God, it shall not
 11227	perish with me.  This manuscript, signed with my name, complete with
 11228	the history of my life, will be shut up in a little floating case.  The
 11229	last survivor of all of us on board the Nautilus will throw this case
 11230	into the sea, and it will go whither it is borne by the waves."
 11231	
 11232	This man's name! his history written by himself!  His mystery would
 11233	then be revealed some day.
 11234	
 11235	"Captain," I said, "I can but approve of the idea that makes you act
 11236	thus.  The result of your studies must not be lost.  But the means you
 11237	employ seem to me to be primitive.  Who knows where the winds will
 11238	carry this case, and in whose hands it will fall?  Could you not use
 11239	some other means?  Could not you, or one of yours----"
 11240	
 11241	"Never, sir!" he said, hastily interrupting me.
 11242	
 11243	"But I and my companions are ready to keep this manuscript in store;
 11244	and, if you will put us at liberty----"
 11245	
 11246	"At liberty?" said the Captain, rising.
 11247	
 11248	"Yes, sir; that is the subject on which I wish to question you.  For
 11249	seven months we have been here on board, and I ask you to-day, in the
 11250	name of my companions and in my own, if your intention is to keep us
 11251	here always?"
 11252	
 11253	"M. Aronnax, I will answer you to-day as I did seven months ago:
 11254	Whoever enters the Nautilus, must never quit it."
 11255	
 11256	"You impose actual slavery upon us!"
 11257	
 11258	"Give it what name you please."
 11259	
 11260	"But everywhere the slave has the right to regain his liberty."
 11261	
 11262	"Who denies you this right?  Have I ever tried to chain you with an
 11263	oath?"
 11264	
 11265	He looked at me with his arms crossed.
 11266	
 11267	"Sir," I said, "to return a second time to this subject will be neither
 11268	to your nor to my taste; but, as we have entered upon it, let us go
 11269	through with it.  I repeat, it is not only myself whom it concerns.
 11270	Study is to me a relief, a diversion, a passion that could make me
 11271	forget everything.  Like you, I am willing to live obscure, in the
 11272	frail hope of bequeathing one day, to future time, the result of my
 11273	labours.  But it is otherwise with Ned Land.  Every man, worthy of the
 11274	name, deserves some consideration.  Have you thought that love of
 11275	liberty, hatred of slavery, can give rise to schemes of revenge in a
 11276	nature like the Canadian's; that he could think, attempt, and try----"
 11277	
 11278	I was silenced; Captain Nemo rose.
 11279	
 11280	"Whatever Ned Land thinks of, attempts, or tries, what does it matter
 11281	to me?  I did not seek him!  It is not for my pleasure that I keep him
 11282	on board!  As for you, M. Aronnax, you are one of those who can
 11283	understand everything, even silence.  I have nothing more to say to
 11284	you.  Let this first time you have come to treat of this subject be the
 11285	last, for a second time I will not listen to you."
 11286	
 11287	I retired.  Our situation was critical.  I related my conversation to
 11288	my two companions.
 11289	
 11290	"We know now," said Ned, "that we can expect nothing from this man.
 11291	The Nautilus is nearing Long Island.  We will escape, whatever the
 11292	weather may be."
 11293	
 11294	But the sky became more and more threatening.  Symptoms of a hurricane
 11295	became manifest.  The atmosphere was becoming white and misty.  On the
 11296	horizon fine streaks of cirrhous clouds were succeeded by masses of
 11297	cumuli.  Other low clouds passed swiftly by.  The swollen sea rose in
 11298	huge billows.  The birds disappeared with the exception of the petrels,
 11299	those friends of the storm.  The barometer fell sensibly, and indicated
 11300	an extreme extension of the vapours.  The mixture of the storm glass
 11301	was decomposed under the influence of the electricity that pervaded the
 11302	atmosphere.  The tempest burst on the 18th of May, just as the Nautilus
 11303	was floating off Long Island, some miles from the port of New York.  I
 11304	can describe this strife of the elements! for, instead of fleeing to
 11305	the depths of the sea, Captain Nemo, by an unaccountable caprice, would
 11306	brave it at the surface.  The wind blew from the south-west at first.
 11307	Captain Nemo, during the squalls, had taken his place on the platform.
 11308	He had made himself fast, to prevent being washed overboard by the
 11309	monstrous waves.  I had hoisted myself up, and made myself fast also,
 11310	dividing my admiration between the tempest and this extraordinary man
 11311	who was coping with it.  The raging sea was swept by huge cloud-drifts,
 11312	which were actually saturated with the waves.  The Nautilus, sometimes
 11313	lying on its side, sometimes standing up like a mast, rolled and
 11314	pitched terribly.  About five o'clock a torrent of rain fell, that
 11315	lulled neither sea nor wind.  The hurri cane blew nearly forty leagues
 11316	an hour.  It is under these conditions that it overturns houses, breaks
 11317	iron gates, displaces twenty-four pounders.  However, the Nautilus, in
 11318	the midst of the tempest, confirmed the words of a clever engineer,
 11319	"There is no well-constructed hull that cannot defy the sea." This was
 11320	not a resisting rock; it was a steel spindle, obedient and movable,
 11321	without rigging or masts, that braved its fury with impunity.  However,
 11322	I watched these raging waves attentively.  They measured fifteen feet
 11323	in height, and 150 to 175 yards long, and their speed of propagation
 11324	was thirty feet per second.  Their bulk and power increased with the
 11325	depth of the water.  Such waves as these, at the Hebrides, have
 11326	displaced a mass weighing 8,400 lb.  They are they which, in the
 11327	tempest of December 23rd, 1864, after destroying the town of Yeddo, in
 11328	Japan, broke the same day on the shores of America.  The intensity of
 11329	the tempest increased with the night.  The barometer, as in 1860 at
 11330	Reunion during a cyclone, fell seven-tenths at the close of day.  I saw
 11331	a large vessel pass the horizon struggling painfully.  She was trying
 11332	to lie to under half steam, to keep up above the waves.  It was
 11333	probably one of the steamers of the line from New York to Liverpool, or
 11334	Havre.  It soon disappeared in the gloom.  At ten o'clock in the
 11335	evening the sky was on fire.  The atmosphere was streaked with vivid
 11336	lightning.  I could not bear the brightness of it; while the captain,
 11337	looking at it, seemed to envy the spirit of the tempest.  A terrible
 11338	noise filled the air, a complex noise, made up of the howls of the
 11339	crushed waves, the roaring of the wind, and the claps of thunder.  The
 11340	wind veered suddenly to all points of the horizon; and the cyclone,
 11341	rising in the east, returned after passing by the north, west, and
 11342	south, in the inverse course pursued by the circular storm of the
 11343	southern hemisphere.  Ah, that Gulf Stream!  It deserves its name of
 11344	the King of Tempests.  It is that which causes those formidable
 11345	cyclones, by the difference of temperature between its air and its
 11346	currents.  A shower of fire had succeeded the rain.  The drops of water
 11347	were changed to sharp spikes.  One would have thought that Captain Nemo
 11348	was courting a death worthy of himself, a death by lightning.  As the
 11349	Nautilus, pitching dreadfully, raised its steel spur in the air, it
 11350	seemed to act as a conductor, and I saw long sparks burst from it.
 11351	Crushed and without strength I crawled to the panel, opened it, and
 11352	descended to the saloon.  The storm was then at its height.  It was
 11353	impossible to stand upright in the interior of the Nautilus.  Captain
 11354	Nemo came down about twelve.  I heard the reservoirs filling by
 11355	degrees, and the Nautilus sank slowly beneath the waves.  Through the
 11356	open windows in the saloon I saw large fish terrified, passing like
 11357	phantoms in the water.  Some were struck before my eyes.  The Nautilus
 11358	was still descending.  I thought that at about eight fathoms deep we
 11359	should find a calm.  But no! the upper beds were too violently agitated
 11360	for that.  We had to seek repose at more than twenty-five fathoms in
 11361	the bowels of the deep.  But there, what quiet, what silence, what
 11362	peace!  Who could have told that such a hurricane had been let loose on
 11363	the surface of that ocean?
 11364	
 11365	
 11366	
 11367	CHAPTER XX
 11368	
 11369	FROM LATITUDE 47° 24' TO LONGITUDE 17° 28'
 11370	
 11371	In consequence of the storm, we had been thrown eastward once more.
 11372	All hope of escape on the shores of New York or St. Lawrence had faded
 11373	away; and poor Ned, in despair, had isolated himself like Captain Nemo.
 11374	Conseil and I, however, never left each other.  I said that the
 11375	Nautilus had gone aside to the east.  I should have said (to be more
 11376	exact) the north-east. For some days, it wandered first on the surface,
 11377	and then beneath it, amid those fogs so dreaded by sailors.  What
 11378	accidents are due to these thick fogs!  What shocks upon these reefs
 11379	when the wind drowns the breaking of the waves!  What collisions
 11380	between vessels, in spite of their warning lights, whistles, and alarm
 11381	bells!  And the bottoms of these seas look like a field of battle,
 11382	where still lie all the conquered of the ocean; some old and already
 11383	encrusted, others fresh and reflecting from their iron bands and copper
 11384	plates the brilliancy of our lantern.
 11385	
 11386	On the 15th of May we were at the extreme south of the Bank of
 11387	Newfoundland.  This bank consists of alluvia, or large heaps of organic
 11388	matter, brought either from the Equator by the Gulf Stream, or from the
 11389	North Pole by the counter-current of cold water which skirts the
 11390	American coast.  There also are heaped up those erratic blocks which
 11391	are carried along by the broken ice; and close by, a vast charnel-house
 11392	of molluscs, which perish here by millions.  The depth of the sea is
 11393	not great at Newfoundland--not more than some hundreds of fathoms; but
 11394	towards the south is a depression of 1,500 fathoms.  There the Gulf
