 11395	Stream widens.  It loses some of its speed and some of its temperature,
 11396	but it becomes a sea.
 11397	
 11398	It was on the 17th of May, about 500 miles from Heart's Content, at a
 11399	depth of more than 1,400 fathoms, that I saw the electric cable lying
 11400	on the bottom.  Conseil, to whom I had not mentioned it, thought at
 11401	first that it was a gigantic sea-serpent. But I undeceived the worthy
 11402	fellow, and by way of consolation related several particulars in the
 11403	laying of this cable.  The first one was laid in the years 1857 and
 11404	1858; but, after transmitting about 400 telegrams, would not act any
 11405	longer.  In 1863 the engineers constructed an other one, measuring
 11406	2,000 miles in length, and weighing 4,500 tons, which was embarked on
 11407	the Great Eastern.  This attempt also failed.
 11408	
 11409	On the 25th of May the Nautilus, being at a depth of more than 1,918
 11410	fathoms, was on the precise spot where the rupture occurred which
 11411	ruined the enterprise.  It was within 638 miles of the coast of
 11412	Ireland; and at half-past two in the afternoon they discovered that
 11413	communication with Europe had ceased.  The electricians on board
 11414	resolved to cut the cable before fishing it up, and at eleven o'clock
 11415	at night they had recovered the damaged part.  They made another point
 11416	and spliced it, and it was once more submerged.  But some days after it
 11417	broke again, and in the depths of the ocean could not be recaptured.
 11418	The Americans, however, were not discouraged.  Cyrus Field, the bold
 11419	promoter of the enterprise, as he had sunk all his own fortune, set a
 11420	new subscription on foot, which was at once answered, and another cable
 11421	was constructed on better principles.  The bundles of conducting wires
 11422	were each enveloped in gutta-percha, and protected by a wadding of
 11423	hemp, contained in a metallic covering.  The Great Eastern sailed on
 11424	the 13th of July, 1866.  The operation worked well.  But one incident
 11425	occurred.  Several times in unrolling the cable they observed that
 11426	nails had recently been forced into it, evidently with the motive of
 11427	destroying it.  Captain Anderson, the officers, and engineers consulted
 11428	together, and had it posted up that, if the offender was surprised on
 11429	board, he would be thrown without further trial into the sea.  From
 11430	that time the criminal attempt was never repeated.
 11431	
 11432	On the 23rd of July the Great Eastern was not more than 500 miles from
 11433	Newfoundland, when they telegraphed from Ireland the news of the
 11434	armistice concluded between Prussia and Austria after Sadowa.  On the
 11435	27th, in the midst of heavy fogs, they reached the port of Heart's
 11436	Content.  The enterprise was successfully terminated; and for its first
 11437	despatch, young America addressed old Europe in these words of wisdom,
 11438	so rarely understood:  "Glory to God in the highest, and on earth
 11439	peace, goodwill towards men."
 11440	
 11441	I did not expect to find the electric cable in its primitive state,
 11442	such as it was on leaving the manufactory.  The long serpent, covered
 11443	with the remains of shells, bristling with foraminiferae, was encrusted
 11444	with a strong coating which served as a protection against all boring
 11445	molluscs.  It lay quietly sheltered from the motions of the sea, and
 11446	under a favourable pressure for the transmission of the electric spark
 11447	which passes from Europe to America in .32 of a second.  Doubtless this
 11448	cable will last for a great length of time, for they find that the
 11449	gutta-percha covering is improved by the sea-water. Besides, on this
 11450	level, so well chosen, the cable is never so deeply submerged as to
 11451	cause it to break.  The Nautilus followed it to the lowest depth, which
 11452	was more than 2,212 fathoms, and there it lay without any anchorage;
 11453	and then we reached the spot where the accident had taken place in
 11454	1863.  The bottom of the ocean then formed a valley about 100 miles
 11455	broad, in which Mont Blanc might have been placed without its summit
 11456	appearing above the waves.  This valley is closed at the east by a
 11457	perpendicular wall more than 2,000 yards high.  We arrived there on the
 11458	28th of May, and the Nautilus was then not more than 120 miles from
 11459	Ireland.
 11460	
 11461	Was Captain Nemo going to land on the British Isles?  No. To my great
 11462	surprise he made for the south, once more coming back towards European
 11463	seas.  In rounding the Emerald Isle, for one instant I caught sight of
 11464	Cape Clear, and the light which guides the thousands of vessels leaving
 11465	Glasgow or Liverpool.  An important question then arose in my mind.
 11466	Did the Nautilus dare entangle itself in the Manche?  Ned Land, who had
 11467	re-appeared since we had been nearing land, did not cease to question
 11468	me.  How could I answer?  Captain Nemo remained invisible.  After
 11469	having shown the Canadian a glimpse of American shores, was he going to
 11470	show me the coast of France?
 11471	
 11472	But the Nautilus was still going southward.  On the 30th of May, it
 11473	passed in sight of Land's End, between the extreme point of England and
 11474	the Scilly Isles, which were left to starboard.  If we wished to enter
 11475	the Manche, he must go straight to the east.  He did not do so.
 11476	
 11477	During the whole of the 31st of May, the Nautilus described a series of
 11478	circles on the water, which greatly interested me.  It seemed to be
 11479	seeking a spot it had some trouble in finding.  At noon, Captain Nemo
 11480	himself came to work the ship's log.  He spoke no word to me, but
 11481	seemed gloomier than ever.  What could sadden him thus?  Was it his
 11482	proxim ity to European shores?  Had he some recollections of his
 11483	abandoned country?  If not, what did he feel?  Remorse or regret?  For
 11484	a long while this thought haunted my mind, and I had a kind of
 11485	presentiment that before long chance would betray the captain's secrets.
 11486	
 11487	The next day, the 1st of June, the Nautilus continued the same process.
 11488	It was evidently seeking some particular spot in the ocean.  Captain
 11489	Nemo took the sun's altitude as he had done the day before.  The sea
 11490	was beautiful, the sky clear.  About eight miles to the east, a large
 11491	steam vessel could be discerned on the horizon.  No flag fluttered from
 11492	its mast, and I could not discover its nationality.  Some minutes
 11493	before the sun passed the meridian, Captain Nemo took his sextant, and
 11494	watched with great attention.  The perfect rest of the water greatly
 11495	helped the operation.  The Nautilus was motionless; it neither rolled
 11496	nor pitched.
 11497	
 11498	I was on the platform when the altitude was taken, and the Captain
 11499	pronounced these words:  "It is here."
 11500	
 11501	He turned and went below.  Had he seen the vessel which was changing
 11502	its course and seemed to be nearing us?  I could not tell.  I returned
 11503	to the saloon.  The panels closed, I heard the hissing of the water in
 11504	the reservoirs.  The Nautilus began to sink, following a vertical line,
 11505	for its screw communicated no motion to it.  Some minutes later it
 11506	stopped at a depth of more than 420 fathoms, resting on the ground.
 11507	The luminous ceiling was darkened, then the panels were opened, and
 11508	through the glass I saw the sea brilliantly illuminated by the rays of
 11509	our lantern for at least half a mile round us.
 11510	
 11511	I looked to the port side, and saw nothing but an immensity of quiet
 11512	waters.  But to starboard, on the bottom appeared a large protuberance,
 11513	which at once attracted my attention.  One would have thought it a ruin
 11514	buried under a coating of white shells, much resembling a covering of
 11515	snow.  Upon examining the mass attentively, I could recognise the
 11516	ever-thickening form of a vessel bare of its masts, which must have
 11517	sunk.  It certainly belonged to past times.  This wreck, to be thus
 11518	encrusted with the lime of the water, must already be able to count
 11519	many years passed at the bottom of the ocean.
 11520	
 11521	What was this vessel?  Why did the Nautilus visit its tomb?  Could it
 11522	have been aught but a shipwreck which had drawn it under the water?  I
 11523	knew not what to think, when near me in a slow voice I heard Captain
 11524	Nemo say:
 11525	
 11526	"At one time this ship was called the Marseillais.  It carried
 11527	seventy-four guns, and was launched in 1762.  In 1778, the 13th of
 11528	August, commanded by La Poype-Ver trieux, it fought boldly against the
 11529	Preston.  In 1779, on the 4th of July, it was at the taking of Grenada,
 11530	with the squadron of Admiral Estaing.  In 1781, on the 5th of
 11531	September, it took part in the battle of Comte de Grasse, in Chesapeake
 11532	Bay.  In 1794, the French Republic changed its name.  On the 16th of
 11533	April, in the same year, it joined the squadron of Villaret Joyeuse, at
 11534	Brest, being entrusted with the escort of a cargo of corn coming from
 11535	America, under the command of Admiral Van Stebel.  On the 11th and 12th
 11536	Prairal of the second year, this squadron fell in with an English
 11537	vessel.  Sir, to-day is the 13th Prairal, the first of June, 1868.  It
 11538	is now seventy-four years ago, day for day on this very spot, in
 11539	latitude 47° 24', longitude 17° 28', that this vessel, after
 11540	fighting heroically, losing its three masts, with the water in its
 11541	hold, and the third of its crew disabled, preferred sinking with its
 11542	356 sailors to surrendering; and, nailing its colours to the poop,
 11543	disappeared under the waves to the cry of `Long live the Republic!'"
 11544	
 11545	"The Avenger!"  I exclaimed.
 11546	
 11547	"Yes, sir, the Avenger!  A good name!" muttered Captain Nemo, crossing
 11548	his arms.
 11549	
 11550	
 11551	
 11552	CHAPTER XXI
 11553	
 11554	A HECATOMB
 11555	
 11556	The way of describing this unlooked-for scene, the history of the
 11557	patriot ship, told at first so coldly, and the emotion with which this
 11558	strange man pronounced the last words, the name of the Avenger, the
 11559	significance of which could not escape me, all impressed itself deeply
 11560	on my mind.  My eyes did not leave the Captain, who, with his hand
 11561	stretched out to sea, was watching with a glowing eye the glorious
 11562	wreck.  Perhaps I was never to know who he was, from whence he came, or
 11563	where he was going to, but I saw the man move, and apart from the
 11564	_savant_.  It was no common misanthropy which had shut Captain Nemo and
 11565	his companions within the Nautilus, but a hatred, either monstrous or
 11566	sublime, which time could never weaken.  Did this hatred still seek for
 11567	vengeance?  The future would soon teach me that.  But the Nautilus was
 11568	rising slowly to the surface of the sea, and the form of the Avenger
 11569	disappeared by degrees from my sight.  Soon a slight rolling told me
 11570	that we were in the open air.  At that moment a dull boom was heard.  I
 11571	looked at the Captain.  He did not move.
 11572	
 11573	"Captain?" said I.
 11574	
 11575	He did not answer.  I left him and mounted the platform.  Conseil and
 11576	the Canadian were already there.
 11577	
 11578	"Where did that sound come from?"  I asked.
 11579	
 11580	"It was a gunshot," replied Ned Land.
 11581	
 11582	I looked in the direction of the vessel I had already seen.  It was
 11583	nearing the Nautilus, and we could see that it was putting on steam.
 11584	It was within six miles of us.
 11585	
 11586	"What is that ship, Ned?"
 11587	
 11588	"By its rigging, and the height of its lower masts," said the Canadian,
 11589	"I bet she is a ship-of-war. May it reach us; and, if necessary, sink
 11590	this cursed Nautilus."
 11591	
 11592	"Friend Ned," replied Conseil, "what harm can it do to the Nautilus?
 11593	Can it attack it beneath the waves?  Can its cannonade us at the bottom
 11594	of the sea?"
 11595	
 11596	"Tell me, Ned," said I, "can you recognise what country she belongs to?"
 11597	
 11598	The Canadian knitted his eyebrows, dropped his eyelids, and screwed up
 11599	the corners of his eyes, and for a few moments fixed a piercing look
 11600	upon the vessel.
 11601	
 11602	"No, sir," he replied; "I cannot tell what nation she belongs to, for
 11603	she shows no colours.  But I can declare she is a man-of-war, for a
 11604	long pennant flutters from her main mast."
 11605	
 11606	For a quarter of an hour we watched the ship which was steaming towards
 11607	us.  I could not, however, believe that she could see the Nautilus from
 11608	that distance; and still less that she could know what this submarine
 11609	engine was.  Soon the Canadian informed me that she was a large,
 11610	armoured, two-decker ram.  A thick black smoke was pouring from her two
 11611	funnels.  Her closely-furled sails were stopped to her yards.  She
 11612	hoisted no flag at her mizzen-peak. The distance prevented us from
 11613	distinguishing the colours of her pennant, which floated like a thin
 11614	ribbon.  She advanced rapidly.  If Captain Nemo allowed her to
 11615	approach, there was a chance of salvation for us.
 11616	
 11617	"Sir," said Ned Land, "if that vessel passes within a mile of us I
 11618	shall throw myself into the sea, and I should advise you to do the
 11619	same."
 11620	
 11621	I did not reply to the Canadian's suggestion, but continued watching
 11622	the ship.  Whether English, French, American, or Russian, she would be
 11623	sure to take us in if we could only reach her.  Presently a white smoke
 11624	burst from the fore part of the vessel; some seconds after, the water,
 11625	agitated by the fall of a heavy body, splashed the stern of the
 11626	Nautilus, and shortly afterwards a loud explosion struck my ear.
 11627	
 11628	"What! they are firing at us!"  I exclaimed.
 11629	
 11630	"So please you, sir," said Ned, "they have recognised the unicorn, and
 11631	they are firing at us."
 11632	
 11633	"But," I exclaimed, "surely they can see that there are men in the
 11634	case?"
 11635	
 11636	"It is, perhaps, because of that," replied Ned Land, looking at me.
 11637	
 11638	A whole flood of light burst upon my mind.  Doubtless they knew now how
 11639	to believe the stories of the pretended monster.  No doubt, on board
 11640	the Abraham Lincoln, when the Canadian struck it with the harpoon,
 11641	Commander Farragut had recognised in the supposed narwhal a submarine
 11642	vessel, more dangerous than a supernatural cetacean.  Yes, it must have
 11643	been so; and on every sea they were now seeking this engine of
 11644	destruction.  Terrible indeed! if, as we supposed, Captain Nemo
 11645	employed the Nautilus in works of vengeance.  On the night when we were
 11646	imprisoned in that cell, in the midst of the Indian Ocean, had he not
 11647	attacked some vessel?  The man buried in the coral cemetery, had he not
 11648	been a victim to the shock caused by the Nautilus?  Yes, I repeat it,
 11649	it must be so.  One part of the mysterious existence of Captain Nemo
 11650	had been unveiled; and, if his identity had not been recognised, at
 11651	least, the nations united against him were no longer hunting a
 11652	chimerical creature, but a man who had vowed a deadly hatred against
 11653	them.  All the formidable past rose before me.  Instead of meeting
 11654	friends on board the approaching ship, we could only expect pitiless
 11655	enemies.  But the shot rattled about us.  Some of them struck the sea
 11656	and ricochetted, losing themselves in the distance.  But none touched
 11657	the Nautilus.  The vessel was not more than three miles from us.  In
 11658	spite of the serious cannonade, Captain Nemo did not appear on the
 11659	platform; but, if one of the conical projectiles had struck the shell
 11660	of the Nautilus, it would have been fatal.  The Canadian then said,
 11661	"Sir, we must do all we can to get out of this dilemma.  Let us signal
 11662	them.  They will then, perhaps, understand that we are honest folks."
 11663	
 11664	Ned Land took his handkerchief to wave in the air; but he had scarcely
 11665	displayed it, when he was struck down by an iron hand, and fell, in
 11666	spite of his great strength, upon the deck.
 11667	
 11668	"Fool!" exclaimed the Captain, "do you wish to be pierced by the spur
 11669	of the Nautilus before it is hurled at this vessel?"
 11670	
 11671	Captain Nemo was terrible to hear; he was still more terrible to see.
 11672	His face was deadly pale, with a spasm at his heart.  For an instant it
 11673	must have ceased to beat.  His pupils were fearfully contracted.  He
 11674	did not speak, he roared, as, with his body thrown forward, he wrung
 11675	the Canadian's shoulders.  Then, leaving him, and turning to the ship
 11676	of war, whose shot was still raining around him, he exclaimed, with a
 11677	powerful voice, "Ah, ship of an accursed nation, you know who I am!  I
 11678	do not want your colours to know you by!  Look! and I will show you
 11679	mine!"
 11680	
 11681	And on the fore part of the platform Captain Nemo unfurled a black
 11682	flag, similar to the one he had placed at the South Pole.  At that
 11683	moment a shot struck the shell of the Nautilus obliquely, without
 11684	piercing it; and, rebounding near the Captain, was lost in the sea.  He
 11685	shrugged his shoulders; and, addressing me, said shortly, "Go down, you
 11686	and your companions, go down!"
 11687	
 11688	"Sir," I cried, "are you going to attack this vessel?"
 11689	
 11690	"Sir, I am going to sink it."
 11691	
 11692	"You will not do that?"
 11693	
 11694	"I shall do it," he replied coldly.  "And I advise you not to judge me,
 11695	sir.  Fate has shown you what you ought not to have seen.  The attack
 11696	has begun; go down."
 11697	
 11698	"What is this vessel?"
 11699	
 11700	"You do not know?  Very well! so much the better!  Its nationality to
 11701	you, at least, will be a secret.  Go down!"
 11702	
 11703	We could but obey.  About fifteen of the sailors surrounded the
 11704	Captain, looking with implacable hatred at the vessel nearing them.
 11705	One could feel that the same desire of vengeance animated every soul.
 11706	I went down at the moment another projectile struck the Nautilus, and I
 11707	heard the Captain exclaim:
 11708	
 11709	"Strike, mad vessel!  Shower your useless shot!  And then, you will not
 11710	escape the spur of the Nautilus.  But it is not here that you shall
 11711	perish!  I would not have your ruins mingle with those of the Avenger!"
 11712	
 11713	I reached my room.  The Captain and his second had remained on the
 11714	platform.  The screw was set in motion, and the Nautilus, moving with
 11715	speed, was soon beyond the reach of the ship's guns.  But the pursuit
 11716	continued, and Captain Nemo contented himself with keeping his distance.
 11717	
 11718	About four in the afternoon, being no longer able to contain my
 11719	impatience, I went to the central staircase.  The panel was open, and I
 11720	ventured on to the platform.  The Captain was still walking up and down
 11721	with an agitated step.  He was looking at the ship, which was five or
 11722	six miles to leeward.
 11723	
 11724	He was going round it like a wild beast, and, drawing it eastward, he
 11725	allowed them to pursue.  But he did not attack.  Perhaps he still
 11726	hesitated?  I wished to mediate once more.  But I had scarcely spoken,
 11727	when Captain Nemo imposed silence, saying:
 11728	
 11729	"I am the law, and I am the judge!  I am the oppressed, and there is
 11730	the oppressor!  Through him I have lost all that I loved, cherished,
 11731	and venerated--country, wife, children, father, and mother.  I saw all
 11732	perish!  All that I hate is there!  Say no more!"
 11733	
 11734	I cast a last look at the man-of-war, which was putting on steam, and
 11735	rejoined Ned and Conseil.
 11736	
 11737	"We will fly!"  I exclaimed.
 11738	
 11739	"Good!" said Ned.  "What is this vessel?"
 11740	
 11741	"I do not know; but, whatever it is, it will be sunk before night.  In
 11742	any case, it is better to perish with it, than be made accomplices in a
 11743	retaliation the justice of which we cannot judge."
 11744	
 11745	"That is my opinion too," said Ned Land, coolly.  "Let us wait for
 11746	night."
 11747	
 11748	Night arrived.  Deep silence reigned on board.  The compass showed that
 11749	the Nautilus had not altered its course.  It was on the surface,
 11750	rolling slightly.  My companions and I resolved to fly when the vessel
 11751	should be near enough either to hear us or to see us; for the moon,
 11752	which would be full in two or three days, shone brightly.  Once on
 11753	board the ship, if we could not prevent the blow which threatened it,
 11754	we could, at least we would, do all that circumstances would allow.
 11755	Several times I thought the Nautilus was preparing for attack; but
 11756	Captain Nemo contented himself with allowing his adversary to approach,
 11757	and then fled once more before it.
 11758	
 11759	Part of the night passed without any incident.  We watched the
 11760	opportunity for action.  We spoke little, for we were too much moved.
 11761	Ned Land would have thrown himself into the sea, but I forced him to
 11762	wait.  According to my idea, the Nautilus would attack the ship at her
 11763	waterline, and then it would not only be possible, but easy to fly.
 11764	
 11765	At three in the morning, full of uneasiness, I mounted the platform.
 11766	Captain Nemo had not left it.  He was standing at the fore part near
 11767	his flag, which a slight breeze displayed above his head.  He did not
 11768	take his eyes from the vessel.  The intensity of his look seemed to
 11769	attract, and fascinate, and draw it onward more surely than if he had
 11770	been towing it.  The moon was then passing the meridian.  Jupiter was
 11771	rising in the east.  Amid this peaceful scene of nature, sky and ocean
 11772	rivalled each other in tranquillity, the sea offering to the orbs of
 11773	night the finest mirror they could ever have in which to reflect their
 11774	image.  As I thought of the deep calm of these elements, compared with
 11775	all those passions brooding imperceptibly within the Nautilus, I
 11776	shuddered.
 11777	
 11778	The vessel was within two miles of us.  It was ever nearing that
 11779	phosphorescent light which showed the presence of the Nautilus.  I
 11780	could see its green and red lights, and its white lantern hanging from
 11781	the large foremast.  An indistinct vibration quivered through its
 11782	rigging, showing that the furnaces were heated to the uttermost.
 11783	Sheaves of sparks and red ashes flew from the funnels, shining in the
 11784	atmosphere like stars.
 11785	
 11786	I remained thus until six in the morning, without Captain Nemo noticing
 11787	me.  The ship stood about a mile and a half from us, and with the first
 11788	dawn of day the firing began afresh.  The moment could not be far off
 11789	when, the Nautilus attacking its adversary, my companions and myself
 11790	should for ever leave this man.  I was preparing to go down to remind
 11791	them, when the second mounted the platform, accompanied by several
 11792	sailors.  Captain Nemo either did not or would not see them.  Some
 11793	steps were taken which might be called the signal for action.  They
 11794	were very simple.  The iron balustrade around the platform was lowered,
 11795	and the lantern and pilot cages were pushed within the shell until they
 11796	were flush with the deck.  The long surface of the steel cigar no
 11797	longer offered a single point to check its manoeuvres.  I returned to
 11798	the saloon.  The Nautilus still floated; some streaks of light were
 11799	filtering through the liquid beds.  With the undulations of the waves
 11800	the windows were brightened by the red streaks of the rising sun, and
 11801	this dreadful day of the 2nd of June had dawned.
 11802	
 11803	At five o'clock, the log showed that the speed of the Nautilus was
 11804	slackening, and I knew that it was allowing them to draw nearer.
 11805	Besides, the reports were heard more distinctly, and the projectiles,
 11806	labouring through the ambient water, were extinguished with a strange
 11807	hissing noise.
 11808	
 11809	"My friends," said I, "the moment is come.  One grasp of the hand, and
 11810	may God protect us!"
 11811	
 11812	Ned Land was resolute, Conseil calm, myself so nervous that I knew not
 11813	how to contain myself.  We all passed into the library; but the moment
 11814	I pushed the door opening on to the central staircase, I heard the
 11815	upper panel close sharply.  The Canadian rushed on to the stairs, but I
 11816	stopped him.  A well-known hissing noise told me that the water was
 11817	running into the reservoirs, and in a few minutes the Nautilus was some
 11818	yards beneath the surface of the waves.  I understood the manoeuvre.
 11819	It was too late to act.  The Nautilus did not wish to strike at the
 11820	impenetrable cuirass, but below the water-line, where the metallic
 11821	covering no longer protected it.
 11822	
 11823	We were again imprisoned, unwilling witnesses of the dreadful drama
 11824	that was preparing.  We had scarcely time to reflect; taking refuge in
 11825	my room, we looked at each other without speaking.  A deep stupor had
 11826	taken hold of my mind:  thought seemed to stand still.  I was in that
 11827	painful state of expectation preceding a dreadful report.  I waited, I
 11828	listened, every sense was merged in that of hearing!  The speed of the
 11829	Nautilus was accelerated.  It was preparing to rush.  The whole ship
 11830	trembled.  Suddenly I screamed.  I felt the shock, but comparatively
 11831	light.  I felt the penetrating power of the steel spur.  I heard
 11832	rattlings and scrapings.  But the Nautilus, carried along by its
 11833	propelling power, passed through the mass of the vessel like a needle
 11834	through sailcloth!
 11835	
 11836	I could stand it no longer.  Mad, out of my mind, I rushed from my room
 11837	into the saloon.  Captain Nemo was there, mute, gloomy, implacable; he
 11838	was looking through the port panel.  A large mass cast a shadow on the
 11839	water; and, that it might lose nothing of her agony, the Nautilus was
 11840	going down into the abyss with her.  Ten yards from me I saw the open
 11841	shell, through which the water was rushing with the noise of thunder,
 11842	then the double line of guns and the netting.  The bridge was covered
 11843	with black, agitated shadows.
 11844	
 11845	The water was rising.  The poor creatures were crowding the ratlines,
 11846	clinging to the masts, struggling under the water.  It was a human
 11847	ant-heap overtaken by the sea.  Paralysed, stiffened with anguish, my
 11848	hair standing on end, with eyes wide open, panting, without breath, and
 11849	without voice, I too was watching!  An irresistible attraction glued me
 11850	to the glass!  Suddenly an explosion took place.  The compressed air
 11851	blew up her decks, as if the magazines had caught fire.  Then the
 11852	unfortunate vessel sank more rapidly.  Her topmast, laden with victims,
 11853	now appeared; then her spars, bending under the weight of men; and,
 11854	last of all, the top of her mainmast.  Then the dark mass disappeared,
 11855	and with it the dead crew, drawn down by the strong eddy.
 11856	
 11857	I turned to Captain Nemo.  That terrible avenger, a perfect archangel
 11858	of hatred, was still looking.  When all was over, he turned to his
 11859	room, opened the door, and entered.  I followed him with my eyes.  On
 11860	the end wall beneath his heroes, I saw the portrait of a woman, still
 11861	young, and two little children.  Captain Nemo looked at them for some
 11862	moments, stretched his arms towards them, and, kneeling down, burst
 11863	into deep sobs.
 11864	
 11865	
 11866	
 11867	CHAPTER XXII
 11868	
 11869	THE LAST WORDS OF CAPTAIN NEMO
 11870	
 11871	The panels had closed on this dreadful vision, but light had not
 11872	returned to the saloon:  all was silence and darkness within the
 11873	Nautilus.  At wonderful speed, a hundred feet beneath the water, it was
 11874	leaving this desolate spot.  Whither was it going?  To the north or
 11875	south?  Where was the man flying to after such dreadful retaliation?  I
 11876	had returned to my room, where Ned and Conseil had remained silent
 11877	enough.  I felt an insurmountable horror for Captain Nemo.  Whatever he
 11878	had suffered at the hands of these men, he had no right to punish thus.
 11879	He had made me, if not an accomplice, at least a witness of his
 11880	vengeance.  At eleven the electric light reappeared.  I passed into the
 11881	saloon.  It was deserted.  I consulted the different instruments.  The
 11882	Nautilus was flying northward at the rate of twenty-five miles an hour,
 11883	now on the surface, and now thirty feet below it.  On taking the
 11884	bearings by the chart, I saw that we were passing the mouth of the
 11885	Manche, and that our course was hurrying us towards the northern seas
 11886	at a frightful speed.  That night we had crossed two hundred leagues of
 11887	the Atlantic.  The shadows fell, and the sea was covered with darkness
 11888	until the rising of the moon.  I went to my room, but could not sleep.
 11889	I was troubled with dreadful nightmare.  The horrible scene of
 11890	destruction was continually before my eyes.  From that day, who could
 11891	tell into what part of the North Atlantic basin the Nautilus would take
 11892	us?  Still with unaccountable speed.  Still in the midst of these
 11893	northern fogs.  Would it touch at Spitzbergen, or on the shores of Nova
 11894	Zembla?  Should we explore those unknown seas, the White Sea, the Sea
 11895	of Kara, the Gulf of Obi, the Archipelago of Liarrov, and the unknown
 11896	coast of Asia?  I could not say.  I could no longer judge of the time
 11897	that was passing.  The clocks had been stopped on board.  It seemed, as
 11898	in polar countries, that night and day no longer followed their regular
 11899	course.  I felt myself being drawn into that strange region where the
 11900	foundered imagination of Edgar Poe roamed at will.  Like the fabulous
 11901	Gordon Pym, at every moment I expected to see "that veiled human
 11902	figure, of larger proportions than those of any inhabitant of the
 11903	earth, thrown across the cataract which defends the approach to the
 11904	pole." I estimated (though, perhaps, I may be mistaken)--I estimated
 11905	this adventurous course of the Nautilus to have lasted fifteen or
 11906	twenty days.  And I know not how much longer it might have lasted, had
 11907	it not been for the catastrophe which ended this voyage.  Of Captain
 11908	Nemo I saw nothing whatever now, nor of his second.  Not a man of the
 11909	crew was visible for an instant.  The Nautilus was almost incessantly
 11910	under water.  When we came to the surface to renew the air, the panels
 11911	opened and shut mechanically.  There were no more marks on the
 11912	planisphere.  I knew not where we were.  And the Canadian, too, his
 11913	strength and patience at an end, appeared no more.  Conseil could not
 11914	draw a word from him; and, fearing that, in a dreadful fit of madness,
 11915	he might kill himself, watched him with constant devotion.  One morning
 11916	(what date it was I could not say) I had fallen into a heavy sleep
 11917	towards the early hours, a sleep both painful and unhealthy, when I
 11918	suddenly awoke.  Ned Land was leaning over me, saying, in a low voice,
 11919	"We are going to fly."  I sat up.
 11920	
 11921	"When shall we go?"  I asked.
 11922	
 11923	"To-night. All inspection on board the Nautilus seems to have ceased.
 11924	All appear to be stupefied.  You will be ready, sir?"
 11925	
 11926	"Yes; where are we?"
 11927	
 11928	"In sight of land.  I took the reckoning this morning in the
 11929	fog--twenty miles to the east."
 11930	
 11931	"What country is it?"
 11932	
 11933	"I do not know; but, whatever it is, we will take refuge there."
 11934	
 11935	"Yes, Ned, yes.  We will fly to-night, even if the sea should swallow
 11936	us up."
 11937	
 11938	"The sea is bad, the wind violent, but twenty miles in that light boat
 11939	of the Nautilus does not frighten me.  Unknown to the crew, I have been
 11940	able to procure food and some bottles of water."
 11941	
 11942	"I will follow you."
 11943	
 11944	"But," continued the Canadian, "if I am surprised, I will defend
 11945	myself; I will force them to kill me."
 11946	
 11947	"We will die together, friend Ned."
 11948	
 11949	I had made up my mind to all.  The Canadian left me.  I reached the
 11950	platform, on which I could with difficulty support myself against the
 11951	shock of the waves.  The sky was threatening; but, as land was in those
 11952	thick brown shadows, we must fly.  I returned to the saloon, fearing
 11953	and yet hoping to see Captain Nemo, wishing and yet not wishing to see
 11954	him.  What could I have said to him?  Could I hide the involuntary
 11955	horror with which he inspired me?  No. It was better that I should not
 11956	meet him face to face; better to forget him.  And yet----  How long
 11957	seemed that day, the last that I should pass in the Nautilus.  I
 11958	remained alone.  Ned Land and Conseil avoided speaking, for fear of
 11959	betraying themselves.  At six I dined, but I was not hungry; I forced
 11960	myself to eat in spite of my disgust, that I might not weaken myself.
 11961	At half-past six Ned Land came to my room, saying, "We shall not see
 11962	each other again before our departure.  At ten the moon will not be
 11963	risen.  We will profit by the darkness.  Come to the boat; Conseil and
 11964	I will wait for you."
 11965	
 11966	The Canadian went out without giving me time to answer.  Wishing to
 11967	verify the course of the Nautilus, I went to the saloon.  We were
 11968	running N.N.E. at frightful speed, and more than fifty yards deep.  I
 11969	cast a last look on these wonders of nature, on the riches of art
 11970	heaped up in this museum, upon the unrivalled collection destined to
 11971	perish at the bottom of the sea, with him who had formed it.  I wished
 11972	to fix an indelible impression of it in my mind.  I remained an hour
 11973	thus, bathed in the light of that luminous ceiling, and passing in
 11974	review those treasures shining under their glasses.  Then I returned to
 11975	my room.
 11976	
 11977	I dressed myself in strong sea clothing.  I collected my notes, placing
 11978	them carefully about me.  My heart beat loudly.  I could not check its
 11979	pulsations.  Certainly my trouble and agitation would have betrayed me
 11980	to Captain Nemo's eyes.  What was he doing at this moment?  I listened
 11981	at the door of his room.  I heard steps.  Captain Nemo was there.  He
 11982	had not gone to rest.  At every moment I expected to see him appear,
 11983	and ask me why I wished to fly.  I was constantly on the alert.  My
 11984	imagination magnified everything.  The impression became at last so
 11985	poignant that I asked myself if it would not be better to go to the
 11986	Captain's room, see him face to face, and brave him with look and
 11987	gesture.
 11988	
 11989	It was the inspiration of a madman; fortunately I resisted the desire,
 11990	and stretched myself on my bed to quiet my bodily agitation.  My nerves
 11991	were somewhat calmer, but in my excited brain I saw over again all my
 11992	existence on board the Nautilus; every incident, either happy or
 11993	unfortunate, which had happened since my disappearance from the Abraham
 11994	Lincoln--the submarine hunt, the Torres Straits, the savages of Papua,
 11995	the running ashore, the coral cemetery, the passage of Suez, the Island
 11996	of Santorin, the Cretan diver, Vigo Bay, Atlantis, the iceberg, the
 11997	South Pole, the imprisonment in the ice, the fight among the poulps,
 11998	the storm in the Gulf Stream, the Avenger, and the horrible scene of
 11999	the vessel sunk with all her crew.  All these events passed before my
 12000	eyes like scenes in a drama.  Then Captain Nemo seemed to grow
 12001	enormously, his features to assume superhuman proportions.  He was no
 12002	longer my equal, but a man of the waters, the genie of the sea.
 12003	
 12004	It was then half-past nine.  I held my head between my hands to keep it
 12005	from bursting.  I closed my eyes; I would not think any longer.  There
 12006	was another half-hour to wait, another half-hour of a nightmare, which
 12007	might drive me mad.
 12008	
 12009	At that moment I heard the distant strains of the organ, a sad harmony
 12010	to an undefinable chant, the wail of a soul longing to break these
 12011	earthly bonds.  I listened with every sense, scarcely breathing;
 12012	plunged, like Captain Nemo, in that musical ecstasy, which was drawing
 12013	him in spirit to the end of life.
 12014	
 12015	Then a sudden thought terrified me.  Captain Nemo had left his room.
 12016	He was in the saloon, which I must cross to fly.  There I should meet
 12017	him for the last time.  He would see me, perhaps speak to me.  A
 12018	gesture of his might destroy me, a single word chain me on board.
 12019	
 12020	But ten was about to strike.  The moment had come for me to leave my
 12021	room, and join my companions.
 12022	
 12023	I must not hesitate, even if Captain Nemo himself should rise before
 12024	me.  I opened my door carefully; and even then, as it turned on its
 12025	hinges, it seemed to me to make a dreadful noise.  Perhaps it only
 12026	existed in my own imagination.
 12027	
 12028	I crept along the dark stairs of the Nautilus, stopping at each step to
 12029	check the beating of my heart.  I reached the door of the saloon, and
 12030	opened it gently.  It was plunged in profound darkness.  The strains of
 12031	the organ sounded faintly.  Captain Nemo was there.  He did not see me.
 12032	In the full light I do not think he would have noticed me, so entirely
 12033	was he absorbed in the ecstasy.
 12034	
 12035	I crept along the carpet, avoiding the slightest sound which might
 12036	betray my presence.  I was at least five minutes reaching the door, at
 12037	the opposite side, opening into the library.
 12038	
 12039	I was going to open it, when a sigh from Captain Nemo nailed me to the
 12040	spot.  I knew that he was rising.  I could even see him, for the light
 12041	from the library came through to the saloon.  He came towards me
 12042	silently, with his arms crossed, gliding like a spectre rather than
 12043	walking.  His breast was swelling with sobs; and I heard him murmur
 12044	these words (the last which ever struck my ear):
 12045	
 12046	"Almighty God! enough! enough!"
 12047	
 12048	Was it a confession of remorse which thus escaped from this man's
 12049	conscience?
 12050	
 12051	In desperation, I rushed through the library, mounted the central
 12052	staircase, and, following the upper flight, reached the boat.  I crept
 12053	through the opening, which had already admitted my two companions.
 12054	
 12055	"Let us go! let us go!"  I exclaimed.
 12056	
 12057	"Directly!" replied the Canadian.
 12058	
 12059	The orifice in the plates of the Nautilus was first closed, and
 12060	fastened down by means of a false key, with which Ned Land had provided
 12061	himself; the opening in the boat was also closed.  The Canadian began
 12062	to loosen the bolts which still held us to the submarine boat.
 12063	
 12064	Suddenly a noise was heard.  Voices were answering each other loudly.
 12065	What was the matter?  Had they discovered our flight?  I felt Ned Land
 12066	slipping a dagger into my hand.
 12067	
 12068	"Yes," I murmured, "we know how to die!"
 12069	
 12070	The Canadian had stopped in his work.  But one word many times
 12071	repeated, a dreadful word, revealed the cause of the agitation
 12072	spreading on board the Nautilus.  It was not we the crew were looking
 12073	after!
 12074	
 12075	"The maelstrom! the maelstrom!"  Could a more dreadful word in a more
 12076	dreadful situation have sounded in our ears!  We were then upon the
 12077	dangerous coast of Norway.  Was the Nautilus being drawn into this gulf
 12078	at the moment our boat was going to leave its sides?  We knew that at
 12079	the tide the pent-up waters between the islands of Ferroe and Loffoden
 12080	rush with irresistible violence, forming a whirlpool from which no
 12081	vessel ever escapes.  From every point of the horizon enormous waves
 12082	were meeting, forming a gulf justly called the "Navel of the Ocean,"
 12083	whose power of attraction extends to a distance of twelve miles.
 12084	There, not only vessels, but whales are sacrificed, as well as white
 12085	bears from the northern regions.
 12086	
 12087	It is thither that the Nautilus, voluntarily or involuntarily, had been
 12088	run by the Captain.
 12089	
 12090	It was describing a spiral, the circumference of which was lessening by
 12091	degrees, and the boat, which was still fastened to its side, was
 12092	carried along with giddy speed.  I felt that sickly giddiness which
 12093	arises from long-continued whirling round.
 12094	
 12095	We were in dread.  Our horror was at its height, circulation had
 12096	stopped, all nervous influence was annihilated, and we were covered
 12097	with cold sweat, like a sweat of agony!  And what noise around our
 12098	frail bark!  What roarings repeated by the echo miles away!  What an
 12099	uproar was that of the waters broken on the sharp rocks at the bottom,
 12100	where the hardest bodies are crushed, and trees worn away, "with all
 12101	the fur rubbed off," according to the Norwegian phrase!
 12102	
 12103	What a situation to be in!  We rocked frightfully.  The Nautilus
 12104	defended itself like a human being.  Its steel muscles cracked.
 12105	Sometimes it seemed to stand upright, and we with it!
 12106	
 12107	"We must hold on," said Ned, "and look after the bolts.  We may still
 12108	be saved if we stick to the Nautilus."
 12109	
 12110	He had not finished the words, when we heard a crashing noise, the
 12111	bolts gave way, and the boat, torn from its groove, was hurled like a
 12112	stone from a sling into the midst of the whirlpool.
 12113	
 12114	My head struck on a piece of iron, and with the violent shock I lost
 12115	all consciousness.
 12116	
 12117	
 12118	
 12119	CHAPTER XXIII
 12120	
 12121	CONCLUSION
 12122	
 12123	Thus ends the voyage under the seas.  What passed during that
 12124	night--how the boat escaped from the eddies of the maelstrom--how Ned
 12125	Land, Conseil, and myself ever came out of the gulf, I cannot tell.
 12126	
 12127	But when I returned to consciousness, I was lying in a fisherman's hut,
 12128	on the Loffoden Isles.  My two companions, safe and sound, were near me
 12129	holding my hands.  We embraced each other heartily.
 12130	
 12131	At that moment we could not think of returning to France.  The means of
 12132	communication between the north of Norway and the south are rare.  And
 12133	I am therefore obliged to wait for the steamboat running monthly from
 12134	Cape North.
 12135	
 12136	And, among the worthy people who have so kindly received us, I revise
 12137	my record of these adventures once more.  Not a fact has been omitted,
 12138	not a detail exaggerated.  It is a faithful narrative of this
 12139	incredible expedition in an element inaccessible to man, but to which
 12140	Progress will one day open a road.
 12141	
 12142	Shall I be believed?  I do not know.  And it matters little, after all.
 12143	What I now affirm is, that I have a right to speak of these seas, under
 12144	which, in less than ten months, I have crossed 20,000 leagues in that
 12145	submarine tour of the world, which has revealed so many wonders.
 12146	
 12147	But what has become of the Nautilus?  Did it resist the pressure of the
 12148	maelstrom?  Does Captain Nemo still live?  And does he still follow
 12149	under the ocean those frightful retaliations?  Or, did he stop after
 12150	the last hecatomb?
 12151	
 12152	Will the waves one day carry to him this manuscript containing the
 12153	history of his life?  Shall I ever know the name of this man?  Will the
 12154	missing vessel tell us by its nationality that of Captain Nemo?
 12155	
 12156	I hope so.  And I also hope that his powerful vessel has conquered the
 12157	sea at its most terrible gulf, and that the Nautilus has survived where
 12158	so many other vessels have been lost!  If it be so--if Captain Nemo
 12159	still inhabits the ocean, his adopted country, may hatred be appeased
 12160	in that savage heart!  May the contemplation of so many wonders
 12161	extinguish for ever the spirit of vengeance!  May the judge disappear,
 12162	and the philosopher continue the peaceful exploration of the sea!  If
 12163	his destiny be strange, it is also sublime.  Have I not understood it
 12164	myself?  Have I not lived ten months of this unnatural life?  And to
 12165	the question asked by Ecclesiastes three thousand years ago, "That
 12166	which is far off and exceeding deep, who can find it out?" two men
 12167	alone of all now living have the right to give an answer----
 12168	
 12169	CAPTAIN NEMO AND MYSELF.
 12170
